3	3	THE GOLDEN BIRD

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	A certain king had a beautiful garden, and in the garden stood a tree

3	

3	3	which bore golden apples. These apples were always counted, and about

3	

3	3	the time when they began to grow ripe it was found that every night one

3	

3	3	of them was gone. The king became very angry at this, and ordered the

3	

3	3	gardener to keep watch all night under the tree. The gardener set his

3	

3	3	eldest son to watch; but about twelve o’clock he fell asleep, and in

3	

3	3	the morning another of the apples was missing. Then the second son was

3	

3	3	ordered to watch; and at midnight he too fell asleep, and in the morning

3	

3	3	another apple was gone. Then the third son offered to keep watch; but

3	

3	3	the gardener at first would not let him, for fear some harm should come

3	

3	3	to him: however, at last he consented, and the young man laid himself

3	

3	3	under the tree to watch. As the clock struck twelve he heard a rustling

3	

3	3	noise in the air, and a bird came flying that was of pure gold; and as

3	

3	3	it was snapping at one of the apples with its beak, the gardener’s son

3	

3	3	jumped up and shot an arrow at it. But the arrow did the bird no harm;

3	

3	3	only it dropped a golden feather from its tail, and then flew away.

3	

3	3	The golden feather was brought to the king in the morning, and all the

3	

3	3	council was called together. Everyone agreed that it was worth more than

3	

3	3	all the wealth of the kingdom: but the king said, ‘One feather is of no

3	

3	3	use to me, I must have the whole bird.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the gardener’s eldest son set out and thought to find the golden

3	

3	3	bird very easily; and when he had gone but a little way, he came to a

3	

3	3	wood, and by the side of the wood he saw a fox sitting; so he took his

3	

3	3	bow and made ready to shoot at it. Then the fox said, ‘Do not shoot me,

3	

3	3	for I will give you good counsel; I know what your business is, and

3	

3	3	that you want to find the golden bird. You will reach a village in the

3	

3	3	evening; and when you get there, you will see two inns opposite to each

3	

3	3	other, one of which is very pleasant and beautiful to look at: go not in

3	

3	3	there, but rest for the night in the other, though it may appear to you

3	

3	3	to be very poor and mean.’ But the son thought to himself, ‘What can

3	

3	3	such a beast as this know about the matter?’ So he shot his arrow at

3	

3	3	the fox; but he missed it, and it set up its tail above its back and

3	

3	3	ran into the wood. Then he went his way, and in the evening came to

3	

3	3	the village where the two inns were; and in one of these were people

3	

3	3	singing, and dancing, and feasting; but the other looked very dirty,

3	

3	3	and poor. ‘I should be very silly,’ said he, ‘if I went to that shabby

3	

3	3	house, and left this charming place’; so he went into the smart house,

3	

3	3	and ate and drank at his ease, and forgot the bird, and his country too.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Time passed on; and as the eldest son did not come back, and no tidings

3	

3	3	were heard of him, the second son set out, and the same thing happened

3	

3	3	to him. He met the fox, who gave him the good advice: but when he came

3	

3	3	to the two inns, his eldest brother was standing at the window where

3	

3	3	the merrymaking was, and called to him to come in; and he could not

3	

3	3	withstand the temptation, but went in, and forgot the golden bird and

3	

3	3	his country in the same manner.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Time passed on again, and the youngest son too wished to set out into

3	

3	3	the wide world to seek for the golden bird; but his father would not

3	

3	3	listen to it for a long while, for he was very fond of his son, and

3	

3	3	was afraid that some ill luck might happen to him also, and prevent his

3	

3	3	coming back. However, at last it was agreed he should go, for he would

3	

3	3	not rest at home; and as he came to the wood, he met the fox, and heard

3	

3	3	the same good counsel. But he was thankful to the fox, and did not

3	

3	3	attempt his life as his brothers had done; so the fox said, ‘Sit upon my

3	

3	3	tail, and you will travel faster.’ So he sat down, and the fox began to

3	

3	3	run, and away they went over stock and stone so quick that their hair

3	

3	3	whistled in the wind.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When they came to the village, the son followed the fox’s counsel, and

3	

3	3	without looking about him went to the shabby inn and rested there all

3	

3	3	night at his ease. In the morning came the fox again and met him as he

3	

3	3	was beginning his journey, and said, ‘Go straight forward, till you come

3	

3	3	to a castle, before which lie a whole troop of soldiers fast asleep and

3	

3	3	snoring: take no notice of them, but go into the castle and pass on and

3	

3	3	on till you come to a room, where the golden bird sits in a wooden cage;

3	

3	3	close by it stands a beautiful golden cage; but do not try to take the

3	

3	3	bird out of the shabby cage and put it into the handsome one, otherwise

3	

3	3	you will repent it.’ Then the fox stretched out his tail again, and the

3	

3	3	young man sat himself down, and away they went over stock and stone till

3	

3	3	their hair whistled in the wind.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Before the castle gate all was as the fox had said: so the son went in

3	

3	3	and found the chamber where the golden bird hung in a wooden cage, and

3	

3	3	below stood the golden cage, and the three golden apples that had been

3	

3	3	lost were lying close by it. Then thought he to himself, ‘It will be a

3	

3	3	very droll thing to bring away such a fine bird in this shabby cage’; so

3	

3	3	he opened the door and took hold of it and put it into the golden cage.

3	

3	3	But the bird set up such a loud scream that all the soldiers awoke, and

3	

3	3	they took him prisoner and carried him before the king. The next morning

3	

3	3	the court sat to judge him; and when all was heard, it sentenced him to

3	

3	3	die, unless he should bring the king the golden horse which could run as

3	

3	3	swiftly as the wind; and if he did this, he was to have the golden bird

3	

3	3	given him for his own.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So he set out once more on his journey, sighing, and in great despair,

3	

3	3	when on a sudden his friend the fox met him, and said, ‘You see now

3	

3	3	what has happened on account of your not listening to my counsel. I will

3	

3	3	still, however, tell you how to find the golden horse, if you will do as

3	

3	3	I bid you. You must go straight on till you come to the castle where the

3	

3	3	horse stands in his stall: by his side will lie the groom fast asleep

3	

3	3	and snoring: take away the horse quietly, but be sure to put the old

3	

3	3	leathern saddle upon him, and not the golden one that is close by it.’

3	

3	3	Then the son sat down on the fox’s tail, and away they went over stock

3	

3	3	and stone till their hair whistled in the wind.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	All went right, and the groom lay snoring with his hand upon the golden

3	

3	3	saddle. But when the son looked at the horse, he thought it a great pity

3	

3	3	to put the leathern saddle upon it. ‘I will give him the good one,’

3	

3	3	said he; ‘I am sure he deserves it.’ As he took up the golden saddle the

3	

3	3	groom awoke and cried out so loud, that all the guards ran in and took

3	

3	3	him prisoner, and in the morning he was again brought before the court

3	

3	3	to be judged, and was sentenced to die. But it was agreed, that, if he

3	

3	3	could bring thither the beautiful princess, he should live, and have the

3	

3	3	bird and the horse given him for his own.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then he went his way very sorrowful; but the old fox came and said, ‘Why

3	

3	3	did not you listen to me? If you had, you would have carried away

3	

3	3	both the bird and the horse; yet will I once more give you counsel. Go

3	

3	3	straight on, and in the evening you will arrive at a castle. At twelve

3	

3	3	o’clock at night the princess goes to the bathing-house: go up to her

3	

3	3	and give her a kiss, and she will let you lead her away; but take care

3	

3	3	you do not suffer her to go and take leave of her father and mother.’

3	

3	3	Then the fox stretched out his tail, and so away they went over stock

3	

3	3	and stone till their hair whistled again.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	As they came to the castle, all was as the fox had said, and at twelve

3	

3	3	o’clock the young man met the princess going to the bath and gave her the

3	

3	3	kiss, and she agreed to run away with him, but begged with many tears

3	

3	3	that he would let her take leave of her father. At first he refused,

3	

3	3	but she wept still more and more, and fell at his feet, till at last

3	

3	3	he consented; but the moment she came to her father’s house the guards

3	

3	3	awoke and he was taken prisoner again.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then he was brought before the king, and the king said, ‘You shall never

3	

3	3	have my daughter unless in eight days you dig away the hill that stops

3	

3	3	the view from my window.’ Now this hill was so big that the whole world

3	

3	3	could not take it away: and when he had worked for seven days, and had

3	

3	3	done very little, the fox came and said. ‘Lie down and go to sleep; I

3	

3	3	will work for you.’ And in the morning he awoke and the hill was gone;

3	

3	3	so he went merrily to the king, and told him that now that it was

3	

3	3	removed he must give him the princess.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the king was obliged to keep his word, and away went the young man

3	

3	3	and the princess; and the fox came and said to him, ‘We will have all

3	

3	3	three, the princess, the horse, and the bird.’ ‘Ah!’ said the young man,

3	

3	3	‘that would be a great thing, but how can you contrive it?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘If you will only listen,’ said the fox, ‘it can be done. When you come

3	

3	3	to the king, and he asks for the beautiful princess, you must say, “Here

3	

3	3	she is!” Then he will be very joyful; and you will mount the golden

3	

3	3	horse that they are to give you, and put out your hand to take leave of

3	

3	3	them; but shake hands with the princess last. Then lift her quickly on

3	

3	3	to the horse behind you; clap your spurs to his side, and gallop away as

3	

3	3	fast as you can.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	All went right: then the fox said, ‘When you come to the castle where

3	

3	3	the bird is, I will stay with the princess at the door, and you will

3	

3	3	ride in and speak to the king; and when he sees that it is the right

3	

3	3	horse, he will bring out the bird; but you must sit still, and say that

3	

3	3	you want to look at it, to see whether it is the true golden bird; and

3	

3	3	when you get it into your hand, ride away.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	This, too, happened as the fox said; they carried off the bird, the

3	

3	3	princess mounted again, and they rode on to a great wood. Then the fox

3	

3	3	came, and said, ‘Pray kill me, and cut off my head and my feet.’ But the

3	

3	3	young man refused to do it: so the fox said, ‘I will at any rate give

3	

3	3	you good counsel: beware of two things; ransom no one from the gallows,

3	

3	3	and sit down by the side of no river.’ Then away he went. ‘Well,’

3	

3	3	thought the young man, ‘it is no hard matter to keep that advice.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	He rode on with the princess, till at last he came to the village where

3	

3	3	he had left his two brothers. And there he heard a great noise and

3	

3	3	uproar; and when he asked what was the matter, the people said, ‘Two men

3	

3	3	are going to be hanged.’ As he came nearer, he saw that the two men were

3	

3	3	his brothers, who had turned robbers; so he said, ‘Cannot they in any

3	

3	3	way be saved?’ But the people said ‘No,’ unless he would bestow all his

3	

3	3	money upon the rascals and buy their liberty. Then he did not stay to

3	

3	3	think about the matter, but paid what was asked, and his brothers were

3	

3	3	given up, and went on with him towards their home.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And as they came to the wood where the fox first met them, it was so

3	

3	3	cool and pleasant that the two brothers said, ‘Let us sit down by the

3	

3	3	side of the river, and rest a while, to eat and drink.’ So he said,

3	

3	3	‘Yes,’ and forgot the fox’s counsel, and sat down on the side of the

3	

3	3	river; and while he suspected nothing, they came behind, and threw him

3	

3	3	down the bank, and took the princess, the horse, and the bird, and went

3	

3	3	home to the king their master, and said. ‘All this have we won by our

3	

3	3	labour.’ Then there was great rejoicing made; but the horse would not

3	

3	3	eat, the bird would not sing, and the princess wept.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The youngest son fell to the bottom of the river’s bed: luckily it was

3	

3	3	nearly dry, but his bones were almost broken, and the bank was so steep

3	

3	3	that he could find no way to get out. Then the old fox came once more,

3	

3	3	and scolded him for not following his advice; otherwise no evil would

3	

3	3	have befallen him: ‘Yet,’ said he, ‘I cannot leave you here, so lay hold

3	

3	3	of my tail and hold fast.’ Then he pulled him out of the river, and said

3	

3	3	to him, as he got upon the bank, ‘Your brothers have set watch to kill

3	

3	3	you, if they find you in the kingdom.’ So he dressed himself as a poor

3	

3	3	man, and came secretly to the king’s court, and was scarcely within the

3	

3	3	doors when the horse began to eat, and the bird to sing, and the princess

3	

3	3	left off weeping. Then he went to the king, and told him all his

3	

3	3	brothers’ roguery; and they were seized and punished, and he had the

3	

3	3	princess given to him again; and after the king’s death he was heir to

3	

3	3	his kingdom.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	A long while after, he went to walk one day in the wood, and the old fox

3	

3	3	met him, and besought him with tears in his eyes to kill him, and cut

3	

3	3	off his head and feet. And at last he did so, and in a moment the

3	

3	3	fox was changed into a man, and turned out to be the brother of the

3	

3	3	princess, who had been lost a great many many years.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	HANS IN LUCK

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Some men are born to good luck: all they do or try to do comes

3	

3	3	right--all that falls to them is so much gain--all their geese are

3	

3	3	swans--all their cards are trumps--toss them which way you will, they

3	

3	3	will always, like poor puss, alight upon their legs, and only move on so

3	

3	3	much the faster. The world may very likely not always think of them as

3	

3	3	they think of themselves, but what care they for the world? what can it

3	

3	3	know about the matter?

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	One of these lucky beings was neighbour Hans. Seven long years he had

3	

3	3	worked hard for his master. At last he said, ‘Master, my time is up; I

3	

3	3	must go home and see my poor mother once more: so pray pay me my wages

3	

3	3	and let me go.’ And the master said, ‘You have been a faithful and good

3	

3	3	servant, Hans, so your pay shall be handsome.’ Then he gave him a lump

3	

3	3	of silver as big as his head.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Hans took out his pocket-handkerchief, put the piece of silver into it,

3	

3	3	threw it over his shoulder, and jogged off on his road homewards. As he

3	

3	3	went lazily on, dragging one foot after another, a man came in sight,

3	

3	3	trotting gaily along on a capital horse. ‘Ah!’ said Hans aloud, ‘what a

3	

3	3	fine thing it is to ride on horseback! There he sits as easy and happy

3	

3	3	as if he was at home, in the chair by his fireside; he trips against no

3	

3	3	stones, saves shoe-leather, and gets on he hardly knows how.’ Hans did

3	

3	3	not speak so softly but the horseman heard it all, and said, ‘Well,

3	

3	3	friend, why do you go on foot then?’ ‘Ah!’ said he, ‘I have this load to

3	

3	3	carry: to be sure it is silver, but it is so heavy that I can’t hold up

3	

3	3	my head, and you must know it hurts my shoulder sadly.’ ‘What do you say

3	

3	3	of making an exchange?’ said the horseman. ‘I will give you my horse,

3	

3	3	and you shall give me the silver; which will save you a great deal of

3	

3	3	trouble in carrying such a heavy load about with you.’ ‘With all my

3	

3	3	heart,’ said Hans: ‘but as you are so kind to me, I must tell you one

3	

3	3	thing--you will have a weary task to draw that silver about with you.’

3	

3	3	However, the horseman got off, took the silver, helped Hans up, gave him

3	

3	3	the bridle into one hand and the whip into the other, and said, ‘When

3	

3	3	you want to go very fast, smack your lips loudly together, and cry

3	

3	3	“Jip!”’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Hans was delighted as he sat on the horse, drew himself up, squared his

3	

3	3	elbows, turned out his toes, cracked his whip, and rode merrily off, one

3	

3	3	minute whistling a merry tune, and another singing,

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘No care and no sorrow,

3	

3	3	  A fig for the morrow!

3	

3	3	  We’ll laugh and be merry,

3	

3	3	  Sing neigh down derry!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	After a time he thought he should like to go a little faster, so he

3	

3	3	smacked his lips and cried ‘Jip!’ Away went the horse full gallop; and

3	

3	3	before Hans knew what he was about, he was thrown off, and lay on his

3	

3	3	back by the road-side. His horse would have ran off, if a shepherd who

3	

3	3	was coming by, driving a cow, had not stopped it. Hans soon came to

3	

3	3	himself, and got upon his legs again, sadly vexed, and said to the

3	

3	3	shepherd, ‘This riding is no joke, when a man has the luck to get upon

3	

3	3	a beast like this that stumbles and flings him off as if it would break

3	

3	3	his neck. However, I’m off now once for all: I like your cow now a great

3	

3	3	deal better than this smart beast that played me this trick, and has

3	

3	3	spoiled my best coat, you see, in this puddle; which, by the by, smells

3	

3	3	not very like a nosegay. One can walk along at one’s leisure behind that

3	

3	3	cow--keep good company, and have milk, butter, and cheese, every day,

3	

3	3	into the bargain. What would I give to have such a prize!’ ‘Well,’ said

3	

3	3	the shepherd, ‘if you are so fond of her, I will change my cow for your

3	

3	3	horse; I like to do good to my neighbours, even though I lose by it

3	

3	3	myself.’ ‘Done!’ said Hans, merrily. ‘What a noble heart that good man

3	

3	3	has!’ thought he. Then the shepherd jumped upon the horse, wished Hans

3	

3	3	and the cow good morning, and away he rode.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Hans brushed his coat, wiped his face and hands, rested a while, and

3	

3	3	then drove off his cow quietly, and thought his bargain a very lucky

3	

3	3	one. ‘If I have only a piece of bread (and I certainly shall always be

3	

3	3	able to get that), I can, whenever I like, eat my butter and cheese with

3	

3	3	it; and when I am thirsty I can milk my cow and drink the milk: and what

3	

3	3	can I wish for more?’ When he came to an inn, he halted, ate up all his

3	

3	3	bread, and gave away his last penny for a glass of beer. When he had

3	

3	3	rested himself he set off again, driving his cow towards his mother’s

3	

3	3	village. But the heat grew greater as soon as noon came on, till at

3	

3	3	last, as he found himself on a wide heath that would take him more than

3	

3	3	an hour to cross, he began to be so hot and parched that his tongue

3	

3	3	clave to the roof of his mouth. ‘I can find a cure for this,’ thought

3	

3	3	he; ‘now I will milk my cow and quench my thirst’: so he tied her to the

3	

3	3	stump of a tree, and held his leathern cap to milk into; but not a drop

3	

3	3	was to be had. Who would have thought that this cow, which was to bring

3	

3	3	him milk and butter and cheese, was all that time utterly dry? Hans had

3	

3	3	not thought of looking to that.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	While he was trying his luck in milking, and managing the matter very

3	

3	3	clumsily, the uneasy beast began to think him very troublesome; and at

3	

3	3	last gave him such a kick on the head as knocked him down; and there he

3	

3	3	lay a long while senseless. Luckily a butcher soon came by, driving a

3	

3	3	pig in a wheelbarrow. ‘What is the matter with you, my man?’ said the

3	

3	3	butcher, as he helped him up. Hans told him what had happened, how he

3	

3	3	was dry, and wanted to milk his cow, but found the cow was dry too. Then

3	

3	3	the butcher gave him a flask of ale, saying, ‘There, drink and refresh

3	

3	3	yourself; your cow will give you no milk: don’t you see she is an old

3	

3	3	beast, good for nothing but the slaughter-house?’ ‘Alas, alas!’ said

3	

3	3	Hans, ‘who would have thought it? What a shame to take my horse, and

3	

3	3	give me only a dry cow! If I kill her, what will she be good for? I hate

3	

3	3	cow-beef; it is not tender enough for me. If it were a pig now--like

3	

3	3	that fat gentleman you are driving along at his ease--one could do

3	

3	3	something with it; it would at any rate make sausages.’ ‘Well,’ said

3	

3	3	the butcher, ‘I don’t like to say no, when one is asked to do a kind,

3	

3	3	neighbourly thing. To please you I will change, and give you my fine fat

3	

3	3	pig for the cow.’ ‘Heaven reward you for your kindness and self-denial!’

3	

3	3	said Hans, as he gave the butcher the cow; and taking the pig off the

3	

3	3	wheel-barrow, drove it away, holding it by the string that was tied to

3	

3	3	its leg.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So on he jogged, and all seemed now to go right with him: he had met

3	

3	3	with some misfortunes, to be sure; but he was now well repaid for all.

3	

3	3	How could it be otherwise with such a travelling companion as he had at

3	

3	3	last got?

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The next man he met was a countryman carrying a fine white goose. The

3	

3	3	countryman stopped to ask what was o’clock; this led to further chat;

3	

3	3	and Hans told him all his luck, how he had so many good bargains, and

3	

3	3	how all the world went gay and smiling with him. The countryman then

3	

3	3	began to tell his tale, and said he was going to take the goose to a

3	

3	3	christening. ‘Feel,’ said he, ‘how heavy it is, and yet it is only eight

3	

3	3	weeks old. Whoever roasts and eats it will find plenty of fat upon it,

3	

3	3	it has lived so well!’ ‘You’re right,’ said Hans, as he weighed it in

3	

3	3	his hand; ‘but if you talk of fat, my pig is no trifle.’ Meantime the

3	

3	3	countryman began to look grave, and shook his head. ‘Hark ye!’ said he,

3	

3	3	‘my worthy friend, you seem a good sort of fellow, so I can’t help doing

3	

3	3	you a kind turn. Your pig may get you into a scrape. In the village I

3	

3	3	just came from, the squire has had a pig stolen out of his sty. I was

3	

3	3	dreadfully afraid when I saw you that you had got the squire’s pig. If

3	

3	3	you have, and they catch you, it will be a bad job for you. The least

3	

3	3	they will do will be to throw you into the horse-pond. Can you swim?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Poor Hans was sadly frightened. ‘Good man,’ cried he, ‘pray get me out

3	

3	3	of this scrape. I know nothing of where the pig was either bred or born;

3	

3	3	but he may have been the squire’s for aught I can tell: you know this

3	

3	3	country better than I do, take my pig and give me the goose.’ ‘I ought

3	

3	3	to have something into the bargain,’ said the countryman; ‘give a fat

3	

3	3	goose for a pig, indeed! ‘Tis not everyone would do so much for you as

3	

3	3	that. However, I will not be hard upon you, as you are in trouble.’ Then

3	

3	3	he took the string in his hand, and drove off the pig by a side path;

3	

3	3	while Hans went on the way homewards free from care. ‘After all,’

3	

3	3	thought he, ‘that chap is pretty well taken in. I don’t care whose pig

3	

3	3	it is, but wherever it came from it has been a very good friend to me. I

3	

3	3	have much the best of the bargain. First there will be a capital roast;

3	

3	3	then the fat will find me in goose-grease for six months; and then there

3	

3	3	are all the beautiful white feathers. I will put them into my pillow,

3	

3	3	and then I am sure I shall sleep soundly without rocking. How happy my

3	

3	3	mother will be! Talk of a pig, indeed! Give me a fine fat goose.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	As he came to the next village, he saw a scissor-grinder with his wheel,

3	

3	3	working and singing,

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘O’er hill and o’er dale

3	

3	3	  So happy I roam,

3	

3	3	  Work light and live well,

3	

3	3	  All the world is my home;

3	

3	3	  Then who so blythe, so merry as I?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Hans stood looking on for a while, and at last said, ‘You must be well

3	

3	3	off, master grinder! you seem so happy at your work.’ ‘Yes,’ said the

3	

3	3	other, ‘mine is a golden trade; a good grinder never puts his hand

3	

3	3	into his pocket without finding money in it--but where did you get that

3	

3	3	beautiful goose?’ ‘I did not buy it, I gave a pig for it.’ ‘And where

3	

3	3	did you get the pig?’ ‘I gave a cow for it.’ ‘And the cow?’ ‘I gave a

3	

3	3	horse for it.’ ‘And the horse?’ ‘I gave a lump of silver as big as my

3	

3	3	head for it.’ ‘And the silver?’ ‘Oh! I worked hard for that seven long

3	

3	3	years.’ ‘You have thriven well in the world hitherto,’ said the grinder,

3	

3	3	‘now if you could find money in your pocket whenever you put your hand

3	

3	3	in it, your fortune would be made.’ ‘Very true: but how is that to be

3	

3	3	managed?’ ‘How? Why, you must turn grinder like myself,’ said the other;

3	

3	3	‘you only want a grindstone; the rest will come of itself. Here is one

3	

3	3	that is but little the worse for wear: I would not ask more than the

3	

3	3	value of your goose for it--will you buy?’ ‘How can you ask?’ said

3	

3	3	Hans; ‘I should be the happiest man in the world, if I could have money

3	

3	3	whenever I put my hand in my pocket: what could I want more? there’s

3	

3	3	the goose.’ ‘Now,’ said the grinder, as he gave him a common rough stone

3	

3	3	that lay by his side, ‘this is a most capital stone; do but work it well

3	

3	3	enough, and you can make an old nail cut with it.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Hans took the stone, and went his way with a light heart: his eyes

3	

3	3	sparkled for joy, and he said to himself, ‘Surely I must have been born

3	

3	3	in a lucky hour; everything I could want or wish for comes of itself.

3	

3	3	People are so kind; they seem really to think I do them a favour in

3	

3	3	letting them make me rich, and giving me good bargains.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Meantime he began to be tired, and hungry too, for he had given away his

3	

3	3	last penny in his joy at getting the cow.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	At last he could go no farther, for the stone tired him sadly: and he

3	

3	3	dragged himself to the side of a river, that he might take a drink of

3	

3	3	water, and rest a while. So he laid the stone carefully by his side on

3	

3	3	the bank: but, as he stooped down to drink, he forgot it, pushed it a

3	

3	3	little, and down it rolled, plump into the stream.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	For a while he watched it sinking in the deep clear water; then sprang

3	

3	3	up and danced for joy, and again fell upon his knees and thanked Heaven,

3	

3	3	with tears in his eyes, for its kindness in taking away his only plague,

3	

3	3	the ugly heavy stone.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘How happy am I!’ cried he; ‘nobody was ever so lucky as I.’ Then up he

3	

3	3	got with a light heart, free from all his troubles, and walked on till

3	

3	3	he reached his mother’s house, and told her how very easy the road to

3	

3	3	good luck was.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	JORINDA AND JORINDEL

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once an old castle, that stood in the middle of a deep gloomy

3	

3	3	wood, and in the castle lived an old fairy. Now this fairy could take

3	

3	3	any shape she pleased. All the day long she flew about in the form of

3	

3	3	an owl, or crept about the country like a cat; but at night she always

3	

3	3	became an old woman again. When any young man came within a hundred

3	

3	3	paces of her castle, he became quite fixed, and could not move a step

3	

3	3	till she came and set him free; which she would not do till he had given

3	

3	3	her his word never to come there again: but when any pretty maiden came

3	

3	3	within that space she was changed into a bird, and the fairy put her

3	

3	3	into a cage, and hung her up in a chamber in the castle. There were

3	

3	3	seven hundred of these cages hanging in the castle, and all with

3	

3	3	beautiful birds in them.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Now there was once a maiden whose name was Jorinda. She was prettier

3	

3	3	than all the pretty girls that ever were seen before, and a shepherd

3	

3	3	lad, whose name was Jorindel, was very fond of her, and they were soon

3	

3	3	to be married. One day they went to walk in the wood, that they might be

3	

3	3	alone; and Jorindel said, ‘We must take care that we don’t go too near

3	

3	3	to the fairy’s castle.’ It was a beautiful evening; the last rays of the

3	

3	3	setting sun shone bright through the long stems of the trees upon

3	

3	3	the green underwood beneath, and the turtle-doves sang from the tall

3	

3	3	birches.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Jorinda sat down to gaze upon the sun; Jorindel sat by her side; and

3	

3	3	both felt sad, they knew not why; but it seemed as if they were to be

3	

3	3	parted from one another for ever. They had wandered a long way; and when

3	

3	3	they looked to see which way they should go home, they found themselves

3	

3	3	at a loss to know what path to take.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The sun was setting fast, and already half of its circle had sunk behind

3	

3	3	the hill: Jorindel on a sudden looked behind him, and saw through the

3	

3	3	bushes that they had, without knowing it, sat down close under the old

3	

3	3	walls of the castle. Then he shrank for fear, turned pale, and trembled.

3	

3	3	Jorinda was just singing,

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘The ring-dove sang from the willow spray,

3	

3	3	  Well-a-day! Well-a-day!

3	

3	3	  He mourn’d for the fate of his darling mate,

3	

3	3	  Well-a-day!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	when her song stopped suddenly. Jorindel turned to see the reason, and

3	

3	3	beheld his Jorinda changed into a nightingale, so that her song ended

3	

3	3	with a mournful _jug, jug_. An owl with fiery eyes flew three times

3	

3	3	round them, and three times screamed:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Tu whu! Tu whu! Tu whu!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Jorindel could not move; he stood fixed as a stone, and could neither

3	

3	3	weep, nor speak, nor stir hand or foot. And now the sun went quite down;

3	

3	3	the gloomy night came; the owl flew into a bush; and a moment after the

3	

3	3	old fairy came forth pale and meagre, with staring eyes, and a nose and

3	

3	3	chin that almost met one another.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	She mumbled something to herself, seized the nightingale, and went away

3	

3	3	with it in her hand. Poor Jorindel saw the nightingale was gone--but

3	

3	3	what could he do? He could not speak, he could not move from the spot

3	

3	3	where he stood. At last the fairy came back and sang with a hoarse

3	

3	3	voice:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Till the prisoner is fast,

3	

3	3	  And her doom is cast,

3	

3	3	  There stay! Oh, stay!

3	

3	3	  When the charm is around her,

3	

3	3	  And the spell has bound her,

3	

3	3	  Hie away! away!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	On a sudden Jorindel found himself free. Then he fell on his knees

3	

3	3	before the fairy, and prayed her to give him back his dear Jorinda: but

3	

3	3	she laughed at him, and said he should never see her again; then she

3	

3	3	went her way.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	He prayed, he wept, he sorrowed, but all in vain. ‘Alas!’ he said, ‘what

3	

3	3	will become of me?’ He could not go back to his own home, so he went to

3	

3	3	a strange village, and employed himself in keeping sheep. Many a time

3	

3	3	did he walk round and round as near to the hated castle as he dared go,

3	

3	3	but all in vain; he heard or saw nothing of Jorinda.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	At last he dreamt one night that he found a beautiful purple flower,

3	

3	3	and that in the middle of it lay a costly pearl; and he dreamt that he

3	

3	3	plucked the flower, and went with it in his hand into the castle, and

3	

3	3	that everything he touched with it was disenchanted, and that there he

3	

3	3	found his Jorinda again.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	In the morning when he awoke, he began to search over hill and dale for

3	

3	3	this pretty flower; and eight long days he sought for it in vain: but

3	

3	3	on the ninth day, early in the morning, he found the beautiful purple

3	

3	3	flower; and in the middle of it was a large dewdrop, as big as a costly

3	

3	3	pearl. Then he plucked the flower, and set out and travelled day and

3	

3	3	night, till he came again to the castle.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	He walked nearer than a hundred paces to it, and yet he did not become

3	

3	3	fixed as before, but found that he could go quite close up to the door.

3	

3	3	Jorindel was very glad indeed to see this. Then he touched the door with

3	

3	3	the flower, and it sprang open; so that he went in through the court,

3	

3	3	and listened when he heard so many birds singing. At last he came to the

3	

3	3	chamber where the fairy sat, with the seven hundred birds singing in

3	

3	3	the seven hundred cages. When she saw Jorindel she was very angry, and

3	

3	3	screamed with rage; but she could not come within two yards of him, for

3	

3	3	the flower he held in his hand was his safeguard. He looked around at

3	

3	3	the birds, but alas! there were many, many nightingales, and how then

3	

3	3	should he find out which was his Jorinda? While he was thinking what to

3	

3	3	do, he saw the fairy had taken down one of the cages, and was making the

3	

3	3	best of her way off through the door. He ran or flew after her, touched

3	

3	3	the cage with the flower, and Jorinda stood before him, and threw her

3	

3	3	arms round his neck looking as beautiful as ever, as beautiful as when

3	

3	3	they walked together in the wood.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then he touched all the other birds with the flower, so that they all

3	

3	3	took their old forms again; and he took Jorinda home, where they were

3	

3	3	married, and lived happily together many years: and so did a good many

3	

3	3	other lads, whose maidens had been forced to sing in the old fairy’s

3	

3	3	cages by themselves, much longer than they liked.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE TRAVELLING MUSICIANS

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	An honest farmer had once an ass that had been a faithful servant to him

3	

3	3	a great many years, but was now growing old and every day more and more

3	

3	3	unfit for work. His master therefore was tired of keeping him and

3	

3	3	began to think of putting an end to him; but the ass, who saw that some

3	

3	3	mischief was in the wind, took himself slyly off, and began his journey

3	

3	3	towards the great city, ‘For there,’ thought he, ‘I may turn musician.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	After he had travelled a little way, he spied a dog lying by the

3	

3	3	roadside and panting as if he were tired. ‘What makes you pant so, my

3	

3	3	friend?’ said the ass. ‘Alas!’ said the dog, ‘my master was going to

3	

3	3	knock me on the head, because I am old and weak, and can no longer make

3	

3	3	myself useful to him in hunting; so I ran away; but what can I do to

3	

3	3	earn my livelihood?’ ‘Hark ye!’ said the ass, ‘I am going to the great

3	

3	3	city to turn musician: suppose you go with me, and try what you can

3	

3	3	do in the same way?’ The dog said he was willing, and they jogged on

3	

3	3	together.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	They had not gone far before they saw a cat sitting in the middle of the

3	

3	3	road and making a most rueful face. ‘Pray, my good lady,’ said the ass,

3	

3	3	‘what’s the matter with you? You look quite out of spirits!’ ‘Ah, me!’

3	

3	3	said the cat, ‘how can one be in good spirits when one’s life is in

3	

3	3	danger? Because I am beginning to grow old, and had rather lie at my

3	

3	3	ease by the fire than run about the house after the mice, my mistress

3	

3	3	laid hold of me, and was going to drown me; and though I have been lucky

3	

3	3	enough to get away from her, I do not know what I am to live upon.’

3	

3	3	‘Oh,’ said the ass, ‘by all means go with us to the great city; you are

3	

3	3	a good night singer, and may make your fortune as a musician.’ The cat

3	

3	3	was pleased with the thought, and joined the party.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Soon afterwards, as they were passing by a farmyard, they saw a cock

3	

3	3	perched upon a gate, and screaming out with all his might and main.

3	

3	3	‘Bravo!’ said the ass; ‘upon my word, you make a famous noise; pray what

3	

3	3	is all this about?’ ‘Why,’ said the cock, ‘I was just now saying that

3	

3	3	we should have fine weather for our washing-day, and yet my mistress and

3	

3	3	the cook don’t thank me for my pains, but threaten to cut off my

3	

3	3	head tomorrow, and make broth of me for the guests that are coming

3	

3	3	on Sunday!’ ‘Heaven forbid!’ said the ass, ‘come with us Master

3	

3	3	Chanticleer; it will be better, at any rate, than staying here to have

3	

3	3	your head cut off! Besides, who knows? If we care to sing in tune, we

3	

3	3	may get up some kind of a concert; so come along with us.’ ‘With all my

3	

3	3	heart,’ said the cock: so they all four went on jollily together.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	They could not, however, reach the great city the first day; so when

3	

3	3	night came on, they went into a wood to sleep. The ass and the dog laid

3	

3	3	themselves down under a great tree, and the cat climbed up into the

3	

3	3	branches; while the cock, thinking that the higher he sat the safer he

3	

3	3	should be, flew up to the very top of the tree, and then, according to

3	

3	3	his custom, before he went to sleep, looked out on all sides of him to

3	

3	3	see that everything was well. In doing this, he saw afar off something

3	

3	3	bright and shining and calling to his companions said, ‘There must be a

3	

3	3	house no great way off, for I see a light.’ ‘If that be the case,’ said

3	

3	3	the ass, ‘we had better change our quarters, for our lodging is not the

3	

3	3	best in the world!’ ‘Besides,’ added the dog, ‘I should not be the

3	

3	3	worse for a bone or two, or a bit of meat.’ So they walked off together

3	

3	3	towards the spot where Chanticleer had seen the light, and as they drew

3	

3	3	near it became larger and brighter, till they at last came close to a

3	

3	3	house in which a gang of robbers lived.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The ass, being the tallest of the company, marched up to the window and

3	

3	3	peeped in. ‘Well, Donkey,’ said Chanticleer, ‘what do you see?’ ‘What

3	

3	3	do I see?’ replied the ass. ‘Why, I see a table spread with all kinds of

3	

3	3	good things, and robbers sitting round it making merry.’ ‘That would

3	

3	3	be a noble lodging for us,’ said the cock. ‘Yes,’ said the ass, ‘if we

3	

3	3	could only get in’; so they consulted together how they should contrive

3	

3	3	to get the robbers out; and at last they hit upon a plan. The ass placed

3	

3	3	himself upright on his hind legs, with his forefeet resting against the

3	

3	3	window; the dog got upon his back; the cat scrambled up to the dog’s

3	

3	3	shoulders, and the cock flew up and sat upon the cat’s head. When

3	

3	3	all was ready a signal was given, and they began their music. The ass

3	

3	3	brayed, the dog barked, the cat mewed, and the cock screamed; and then

3	

3	3	they all broke through the window at once, and came tumbling into

3	

3	3	the room, amongst the broken glass, with a most hideous clatter! The

3	

3	3	robbers, who had been not a little frightened by the opening concert,

3	

3	3	had now no doubt that some frightful hobgoblin had broken in upon them,

3	

3	3	and scampered away as fast as they could.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The coast once clear, our travellers soon sat down and dispatched what

3	

3	3	the robbers had left, with as much eagerness as if they had not expected

3	

3	3	to eat again for a month. As soon as they had satisfied themselves, they

3	

3	3	put out the lights, and each once more sought out a resting-place to

3	

3	3	his own liking. The donkey laid himself down upon a heap of straw in

3	

3	3	the yard, the dog stretched himself upon a mat behind the door, the

3	

3	3	cat rolled herself up on the hearth before the warm ashes, and the

3	

3	3	cock perched upon a beam on the top of the house; and, as they were all

3	

3	3	rather tired with their journey, they soon fell asleep.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But about midnight, when the robbers saw from afar that the lights were

3	

3	3	out and that all seemed quiet, they began to think that they had been in

3	

3	3	too great a hurry to run away; and one of them, who was bolder than

3	

3	3	the rest, went to see what was going on. Finding everything still, he

3	

3	3	marched into the kitchen, and groped about till he found a match in

3	

3	3	order to light a candle; and then, espying the glittering fiery eyes of

3	

3	3	the cat, he mistook them for live coals, and held the match to them to

3	

3	3	light it. But the cat, not understanding this joke, sprang at his face,

3	

3	3	and spat, and scratched at him. This frightened him dreadfully, and away

3	

3	3	he ran to the back door; but there the dog jumped up and bit him in the

3	

3	3	leg; and as he was crossing over the yard the ass kicked him; and the

3	

3	3	cock, who had been awakened by the noise, crowed with all his might. At

3	

3	3	this the robber ran back as fast as he could to his comrades, and told

3	

3	3	the captain how a horrid witch had got into the house, and had spat at

3	

3	3	him and scratched his face with her long bony fingers; how a man with a

3	

3	3	knife in his hand had hidden himself behind the door, and stabbed him

3	

3	3	in the leg; how a black monster stood in the yard and struck him with a

3	

3	3	club, and how the devil had sat upon the top of the house and cried out,

3	

3	3	‘Throw the rascal up here!’ After this the robbers never dared to go

3	

3	3	back to the house; but the musicians were so pleased with their quarters

3	

3	3	that they took up their abode there; and there they are, I dare say, at

3	

3	3	this very day.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	OLD SULTAN

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	A shepherd had a faithful dog, called Sultan, who was grown very old,

3	

3	3	and had lost all his teeth. And one day when the shepherd and his wife

3	

3	3	were standing together before the house the shepherd said, ‘I will shoot

3	

3	3	old Sultan tomorrow morning, for he is of no use now.’ But his wife

3	

3	3	said, ‘Pray let the poor faithful creature live; he has served us well a

3	

3	3	great many years, and we ought to give him a livelihood for the rest of

3	

3	3	his days.’ ‘But what can we do with him?’ said the shepherd, ‘he has not

3	

3	3	a tooth in his head, and the thieves don’t care for him at all; to

3	

3	3	be sure he has served us, but then he did it to earn his livelihood;

3	

3	3	tomorrow shall be his last day, depend upon it.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Poor Sultan, who was lying close by them, heard all that the shepherd

3	

3	3	and his wife said to one another, and was very much frightened to think

3	

3	3	tomorrow would be his last day; so in the evening he went to his good

3	

3	3	friend the wolf, who lived in the wood, and told him all his sorrows,

3	

3	3	and how his master meant to kill him in the morning. ‘Make yourself

3	

3	3	easy,’ said the wolf, ‘I will give you some good advice. Your master,

3	

3	3	you know, goes out every morning very early with his wife into the

3	

3	3	field; and they take their little child with them, and lay it down

3	

3	3	behind the hedge in the shade while they are at work. Now do you lie

3	

3	3	down close by the child, and pretend to be watching it, and I will come

3	

3	3	out of the wood and run away with it; you must run after me as fast as

3	

3	3	you can, and I will let it drop; then you may carry it back, and they

3	

3	3	will think you have saved their child, and will be so thankful to you

3	

3	3	that they will take care of you as long as you live.’ The dog liked this

3	

3	3	plan very well; and accordingly so it was managed. The wolf ran with the

3	

3	3	child a little way; the shepherd and his wife screamed out; but Sultan

3	

3	3	soon overtook him, and carried the poor little thing back to his master

3	

3	3	and mistress. Then the shepherd patted him on the head, and said, ‘Old

3	

3	3	Sultan has saved our child from the wolf, and therefore he shall live

3	

3	3	and be well taken care of, and have plenty to eat. Wife, go home, and

3	

3	3	give him a good dinner, and let him have my old cushion to sleep on

3	

3	3	as long as he lives.’ So from this time forward Sultan had all that he

3	

3	3	could wish for.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Soon afterwards the wolf came and wished him joy, and said, ‘Now, my

3	

3	3	good fellow, you must tell no tales, but turn your head the other way

3	

3	3	when I want to taste one of the old shepherd’s fine fat sheep.’ ‘No,’

3	

3	3	said the Sultan; ‘I will be true to my master.’ However, the wolf

3	

3	3	thought he was in joke, and came one night to get a dainty morsel. But

3	

3	3	Sultan had told his master what the wolf meant to do; so he laid wait

3	

3	3	for him behind the barn door, and when the wolf was busy looking out for

3	

3	3	a good fat sheep, he had a stout cudgel laid about his back, that combed

3	

3	3	his locks for him finely.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the wolf was very angry, and called Sultan ‘an old rogue,’ and

3	

3	3	swore he would have his revenge. So the next morning the wolf sent the

3	

3	3	boar to challenge Sultan to come into the wood to fight the matter. Now

3	

3	3	Sultan had nobody he could ask to be his second but the shepherd’s old

3	

3	3	three-legged cat; so he took her with him, and as the poor thing limped

3	

3	3	along with some trouble, she stuck up her tail straight in the air.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The wolf and the wild boar were first on the ground; and when they

3	

3	3	espied their enemies coming, and saw the cat’s long tail standing

3	

3	3	straight in the air, they thought she was carrying a sword for Sultan to

3	

3	3	fight with; and every time she limped, they thought she was picking up

3	

3	3	a stone to throw at them; so they said they should not like this way of

3	

3	3	fighting, and the boar lay down behind a bush, and the wolf jumped

3	

3	3	up into a tree. Sultan and the cat soon came up, and looked about and

3	

3	3	wondered that no one was there. The boar, however, had not quite hidden

3	

3	3	himself, for his ears stuck out of the bush; and when he shook one of

3	

3	3	them a little, the cat, seeing something move, and thinking it was a

3	

3	3	mouse, sprang upon it, and bit and scratched it, so that the boar jumped

3	

3	3	up and grunted, and ran away, roaring out, ‘Look up in the tree, there

3	

3	3	sits the one who is to blame.’ So they looked up, and espied the wolf

3	

3	3	sitting amongst the branches; and they called him a cowardly rascal,

3	

3	3	and would not suffer him to come down till he was heartily ashamed of

3	

3	3	himself, and had promised to be good friends again with old Sultan.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE STRAW, THE COAL, AND THE BEAN

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	In a village dwelt a poor old woman, who had gathered together a dish

3	

3	3	of beans and wanted to cook them. So she made a fire on her hearth, and

3	

3	3	that it might burn the quicker, she lighted it with a handful of straw.

3	

3	3	When she was emptying the beans into the pan, one dropped without her

3	

3	3	observing it, and lay on the ground beside a straw, and soon afterwards

3	

3	3	a burning coal from the fire leapt down to the two. Then the straw

3	

3	3	began and said: ‘Dear friends, from whence do you come here?’ The coal

3	

3	3	replied: ‘I fortunately sprang out of the fire, and if I had not escaped

3	

3	3	by sheer force, my death would have been certain,--I should have been

3	

3	3	burnt to ashes.’ The bean said: ‘I too have escaped with a whole skin,

3	

3	3	but if the old woman had got me into the pan, I should have been made

3	

3	3	into broth without any mercy, like my comrades.’ ‘And would a better

3	

3	3	fate have fallen to my lot?’ said the straw. ‘The old woman has

3	

3	3	destroyed all my brethren in fire and smoke; she seized sixty of them at

3	

3	3	once, and took their lives. I luckily slipped through her fingers.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘But what are we to do now?’ said the coal.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘I think,’ answered the bean, ‘that as we have so fortunately escaped

3	

3	3	death, we should keep together like good companions, and lest a new

3	

3	3	mischance should overtake us here, we should go away together, and

3	

3	3	repair to a foreign country.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The proposition pleased the two others, and they set out on their way

3	

3	3	together. Soon, however, they came to a little brook, and as there was

3	

3	3	no bridge or foot-plank, they did not know how they were to get over

3	

3	3	it. The straw hit on a good idea, and said: ‘I will lay myself straight

3	

3	3	across, and then you can walk over on me as on a bridge.’ The straw

3	

3	3	therefore stretched itself from one bank to the other, and the coal,

3	

3	3	who was of an impetuous disposition, tripped quite boldly on to the

3	

3	3	newly-built bridge. But when she had reached the middle, and heard the

3	

3	3	water rushing beneath her, she was after all, afraid, and stood still,

3	

3	3	and ventured no farther. The straw, however, began to burn, broke in

3	

3	3	two pieces, and fell into the stream. The coal slipped after her, hissed

3	

3	3	when she got into the water, and breathed her last. The bean, who had

3	

3	3	prudently stayed behind on the shore, could not but laugh at the event,

3	

3	3	was unable to stop, and laughed so heartily that she burst. It would

3	

3	3	have been all over with her, likewise, if, by good fortune, a tailor who

3	

3	3	was travelling in search of work, had not sat down to rest by the brook.

3	

3	3	As he had a compassionate heart he pulled out his needle and thread,

3	

3	3	and sewed her together. The bean thanked him most prettily, but as the

3	

3	3	tailor used black thread, all beans since then have a black seam.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	BRIAR ROSE

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	A king and queen once upon a time reigned in a country a great way off,

3	

3	3	where there were in those days fairies. Now this king and queen had

3	

3	3	plenty of money, and plenty of fine clothes to wear, and plenty of

3	

3	3	good things to eat and drink, and a coach to ride out in every day: but

3	

3	3	though they had been married many years they had no children, and this

3	

3	3	grieved them very much indeed. But one day as the queen was walking

3	

3	3	by the side of the river, at the bottom of the garden, she saw a poor

3	

3	3	little fish, that had thrown itself out of the water, and lay gasping

3	

3	3	and nearly dead on the bank. Then the queen took pity on the little

3	

3	3	fish, and threw it back again into the river; and before it swam away

3	

3	3	it lifted its head out of the water and said, ‘I know what your wish is,

3	

3	3	and it shall be fulfilled, in return for your kindness to me--you will

3	

3	3	soon have a daughter.’ What the little fish had foretold soon came to

3	

3	3	pass; and the queen had a little girl, so very beautiful that the king

3	

3	3	could not cease looking on it for joy, and said he would hold a great

3	

3	3	feast and make merry, and show the child to all the land. So he asked

3	

3	3	his kinsmen, and nobles, and friends, and neighbours. But the queen

3	

3	3	said, ‘I will have the fairies also, that they might be kind and good

3	

3	3	to our little daughter.’ Now there were thirteen fairies in the kingdom;

3	

3	3	but as the king and queen had only twelve golden dishes for them to eat

3	

3	3	out of, they were forced to leave one of the fairies without asking her.

3	

3	3	So twelve fairies came, each with a high red cap on her head, and red

3	

3	3	shoes with high heels on her feet, and a long white wand in her hand:

3	

3	3	and after the feast was over they gathered round in a ring and gave all

3	

3	3	their best gifts to the little princess. One gave her goodness, another

3	

3	3	beauty, another riches, and so on till she had all that was good in the

3	

3	3	world.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Just as eleven of them had done blessing her, a great noise was heard in

3	

3	3	the courtyard, and word was brought that the thirteenth fairy was

3	

3	3	come, with a black cap on her head, and black shoes on her feet, and a

3	

3	3	broomstick in her hand: and presently up she came into the dining-hall.

3	

3	3	Now, as she had not been asked to the feast she was very angry, and

3	

3	3	scolded the king and queen very much, and set to work to take her

3	

3	3	revenge. So she cried out, ‘The king’s daughter shall, in her fifteenth

3	

3	3	year, be wounded by a spindle, and fall down dead.’ Then the twelfth of

3	

3	3	the friendly fairies, who had not yet given her gift, came forward, and

3	

3	3	said that the evil wish must be fulfilled, but that she could soften its

3	

3	3	mischief; so her gift was, that the king’s daughter, when the spindle

3	

3	3	wounded her, should not really die, but should only fall asleep for a

3	

3	3	hundred years.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	However, the king hoped still to save his dear child altogether from

3	

3	3	the threatened evil; so he ordered that all the spindles in the kingdom

3	

3	3	should be bought up and burnt. But all the gifts of the first eleven

3	

3	3	fairies were in the meantime fulfilled; for the princess was so

3	

3	3	beautiful, and well behaved, and good, and wise, that everyone who knew

3	

3	3	her loved her.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	It happened that, on the very day she was fifteen years old, the king

3	

3	3	and queen were not at home, and she was left alone in the palace. So she

3	

3	3	roved about by herself, and looked at all the rooms and chambers, till

3	

3	3	at last she came to an old tower, to which there was a narrow staircase

3	

3	3	ending with a little door. In the door there was a golden key, and when

3	

3	3	she turned it the door sprang open, and there sat an old lady spinning

3	

3	3	away very busily. ‘Why, how now, good mother,’ said the princess; ‘what

3	

3	3	are you doing there?’ ‘Spinning,’ said the old lady, and nodded her

3	

3	3	head, humming a tune, while buzz! went the wheel. ‘How prettily that

3	

3	3	little thing turns round!’ said the princess, and took the spindle

3	

3	3	and began to try and spin. But scarcely had she touched it, before the

3	

3	3	fairy’s prophecy was fulfilled; the spindle wounded her, and she fell

3	

3	3	down lifeless on the ground.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	However, she was not dead, but had only fallen into a deep sleep; and

3	

3	3	the king and the queen, who had just come home, and all their court,

3	

3	3	fell asleep too; and the horses slept in the stables, and the dogs in

3	

3	3	the court, the pigeons on the house-top, and the very flies slept upon

3	

3	3	the walls. Even the fire on the hearth left off blazing, and went to

3	

3	3	sleep; the jack stopped, and the spit that was turning about with a

3	

3	3	goose upon it for the king’s dinner stood still; and the cook, who was

3	

3	3	at that moment pulling the kitchen-boy by the hair to give him a box

3	

3	3	on the ear for something he had done amiss, let him go, and both fell

3	

3	3	asleep; the butler, who was slyly tasting the ale, fell asleep with the

3	

3	3	jug at his lips: and thus everything stood still, and slept soundly.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	A large hedge of thorns soon grew round the palace, and every year it

3	

3	3	became higher and thicker; till at last the old palace was surrounded

3	

3	3	and hidden, so that not even the roof or the chimneys could be seen. But

3	

3	3	there went a report through all the land of the beautiful sleeping Briar

3	

3	3	Rose (for so the king’s daughter was called): so that, from time to

3	

3	3	time, several kings’ sons came, and tried to break through the thicket

3	

3	3	into the palace. This, however, none of them could ever do; for the

3	

3	3	thorns and bushes laid hold of them, as it were with hands; and there

3	

3	3	they stuck fast, and died wretchedly.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	After many, many years there came a king’s son into that land: and an

3	

3	3	old man told him the story of the thicket of thorns; and how a beautiful

3	

3	3	palace stood behind it, and how a wonderful princess, called Briar Rose,

3	

3	3	lay in it asleep, with all her court. He told, too, how he had heard

3	

3	3	from his grandfather that many, many princes had come, and had tried to

3	

3	3	break through the thicket, but that they had all stuck fast in it, and

3	

3	3	died. Then the young prince said, ‘All this shall not frighten me; I

3	

3	3	will go and see this Briar Rose.’ The old man tried to hinder him, but

3	

3	3	he was bent upon going.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Now that very day the hundred years were ended; and as the prince came

3	

3	3	to the thicket he saw nothing but beautiful flowering shrubs, through

3	

3	3	which he went with ease, and they shut in after him as thick as ever.

3	

3	3	Then he came at last to the palace, and there in the court lay the dogs

3	

3	3	asleep; and the horses were standing in the stables; and on the roof sat

3	

3	3	the pigeons fast asleep, with their heads under their wings. And when he

3	

3	3	came into the palace, the flies were sleeping on the walls; the spit

3	

3	3	was standing still; the butler had the jug of ale at his lips, going

3	

3	3	to drink a draught; the maid sat with a fowl in her lap ready to be

3	

3	3	plucked; and the cook in the kitchen was still holding up her hand, as

3	

3	3	if she was going to beat the boy.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then he went on still farther, and all was so still that he could hear

3	

3	3	every breath he drew; till at last he came to the old tower, and opened

3	

3	3	the door of the little room in which Briar Rose was; and there she lay,

3	

3	3	fast asleep on a couch by the window. She looked so beautiful that he

3	

3	3	could not take his eyes off her, so he stooped down and gave her a kiss.

3	

3	3	But the moment he kissed her she opened her eyes and awoke, and smiled

3	

3	3	upon him; and they went out together; and soon the king and queen also

3	

3	3	awoke, and all the court, and gazed on each other with great wonder.

3	

3	3	And the horses shook themselves, and the dogs jumped up and barked; the

3	

3	3	pigeons took their heads from under their wings, and looked about and

3	

3	3	flew into the fields; the flies on the walls buzzed again; the fire in

3	

3	3	the kitchen blazed up; round went the jack, and round went the spit,

3	

3	3	with the goose for the king’s dinner upon it; the butler finished his

3	

3	3	draught of ale; the maid went on plucking the fowl; and the cook gave

3	

3	3	the boy the box on his ear.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And then the prince and Briar Rose were married, and the wedding feast

3	

3	3	was given; and they lived happily together all their lives long.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE DOG AND THE SPARROW

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	A shepherd’s dog had a master who took no care of him, but often let him

3	

3	3	suffer the greatest hunger. At last he could bear it no longer; so he

3	

3	3	took to his heels, and off he ran in a very sad and sorrowful mood.

3	

3	3	On the road he met a sparrow that said to him, ‘Why are you so sad,

3	

3	3	my friend?’ ‘Because,’ said the dog, ‘I am very very hungry, and have

3	

3	3	nothing to eat.’ ‘If that be all,’ answered the sparrow, ‘come with me

3	

3	3	into the next town, and I will soon find you plenty of food.’ So on they

3	

3	3	went together into the town: and as they passed by a butcher’s shop,

3	

3	3	the sparrow said to the dog, ‘Stand there a little while till I peck you

3	

3	3	down a piece of meat.’ So the sparrow perched upon the shelf: and having

3	

3	3	first looked carefully about her to see if anyone was watching her, she

3	

3	3	pecked and scratched at a steak that lay upon the edge of the shelf,

3	

3	3	till at last down it fell. Then the dog snapped it up, and scrambled

3	

3	3	away with it into a corner, where he soon ate it all up. ‘Well,’ said

3	

3	3	the sparrow, ‘you shall have some more if you will; so come with me to

3	

3	3	the next shop, and I will peck you down another steak.’ When the dog had

3	

3	3	eaten this too, the sparrow said to him, ‘Well, my good friend, have you

3	

3	3	had enough now?’ ‘I have had plenty of meat,’ answered he, ‘but I should

3	

3	3	like to have a piece of bread to eat after it.’ ‘Come with me then,’

3	

3	3	said the sparrow, ‘and you shall soon have that too.’ So she took him

3	

3	3	to a baker’s shop, and pecked at two rolls that lay in the window, till

3	

3	3	they fell down: and as the dog still wished for more, she took him to

3	

3	3	another shop and pecked down some more for him. When that was eaten, the

3	

3	3	sparrow asked him whether he had had enough now. ‘Yes,’ said he; ‘and

3	

3	3	now let us take a walk a little way out of the town.’ So they both went

3	

3	3	out upon the high road; but as the weather was warm, they had not gone

3	

3	3	far before the dog said, ‘I am very much tired--I should like to take a

3	

3	3	nap.’ ‘Very well,’ answered the sparrow, ‘do so, and in the meantime

3	

3	3	I will perch upon that bush.’ So the dog stretched himself out on the

3	

3	3	road, and fell fast asleep. Whilst he slept, there came by a carter with

3	

3	3	a cart drawn by three horses, and loaded with two casks of wine. The

3	

3	3	sparrow, seeing that the carter did not turn out of the way, but would

3	

3	3	go on in the track in which the dog lay, so as to drive over him, called

3	

3	3	out, ‘Stop! stop! Mr Carter, or it shall be the worse for you.’ But the

3	

3	3	carter, grumbling to himself, ‘You make it the worse for me, indeed!

3	

3	3	what can you do?’ cracked his whip, and drove his cart over the poor

3	

3	3	dog, so that the wheels crushed him to death. ‘There,’ cried the

3	

3	3	sparrow, ‘thou cruel villain, thou hast killed my friend the dog. Now

3	

3	3	mind what I say. This deed of thine shall cost thee all thou art worth.’

3	

3	3	‘Do your worst, and welcome,’ said the brute, ‘what harm can you do me?’

3	

3	3	and passed on. But the sparrow crept under the tilt of the cart, and

3	

3	3	pecked at the bung of one of the casks till she loosened it; and then

3	

3	3	all the wine ran out, without the carter seeing it. At last he looked

3	

3	3	round, and saw that the cart was dripping, and the cask quite empty.

3	

3	3	‘What an unlucky wretch I am!’ cried he. ‘Not wretch enough yet!’ said

3	

3	3	the sparrow, as she alighted upon the head of one of the horses, and

3	

3	3	pecked at him till he reared up and kicked. When the carter saw this,

3	

3	3	he drew out his hatchet and aimed a blow at the sparrow, meaning to kill

3	

3	3	her; but she flew away, and the blow fell upon the poor horse’s head

3	

3	3	with such force, that he fell down dead. ‘Unlucky wretch that I am!’

3	

3	3	cried he. ‘Not wretch enough yet!’ said the sparrow. And as the carter

3	

3	3	went on with the other two horses, she again crept under the tilt of the

3	

3	3	cart, and pecked out the bung of the second cask, so that all the wine

3	

3	3	ran out. When the carter saw this, he again cried out, ‘Miserable wretch

3	

3	3	that I am!’ But the sparrow answered, ‘Not wretch enough yet!’ and

3	

3	3	perched on the head of the second horse, and pecked at him too. The

3	

3	3	carter ran up and struck at her again with his hatchet; but away she

3	

3	3	flew, and the blow fell upon the second horse and killed him on the

3	

3	3	spot. ‘Unlucky wretch that I am!’ said he. ‘Not wretch enough yet!’ said

3	

3	3	the sparrow; and perching upon the third horse, she began to peck him

3	

3	3	too. The carter was mad with fury; and without looking about him, or

3	

3	3	caring what he was about, struck again at the sparrow; but killed his

3	

3	3	third horse as he done the other two. ‘Alas! miserable wretch that I

3	

3	3	am!’ cried he. ‘Not wretch enough yet!’ answered the sparrow as she flew

3	

3	3	away; ‘now will I plague and punish thee at thy own house.’ The

3	

3	3	carter was forced at last to leave his cart behind him, and to go home

3	

3	3	overflowing with rage and vexation. ‘Alas!’ said he to his wife, ‘what

3	

3	3	ill luck has befallen me!--my wine is all spilt, and my horses all three

3	

3	3	dead.’ ‘Alas! husband,’ replied she, ‘and a wicked bird has come into

3	

3	3	the house, and has brought with her all the birds in the world, I am

3	

3	3	sure, and they have fallen upon our corn in the loft, and are eating it

3	

3	3	up at such a rate!’ Away ran the husband upstairs, and saw thousands of

3	

3	3	birds sitting upon the floor eating up his corn, with the sparrow in the

3	

3	3	midst of them. ‘Unlucky wretch that I am!’ cried the carter; for he saw

3	

3	3	that the corn was almost all gone. ‘Not wretch enough yet!’ said the

3	

3	3	sparrow; ‘thy cruelty shall cost thee thy life yet!’ and away she flew.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The carter seeing that he had thus lost all that he had, went down

3	

3	3	into his kitchen; and was still not sorry for what he had done, but sat

3	

3	3	himself angrily and sulkily in the chimney corner. But the sparrow sat

3	

3	3	on the outside of the window, and cried ‘Carter! thy cruelty shall cost

3	

3	3	thee thy life!’ With that he jumped up in a rage, seized his hatchet,

3	

3	3	and threw it at the sparrow; but it missed her, and only broke the

3	

3	3	window. The sparrow now hopped in, perched upon the window-seat, and

3	

3	3	cried, ‘Carter! it shall cost thee thy life!’ Then he became mad and

3	

3	3	blind with rage, and struck the window-seat with such force that he

3	

3	3	cleft it in two: and as the sparrow flew from place to place, the carter

3	

3	3	and his wife were so furious, that they broke all their furniture,

3	

3	3	glasses, chairs, benches, the table, and at last the walls, without

3	

3	3	touching the bird at all. In the end, however, they caught her: and the

3	

3	3	wife said, ‘Shall I kill her at once?’ ‘No,’ cried he, ‘that is letting

3	

3	3	her off too easily: she shall die a much more cruel death; I will eat

3	

3	3	her.’ But the sparrow began to flutter about, and stretch out her neck

3	

3	3	and cried, ‘Carter! it shall cost thee thy life yet!’ With that he

3	

3	3	could wait no longer: so he gave his wife the hatchet, and cried, ‘Wife,

3	

3	3	strike at the bird and kill her in my hand.’ And the wife struck; but

3	

3	3	she missed her aim, and hit her husband on the head so that he fell down

3	

3	3	dead, and the sparrow flew quietly home to her nest.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE TWELVE DANCING PRINCESSES

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was a king who had twelve beautiful daughters. They slept in

3	

3	3	twelve beds all in one room; and when they went to bed, the doors were

3	

3	3	shut and locked up; but every morning their shoes were found to be quite

3	

3	3	worn through as if they had been danced in all night; and yet nobody

3	

3	3	could find out how it happened, or where they had been.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the king made it known to all the land, that if any person could

3	

3	3	discover the secret, and find out where it was that the princesses

3	

3	3	danced in the night, he should have the one he liked best for his

3	

3	3	wife, and should be king after his death; but whoever tried and did not

3	

3	3	succeed, after three days and nights, should be put to death.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	A king’s son soon came. He was well entertained, and in the evening was

3	

3	3	taken to the chamber next to the one where the princesses lay in their

3	

3	3	twelve beds. There he was to sit and watch where they went to dance;

3	

3	3	and, in order that nothing might pass without his hearing it, the door

3	

3	3	of his chamber was left open. But the king’s son soon fell asleep; and

3	

3	3	when he awoke in the morning he found that the princesses had all been

3	

3	3	dancing, for the soles of their shoes were full of holes. The same thing

3	

3	3	happened the second and third night: so the king ordered his head to be

3	

3	3	cut off. After him came several others; but they had all the same luck,

3	

3	3	and all lost their lives in the same manner.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Now it chanced that an old soldier, who had been wounded in battle

3	

3	3	and could fight no longer, passed through the country where this king

3	

3	3	reigned: and as he was travelling through a wood, he met an old woman,

3	

3	3	who asked him where he was going. ‘I hardly know where I am going, or

3	

3	3	what I had better do,’ said the soldier; ‘but I think I should like very

3	

3	3	well to find out where it is that the princesses dance, and then in time

3	

3	3	I might be a king.’ ‘Well,’ said the old dame, ‘that is no very hard

3	

3	3	task: only take care not to drink any of the wine which one of the

3	

3	3	princesses will bring to you in the evening; and as soon as she leaves

3	

3	3	you pretend to be fast asleep.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then she gave him a cloak, and said, ‘As soon as you put that on

3	

3	3	you will become invisible, and you will then be able to follow the

3	

3	3	princesses wherever they go.’ When the soldier heard all this good

3	

3	3	counsel, he determined to try his luck: so he went to the king, and said

3	

3	3	he was willing to undertake the task.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	He was as well received as the others had been, and the king ordered

3	

3	3	fine royal robes to be given him; and when the evening came he was led

3	

3	3	to the outer chamber. Just as he was going to lie down, the eldest of

3	

3	3	the princesses brought him a cup of wine; but the soldier threw it all

3	

3	3	away secretly, taking care not to drink a drop. Then he laid himself

3	

3	3	down on his bed, and in a little while began to snore very loud as if

3	

3	3	he was fast asleep. When the twelve princesses heard this they laughed

3	

3	3	heartily; and the eldest said, ‘This fellow too might have done a wiser

3	

3	3	thing than lose his life in this way!’ Then they rose up and opened

3	

3	3	their drawers and boxes, and took out all their fine clothes, and

3	

3	3	dressed themselves at the glass, and skipped about as if they were eager

3	

3	3	to begin dancing. But the youngest said, ‘I don’t know how it is, while

3	

3	3	you are so happy I feel very uneasy; I am sure some mischance will

3	

3	3	befall us.’ ‘You simpleton,’ said the eldest, ‘you are always afraid;

3	

3	3	have you forgotten how many kings’ sons have already watched in vain?

3	

3	3	And as for this soldier, even if I had not given him his sleeping

3	

3	3	draught, he would have slept soundly enough.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When they were all ready, they went and looked at the soldier; but he

3	

3	3	snored on, and did not stir hand or foot: so they thought they were

3	

3	3	quite safe; and the eldest went up to her own bed and clapped her hands,

3	

3	3	and the bed sank into the floor and a trap-door flew open. The soldier

3	

3	3	saw them going down through the trap-door one after another, the eldest

3	

3	3	leading the way; and thinking he had no time to lose, he jumped up, put

3	

3	3	on the cloak which the old woman had given him, and followed them;

3	

3	3	but in the middle of the stairs he trod on the gown of the youngest

3	

3	3	princess, and she cried out to her sisters, ‘All is not right; someone

3	

3	3	took hold of my gown.’ ‘You silly creature!’ said the eldest, ‘it is

3	

3	3	nothing but a nail in the wall.’ Then down they all went, and at the

3	

3	3	bottom they found themselves in a most delightful grove of trees; and

3	

3	3	the leaves were all of silver, and glittered and sparkled beautifully.

3	

3	3	The soldier wished to take away some token of the place; so he broke

3	

3	3	off a little branch, and there came a loud noise from the tree. Then the

3	

3	3	youngest daughter said again, ‘I am sure all is not right--did not you

3	

3	3	hear that noise? That never happened before.’ But the eldest said, ‘It

3	

3	3	is only our princes, who are shouting for joy at our approach.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then they came to another grove of trees, where all the leaves were of

3	

3	3	gold; and afterwards to a third, where the leaves were all glittering

3	

3	3	diamonds. And the soldier broke a branch from each; and every time there

3	

3	3	was a loud noise, which made the youngest sister tremble with fear; but

3	

3	3	the eldest still said, it was only the princes, who were crying for joy.

3	

3	3	So they went on till they came to a great lake; and at the side of the

3	

3	3	lake there lay twelve little boats with twelve handsome princes in them,

3	

3	3	who seemed to be waiting there for the princesses.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	One of the princesses went into each boat, and the soldier stepped into

3	

3	3	the same boat with the youngest. As they were rowing over the lake, the

3	

3	3	prince who was in the boat with the youngest princess and the soldier

3	

3	3	said, ‘I do not know why it is, but though I am rowing with all my might

3	

3	3	we do not get on so fast as usual, and I am quite tired: the boat

3	

3	3	seems very heavy today.’ ‘It is only the heat of the weather,’ said the

3	

3	3	princess: ‘I feel it very warm too.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	On the other side of the lake stood a fine illuminated castle, from

3	

3	3	which came the merry music of horns and trumpets. There they all landed,

3	

3	3	and went into the castle, and each prince danced with his princess; and

3	

3	3	the soldier, who was all the time invisible, danced with them too; and

3	

3	3	when any of the princesses had a cup of wine set by her, he drank it

3	

3	3	all up, so that when she put the cup to her mouth it was empty. At this,

3	

3	3	too, the youngest sister was terribly frightened, but the eldest always

3	

3	3	silenced her. They danced on till three o’clock in the morning, and then

3	

3	3	all their shoes were worn out, so that they were obliged to leave off.

3	

3	3	The princes rowed them back again over the lake (but this time the

3	

3	3	soldier placed himself in the boat with the eldest princess); and on the

3	

3	3	opposite shore they took leave of each other, the princesses promising

3	

3	3	to come again the next night.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When they came to the stairs, the soldier ran on before the princesses,

3	

3	3	and laid himself down; and as the twelve sisters slowly came up very

3	

3	3	much tired, they heard him snoring in his bed; so they said, ‘Now all

3	

3	3	is quite safe’; then they undressed themselves, put away their fine

3	

3	3	clothes, pulled off their shoes, and went to bed. In the morning the

3	

3	3	soldier said nothing about what had happened, but determined to see more

3	

3	3	of this strange adventure, and went again the second and third night;

3	

3	3	and every thing happened just as before; the princesses danced each time

3	

3	3	till their shoes were worn to pieces, and then returned home. However,

3	

3	3	on the third night the soldier carried away one of the golden cups as a

3	

3	3	token of where he had been.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	As soon as the time came when he was to declare the secret, he was taken

3	

3	3	before the king with the three branches and the golden cup; and the

3	

3	3	twelve princesses stood listening behind the door to hear what he would

3	

3	3	say. And when the king asked him. ‘Where do my twelve daughters dance at

3	

3	3	night?’ he answered, ‘With twelve princes in a castle under ground.’ And

3	

3	3	then he told the king all that had happened, and showed him the three

3	

3	3	branches and the golden cup which he had brought with him. Then the king

3	

3	3	called for the princesses, and asked them whether what the soldier said

3	

3	3	was true: and when they saw that they were discovered, and that it was

3	

3	3	of no use to deny what had happened, they confessed it all. And the king

3	

3	3	asked the soldier which of them he would choose for his wife; and he

3	

3	3	answered, ‘I am not very young, so I will have the eldest.’--And they

3	

3	3	were married that very day, and the soldier was chosen to be the king’s

3	

3	3	heir.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE FISHERMAN AND HIS WIFE

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once a fisherman who lived with his wife in a pigsty, close

3	

3	3	by the seaside. The fisherman used to go out all day long a-fishing; and

3	

3	3	one day, as he sat on the shore with his rod, looking at the sparkling

3	

3	3	waves and watching his line, all on a sudden his float was dragged away

3	

3	3	deep into the water: and in drawing it up he pulled out a great fish.

3	

3	3	But the fish said, ‘Pray let me live! I am not a real fish; I am an

3	

3	3	enchanted prince: put me in the water again, and let me go!’ ‘Oh, ho!’

3	

3	3	said the man, ‘you need not make so many words about the matter; I will

3	

3	3	have nothing to do with a fish that can talk: so swim away, sir, as soon

3	

3	3	as you please!’ Then he put him back into the water, and the fish darted

3	

3	3	straight down to the bottom, and left a long streak of blood behind him

3	

3	3	on the wave.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the fisherman went home to his wife in the pigsty, he told her how

3	

3	3	he had caught a great fish, and how it had told him it was an enchanted

3	

3	3	prince, and how, on hearing it speak, he had let it go again. ‘Did not

3	

3	3	you ask it for anything?’ said the wife, ‘we live very wretchedly here,

3
