3	3	in this nasty dirty pigsty; do go back and tell the fish we want a snug

3	

3	3	little cottage.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The fisherman did not much like the business: however, he went to the

3	

3	3	seashore; and when he came back there the water looked all yellow and

3	

3	3	green. And he stood at the water’s edge, and said:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘O man of the sea!

3	

3	3	  Hearken to me!

3	

3	3	  My wife Ilsabill

3	

3	3	  Will have her own will,

3	

3	3	  And hath sent me to beg a boon of thee!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the fish came swimming to him, and said, ‘Well, what is her will?

3	

3	3	What does your wife want?’ ‘Ah!’ said the fisherman, ‘she says that when

3	

3	3	I had caught you, I ought to have asked you for something before I let

3	

3	3	you go; she does not like living any longer in the pigsty, and wants

3	

3	3	a snug little cottage.’ ‘Go home, then,’ said the fish; ‘she is in the

3	

3	3	cottage already!’ So the man went home, and saw his wife standing at the

3	

3	3	door of a nice trim little cottage. ‘Come in, come in!’ said she; ‘is

3	

3	3	not this much better than the filthy pigsty we had?’ And there was a

3	

3	3	parlour, and a bedchamber, and a kitchen; and behind the cottage there

3	

3	3	was a little garden, planted with all sorts of flowers and fruits; and

3	

3	3	there was a courtyard behind, full of ducks and chickens. ‘Ah!’ said the

3	

3	3	fisherman, ‘how happily we shall live now!’ ‘We will try to do so, at

3	

3	3	least,’ said his wife.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Everything went right for a week or two, and then Dame Ilsabill said,

3	

3	3	‘Husband, there is not near room enough for us in this cottage; the

3	

3	3	courtyard and the garden are a great deal too small; I should like to

3	

3	3	have a large stone castle to live in: go to the fish again and tell him

3	

3	3	to give us a castle.’ ‘Wife,’ said the fisherman, ‘I don’t like to go to

3	

3	3	him again, for perhaps he will be angry; we ought to be easy with this

3	

3	3	pretty cottage to live in.’ ‘Nonsense!’ said the wife; ‘he will do it

3	

3	3	very willingly, I know; go along and try!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The fisherman went, but his heart was very heavy: and when he came to

3	

3	3	the sea, it looked blue and gloomy, though it was very calm; and he went

3	

3	3	close to the edge of the waves, and said:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘O man of the sea!

3	

3	3	  Hearken to me!

3	

3	3	  My wife Ilsabill

3	

3	3	  Will have her own will,

3	

3	3	  And hath sent me to beg a boon of thee!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Well, what does she want now?’ said the fish. ‘Ah!’ said the man,

3	

3	3	dolefully, ‘my wife wants to live in a stone castle.’ ‘Go home, then,’

3	

3	3	said the fish; ‘she is standing at the gate of it already.’ So away went

3	

3	3	the fisherman, and found his wife standing before the gate of a great

3	

3	3	castle. ‘See,’ said she, ‘is not this grand?’ With that they went into

3	

3	3	the castle together, and found a great many servants there, and the

3	

3	3	rooms all richly furnished, and full of golden chairs and tables; and

3	

3	3	behind the castle was a garden, and around it was a park half a

3	

3	3	mile long, full of sheep, and goats, and hares, and deer; and in the

3	

3	3	courtyard were stables and cow-houses. ‘Well,’ said the man, ‘now we

3	

3	3	will live cheerful and happy in this beautiful castle for the rest of

3	

3	3	our lives.’ ‘Perhaps we may,’ said the wife; ‘but let us sleep upon it,

3	

3	3	before we make up our minds to that.’ So they went to bed.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The next morning when Dame Ilsabill awoke it was broad daylight, and

3	

3	3	she jogged the fisherman with her elbow, and said, ‘Get up, husband,

3	

3	3	and bestir yourself, for we must be king of all the land.’ ‘Wife, wife,’

3	

3	3	said the man, ‘why should we wish to be the king? I will not be king.’

3	

3	3	‘Then I will,’ said she. ‘But, wife,’ said the fisherman, ‘how can you

3	

3	3	be king--the fish cannot make you a king?’ ‘Husband,’ said she, ‘say

3	

3	3	no more about it, but go and try! I will be king.’ So the man went away

3	

3	3	quite sorrowful to think that his wife should want to be king. This time

3	

3	3	the sea looked a dark grey colour, and was overspread with curling waves

3	

3	3	and the ridges of foam as he cried out:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘O man of the sea!

3	

3	3	  Hearken to me!

3	

3	3	  My wife Ilsabill

3	

3	3	  Will have her own will,

3	

3	3	  And hath sent me to beg a boon of thee!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Well, what would she have now?’ said the fish. ‘Alas!’ said the poor

3	

3	3	man, ‘my wife wants to be king.’ ‘Go home,’ said the fish; ‘she is king

3	

3	3	already.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the fisherman went home; and as he came close to the palace he saw

3	

3	3	a troop of soldiers, and heard the sound of drums and trumpets. And when

3	

3	3	he went in he saw his wife sitting on a throne of gold and diamonds,

3	

3	3	with a golden crown upon her head; and on each side of her stood six

3	

3	3	fair maidens, each a head taller than the other. ‘Well, wife,’ said the

3	

3	3	fisherman, ‘are you king?’ ‘Yes,’ said she, ‘I am king.’ And when he had

3	

3	3	looked at her for a long time, he said, ‘Ah, wife! what a fine thing it

3	

3	3	is to be king! Now we shall never have anything more to wish for as long

3	

3	3	as we live.’ ‘I don’t know how that may be,’ said she; ‘never is a long

3	

3	3	time. I am king, it is true; but I begin to be tired of that, and I

3	

3	3	think I should like to be emperor.’ ‘Alas, wife! why should you wish to

3	

3	3	be emperor?’ said the fisherman. ‘Husband,’ said she, ‘go to the fish!

3	

3	3	I say I will be emperor.’ ‘Ah, wife!’ replied the fisherman, ‘the fish

3	

3	3	cannot make an emperor, I am sure, and I should not like to ask him for

3	

3	3	such a thing.’ ‘I am king,’ said Ilsabill, ‘and you are my slave; so go

3	

3	3	at once!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So the fisherman was forced to go; and he muttered as he went along,

3	

3	3	‘This will come to no good, it is too much to ask; the fish will be

3	

3	3	tired at last, and then we shall be sorry for what we have done.’ He

3	

3	3	soon came to the seashore; and the water was quite black and muddy, and

3	

3	3	a mighty whirlwind blew over the waves and rolled them about, but he

3	

3	3	went as near as he could to the water’s brink, and said:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘O man of the sea!

3	

3	3	  Hearken to me!

3	

3	3	  My wife Ilsabill

3	

3	3	  Will have her own will,

3	

3	3	  And hath sent me to beg a boon of thee!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘What would she have now?’ said the fish. ‘Ah!’ said the fisherman,

3	

3	3	‘she wants to be emperor.’ ‘Go home,’ said the fish; ‘she is emperor

3	

3	3	already.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So he went home again; and as he came near he saw his wife Ilsabill

3	

3	3	sitting on a very lofty throne made of solid gold, with a great crown on

3	

3	3	her head full two yards high; and on each side of her stood her guards

3	

3	3	and attendants in a row, each one smaller than the other, from the

3	

3	3	tallest giant down to a little dwarf no bigger than my finger. And

3	

3	3	before her stood princes, and dukes, and earls: and the fisherman went

3	

3	3	up to her and said, ‘Wife, are you emperor?’ ‘Yes,’ said she, ‘I am

3	

3	3	emperor.’ ‘Ah!’ said the man, as he gazed upon her, ‘what a fine thing

3	

3	3	it is to be emperor!’ ‘Husband,’ said she, ‘why should we stop at being

3	

3	3	emperor? I will be pope next.’ ‘O wife, wife!’ said he, ‘how can you be

3	

3	3	pope? there is but one pope at a time in Christendom.’ ‘Husband,’ said

3	

3	3	she, ‘I will be pope this very day.’ ‘But,’ replied the husband, ‘the

3	

3	3	fish cannot make you pope.’ ‘What nonsense!’ said she; ‘if he can make

3	

3	3	an emperor, he can make a pope: go and try him.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So the fisherman went. But when he came to the shore the wind was raging

3	

3	3	and the sea was tossed up and down in boiling waves, and the ships were

3	

3	3	in trouble, and rolled fearfully upon the tops of the billows. In the

3	

3	3	middle of the heavens there was a little piece of blue sky, but towards

3	

3	3	the south all was red, as if a dreadful storm was rising. At this sight

3	

3	3	the fisherman was dreadfully frightened, and he trembled so that his

3	

3	3	knees knocked together: but still he went down near to the shore, and

3	

3	3	said:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘O man of the sea!

3	

3	3	  Hearken to me!

3	

3	3	  My wife Ilsabill

3	

3	3	  Will have her own will,

3	

3	3	  And hath sent me to beg a boon of thee!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘What does she want now?’ said the fish. ‘Ah!’ said the fisherman, ‘my

3	

3	3	wife wants to be pope.’ ‘Go home,’ said the fish; ‘she is pope already.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the fisherman went home, and found Ilsabill sitting on a throne

3	

3	3	that was two miles high. And she had three great crowns on her head, and

3	

3	3	around her stood all the pomp and power of the Church. And on each side

3	

3	3	of her were two rows of burning lights, of all sizes, the greatest as

3	

3	3	large as the highest and biggest tower in the world, and the least no

3	

3	3	larger than a small rushlight. ‘Wife,’ said the fisherman, as he looked

3	

3	3	at all this greatness, ‘are you pope?’ ‘Yes,’ said she, ‘I am pope.’

3	

3	3	‘Well, wife,’ replied he, ‘it is a grand thing to be pope; and now

3	

3	3	you must be easy, for you can be nothing greater.’ ‘I will think about

3	

3	3	that,’ said the wife. Then they went to bed: but Dame Ilsabill could not

3	

3	3	sleep all night for thinking what she should be next. At last, as she

3	

3	3	was dropping asleep, morning broke, and the sun rose. ‘Ha!’ thought she,

3	

3	3	as she woke up and looked at it through the window, ‘after all I cannot

3	

3	3	prevent the sun rising.’ At this thought she was very angry, and wakened

3	

3	3	her husband, and said, ‘Husband, go to the fish and tell him I must

3	

3	3	be lord of the sun and moon.’ The fisherman was half asleep, but the

3	

3	3	thought frightened him so much that he started and fell out of bed.

3	

3	3	‘Alas, wife!’ said he, ‘cannot you be easy with being pope?’ ‘No,’

3	

3	3	said she, ‘I am very uneasy as long as the sun and moon rise without my

3	

3	3	leave. Go to the fish at once!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the man went shivering with fear; and as he was going down to

3	

3	3	the shore a dreadful storm arose, so that the trees and the very rocks

3	

3	3	shook. And all the heavens became black with stormy clouds, and the

3	

3	3	lightnings played, and the thunders rolled; and you might have seen in

3	

3	3	the sea great black waves, swelling up like mountains with crowns of

3	

3	3	white foam upon their heads. And the fisherman crept towards the sea,

3	

3	3	and cried out, as well as he could:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘O man of the sea!

3	

3	3	  Hearken to me!

3	

3	3	  My wife Ilsabill

3	

3	3	  Will have her own will,

3	

3	3	  And hath sent me to beg a boon of thee!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘What does she want now?’ said the fish. ‘Ah!’ said he, ‘she wants to

3	

3	3	be lord of the sun and moon.’ ‘Go home,’ said the fish, ‘to your pigsty

3	

3	3	again.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And there they live to this very day.

3	

3	3

3	3	THE WILLOW-WREN AND THE BEAR

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Once in summer-time the bear and the wolf were walking in the forest,

3	

3	3	and the bear heard a bird singing so beautifully that he said: ‘Brother

3	

3	3	wolf, what bird is it that sings so well?’ ‘That is the King of birds,’

3	

3	3	said the wolf, ‘before whom we must bow down.’ In reality the bird was

3	

3	3	the willow-wren. ‘IF that’s the case,’ said the bear, ‘I should very

3	

3	3	much like to see his royal palace; come, take me thither.’ ‘That is not

3	

3	3	done quite as you seem to think,’ said the wolf; ‘you must wait until

3	

3	3	the Queen comes,’ Soon afterwards, the Queen arrived with some food in

3	

3	3	her beak, and the lord King came too, and they began to feed their young

3	

3	3	ones. The bear would have liked to go at once, but the wolf held him

3	

3	3	back by the sleeve, and said: ‘No, you must wait until the lord and lady

3	

3	3	Queen have gone away again.’ So they took stock of the hole where the

3	

3	3	nest lay, and trotted away. The bear, however, could not rest until he

3	

3	3	had seen the royal palace, and when a short time had passed, went to it

3	

3	3	again. The King and Queen had just flown out, so he peeped in and saw

3	

3	3	five or six young ones lying there. ‘Is that the royal palace?’ cried

3	

3	3	the bear; ‘it is a wretched palace, and you are not King’s children, you

3	

3	3	are disreputable children!’ When the young wrens heard that, they were

3	

3	3	frightfully angry, and screamed: ‘No, that we are not! Our parents are

3	

3	3	honest people! Bear, you will have to pay for that!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The bear and the wolf grew uneasy, and turned back and went into their

3	

3	3	holes. The young willow-wrens, however, continued to cry and scream, and

3	

3	3	when their parents again brought food they said: ‘We will not so much as

3	

3	3	touch one fly’s leg, no, not if we were dying of hunger, until you have

3	

3	3	settled whether we are respectable children or not; the bear has been

3	

3	3	here and has insulted us!’ Then the old King said: ‘Be easy, he shall

3	

3	3	be punished,’ and he at once flew with the Queen to the bear’s cave, and

3	

3	3	called in: ‘Old Growler, why have you insulted my children? You shall

3	

3	3	suffer for it--we will punish you by a bloody war.’ Thus war was

3	

3	3	announced to the Bear, and all four-footed animals were summoned to take

3	

3	3	part in it, oxen, asses, cows, deer, and every other animal the earth

3	

3	3	contained. And the willow-wren summoned everything which flew in the

3	

3	3	air, not only birds, large and small, but midges, and hornets, bees and

3	

3	3	flies had to come.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the time came for the war to begin, the willow-wren sent out spies

3	

3	3	to discover who was the enemy’s commander-in-chief. The gnat, who was

3	

3	3	the most crafty, flew into the forest where the enemy was assembled,

3	

3	3	and hid herself beneath a leaf of the tree where the password was to be

3	

3	3	announced. There stood the bear, and he called the fox before him

3	

3	3	and said: ‘Fox, you are the most cunning of all animals, you shall be

3	

3	3	general and lead us.’ ‘Good,’ said the fox, ‘but what signal shall we

3	

3	3	agree upon?’ No one knew that, so the fox said: ‘I have a fine long

3	

3	3	bushy tail, which almost looks like a plume of red feathers. When I lift

3	

3	3	my tail up quite high, all is going well, and you must charge; but if I

3	

3	3	let it hang down, run away as fast as you can.’ When the gnat had heard

3	

3	3	that, she flew away again, and revealed everything, down to the minutest

3	

3	3	detail, to the willow-wren. When day broke, and the battle was to begin,

3	

3	3	all the four-footed animals came running up with such a noise that the

3	

3	3	earth trembled. The willow-wren with his army also came flying through

3	

3	3	the air with such a humming, and whirring, and swarming that every one

3	

3	3	was uneasy and afraid, and on both sides they advanced against each

3	

3	3	other. But the willow-wren sent down the hornet, with orders to settle

3	

3	3	beneath the fox’s tail, and sting with all his might. When the fox felt

3	

3	3	the first string, he started so that he lifted one leg, from pain, but

3	

3	3	he bore it, and still kept his tail high in the air; at the second

3	

3	3	sting, he was forced to put it down for a moment; at the third, he could

3	

3	3	hold out no longer, screamed, and put his tail between his legs. When

3	

3	3	the animals saw that, they thought all was lost, and began to flee, each

3	

3	3	into his hole, and the birds had won the battle.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the King and Queen flew home to their children and cried:

3	

3	3	‘Children, rejoice, eat and drink to your heart’s content, we have won

3	

3	3	the battle!’ But the young wrens said: ‘We will not eat yet, the bear

3	

3	3	must come to the nest, and beg for pardon and say that we are honourable

3	

3	3	children, before we will do that.’ Then the willow-wren flew to the

3	

3	3	bear’s hole and cried: ‘Growler, you are to come to the nest to my

3	

3	3	children, and beg their pardon, or else every rib of your body shall

3	

3	3	be broken.’ So the bear crept thither in the greatest fear, and begged

3	

3	3	their pardon. And now at last the young wrens were satisfied, and sat

3	

3	3	down together and ate and drank, and made merry till quite late into the

3	

3	3	night.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE FROG-PRINCE

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	One fine evening a young princess put on her bonnet and clogs, and went

3	

3	3	out to take a walk by herself in a wood; and when she came to a cool

3	

3	3	spring of water, that rose in the midst of it, she sat herself down

3	

3	3	to rest a while. Now she had a golden ball in her hand, which was her

3	

3	3	favourite plaything; and she was always tossing it up into the air, and

3	

3	3	catching it again as it fell. After a time she threw it up so high that

3	

3	3	she missed catching it as it fell; and the ball bounded away, and rolled

3	

3	3	along upon the ground, till at last it fell down into the spring. The

3	

3	3	princess looked into the spring after her ball, but it was very deep, so

3	

3	3	deep that she could not see the bottom of it. Then she began to bewail

3	

3	3	her loss, and said, ‘Alas! if I could only get my ball again, I would

3	

3	3	give all my fine clothes and jewels, and everything that I have in the

3	

3	3	world.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Whilst she was speaking, a frog put its head out of the water, and said,

3	

3	3	‘Princess, why do you weep so bitterly?’ ‘Alas!’ said she, ‘what can you

3	

3	3	do for me, you nasty frog? My golden ball has fallen into the spring.’

3	

3	3	The frog said, ‘I want not your pearls, and jewels, and fine clothes;

3	

3	3	but if you will love me, and let me live with you and eat from off

3	

3	3	your golden plate, and sleep upon your bed, I will bring you your ball

3	

3	3	again.’ ‘What nonsense,’ thought the princess, ‘this silly frog is

3	

3	3	talking! He can never even get out of the spring to visit me, though

3	

3	3	he may be able to get my ball for me, and therefore I will tell him he

3	

3	3	shall have what he asks.’ So she said to the frog, ‘Well, if you will

3	

3	3	bring me my ball, I will do all you ask.’ Then the frog put his head

3	

3	3	down, and dived deep under the water; and after a little while he came

3	

3	3	up again, with the ball in his mouth, and threw it on the edge of the

3	

3	3	spring. As soon as the young princess saw her ball, she ran to pick

3	

3	3	it up; and she was so overjoyed to have it in her hand again, that she

3	

3	3	never thought of the frog, but ran home with it as fast as she could.

3	

3	3	The frog called after her, ‘Stay, princess, and take me with you as you

3	

3	3	said,’ But she did not stop to hear a word.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The next day, just as the princess had sat down to dinner, she heard a

3	

3	3	strange noise--tap, tap--plash, plash--as if something was coming up the

3	

3	3	marble staircase: and soon afterwards there was a gentle knock at the

3	

3	3	door, and a little voice cried out and said:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Open the door, my princess dear,

3	

3	3	  Open the door to thy true love here!

3	

3	3	  And mind the words that thou and I said

3	

3	3	  By the fountain cool, in the greenwood shade.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the princess ran to the door and opened it, and there she saw

3	

3	3	the frog, whom she had quite forgotten. At this sight she was sadly

3	

3	3	frightened, and shutting the door as fast as she could came back to her

3	

3	3	seat. The king, her father, seeing that something had frightened her,

3	

3	3	asked her what was the matter. ‘There is a nasty frog,’ said she, ‘at

3	

3	3	the door, that lifted my ball for me out of the spring this morning: I

3	

3	3	told him that he should live with me here, thinking that he could never

3	

3	3	get out of the spring; but there he is at the door, and he wants to come

3	

3	3	in.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	While she was speaking the frog knocked again at the door, and said:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Open the door, my princess dear,

3	

3	3	  Open the door to thy true love here!

3	

3	3	  And mind the words that thou and I said

3	

3	3	  By the fountain cool, in the greenwood shade.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the king said to the young princess, ‘As you have given your word

3	

3	3	you must keep it; so go and let him in.’ She did so, and the frog hopped

3	

3	3	into the room, and then straight on--tap, tap--plash, plash--from the

3	

3	3	bottom of the room to the top, till he came up close to the table where

3	

3	3	the princess sat. ‘Pray lift me upon chair,’ said he to the princess,

3	

3	3	‘and let me sit next to you.’ As soon as she had done this, the frog

3	

3	3	said, ‘Put your plate nearer to me, that I may eat out of it.’ This

3	

3	3	she did, and when he had eaten as much as he could, he said, ‘Now I am

3	

3	3	tired; carry me upstairs, and put me into your bed.’ And the princess,

3	

3	3	though very unwilling, took him up in her hand, and put him upon the

3	

3	3	pillow of her own bed, where he slept all night long. As soon as it was

3	

3	3	light he jumped up, hopped downstairs, and went out of the house.

3	

3	3	‘Now, then,’ thought the princess, ‘at last he is gone, and I shall be

3	

3	3	troubled with him no more.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But she was mistaken; for when night came again she heard the same

3	

3	3	tapping at the door; and the frog came once more, and said:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Open the door, my princess dear,

3	

3	3	  Open the door to thy true love here!

3	

3	3	  And mind the words that thou and I said

3	

3	3	  By the fountain cool, in the greenwood shade.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And when the princess opened the door the frog came in, and slept upon

3	

3	3	her pillow as before, till the morning broke. And the third night he did

3	

3	3	the same. But when the princess awoke on the following morning she was

3	

3	3	astonished to see, instead of the frog, a handsome prince, gazing on her

3	

3	3	with the most beautiful eyes she had ever seen, and standing at the head

3	

3	3	of her bed.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	He told her that he had been enchanted by a spiteful fairy, who had

3	

3	3	changed him into a frog; and that he had been fated so to abide till

3	

3	3	some princess should take him out of the spring, and let him eat from

3	

3	3	her plate, and sleep upon her bed for three nights. ‘You,’ said the

3	

3	3	prince, ‘have broken his cruel charm, and now I have nothing to wish for

3	

3	3	but that you should go with me into my father’s kingdom, where I will

3	

3	3	marry you, and love you as long as you live.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The young princess, you may be sure, was not long in saying ‘Yes’ to

3	

3	3	all this; and as they spoke a gay coach drove up, with eight beautiful

3	

3	3	horses, decked with plumes of feathers and a golden harness; and behind

3	

3	3	the coach rode the prince’s servant, faithful Heinrich, who had bewailed

3	

3	3	the misfortunes of his dear master during his enchantment so long and so

3	

3	3	bitterly, that his heart had well-nigh burst.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	They then took leave of the king, and got into the coach with eight

3	

3	3	horses, and all set out, full of joy and merriment, for the prince’s

3	

3	3	kingdom, which they reached safely; and there they lived happily a great

3	

3	3	many years.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	CAT AND MOUSE IN PARTNERSHIP

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	A certain cat had made the acquaintance of a mouse, and had said so much

3	

3	3	to her about the great love and friendship she felt for her, that at

3	

3	3	length the mouse agreed that they should live and keep house together.

3	

3	3	‘But we must make a provision for winter, or else we shall suffer

3	

3	3	from hunger,’ said the cat; ‘and you, little mouse, cannot venture

3	

3	3	everywhere, or you will be caught in a trap some day.’ The good advice

3	

3	3	was followed, and a pot of fat was bought, but they did not know where

3	

3	3	to put it. At length, after much consideration, the cat said: ‘I know no

3	

3	3	place where it will be better stored up than in the church, for no one

3	

3	3	dares take anything away from there. We will set it beneath the altar,

3	

3	3	and not touch it until we are really in need of it.’ So the pot was

3	

3	3	placed in safety, but it was not long before the cat had a great

3	

3	3	yearning for it, and said to the mouse: ‘I want to tell you something,

3	

3	3	little mouse; my cousin has brought a little son into the world, and has

3	

3	3	asked me to be godmother; he is white with brown spots, and I am to hold

3	

3	3	him over the font at the christening. Let me go out today, and you look

3	

3	3	after the house by yourself.’ ‘Yes, yes,’ answered the mouse, ‘by all

3	

3	3	means go, and if you get anything very good to eat, think of me. I

3	

3	3	should like a drop of sweet red christening wine myself.’ All this,

3	

3	3	however, was untrue; the cat had no cousin, and had not been asked to

3	

3	3	be godmother. She went straight to the church, stole to the pot of fat,

3	

3	3	began to lick at it, and licked the top of the fat off. Then she took a

3	

3	3	walk upon the roofs of the town, looked out for opportunities, and then

3	

3	3	stretched herself in the sun, and licked her lips whenever she thought

3	

3	3	of the pot of fat, and not until it was evening did she return home.

3	

3	3	‘Well, here you are again,’ said the mouse, ‘no doubt you have had a

3	

3	3	merry day.’ ‘All went off well,’ answered the cat. ‘What name did they

3	

3	3	give the child?’ ‘Top off!’ said the cat quite coolly. ‘Top off!’ cried

3	

3	3	the mouse, ‘that is a very odd and uncommon name, is it a usual one in

3	

3	3	your family?’ ‘What does that matter,’ said the cat, ‘it is no worse

3	

3	3	than Crumb-stealer, as your godchildren are called.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Before long the cat was seized by another fit of yearning. She said to

3	

3	3	the mouse: ‘You must do me a favour, and once more manage the house for

3	

3	3	a day alone. I am again asked to be godmother, and, as the child has a

3	

3	3	white ring round its neck, I cannot refuse.’ The good mouse consented,

3	

3	3	but the cat crept behind the town walls to the church, and devoured

3	

3	3	half the pot of fat. ‘Nothing ever seems so good as what one keeps to

3	

3	3	oneself,’ said she, and was quite satisfied with her day’s work. When

3	

3	3	she went home the mouse inquired: ‘And what was the child christened?’

3	

3	3	‘Half-done,’ answered the cat. ‘Half-done! What are you saying? I

3	

3	3	never heard the name in my life, I’ll wager anything it is not in the

3	

3	3	calendar!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The cat’s mouth soon began to water for some more licking. ‘All good

3	

3	3	things go in threes,’ said she, ‘I am asked to stand godmother again.

3	

3	3	The child is quite black, only it has white paws, but with that

3	

3	3	exception, it has not a single white hair on its whole body; this only

3	

3	3	happens once every few years, you will let me go, won’t you?’ ‘Top-off!

3	

3	3	Half-done!’ answered the mouse, ‘they are such odd names, they make me

3	

3	3	very thoughtful.’ ‘You sit at home,’ said the cat, ‘in your dark-grey

3	

3	3	fur coat and long tail, and are filled with fancies, that’s because

3	

3	3	you do not go out in the daytime.’ During the cat’s absence the mouse

3	

3	3	cleaned the house, and put it in order, but the greedy cat entirely

3	

3	3	emptied the pot of fat. ‘When everything is eaten up one has some

3	

3	3	peace,’ said she to herself, and well filled and fat she did not return

3	

3	3	home till night. The mouse at once asked what name had been given to

3	

3	3	the third child. ‘It will not please you more than the others,’ said the

3	

3	3	cat. ‘He is called All-gone.’ ‘All-gone,’ cried the mouse ‘that is the

3	

3	3	most suspicious name of all! I have never seen it in print. All-gone;

3	

3	3	what can that mean?’ and she shook her head, curled herself up, and lay

3	

3	3	down to sleep.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	From this time forth no one invited the cat to be godmother, but

3	

3	3	when the winter had come and there was no longer anything to be found

3	

3	3	outside, the mouse thought of their provision, and said: ‘Come, cat,

3	

3	3	we will go to our pot of fat which we have stored up for ourselves--we

3	

3	3	shall enjoy that.’ ‘Yes,’ answered the cat, ‘you will enjoy it as much

3	

3	3	as you would enjoy sticking that dainty tongue of yours out of the

3	

3	3	window.’ They set out on their way, but when they arrived, the pot of

3	

3	3	fat certainly was still in its place, but it was empty. ‘Alas!’ said the

3	

3	3	mouse, ‘now I see what has happened, now it comes to light! You are a true

3	

3	3	friend! You have devoured all when you were standing godmother. First

3	

3	3	top off, then half-done, then--’ ‘Will you hold your tongue,’ cried the

3	

3	3	cat, ‘one word more, and I will eat you too.’ ‘All-gone’ was already on

3	

3	3	the poor mouse’s lips; scarcely had she spoken it before the cat sprang

3	

3	3	on her, seized her, and swallowed her down. Verily, that is the way of

3	

3	3	the world.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE GOOSE-GIRL

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The king of a great land died, and left his queen to take care of their

3	

3	3	only child. This child was a daughter, who was very beautiful; and her

3	

3	3	mother loved her dearly, and was very kind to her. And there was a good

3	

3	3	fairy too, who was fond of the princess, and helped her mother to watch

3	

3	3	over her. When she grew up, she was betrothed to a prince who lived a

3	

3	3	great way off; and as the time drew near for her to be married, she

3	

3	3	got ready to set off on her journey to his country. Then the queen her

3	

3	3	mother, packed up a great many costly things; jewels, and gold, and

3	

3	3	silver; trinkets, fine dresses, and in short everything that became a

3	

3	3	royal bride. And she gave her a waiting-maid to ride with her, and give

3	

3	3	her into the bridegroom’s hands; and each had a horse for the journey.

3	

3	3	Now the princess’s horse was the fairy’s gift, and it was called Falada,

3	

3	3	and could speak.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the time came for them to set out, the fairy went into her

3	

3	3	bed-chamber, and took a little knife, and cut off a lock of her hair,

3	

3	3	and gave it to the princess, and said, ‘Take care of it, dear child; for

3	

3	3	it is a charm that may be of use to you on the road.’ Then they all took

3	

3	3	a sorrowful leave of the princess; and she put the lock of hair into

3	

3	3	her bosom, got upon her horse, and set off on her journey to her

3	

3	3	bridegroom’s kingdom.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	One day, as they were riding along by a brook, the princess began to

3	

3	3	feel very thirsty: and she said to her maid, ‘Pray get down, and fetch

3	

3	3	me some water in my golden cup out of yonder brook, for I want to

3	

3	3	drink.’ ‘Nay,’ said the maid, ‘if you are thirsty, get off yourself, and

3	

3	3	stoop down by the water and drink; I shall not be your waiting-maid any

3	

3	3	longer.’ Then she was so thirsty that she got down, and knelt over the

3	

3	3	little brook, and drank; for she was frightened, and dared not bring out

3	

3	3	her golden cup; and she wept and said, ‘Alas! what will become of me?’

3	

3	3	And the lock answered her, and said:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Alas! alas! if thy mother knew it,

3	

3	3	  Sadly, sadly, would she rue it.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But the princess was very gentle and meek, so she said nothing to her

3	

3	3	maid’s ill behaviour, but got upon her horse again.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then all rode farther on their journey, till the day grew so warm, and

3	

3	3	the sun so scorching, that the bride began to feel very thirsty again;

3	

3	3	and at last, when they came to a river, she forgot her maid’s rude

3	

3	3	speech, and said, ‘Pray get down, and fetch me some water to drink in

3	

3	3	my golden cup.’ But the maid answered her, and even spoke more haughtily

3	

3	3	than before: ‘Drink if you will, but I shall not be your waiting-maid.’

3	

3	3	Then the princess was so thirsty that she got off her horse, and lay

3	

3	3	down, and held her head over the running stream, and cried and said,

3	

3	3	‘What will become of me?’ And the lock of hair answered her again:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Alas! alas! if thy mother knew it,

3	

3	3	  Sadly, sadly, would she rue it.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And as she leaned down to drink, the lock of hair fell from her bosom,

3	

3	3	and floated away with the water. Now she was so frightened that she did

3	

3	3	not see it; but her maid saw it, and was very glad, for she knew the

3	

3	3	charm; and she saw that the poor bride would be in her power, now that

3	

3	3	she had lost the hair. So when the bride had done drinking, and would

3	

3	3	have got upon Falada again, the maid said, ‘I shall ride upon Falada,

3	

3	3	and you may have my horse instead’; so she was forced to give up her

3	

3	3	horse, and soon afterwards to take off her royal clothes and put on her

3	

3	3	maid’s shabby ones.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	At last, as they drew near the end of their journey, this treacherous

3	

3	3	servant threatened to kill her mistress if she ever told anyone what had

3	

3	3	happened. But Falada saw it all, and marked it well.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the waiting-maid got upon Falada, and the real bride rode upon the

3	

3	3	other horse, and they went on in this way till at last they came to the

3	

3	3	royal court. There was great joy at their coming, and the prince flew to

3	

3	3	meet them, and lifted the maid from her horse, thinking she was the one

3	

3	3	who was to be his wife; and she was led upstairs to the royal chamber;

3	

3	3	but the true princess was told to stay in the court below.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Now the old king happened just then to have nothing else to do; so he

3	

3	3	amused himself by sitting at his kitchen window, looking at what was

3	

3	3	going on; and he saw her in the courtyard. As she looked very pretty,

3	

3	3	and too delicate for a waiting-maid, he went up into the royal chamber

3	

3	3	to ask the bride who it was she had brought with her, that was thus left

3	

3	3	standing in the court below. ‘I brought her with me for the sake of her

3	

3	3	company on the road,’ said she; ‘pray give the girl some work to do,

3	

3	3	that she may not be idle.’ The old king could not for some time think

3	

3	3	of any work for her to do; but at last he said, ‘I have a lad who takes

3	

3	3	care of my geese; she may go and help him.’ Now the name of this lad,

3	

3	3	that the real bride was to help in watching the king’s geese, was

3	

3	3	Curdken.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But the false bride said to the prince, ‘Dear husband, pray do me one

3	

3	3	piece of kindness.’ ‘That I will,’ said the prince. ‘Then tell one of

3	

3	3	your slaughterers to cut off the head of the horse I rode upon, for it

3	

3	3	was very unruly, and plagued me sadly on the road’; but the truth was,

3	

3	3	she was very much afraid lest Falada should some day or other speak, and

3	

3	3	tell all she had done to the princess. She carried her point, and the

3	

3	3	faithful Falada was killed; but when the true princess heard of it, she

3	

3	3	wept, and begged the man to nail up Falada’s head against a large

3	

3	3	dark gate of the city, through which she had to pass every morning

3	

3	3	and evening, that there she might still see him sometimes. Then the

3	

3	3	slaughterer said he would do as she wished; and cut off the head, and

3	

3	3	nailed it up under the dark gate.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Early the next morning, as she and Curdken went out through the gate,

3	

3	3	she said sorrowfully:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Falada, Falada, there thou hangest!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	and the head answered:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Bride, bride, there thou gangest!

3	

3	3	  Alas! alas! if thy mother knew it,

3	

3	3	  Sadly, sadly, would she rue it.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then they went out of the city, and drove the geese on. And when she

3	

3	3	came to the meadow, she sat down upon a bank there, and let down her

3	

3	3	waving locks of hair, which were all of pure silver; and when Curdken

3	

3	3	saw it glitter in the sun, he ran up, and would have pulled some of the

3	

3	3	locks out, but she cried:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Blow, breezes, blow!

3	

3	3	  Let Curdken’s hat go!

3	

3	3	  Blow, breezes, blow!

3	

3	3	  Let him after it go!

3	

3	3	  O’er hills, dales, and rocks,

3	

3	3	  Away be it whirl’d

3	

3	3	  Till the silvery locks

3	

3	3	  Are all comb’d and curl’d!

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then there came a wind, so strong that it blew off Curdken’s hat; and

3	

3	3	away it flew over the hills: and he was forced to turn and run after

3	

3	3	it; till, by the time he came back, she had done combing and curling her

3	

3	3	hair, and had put it up again safe. Then he was very angry and sulky,

3	

3	3	and would not speak to her at all; but they watched the geese until it

3	

3	3	grew dark in the evening, and then drove them homewards.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The next morning, as they were going through the dark gate, the poor

3	

3	3	girl looked up at Falada’s head, and cried:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Falada, Falada, there thou hangest!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	and the head answered:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Bride, bride, there thou gangest!

3	

3	3	  Alas! alas! if thy mother knew it,

3	

3	3	  Sadly, sadly, would she rue it.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then she drove on the geese, and sat down again in the meadow, and began

3	

3	3	to comb out her hair as before; and Curdken ran up to her, and wanted to

3	

3	3	take hold of it; but she cried out quickly:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Blow, breezes, blow!

3	

3	3	  Let Curdken’s hat go!

3	

3	3	  Blow, breezes, blow!

3	

3	3	  Let him after it go!

3	

3	3	  O’er hills, dales, and rocks,

3	

3	3	  Away be it whirl’d

3	

3	3	  Till the silvery locks

3	

3	3	  Are all comb’d and curl’d!

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the wind came and blew away his hat; and off it flew a great way,

3	

3	3	over the hills and far away, so that he had to run after it; and when

3	

3	3	he came back she had bound up her hair again, and all was safe. So they

3	

3	3	watched the geese till it grew dark.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	In the evening, after they came home, Curdken went to the old king, and

3	

3	3	said, ‘I cannot have that strange girl to help me to keep the geese any

3	

3	3	longer.’ ‘Why?’ said the king. ‘Because, instead of doing any good, she

3	

3	3	does nothing but tease me all day long.’ Then the king made him tell him

3	

3	3	what had happened. And Curdken said, ‘When we go in the morning through

3	

3	3	the dark gate with our flock of geese, she cries and talks with the head

3	

3	3	of a horse that hangs upon the wall, and says:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Falada, Falada, there thou hangest!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	and the head answers:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Bride, bride, there thou gangest!

3	

3	3	  Alas! alas! if thy mother knew it,

3	

3	3	  Sadly, sadly, would she rue it.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And Curdken went on telling the king what had happened upon the meadow

3	

3	3	where the geese fed; how his hat was blown away; and how he was forced

3	

3	3	to run after it, and to leave his flock of geese to themselves. But the

3	

3	3	old king told the boy to go out again the next day: and when morning

3	

3	3	came, he placed himself behind the dark gate, and heard how she spoke

3	

3	3	to Falada, and how Falada answered. Then he went into the field, and

3	

3	3	hid himself in a bush by the meadow’s side; and he soon saw with his own

3	

3	3	eyes how they drove the flock of geese; and how, after a little time,

3	

3	3	she let down her hair that glittered in the sun. And then he heard her

3	

3	3	say:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Blow, breezes, blow!

3	

3	3	  Let Curdken’s hat go!

3	

3	3	  Blow, breezes, blow!

3	

3	3	  Let him after it go!

3	

3	3	  O’er hills, dales, and rocks,

3	

3	3	  Away be it whirl’d

3	

3	3	  Till the silvery locks

3	

3	3	  Are all comb’d and curl’d!

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And soon came a gale of wind, and carried away Curdken’s hat, and away

3	

3	3	went Curdken after it, while the girl went on combing and curling her

3	

3	3	hair. All this the old king saw: so he went home without being seen; and

3	

3	3	when the little goose-girl came back in the evening he called her aside,

3	

3	3	and asked her why she did so: but she burst into tears, and said, ‘That

3	

3	3	I must not tell you or any man, or I shall lose my life.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But the old king begged so hard, that she had no peace till she had told

3	

3	3	him all the tale, from beginning to end, word for word. And it was very

3	

3	3	lucky for her that she did so, for when she had done the king ordered

3	

3	3	royal clothes to be put upon her, and gazed on her with wonder, she was

3	

3	3	so beautiful. Then he called his son and told him that he had only a

3	

3	3	false bride; for that she was merely a waiting-maid, while the true

3	

3	3	bride stood by. And the young king rejoiced when he saw her beauty, and

3	

3	3	heard how meek and patient she had been; and without saying anything to

3	

3	3	the false bride, the king ordered a great feast to be got ready for all

3	

3	3	his court. The bridegroom sat at the top, with the false princess on one

3	

3	3	side, and the true one on the other; but nobody knew her again, for her

3	

3	3	beauty was quite dazzling to their eyes; and she did not seem at all

3	

3	3	like the little goose-girl, now that she had her brilliant dress on.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When they had eaten and drank, and were very merry, the old king said

3	

3	3	he would tell them a tale. So he began, and told all the story of the

3	

3	3	princess, as if it was one that he had once heard; and he asked the

3	

3	3	true waiting-maid what she thought ought to be done to anyone who would

3	

3	3	behave thus. ‘Nothing better,’ said this false bride, ‘than that she

3	

3	3	should be thrown into a cask stuck round with sharp nails, and that

3	

3	3	two white horses should be put to it, and should drag it from street to

3	

3	3	street till she was dead.’ ‘Thou art she!’ said the old king; ‘and as

3	

3	3	thou has judged thyself, so shall it be done to thee.’ And the young

3	

3	3	king was then married to his true wife, and they reigned over the

3	

3	3	kingdom in peace and happiness all their lives; and the good fairy came

3	

3	3	to see them, and restored the faithful Falada to life again.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE ADVENTURES OF CHANTICLEER AND PARTLET

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	1. HOW THEY WENT TO THE MOUNTAINS TO EAT NUTS

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘The nuts are quite ripe now,’ said Chanticleer to his wife Partlet,

3	

3	3	‘suppose we go together to the mountains, and eat as many as we can,

3	

3	3	before the squirrel takes them all away.’ ‘With all my heart,’ said

3	

3	3	Partlet, ‘let us go and make a holiday of it together.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So they went to the mountains; and as it was a lovely day, they stayed

3	

3	3	there till the evening. Now, whether it was that they had eaten so many

3	

3	3	nuts that they could not walk, or whether they were lazy and would not,

3	

3	3	I do not know: however, they took it into their heads that it did not

3	

3	3	become them to go home on foot. So Chanticleer began to build a little

3	

3	3	carriage of nutshells: and when it was finished, Partlet jumped into

3	

3	3	it and sat down, and bid Chanticleer harness himself to it and draw her

3	

3	3	home. ‘That’s a good joke!’ said Chanticleer; ‘no, that will never do;

3	

3	3	I had rather by half walk home; I’ll sit on the box and be coachman,

3	

3	3	if you like, but I’ll not draw.’ While this was passing, a duck came

3	

3	3	quacking up and cried out, ‘You thieving vagabonds, what business have

3	

3	3	you in my grounds? I’ll give it you well for your insolence!’ and upon

3	

3	3	that she fell upon Chanticleer most lustily. But Chanticleer was no

3	

3	3	coward, and returned the duck’s blows with his sharp spurs so fiercely

3	

3	3	that she soon began to cry out for mercy; which was only granted her

3	

3	3	upon condition that she would draw the carriage home for them. This she

3	

3	3	agreed to do; and Chanticleer got upon the box, and drove, crying, ‘Now,

3	

3	3	duck, get on as fast as you can.’ And away they went at a pretty good

3	

3	3	pace.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	After they had travelled along a little way, they met a needle and a pin

3	

3	3	walking together along the road: and the needle cried out, ‘Stop, stop!’

3	

3	3	and said it was so dark that they could hardly find their way, and such

3	

3	3	dirty walking they could not get on at all: he told them that he and his

3	

3	3	friend, the pin, had been at a public-house a few miles off, and had sat

3	

3	3	drinking till they had forgotten how late it was; he begged therefore

3	

3	3	that the travellers would be so kind as to give them a lift in their

3	

3	3	carriage. Chanticleer observing that they were but thin fellows, and not

3	

3	3	likely to take up much room, told them they might ride, but made them

3	

3	3	promise not to dirty the wheels of the carriage in getting in, nor to

3	

3	3	tread on Partlet’s toes.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Late at night they arrived at an inn; and as it was bad travelling in

3	

3	3	the dark, and the duck seemed much tired, and waddled about a good

3	

3	3	deal from one side to the other, they made up their minds to fix their

3	

3	3	quarters there: but the landlord at first was unwilling, and said his

3	

3	3	house was full, thinking they might not be very respectable company:

3	

3	3	however, they spoke civilly to him, and gave him the egg which Partlet

3	

3	3	had laid by the way, and said they would give him the duck, who was in

3	

3	3	the habit of laying one every day: so at last he let them come in, and

3	

3	3	they bespoke a handsome supper, and spent the evening very jollily.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Early in the morning, before it was quite light, and when nobody was

3	

3	3	stirring in the inn, Chanticleer awakened his wife, and, fetching the

3	

3	3	egg, they pecked a hole in it, ate it up, and threw the shells into the

3	

3	3	fireplace: they then went to the pin and needle, who were fast asleep,

3	

3	3	and seizing them by the heads, stuck one into the landlord’s easy chair

3	

3	3	and the other into his handkerchief; and, having done this, they crept

3	

3	3	away as softly as possible. However, the duck, who slept in the open

3	

3	3	air in the yard, heard them coming, and jumping into the brook which ran

3	

3	3	close by the inn, soon swam out of their reach.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	An hour or two afterwards the landlord got up, and took his handkerchief

3	

3	3	to wipe his face, but the pin ran into him and pricked him: then he

3	

3	3	walked into the kitchen to light his pipe at the fire, but when he

3	

3	3	stirred it up the eggshells flew into his eyes, and almost blinded him.

3	

3	3	‘Bless me!’ said he, ‘all the world seems to have a design against my

3	

3	3	head this morning’: and so saying, he threw himself sulkily into his

3	

3	3	easy chair; but, oh dear! the needle ran into him; and this time the

3	

3	3	pain was not in his head. He now flew into a very great passion, and,

3	

3	3	suspecting the company who had come in the night before, he went to look

3	

3	3	after them, but they were all off; so he swore that he never again

3	

3	3	would take in such a troop of vagabonds, who ate a great deal, paid no

3	

3	3	reckoning, and gave him nothing for his trouble but their apish tricks.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	2. HOW CHANTICLEER AND PARTLET WENT TO VISIT MR KORBES

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Another day, Chanticleer and Partlet wished to ride out together;

3	

3	3	so Chanticleer built a handsome carriage with four red wheels, and

3	

3	3	harnessed six mice to it; and then he and Partlet got into the carriage,

3	

3	3	and away they drove. Soon afterwards a cat met them, and said, ‘Where

3	

3	3	are you going?’ And Chanticleer replied,

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘All on our way

3	

3	3	  A visit to pay

3	

3	3	  To Mr Korbes, the fox, today.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the cat said, ‘Take me with you,’ Chanticleer said, ‘With all my

3	

3	3	heart: get up behind, and be sure you do not fall off.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Take care of this handsome coach of mine,

3	

3	3	  Nor dirty my pretty red wheels so fine!

3	

3	3	  Now, mice, be ready,

3	

3	3	  And, wheels, run steady!

3	

3	3	  For we are going a visit to pay

3	

3	3	  To Mr Korbes, the fox, today.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Soon after came up a millstone, an egg, a duck, and a pin; and

3	

3	3	Chanticleer gave them all leave to get into the carriage and go with

3	

3	3	them.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When they arrived at Mr Korbes’s house, he was not at home; so the mice

3	

3	3	drew the carriage into the coach-house, Chanticleer and Partlet flew

3	

3	3	upon a beam, the cat sat down in the fireplace, the duck got into

3	

3	3	the washing cistern, the pin stuck himself into the bed pillow, the

3	

3	3	millstone laid himself over the house door, and the egg rolled himself

3	

3	3	up in the towel.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When Mr Korbes came home, he went to the fireplace to make a fire; but

3	

3	3	the cat threw all the ashes in his eyes: so he ran to the kitchen to

3	

3	3	wash himself; but there the duck splashed all the water in his face; and

3	

3	3	when he tried to wipe himself, the egg broke to pieces in the towel all

3	

3	3	over his face and eyes. Then he was very angry, and went without his

3	

3	3	supper to bed; but when he laid his head on the pillow, the pin ran into

3	

3	3	his cheek: at this he became quite furious, and, jumping up, would have

3	

3	3	run out of the house; but when he came to the door, the millstone fell

3	

3	3	down on his head, and killed him on the spot.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	3. HOW PARTLET DIED AND WAS BURIED, AND HOW CHANTICLEER DIED OF GRIEF

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Another day Chanticleer and Partlet agreed to go again to the mountains

3	

3	3	to eat nuts; and it was settled that all the nuts which they found

3	

3	3	should be shared equally between them. Now Partlet found a very large

3	

3	3	nut; but she said nothing about it to Chanticleer, and kept it all to

3	

3	3	herself: however, it was so big that she could not swallow it, and it

3	

3	3	stuck in her throat. Then she was in a great fright, and cried out to

3	

3	3	Chanticleer, ‘Pray run as fast as you can, and fetch me some water, or I

3	

3	3	shall be choked.’ Chanticleer ran as fast as he could to the river, and

3	

3	3	said, ‘River, give me some water, for Partlet lies in the mountain, and

3	

3	3	will be choked by a great nut.’ The river said, ‘Run first to the bride,

3	

3	3	and ask her for a silken cord to draw up the water.’ Chanticleer ran to

3	

3	3	the bride, and said, ‘Bride, you must give me a silken cord, for then

3	

3	3	the river will give me water, and the water I will carry to Partlet, who

3	

3	3	lies on the mountain, and will be choked by a great nut.’ But the bride

3	

3	3	said, ‘Run first, and bring me my garland that is hanging on a willow

3	

3	3	in the garden.’ Then Chanticleer ran to the garden, and took the garland

3	

3	3	from the bough where it hung, and brought it to the bride; and then

3	

3	3	the bride gave him the silken cord, and he took the silken cord to

3	

3	3	the river, and the river gave him water, and he carried the water to

3	

3	3	Partlet; but in the meantime she was choked by the great nut, and lay

3	

3	3	quite dead, and never moved any more.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then Chanticleer was very sorry, and cried bitterly; and all the beasts

3	

3	3	came and wept with him over poor Partlet. And six mice built a little

3	

3	3	hearse to carry her to her grave; and when it was ready they harnessed

3	

3	3	themselves before it, and Chanticleer drove them. On the way they

3	

3	3	met the fox. ‘Where are you going, Chanticleer?’ said he. ‘To bury my

3	

3	3	Partlet,’ said the other. ‘May I go with you?’ said the fox. ‘Yes; but

3	

3	3	you must get up behind, or my horses will not be able to draw you.’ Then

3	

3	3	the fox got up behind; and presently the wolf, the bear, the goat, and

3	

3	3	all the beasts of the wood, came and climbed upon the hearse.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So on they went till they came to a rapid stream. ‘How shall we get

3	

3	3	over?’ said Chanticleer. Then said a straw, ‘I will lay myself across,

3	

3	3	and you may pass over upon me.’ But as the mice were going over, the

3	

3	3	straw slipped away and fell into the water, and the six mice all fell in

3	

3	3	and were drowned. What was to be done? Then a large log of wood came

3	

3	3	and said, ‘I am big enough; I will lay myself across the stream, and you

3	

3	3	shall pass over upon me.’ So he laid himself down; but they managed

3	

3	3	so clumsily, that the log of wood fell in and was carried away by the

3	

3	3	stream. Then a stone, who saw what had happened, came up and kindly

3	

3	3	offered to help poor Chanticleer by laying himself across the stream;

3	

3	3	and this time he got safely to the other side with the hearse, and

3	

3	3	managed to get Partlet out of it; but the fox and the other mourners,

3	

3	3	who were sitting behind, were too heavy, and fell back into the water

3	

3	3	and were all carried away by the stream and drowned.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Thus Chanticleer was left alone with his dead Partlet; and having dug

3	

3	3	a grave for her, he laid her in it, and made a little hillock over her.

3	

3	3	Then he sat down by the grave, and wept and mourned, till at last he

3	

3	3	died too; and so all were dead.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	RAPUNZEL

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There were once a man and a woman who had long in vain wished for a

3	

3	3	child. At length the woman hoped that God was about to grant her desire.

3	

3	3	These people had a little window at the back of their house from which

3	

3	3	a splendid garden could be seen, which was full of the most beautiful

3	

3	3	flowers and herbs. It was, however, surrounded by a high wall, and no

3	

3	3	one dared to go into it because it belonged to an enchantress, who had

3	

3	3	great power and was dreaded by all the world. One day the woman was

3	

3	3	standing by this window and looking down into the garden, when she saw a

3	

3	3	bed which was planted with the most beautiful rampion (rapunzel), and it

3	

3	3	looked so fresh and green that she longed for it, she quite pined away,

3	

3	3	and began to look pale and miserable. Then her husband was alarmed, and

3	

3	3	asked: ‘What ails you, dear wife?’ ‘Ah,’ she replied, ‘if I can’t eat

3	

3	3	some of the rampion, which is in the garden behind our house, I shall

3	

3	3	die.’ The man, who loved her, thought: ‘Sooner than let your wife die,

3	

3	3	bring her some of the rampion yourself, let it cost what it will.’

3	

3	3	At twilight, he clambered down over the wall into the garden of the

3	

3	3	enchantress, hastily clutched a handful of rampion, and took it to his

3	

3	3	wife. She at once made herself a salad of it, and ate it greedily. It

3	

3	3	tasted so good to her--so very good, that the next day she longed for it

3	

3	3	three times as much as before. If he was to have any rest, her husband

3	

3	3	must once more descend into the garden. In the gloom of evening

3	

3	3	therefore, he let himself down again; but when he had clambered down the

3	

3	3	wall he was terribly afraid, for he saw the enchantress standing before

3	

3	3	him. ‘How can you dare,’ said she with angry look, ‘descend into my

3	

3	3	garden and steal my rampion like a thief? You shall suffer for it!’

3	

3	3	‘Ah,’ answered he, ‘let mercy take the place of justice, I only made

3	

3	3	up my mind to do it out of necessity. My wife saw your rampion from the

3	

3	3	window, and felt such a longing for it that she would have died if she

3	

3	3	had not got some to eat.’ Then the enchantress allowed her anger to be

3	

3	3	softened, and said to him: ‘If the case be as you say, I will allow

3	

3	3	you to take away with you as much rampion as you will, only I make one

3	

3	3	condition, you must give me the child which your wife will bring into

3	

3	3	the world; it shall be well treated, and I will care for it like a

3	

3	3	mother.’ The man in his terror consented to everything, and when the

3	

3	3	woman was brought to bed, the enchantress appeared at once, gave the

3	

3	3	child the name of Rapunzel, and took it away with her.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Rapunzel grew into the most beautiful child under the sun. When she was

3	

3	3	twelve years old, the enchantress shut her into a tower, which lay in

3	

3	3	a forest, and had neither stairs nor door, but quite at the top was a

3	

3	3	little window. When the enchantress wanted to go in, she placed herself

3	

3	3	beneath it and cried:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Rapunzel, Rapunzel,

3	

3	3	  Let down your hair to me.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Rapunzel had magnificent long hair, fine as spun gold, and when she

3	

3	3	heard the voice of the enchantress she unfastened her braided tresses,

3	

3	3	wound them round one of the hooks of the window above, and then the hair

3	

3	3	fell twenty ells down, and the enchantress climbed up by it.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	After a year or two, it came to pass that the king’s son rode through

3	

3	3	the forest and passed by the tower. Then he heard a song, which was so

3	

3	3	charming that he stood still and listened. This was Rapunzel, who in her

3	

3	3	solitude passed her time in letting her sweet voice resound. The king’s

3	

3	3	son wanted to climb up to her, and looked for the door of the tower,

3	

3	3	but none was to be found. He rode home, but the singing had so deeply

3	

3	3	touched his heart, that every day he went out into the forest and

3	

3	3	listened to it. Once when he was thus standing behind a tree, he saw

3	

3	3	that an enchantress came there, and he heard how she cried:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Rapunzel, Rapunzel,

3	

3	3	  Let down your hair to me.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then Rapunzel let down the braids of her hair, and the enchantress

3	

3	3	climbed up to her. ‘If that is the ladder by which one mounts, I too

3	

3	3	will try my fortune,’ said he, and the next day when it began to grow

3	

3	3	dark, he went to the tower and cried:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Rapunzel, Rapunzel,

3	

3	3	  Let down your hair to me.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Immediately the hair fell down and the king’s son climbed up.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	At first Rapunzel was terribly frightened when a man, such as her eyes

3	

3	3	had never yet beheld, came to her; but the king’s son began to talk to

3	

3	3	her quite like a friend, and told her that his heart had been so stirred

3	

3	3	that it had let him have no rest, and he had been forced to see her.

3	

3	3	Then Rapunzel lost her fear, and when he asked her if she would take

3	

3	3	him for her husband, and she saw that he was young and handsome, she

3	

3	3	thought: ‘He will love me more than old Dame Gothel does’; and she said

3	

3	3	yes, and laid her hand in his. She said: ‘I will willingly go away with

3	

3	3	you, but I do not know how to get down. Bring with you a skein of silk

3	

3	3	every time that you come, and I will weave a ladder with it, and when

3	

3	3	that is ready I will descend, and you will take me on your horse.’ They

3	

3	3	agreed that until that time he should come to her every evening, for the

3	

3	3	old woman came by day. The enchantress remarked nothing of this, until

3	

3	3	once Rapunzel said to her: ‘Tell me, Dame Gothel, how it happens that

3	

3	3	you are so much heavier for me to draw up than the young king’s son--he

3	

3	3	is with me in a moment.’ ‘Ah! you wicked child,’ cried the enchantress.

3	

3	3	‘What do I hear you say! I thought I had separated you from all

3	

3	3	the world, and yet you have deceived me!’ In her anger she clutched

3	

3	3	Rapunzel’s beautiful tresses, wrapped them twice round her left hand,

3	

3	3	seized a pair of scissors with the right, and snip, snap, they were cut

3	

3	3	off, and the lovely braids lay on the ground. And she was so pitiless

3	

3	3	that she took poor Rapunzel into a desert where she had to live in great

3	

3	3	grief and misery.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	On the same day that she cast out Rapunzel, however, the enchantress

3	

3	3	fastened the braids of hair, which she had cut off, to the hook of the

3	

3	3	window, and when the king’s son came and cried:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Rapunzel, Rapunzel,

3	

3	3	  Let down your hair to me.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	she let the hair down. The king’s son ascended, but instead of finding

3	

3	3	his dearest Rapunzel, he found the enchantress, who gazed at him with

3	

3	3	wicked and venomous looks. ‘Aha!’ she cried mockingly, ‘you would fetch

3	

3	3	your dearest, but the beautiful bird sits no longer singing in the nest;

3	

3	3	the cat has got it, and will scratch out your eyes as well. Rapunzel is

3	

3	3	lost to you; you will never see her again.’ The king’s son was beside

3	

3	3	himself with pain, and in his despair he leapt down from the tower. He

3	

3	3	escaped with his life, but the thorns into which he fell pierced his

3	

3	3	eyes. Then he wandered quite blind about the forest, ate nothing but

3	

3	3	roots and berries, and did naught but lament and weep over the loss of

3	

3	3	his dearest wife. Thus he roamed about in misery for some years, and at

3	

3	3	length came to the desert where Rapunzel, with the twins to which she

3	

3	3	had given birth, a boy and a girl, lived in wretchedness. He heard a

3	

3	3	voice, and it seemed so familiar to him that he went towards it, and

3	

3	3	when he approached, Rapunzel knew him and fell on his neck and wept. Two

3	

3	3	of her tears wetted his eyes and they grew clear again, and he could

3	

3	3	see with them as before. He led her to his kingdom where he was

3	

3	3	joyfully received, and they lived for a long time afterwards, happy and

3	

3	3	contented.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	FUNDEVOGEL

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once a forester who went into the forest to hunt, and as

3	

3	3	he entered it he heard a sound of screaming as if a little child were

3	

3	3	there. He followed the sound, and at last came to a high tree, and at

3	

3	3	the top of this a little child was sitting, for the mother had fallen

3	

3	3	asleep under the tree with the child, and a bird of prey had seen it in

3	

3	3	her arms, had flown down, snatched it away, and set it on the high tree.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The forester climbed up, brought the child down, and thought to himself:

3	

3	3	‘You will take him home with you, and bring him up with your Lina.’ He

3	

3	3	took it home, therefore, and the two children grew up together. And the

3	

3	3	one, which he had found on a tree was called Fundevogel, because a bird

3	

3	3	had carried it away. Fundevogel and Lina loved each other so dearly that

3	

3	3	when they did not see each other they were sad.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Now the forester had an old cook, who one evening took two pails and

3	

3	3	began to fetch water, and did not go once only, but many times, out

3	

3	3	to the spring. Lina saw this and said, ‘Listen, old Sanna, why are you

3	

3	3	fetching so much water?’ ‘If you will never repeat it to anyone, I will

3	

3	3	tell you why.’ So Lina said, no, she would never repeat it to anyone,

3	

3	3	and then the cook said: ‘Early tomorrow morning, when the forester

3	

3	3	is out hunting, I will heat the water, and when it is boiling in the

3	

3	3	kettle, I will throw in Fundevogel, and will boil him in it.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Early next morning the forester got up and went out hunting, and when he

3	

3	3	was gone the children were still in bed. Then Lina said to Fundevogel:

3	

3	3	‘If you will never leave me, I too will never leave you.’ Fundevogel

3	

3	3	said: ‘Neither now, nor ever will I leave you.’ Then said Lina: ‘Then

3	

3	3	will I tell you. Last night, old Sanna carried so many buckets of water

3	

3	3	into the house that I asked her why she was doing that, and she said

3	

3	3	that if I would promise not to tell anyone, and she said that early

3	

3	3	tomorrow morning when father was out hunting, she would set the kettle

3	

3	3	full of water, throw you into it and boil you; but we will get up

3	

3	3	quickly, dress ourselves, and go away together.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The two children therefore got up, dressed themselves quickly, and went

3	

3	3	away. When the water in the kettle was boiling, the cook went into the

3	

3	3	bedroom to fetch Fundevogel and throw him into it. But when she came in,

3	

3	3	and went to the beds, both the children were gone. Then she was terribly

3	

3	3	alarmed, and she said to herself: ‘What shall I say now when the

3	

3	3	forester comes home and sees that the children are gone? They must be

3	

3	3	followed instantly to get them back again.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the cook sent three servants after them, who were to run and

3	

3	3	overtake the children. The children, however, were sitting outside the

3	

3	3	forest, and when they saw from afar the three servants running, Lina

3	

3	3	said to Fundevogel: ‘Never leave me, and I will never leave you.’

3	

3	3	Fundevogel said: ‘Neither now, nor ever.’ Then said Lina: ‘Do you become

3	

3	3	a rose-tree, and I the rose upon it.’ When the three servants came to

3	

3	3	the forest, nothing was there but a rose-tree and one rose on it, but

3	

3	3	the children were nowhere. Then said they: ‘There is nothing to be done

3	

3	3	here,’ and they went home and told the cook that they had seen nothing

3	

3	3	in the forest but a little rose-bush with one rose on it. Then the

3	

3	3	old cook scolded and said: ‘You simpletons, you should have cut the

3	

3	3	rose-bush in two, and have broken off the rose and brought it home with

3	

3	3	you; go, and do it at once.’ They had therefore to go out and look for

3	

3	3	the second time. The children, however, saw them coming from a distance.

3	

3	3	Then Lina said: ‘Fundevogel, never leave me, and I will never leave

3	

3	3	you.’ Fundevogel said: ‘Neither now; nor ever.’ Said Lina: ‘Then do you

3	

3	3	become a church, and I’ll be the chandelier in it.’ So when the three

3	

3	3	servants came, nothing was there but a church, with a chandelier in

3	

3	3	it. They said therefore to each other: ‘What can we do here, let us go

3	

3	3	home.’ When they got home, the cook asked if they had not found them;

3	

3	3	so they said no, they had found nothing but a church, and there was a

3	

3	3	chandelier in it. And the cook scolded them and said: ‘You fools! why

3	

3	3	did you not pull the church to pieces, and bring the chandelier home

3	

3	3	with you?’ And now the old cook herself got on her legs, and went with

3	

3	3	the three servants in pursuit of the children. The children, however,

3	

3	3	saw from afar that the three servants were coming, and the cook waddling

3	

3	3	after them. Then said Lina: ‘Fundevogel, never leave me, and I will

3	

3	3	never leave you.’ Then said Fundevogel: ‘Neither now, nor ever.’

3	

3	3	Said Lina: ‘Be a fishpond, and I will be the duck upon it.’ The cook,

3	

3	3	however, came up to them, and when she saw the pond she lay down by it,

3	

3	3	and was about to drink it up. But the duck swam quickly to her, seized

3	

3	3	her head in its beak and drew her into the water, and there the old

3	

3	3	witch had to drown. Then the children went home together, and were

3	

3	3	heartily delighted, and if they have not died, they are living still.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE VALIANT LITTLE TAILOR

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	One summer’s morning a little tailor was sitting on his table by the

3	

3	3	window; he was in good spirits, and sewed with all his might. Then came

3	

3	3	a peasant woman down the street crying: ‘Good jams, cheap! Good jams,

3	

3	3	cheap!’ This rang pleasantly in the tailor’s ears; he stretched his

3	

3	3	delicate head out of the window, and called: ‘Come up here, dear woman;

3	

3	3	here you will get rid of your goods.’ The woman came up the three steps

3	

3	3	to the tailor with her heavy basket, and he made her unpack all the pots

3	

3	3	for him. He inspected each one, lifted it up, put his nose to it, and

3	

3	3	at length said: ‘The jam seems to me to be good, so weigh me out four

3	

3	3	ounces, dear woman, and if it is a quarter of a pound that is of no

3	

3	3	consequence.’ The woman who had hoped to find a good sale, gave him

3	

3	3	what he desired, but went away quite angry and grumbling. ‘Now, this jam

3	

3	3	shall be blessed by God,’ cried the little tailor, ‘and give me health

3	

3	3	and strength’; so he brought the bread out of the cupboard, cut himself

3	

3	3	a piece right across the loaf and spread the jam over it. ‘This won’t

3	

3	3	taste bitter,’ said he, ‘but I will just finish the jacket before I

3	

3	3	take a bite.’ He laid the bread near him, sewed on, and in his joy, made

3	

3	3	bigger and bigger stitches. In the meantime the smell of the sweet jam

3	

3	3	rose to where the flies were sitting in great numbers, and they were

3	

3	3	attracted and descended on it in hosts. ‘Hi! who invited you?’ said the

3	

3	3	little tailor, and drove the unbidden guests away. The flies, however,

3	

3	3	who understood no German, would not be turned away, but came back

3	

3	3	again in ever-increasing companies. The little tailor at last lost all

3	

3	3	patience, and drew a piece of cloth from the hole under his work-table,

3	

3	3	and saying: ‘Wait, and I will give it to you,’ struck it mercilessly on

3	

3	3	them. When he drew it away and counted, there lay before him no fewer

3	

3	3	than seven, dead and with legs stretched out. ‘Are you a fellow of that

3	

3	3	sort?’ said he, and could not help admiring his own bravery. ‘The whole

3	

3	3	town shall know of this!’ And the little tailor hastened to cut himself

3	

3	3	a girdle, stitched it, and embroidered on it in large letters: ‘Seven at

3	

3	3	one stroke!’ ‘What, the town!’ he continued, ‘the whole world shall hear

3	

3	3	of it!’ and his heart wagged with joy like a lamb’s tail. The tailor

3	

3	3	put on the girdle, and resolved to go forth into the world, because he

3	

3	3	thought his workshop was too small for his valour. Before he went away,

3	

3	3	he sought about in the house to see if there was anything which he could

3	

3	3	take with him; however, he found nothing but an old cheese, and that

3	

3	3	he put in his pocket. In front of the door he observed a bird which

3	

3	3	had caught itself in the thicket. It had to go into his pocket with the

3	

3	3	cheese. Now he took to the road boldly, and as he was light and nimble,

3	

3	3	he felt no fatigue. The road led him up a mountain, and when he had

3	

3	3	reached the highest point of it, there sat a powerful giant looking

3	

3	3	peacefully about him. The little tailor went bravely up, spoke to him,

3	

3	3	and said: ‘Good day, comrade, so you are sitting there overlooking the

3	

3	3	wide-spread world! I am just on my way thither, and want to try my luck.

3	

3	3	Have you any inclination to go with me?’ The giant looked contemptuously

3	

3	3	at the tailor, and said: ‘You ragamuffin! You miserable creature!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Oh, indeed?’ answered the little tailor, and unbuttoned his coat, and

3	

3	3	showed the giant the girdle, ‘there may you read what kind of a man I

3	

3	3	am!’ The giant read: ‘Seven at one stroke,’ and thought that they had

3	

3	3	been men whom the tailor had killed, and began to feel a little respect

3	

3	3	for the tiny fellow. Nevertheless, he wished to try him first, and took

3	

3	3	a stone in his hand and squeezed it together so that water dropped out

3	

3	3	of it. ‘Do that likewise,’ said the giant, ‘if you have strength.’ ‘Is

3	

3	3	that all?’ said the tailor, ‘that is child’s play with us!’ and put his

3	

3	3	hand into his pocket, brought out the soft cheese, and pressed it until

3	

3	3	the liquid ran out of it. ‘Faith,’ said he, ‘that was a little better,

3	

3	3	wasn’t it?’ The giant did not know what to say, and could not believe it

3	

3	3	of the little man. Then the giant picked up a stone and threw it so high

3	

3	3	that the eye could scarcely follow it. ‘Now, little mite of a man, do

3	

3	3	that likewise,’ ‘Well thrown,’ said the tailor, ‘but after all the stone

3	

3	3	came down to earth again; I will throw you one which shall never come

3	

3	3	back at all,’ and he put his hand into his pocket, took out the bird,

3	

3	3	and threw it into the air. The bird, delighted with its liberty,

3	

3	3	rose, flew away and did not come back. ‘How does that shot please you,

3	

3	3	comrade?’ asked the tailor. ‘You can certainly throw,’ said the giant,

3	

3	3	‘but now we will see if you are able to carry anything properly.’ He

3	

3	3	took the little tailor to a mighty oak tree which lay there felled on

3	

3	3	the ground, and said: ‘If you are strong enough, help me to carry the

3	

3	3	tree out of the forest.’ ‘Readily,’ answered the little man; ‘take you

3	

3	3	the trunk on your shoulders, and I will raise up the branches and twigs;

3	

3	3	after all, they are the heaviest.’ The giant took the trunk on his

3	

3	3	shoulder, but the tailor seated himself on a branch, and the giant, who

3	

3	3	could not look round, had to carry away the whole tree, and the little

3	

3	3	tailor into the bargain: he behind, was quite merry and happy, and

3	

3	3	whistled the song: ‘Three tailors rode forth from the gate,’ as if

3	

3	3	carrying the tree were child’s play. The giant, after he had dragged the

3	

3	3	heavy burden part of the way, could go no further, and cried: ‘Hark

3	

3	3	you, I shall have to let the tree fall!’ The tailor sprang nimbly down,

3	

3	3	seized the tree with both arms as if he had been carrying it, and said

3	

3	3	to the giant: ‘You are such a great fellow, and yet cannot even carry

3	

3	3	the tree!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	They went on together, and as they passed a cherry-tree, the giant laid

3	

3	3	hold of the top of the tree where the ripest fruit was hanging, bent it

3	

3	3	down, gave it into the tailor’s hand, and bade him eat. But the little

3	

3	3	tailor was much too weak to hold the tree, and when the giant let it go,

3	

3	3	it sprang back again, and the tailor was tossed into the air with it.

3	

3	3	When he had fallen down again without injury, the giant said: ‘What is

3	

3	3	this? Have you not strength enough to hold the weak twig?’ ‘There is no

3	

3	3	lack of strength,’ answered the little tailor. ‘Do you think that could

3	

3	3	be anything to a man who has struck down seven at one blow? I leapt over

3	

3	3	the tree because the huntsmen are shooting down there in the thicket.

3	

3	3	Jump as I did, if you can do it.’ The giant made the attempt but he

3	

3	3	could not get over the tree, and remained hanging in the branches, so

3	

3	3	that in this also the tailor kept the upper hand.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The giant said: ‘If you are such a valiant fellow, come with me into our

3	

3	3	cavern and spend the night with us.’ The little tailor was willing, and

3	

3	3	followed him. When they went into the cave, other giants were sitting

3	

3	3	there by the fire, and each of them had a roasted sheep in his hand and

3	

3	3	was eating it. The little tailor looked round and thought: ‘It is much

3	

3	3	more spacious here than in my workshop.’ The giant showed him a bed, and

3	

3	3	said he was to lie down in it and sleep. The bed, however, was too

3	

3	3	big for the little tailor; he did not lie down in it, but crept into

3	

3	3	a corner. When it was midnight, and the giant thought that the little

3	

3	3	tailor was lying in a sound sleep, he got up, took a great iron bar,

3	

3	3	cut through the bed with one blow, and thought he had finished off the

3	

3	3	grasshopper for good. With the earliest dawn the giants went into the

3	

3	3	forest, and had quite forgotten the little tailor, when all at once he

3	

3	3	walked up to them quite merrily and boldly. The giants were terrified,

3	

3	3	they were afraid that he would strike them all dead, and ran away in a

3	

3	3	great hurry.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The little tailor went onwards, always following his own pointed nose.

3	

3	3	After he had walked for a long time, he came to the courtyard of a royal

3	

3	3	palace, and as he felt weary, he lay down on the grass and fell asleep.

3	

3	3	Whilst he lay there, the people came and inspected him on all sides, and

3	

3	3	read on his girdle: ‘Seven at one stroke.’ ‘Ah!’ said they, ‘what does

3	

3	3	the great warrior want here in the midst of peace? He must be a mighty

3	

3	3	lord.’ They went and announced him to the king, and gave it as their

3	

3	3	opinion that if war should break out, this would be a weighty and useful

3	

3	3	man who ought on no account to be allowed to depart. The counsel pleased

3	

3	3	the king, and he sent one of his courtiers to the little tailor to offer

3	

3	3	him military service when he awoke. The ambassador remained standing by

3	

3	3	the sleeper, waited until he stretched his limbs and opened his eyes,

3	

3	3	and then conveyed to him this proposal. ‘For this very reason have

3	

3	3	I come here,’ the tailor replied, ‘I am ready to enter the king’s

3	

3	3	service.’ He was therefore honourably received, and a special dwelling

3	

3	3	was assigned him.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The soldiers, however, were set against the little tailor, and wished

3
