3	3	him a thousand miles away. ‘What is to be the end of this?’ they said

3	

3	3	among themselves. ‘If we quarrel with him, and he strikes about him,

3	

3	3	seven of us will fall at every blow; not one of us can stand against

3	

3	3	him.’ They came therefore to a decision, betook themselves in a body to

3	

3	3	the king, and begged for their dismissal. ‘We are not prepared,’ said

3	

3	3	they, ‘to stay with a man who kills seven at one stroke.’ The king was

3	

3	3	sorry that for the sake of one he should lose all his faithful servants,

3	

3	3	wished that he had never set eyes on the tailor, and would willingly

3	

3	3	have been rid of him again. But he did not venture to give him his

3	

3	3	dismissal, for he dreaded lest he should strike him and all his people

3	

3	3	dead, and place himself on the royal throne. He thought about it for a

3	

3	3	long time, and at last found good counsel. He sent to the little tailor

3	

3	3	and caused him to be informed that as he was a great warrior, he had one

3	

3	3	request to make to him. In a forest of his country lived two giants,

3	

3	3	who caused great mischief with their robbing, murdering, ravaging,

3	

3	3	and burning, and no one could approach them without putting himself in

3	

3	3	danger of death. If the tailor conquered and killed these two giants, he

3	

3	3	would give him his only daughter to wife, and half of his kingdom as a

3	

3	3	dowry, likewise one hundred horsemen should go with him to assist him.

3	

3	3	‘That would indeed be a fine thing for a man like me!’ thought the

3	

3	3	little tailor. ‘One is not offered a beautiful princess and half a

3	

3	3	kingdom every day of one’s life!’ ‘Oh, yes,’ he replied, ‘I will soon

3	

3	3	subdue the giants, and do not require the help of the hundred horsemen

3	

3	3	to do it; he who can hit seven with one blow has no need to be afraid of

3	

3	3	two.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The little tailor went forth, and the hundred horsemen followed him.

3	

3	3	When he came to the outskirts of the forest, he said to his followers:

3	

3	3	‘Just stay waiting here, I alone will soon finish off the giants.’ Then

3	

3	3	he bounded into the forest and looked about right and left. After a

3	

3	3	while he perceived both giants. They lay sleeping under a tree, and

3	

3	3	snored so that the branches waved up and down. The little tailor, not

3	

3	3	idle, gathered two pocketsful of stones, and with these climbed up the

3	

3	3	tree. When he was halfway up, he slipped down by a branch, until he sat

3	

3	3	just above the sleepers, and then let one stone after another fall on

3	

3	3	the breast of one of the giants. For a long time the giant felt nothing,

3	

3	3	but at last he awoke, pushed his comrade, and said: ‘Why are you

3	

3	3	knocking me?’ ‘You must be dreaming,’ said the other, ‘I am not knocking

3	

3	3	you.’ They laid themselves down to sleep again, and then the tailor

3	

3	3	threw a stone down on the second. ‘What is the meaning of this?’ cried

3	

3	3	the other ‘Why are you pelting me?’ ‘I am not pelting you,’ answered

3	

3	3	the first, growling. They disputed about it for a time, but as they were

3	

3	3	weary they let the matter rest, and their eyes closed once more. The

3	

3	3	little tailor began his game again, picked out the biggest stone, and

3	

3	3	threw it with all his might on the breast of the first giant. ‘That

3	

3	3	is too bad!’ cried he, and sprang up like a madman, and pushed his

3	

3	3	companion against the tree until it shook. The other paid him back in

3	

3	3	the same coin, and they got into such a rage that they tore up trees and

3	

3	3	belaboured each other so long, that at last they both fell down dead on

3	

3	3	the ground at the same time. Then the little tailor leapt down. ‘It is

3	

3	3	a lucky thing,’ said he, ‘that they did not tear up the tree on which

3	

3	3	I was sitting, or I should have had to sprint on to another like a

3	

3	3	squirrel; but we tailors are nimble.’ He drew out his sword and gave

3	

3	3	each of them a couple of thrusts in the breast, and then went out to the

3	

3	3	horsemen and said: ‘The work is done; I have finished both of them

3	

3	3	off, but it was hard work! They tore up trees in their sore need, and

3	

3	3	defended themselves with them, but all that is to no purpose when a man

3	

3	3	like myself comes, who can kill seven at one blow.’ ‘But are you not

3	

3	3	wounded?’ asked the horsemen. ‘You need not concern yourself about

3	

3	3	that,’ answered the tailor, ‘they have not bent one hair of mine.’ The

3	

3	3	horsemen would not believe him, and rode into the forest; there they

3	

3	3	found the giants swimming in their blood, and all round about lay the

3	

3	3	torn-up trees.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The little tailor demanded of the king the promised reward; he, however,

3	

3	3	repented of his promise, and again bethought himself how he could get

3	

3	3	rid of the hero. ‘Before you receive my daughter, and the half of my

3	

3	3	kingdom,’ said he to him, ‘you must perform one more heroic deed. In

3	

3	3	the forest roams a unicorn which does great harm, and you must catch

3	

3	3	it first.’ ‘I fear one unicorn still less than two giants. Seven at one

3	

3	3	blow, is my kind of affair.’ He took a rope and an axe with him, went

3	

3	3	forth into the forest, and again bade those who were sent with him to

3	

3	3	wait outside. He had not long to seek. The unicorn soon came towards

3	

3	3	him, and rushed directly on the tailor, as if it would gore him with its

3	

3	3	horn without more ado. ‘Softly, softly; it can’t be done as quickly as

3	

3	3	that,’ said he, and stood still and waited until the animal was quite

3	

3	3	close, and then sprang nimbly behind the tree. The unicorn ran against

3	

3	3	the tree with all its strength, and stuck its horn so fast in the trunk

3	

3	3	that it had not the strength enough to draw it out again, and thus it

3	

3	3	was caught. ‘Now, I have got the bird,’ said the tailor, and came out

3	

3	3	from behind the tree and put the rope round its neck, and then with his

3	

3	3	axe he hewed the horn out of the tree, and when all was ready he led the

3	

3	3	beast away and took it to the king.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The king still would not give him the promised reward, and made a third

3	

3	3	demand. Before the wedding the tailor was to catch him a wild boar that

3	

3	3	made great havoc in the forest, and the huntsmen should give him their

3	

3	3	help. ‘Willingly,’ said the tailor, ‘that is child’s play!’ He did not

3	

3	3	take the huntsmen with him into the forest, and they were well pleased

3	

3	3	that he did not, for the wild boar had several times received them in

3	

3	3	such a manner that they had no inclination to lie in wait for him. When

3	

3	3	the boar perceived the tailor, it ran on him with foaming mouth and

3	

3	3	whetted tusks, and was about to throw him to the ground, but the hero

3	

3	3	fled and sprang into a chapel which was near and up to the window at

3	

3	3	once, and in one bound out again. The boar ran after him, but the tailor

3	

3	3	ran round outside and shut the door behind it, and then the raging

3	

3	3	beast, which was much too heavy and awkward to leap out of the window,

3	

3	3	was caught. The little tailor called the huntsmen thither that they

3	

3	3	might see the prisoner with their own eyes. The hero, however, went to

3	

3	3	the king, who was now, whether he liked it or not, obliged to keep his

3	

3	3	promise, and gave his daughter and the half of his kingdom. Had he known

3	

3	3	that it was no warlike hero, but a little tailor who was standing before

3	

3	3	him, it would have gone to his heart still more than it did. The wedding

3	

3	3	was held with great magnificence and small joy, and out of a tailor a

3	

3	3	king was made.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	After some time the young queen heard her husband say in his dreams at

3	

3	3	night: ‘Boy, make me the doublet, and patch the pantaloons, or else I

3	

3	3	will rap the yard-measure over your ears.’ Then she discovered in what

3	

3	3	state of life the young lord had been born, and next morning complained

3	

3	3	of her wrongs to her father, and begged him to help her to get rid of

3	

3	3	her husband, who was nothing else but a tailor. The king comforted her

3	

3	3	and said: ‘Leave your bedroom door open this night, and my servants

3	

3	3	shall stand outside, and when he has fallen asleep shall go in, bind

3	

3	3	him, and take him on board a ship which shall carry him into the wide

3	

3	3	world.’ The woman was satisfied with this; but the king’s armour-bearer,

3	

3	3	who had heard all, was friendly with the young lord, and informed him of

3	

3	3	the whole plot. ‘I’ll put a screw into that business,’ said the little

3	

3	3	tailor. At night he went to bed with his wife at the usual time, and

3	

3	3	when she thought that he had fallen asleep, she got up, opened the door,

3	

3	3	and then lay down again. The little tailor, who was only pretending to

3	

3	3	be asleep, began to cry out in a clear voice: ‘Boy, make me the doublet

3	

3	3	and patch me the pantaloons, or I will rap the yard-measure over your

3	

3	3	ears. I smote seven at one blow. I killed two giants, I brought away one

3	

3	3	unicorn, and caught a wild boar, and am I to fear those who are standing

3	

3	3	outside the room.’ When these men heard the tailor speaking thus, they

3	

3	3	were overcome by a great dread, and ran as if the wild huntsman were

3	

3	3	behind them, and none of them would venture anything further against

3	

3	3	him. So the little tailor was and remained a king to the end of his

3	

3	3	life.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	HANSEL AND GRETEL

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Hard by a great forest dwelt a poor wood-cutter with his wife and his

3	

3	3	two children. The boy was called Hansel and the girl Gretel. He had

3	

3	3	little to bite and to break, and once when great dearth fell on the

3	

3	3	land, he could no longer procure even daily bread. Now when he thought

3	

3	3	over this by night in his bed, and tossed about in his anxiety, he

3	

3	3	groaned and said to his wife: ‘What is to become of us? How are we

3	

3	3	to feed our poor children, when we no longer have anything even for

3	

3	3	ourselves?’ ‘I’ll tell you what, husband,’ answered the woman, ‘early

3	

3	3	tomorrow morning we will take the children out into the forest to where

3	

3	3	it is the thickest; there we will light a fire for them, and give each

3	

3	3	of them one more piece of bread, and then we will go to our work and

3	

3	3	leave them alone. They will not find the way home again, and we shall be

3	

3	3	rid of them.’ ‘No, wife,’ said the man, ‘I will not do that; how can I

3	

3	3	bear to leave my children alone in the forest?--the wild animals would

3	

3	3	soon come and tear them to pieces.’ ‘O, you fool!’ said she, ‘then we

3	

3	3	must all four die of hunger, you may as well plane the planks for our

3	

3	3	coffins,’ and she left him no peace until he consented. ‘But I feel very

3	

3	3	sorry for the poor children, all the same,’ said the man.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The two children had also not been able to sleep for hunger, and had

3	

3	3	heard what their stepmother had said to their father. Gretel wept

3	

3	3	bitter tears, and said to Hansel: ‘Now all is over with us.’ ‘Be quiet,

3	

3	3	Gretel,’ said Hansel, ‘do not distress yourself, I will soon find a way

3	

3	3	to help us.’ And when the old folks had fallen asleep, he got up, put

3	

3	3	on his little coat, opened the door below, and crept outside. The moon

3	

3	3	shone brightly, and the white pebbles which lay in front of the house

3	

3	3	glittered like real silver pennies. Hansel stooped and stuffed the

3	

3	3	little pocket of his coat with as many as he could get in. Then he went

3	

3	3	back and said to Gretel: ‘Be comforted, dear little sister, and sleep in

3	

3	3	peace, God will not forsake us,’ and he lay down again in his bed. When

3	

3	3	day dawned, but before the sun had risen, the woman came and awoke the

3	

3	3	two children, saying: ‘Get up, you sluggards! we are going into the

3	

3	3	forest to fetch wood.’ She gave each a little piece of bread, and said:

3	

3	3	‘There is something for your dinner, but do not eat it up before then,

3	

3	3	for you will get nothing else.’ Gretel took the bread under her apron,

3	

3	3	as Hansel had the pebbles in his pocket. Then they all set out together

3	

3	3	on the way to the forest. When they had walked a short time, Hansel

3	

3	3	stood still and peeped back at the house, and did so again and again.

3	

3	3	His father said: ‘Hansel, what are you looking at there and staying

3	

3	3	behind for? Pay attention, and do not forget how to use your legs.’ ‘Ah,

3	

3	3	father,’ said Hansel, ‘I am looking at my little white cat, which is

3	

3	3	sitting up on the roof, and wants to say goodbye to me.’ The wife said:

3	

3	3	‘Fool, that is not your little cat, that is the morning sun which is

3	

3	3	shining on the chimneys.’ Hansel, however, had not been looking back at

3	

3	3	the cat, but had been constantly throwing one of the white pebble-stones

3	

3	3	out of his pocket on the road.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When they had reached the middle of the forest, the father said: ‘Now,

3	

3	3	children, pile up some wood, and I will light a fire that you may not

3	

3	3	be cold.’ Hansel and Gretel gathered brushwood together, as high as a

3	

3	3	little hill. The brushwood was lighted, and when the flames were burning

3	

3	3	very high, the woman said: ‘Now, children, lay yourselves down by the

3	

3	3	fire and rest, we will go into the forest and cut some wood. When we

3	

3	3	have done, we will come back and fetch you away.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Hansel and Gretel sat by the fire, and when noon came, each ate a little

3	

3	3	piece of bread, and as they heard the strokes of the wood-axe they

3	

3	3	believed that their father was near. It was not the axe, however, but

3	

3	3	a branch which he had fastened to a withered tree which the wind was

3	

3	3	blowing backwards and forwards. And as they had been sitting such a long

3	

3	3	time, their eyes closed with fatigue, and they fell fast asleep. When

3	

3	3	at last they awoke, it was already dark night. Gretel began to cry and

3	

3	3	said: ‘How are we to get out of the forest now?’ But Hansel comforted

3	

3	3	her and said: ‘Just wait a little, until the moon has risen, and then we

3	

3	3	will soon find the way.’ And when the full moon had risen, Hansel took

3	

3	3	his little sister by the hand, and followed the pebbles which shone like

3	

3	3	newly-coined silver pieces, and showed them the way.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	They walked the whole night long, and by break of day came once more

3	

3	3	to their father’s house. They knocked at the door, and when the woman

3	

3	3	opened it and saw that it was Hansel and Gretel, she said: ‘You naughty

3	

3	3	children, why have you slept so long in the forest?--we thought you were

3	

3	3	never coming back at all!’ The father, however, rejoiced, for it had cut

3	

3	3	him to the heart to leave them behind alone.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Not long afterwards, there was once more great dearth throughout the

3	

3	3	land, and the children heard their mother saying at night to their

3	

3	3	father: ‘Everything is eaten again, we have one half loaf left, and that

3	

3	3	is the end. The children must go, we will take them farther into the

3	

3	3	wood, so that they will not find their way out again; there is no other

3	

3	3	means of saving ourselves!’ The man’s heart was heavy, and he thought:

3	

3	3	‘It would be better for you to share the last mouthful with your

3	

3	3	children.’ The woman, however, would listen to nothing that he had to

3	

3	3	say, but scolded and reproached him. He who says A must say B, likewise,

3	

3	3	and as he had yielded the first time, he had to do so a second time

3	

3	3	also.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The children, however, were still awake and had heard the conversation.

3	

3	3	When the old folks were asleep, Hansel again got up, and wanted to go

3	

3	3	out and pick up pebbles as he had done before, but the woman had locked

3	

3	3	the door, and Hansel could not get out. Nevertheless he comforted his

3	

3	3	little sister, and said: ‘Do not cry, Gretel, go to sleep quietly, the

3	

3	3	good God will help us.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Early in the morning came the woman, and took the children out of their

3	

3	3	beds. Their piece of bread was given to them, but it was still smaller

3	

3	3	than the time before. On the way into the forest Hansel crumbled his

3	

3	3	in his pocket, and often stood still and threw a morsel on the ground.

3	

3	3	‘Hansel, why do you stop and look round?’ said the father, ‘go on.’ ‘I

3	

3	3	am looking back at my little pigeon which is sitting on the roof, and

3	

3	3	wants to say goodbye to me,’ answered Hansel. ‘Fool!’ said the woman,

3	

3	3	‘that is not your little pigeon, that is the morning sun that is shining

3	

3	3	on the chimney.’ Hansel, however little by little, threw all the crumbs

3	

3	3	on the path.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The woman led the children still deeper into the forest, where they had

3	

3	3	never in their lives been before. Then a great fire was again made, and

3	

3	3	the mother said: ‘Just sit there, you children, and when you are tired

3	

3	3	you may sleep a little; we are going into the forest to cut wood, and in

3	

3	3	the evening when we are done, we will come and fetch you away.’ When

3	

3	3	it was noon, Gretel shared her piece of bread with Hansel, who had

3	

3	3	scattered his by the way. Then they fell asleep and evening passed, but

3	

3	3	no one came to the poor children. They did not awake until it was dark

3	

3	3	night, and Hansel comforted his little sister and said: ‘Just wait,

3	

3	3	Gretel, until the moon rises, and then we shall see the crumbs of bread

3	

3	3	which I have strewn about, they will show us our way home again.’ When

3	

3	3	the moon came they set out, but they found no crumbs, for the many

3	

3	3	thousands of birds which fly about in the woods and fields had picked

3	

3	3	them all up. Hansel said to Gretel: ‘We shall soon find the way,’ but

3	

3	3	they did not find it. They walked the whole night and all the next day

3	

3	3	too from morning till evening, but they did not get out of the forest,

3	

3	3	and were very hungry, for they had nothing to eat but two or three

3	

3	3	berries, which grew on the ground. And as they were so weary that their

3	

3	3	legs would carry them no longer, they lay down beneath a tree and fell

3	

3	3	asleep.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	It was now three mornings since they had left their father’s house. They

3	

3	3	began to walk again, but they always came deeper into the forest, and if

3	

3	3	help did not come soon, they must die of hunger and weariness. When it

3	

3	3	was mid-day, they saw a beautiful snow-white bird sitting on a bough,

3	

3	3	which sang so delightfully that they stood still and listened to it. And

3	

3	3	when its song was over, it spread its wings and flew away before them,

3	

3	3	and they followed it until they reached a little house, on the roof of

3	

3	3	which it alighted; and when they approached the little house they saw

3	

3	3	that it was built of bread and covered with cakes, but that the windows

3	

3	3	were of clear sugar. ‘We will set to work on that,’ said Hansel, ‘and

3	

3	3	have a good meal. I will eat a bit of the roof, and you Gretel, can eat

3	

3	3	some of the window, it will taste sweet.’ Hansel reached up above, and

3	

3	3	broke off a little of the roof to try how it tasted, and Gretel leant

3	

3	3	against the window and nibbled at the panes. Then a soft voice cried

3	

3	3	from the parlour:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Nibble, nibble, gnaw,

3	

3	3	  Who is nibbling at my little house?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The children answered:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘The wind, the wind,

3	

3	3	  The heaven-born wind,’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	and went on eating without disturbing themselves. Hansel, who liked the

3	

3	3	taste of the roof, tore down a great piece of it, and Gretel pushed out

3	

3	3	the whole of one round window-pane, sat down, and enjoyed herself with

3	

3	3	it. Suddenly the door opened, and a woman as old as the hills, who

3	

3	3	supported herself on crutches, came creeping out. Hansel and Gretel were

3	

3	3	so terribly frightened that they let fall what they had in their

3	

3	3	hands. The old woman, however, nodded her head, and said: ‘Oh, you dear

3	

3	3	children, who has brought you here? do come in, and stay with me. No

3	

3	3	harm shall happen to you.’ She took them both by the hand, and led them

3	

3	3	into her little house. Then good food was set before them, milk and

3	

3	3	pancakes, with sugar, apples, and nuts. Afterwards two pretty little

3	

3	3	beds were covered with clean white linen, and Hansel and Gretel lay down

3	

3	3	in them, and thought they were in heaven.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The old woman had only pretended to be so kind; she was in reality

3	

3	3	a wicked witch, who lay in wait for children, and had only built the

3	

3	3	little house of bread in order to entice them there. When a child fell

3	

3	3	into her power, she killed it, cooked and ate it, and that was a feast

3	

3	3	day with her. Witches have red eyes, and cannot see far, but they have

3	

3	3	a keen scent like the beasts, and are aware when human beings draw near.

3	

3	3	When Hansel and Gretel came into her neighbourhood, she laughed with

3	

3	3	malice, and said mockingly: ‘I have them, they shall not escape me

3	

3	3	again!’ Early in the morning before the children were awake, she was

3	

3	3	already up, and when she saw both of them sleeping and looking so

3	

3	3	pretty, with their plump and rosy cheeks she muttered to herself: ‘That

3	

3	3	will be a dainty mouthful!’ Then she seized Hansel with her shrivelled

3	

3	3	hand, carried him into a little stable, and locked him in behind a

3	

3	3	grated door. Scream as he might, it would not help him. Then she went to

3	

3	3	Gretel, shook her till she awoke, and cried: ‘Get up, lazy thing, fetch

3	

3	3	some water, and cook something good for your brother, he is in the

3	

3	3	stable outside, and is to be made fat. When he is fat, I will eat him.’

3	

3	3	Gretel began to weep bitterly, but it was all in vain, for she was

3	

3	3	forced to do what the wicked witch commanded.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And now the best food was cooked for poor Hansel, but Gretel got nothing

3	

3	3	but crab-shells. Every morning the woman crept to the little stable, and

3	

3	3	cried: ‘Hansel, stretch out your finger that I may feel if you will soon

3	

3	3	be fat.’ Hansel, however, stretched out a little bone to her, and

3	

3	3	the old woman, who had dim eyes, could not see it, and thought it was

3	

3	3	Hansel’s finger, and was astonished that there was no way of fattening

3	

3	3	him. When four weeks had gone by, and Hansel still remained thin, she

3	

3	3	was seized with impatience and would not wait any longer. ‘Now, then,

3	

3	3	Gretel,’ she cried to the girl, ‘stir yourself, and bring some water.

3	

3	3	Let Hansel be fat or lean, tomorrow I will kill him, and cook him.’ Ah,

3	

3	3	how the poor little sister did lament when she had to fetch the water,

3	

3	3	and how her tears did flow down her cheeks! ‘Dear God, do help us,’ she

3	

3	3	cried. ‘If the wild beasts in the forest had but devoured us, we should

3	

3	3	at any rate have died together.’ ‘Just keep your noise to yourself,’

3	

3	3	said the old woman, ‘it won’t help you at all.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Early in the morning, Gretel had to go out and hang up the cauldron with

3	

3	3	the water, and light the fire. ‘We will bake first,’ said the old woman,

3	

3	3	‘I have already heated the oven, and kneaded the dough.’ She pushed poor

3	

3	3	Gretel out to the oven, from which flames of fire were already darting.

3	

3	3	‘Creep in,’ said the witch, ‘and see if it is properly heated, so that

3	

3	3	we can put the bread in.’ And once Gretel was inside, she intended to

3	

3	3	shut the oven and let her bake in it, and then she would eat her, too.

3	

3	3	But Gretel saw what she had in mind, and said: ‘I do not know how I am

3	

3	3	to do it; how do I get in?’ ‘Silly goose,’ said the old woman. ‘The door

3	

3	3	is big enough; just look, I can get in myself!’ and she crept up and

3	

3	3	thrust her head into the oven. Then Gretel gave her a push that drove

3	

3	3	her far into it, and shut the iron door, and fastened the bolt. Oh! then

3	

3	3	she began to howl quite horribly, but Gretel ran away and the godless

3	

3	3	witch was miserably burnt to death.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Gretel, however, ran like lightning to Hansel, opened his little stable,

3	

3	3	and cried: ‘Hansel, we are saved! The old witch is dead!’ Then Hansel

3	

3	3	sprang like a bird from its cage when the door is opened. How they did

3	

3	3	rejoice and embrace each other, and dance about and kiss each other! And

3	

3	3	as they had no longer any need to fear her, they went into the witch’s

3	

3	3	house, and in every corner there stood chests full of pearls and jewels.

3	

3	3	‘These are far better than pebbles!’ said Hansel, and thrust into his

3	

3	3	pockets whatever could be got in, and Gretel said: ‘I, too, will take

3	

3	3	something home with me,’ and filled her pinafore full. ‘But now we must

3	

3	3	be off,’ said Hansel, ‘that we may get out of the witch’s forest.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When they had walked for two hours, they came to a great stretch of

3	

3	3	water. ‘We cannot cross,’ said Hansel, ‘I see no foot-plank, and no

3	

3	3	bridge.’ ‘And there is also no ferry,’ answered Gretel, ‘but a white

3	

3	3	duck is swimming there: if I ask her, she will help us over.’ Then she

3	

3	3	cried:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Little duck, little duck, dost thou see,

3	

3	3	  Hansel and Gretel are waiting for thee?

3	

3	3	  There’s never a plank, or bridge in sight,

3	

3	3	  Take us across on thy back so white.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The duck came to them, and Hansel seated himself on its back, and told

3	

3	3	his sister to sit by him. ‘No,’ replied Gretel, ‘that will be too heavy

3	

3	3	for the little duck; she shall take us across, one after the other.’ The

3	

3	3	good little duck did so, and when they were once safely across and had

3	

3	3	walked for a short time, the forest seemed to be more and more familiar

3	

3	3	to them, and at length they saw from afar their father’s house. Then

3	

3	3	they began to run, rushed into the parlour, and threw themselves round

3	

3	3	their father’s neck. The man had not known one happy hour since he had

3	

3	3	left the children in the forest; the woman, however, was dead. Gretel

3	

3	3	emptied her pinafore until pearls and precious stones ran about the

3	

3	3	room, and Hansel threw one handful after another out of his pocket to

3	

3	3	add to them. Then all anxiety was at an end, and they lived together

3	

3	3	in perfect happiness. My tale is done, there runs a mouse; whosoever

3	

3	3	catches it, may make himself a big fur cap out of it.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE MOUSE, THE BIRD, AND THE SAUSAGE

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Once upon a time, a mouse, a bird, and a sausage, entered into

3	

3	3	partnership and set up house together. For a long time all went well;

3	

3	3	they lived in great comfort, and prospered so far as to be able to add

3	

3	3	considerably to their stores. The bird’s duty was to fly daily into the

3	

3	3	wood and bring in fuel; the mouse fetched the water, and the sausage saw

3	

3	3	to the cooking.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When people are too well off they always begin to long for something

3	

3	3	new. And so it came to pass, that the bird, while out one day, met a

3	

3	3	fellow bird, to whom he boastfully expatiated on the excellence of his

3	

3	3	household arrangements. But the other bird sneered at him for being a

3	

3	3	poor simpleton, who did all the hard work, while the other two stayed

3	

3	3	at home and had a good time of it. For, when the mouse had made the fire

3	

3	3	and fetched in the water, she could retire into her little room and rest

3	

3	3	until it was time to set the table. The sausage had only to watch the

3	

3	3	pot to see that the food was properly cooked, and when it was near

3	

3	3	dinner-time, he just threw himself into the broth, or rolled in and out

3	

3	3	among the vegetables three or four times, and there they were, buttered,

3	

3	3	and salted, and ready to be served. Then, when the bird came home and

3	

3	3	had laid aside his burden, they sat down to table, and when they had

3	

3	3	finished their meal, they could sleep their fill till the following

3	

3	3	morning: and that was really a very delightful life.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Influenced by those remarks, the bird next morning refused to bring in

3	

3	3	the wood, telling the others that he had been their servant long enough,

3	

3	3	and had been a fool into the bargain, and that it was now time to make a

3	

3	3	change, and to try some other way of arranging the work. Beg and pray

3	

3	3	as the mouse and the sausage might, it was of no use; the bird remained

3	

3	3	master of the situation, and the venture had to be made. They therefore

3	

3	3	drew lots, and it fell to the sausage to bring in the wood, to the mouse

3	

3	3	to cook, and to the bird to fetch the water.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And now what happened? The sausage started in search of wood, the bird

3	

3	3	made the fire, and the mouse put on the pot, and then these two waited

3	

3	3	till the sausage returned with the fuel for the following day. But the

3	

3	3	sausage remained so long away, that they became uneasy, and the bird

3	

3	3	flew out to meet him. He had not flown far, however, when he came across

3	

3	3	a dog who, having met the sausage, had regarded him as his legitimate

3	

3	3	booty, and so seized and swallowed him. The bird complained to the dog

3	

3	3	of this bare-faced robbery, but nothing he said was of any avail, for

3	

3	3	the dog answered that he found false credentials on the sausage, and

3	

3	3	that was the reason his life had been forfeited.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	He picked up the wood, and flew sadly home, and told the mouse all he

3	

3	3	had seen and heard. They were both very unhappy, but agreed to make the

3	

3	3	best of things and to remain with one another.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So now the bird set the table, and the mouse looked after the food and,

3	

3	3	wishing to prepare it in the same way as the sausage, by rolling in and

3	

3	3	out among the vegetables to salt and butter them, she jumped into the

3	

3	3	pot; but she stopped short long before she reached the bottom, having

3	

3	3	already parted not only with her skin and hair, but also with life.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Presently the bird came in and wanted to serve up the dinner, but he

3	

3	3	could nowhere see the cook. In his alarm and flurry, he threw the wood

3	

3	3	here and there about the floor, called and searched, but no cook was to

3	

3	3	be found. Then some of the wood that had been carelessly thrown down,

3	

3	3	caught fire and began to blaze. The bird hastened to fetch some water,

3	

3	3	but his pail fell into the well, and he after it, and as he was unable

3	

3	3	to recover himself, he was drowned.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	MOTHER HOLLE

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Once upon a time there was a widow who had two daughters; one of them

3	

3	3	was beautiful and industrious, the other ugly and lazy. The mother,

3	

3	3	however, loved the ugly and lazy one best, because she was her own

3	

3	3	daughter, and so the other, who was only her stepdaughter, was made

3	

3	3	to do all the work of the house, and was quite the Cinderella of the

3	

3	3	family. Her stepmother sent her out every day to sit by the well in

3	

3	3	the high road, there to spin until she made her fingers bleed. Now it

3	

3	3	chanced one day that some blood fell on to the spindle, and as the girl

3	

3	3	stopped over the well to wash it off, the spindle suddenly sprang out

3	

3	3	of her hand and fell into the well. She ran home crying to tell of her

3	

3	3	misfortune, but her stepmother spoke harshly to her, and after giving

3	

3	3	her a violent scolding, said unkindly, ‘As you have let the spindle fall

3	

3	3	into the well you may go yourself and fetch it out.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The girl went back to the well not knowing what to do, and at last in

3	

3	3	her distress she jumped into the water after the spindle.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	She remembered nothing more until she awoke and found herself in a

3	

3	3	beautiful meadow, full of sunshine, and with countless flowers blooming

3	

3	3	in every direction.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	She walked over the meadow, and presently she came upon a baker’s oven

3	

3	3	full of bread, and the loaves cried out to her, ‘Take us out, take us

3	

3	3	out, or alas! we shall be burnt to a cinder; we were baked through long

3	

3	3	ago.’ So she took the bread-shovel and drew them all out.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	She went on a little farther, till she came to a tree full of apples.

3	

3	3	‘Shake me, shake me, I pray,’ cried the tree; ‘my apples, one and all,

3	

3	3	are ripe.’ So she shook the tree, and the apples came falling down upon

3	

3	3	her like rain; but she continued shaking until there was not a single

3	

3	3	apple left upon it. Then she carefully gathered the apples together in a

3	

3	3	heap and walked on again.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The next thing she came to was a little house, and there she saw an old

3	

3	3	woman looking out, with such large teeth, that she was terrified, and

3	

3	3	turned to run away. But the old woman called after her, ‘What are you

3	

3	3	afraid of, dear child? Stay with me; if you will do the work of my house

3	

3	3	properly for me, I will make you very happy. You must be very careful,

3	

3	3	however, to make my bed in the right way, for I wish you always to shake

3	

3	3	it thoroughly, so that the feathers fly about; then they say, down there

3	

3	3	in the world, that it is snowing; for I am Mother Holle.’ The old woman

3	

3	3	spoke so kindly, that the girl summoned up courage and agreed to enter

3	

3	3	into her service.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	She took care to do everything according to the old woman’s bidding and

3	

3	3	every time she made the bed she shook it with all her might, so that the

3	

3	3	feathers flew about like so many snowflakes. The old woman was as good

3	

3	3	as her word: she never spoke angrily to her, and gave her roast and

3	

3	3	boiled meats every day.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So she stayed on with Mother Holle for some time, and then she began

3	

3	3	to grow unhappy. She could not at first tell why she felt sad, but she

3	

3	3	became conscious at last of great longing to go home; then she knew she

3	

3	3	was homesick, although she was a thousand times better off with Mother

3	

3	3	Holle than with her mother and sister. After waiting awhile, she went

3	

3	3	to Mother Holle and said, ‘I am so homesick, that I cannot stay with

3	

3	3	you any longer, for although I am so happy here, I must return to my own

3	

3	3	people.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then Mother Holle said, ‘I am pleased that you should want to go back

3	

3	3	to your own people, and as you have served me so well and faithfully, I

3	

3	3	will take you home myself.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Thereupon she led the girl by the hand up to a broad gateway. The gate

3	

3	3	was opened, and as the girl passed through, a shower of gold fell upon

3	

3	3	her, and the gold clung to her, so that she was covered with it from

3	

3	3	head to foot.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘That is a reward for your industry,’ said Mother Holle, and as she

3	

3	3	spoke she handed her the spindle which she had dropped into the well.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The gate was then closed, and the girl found herself back in the old

3	

3	3	world close to her mother’s house. As she entered the courtyard, the

3	

3	3	cock who was perched on the well, called out:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Cock-a-doodle-doo!

3	

3	3	  Your golden daughter’s come back to you.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then she went in to her mother and sister, and as she was so richly

3	

3	3	covered with gold, they gave her a warm welcome. She related to them

3	

3	3	all that had happened, and when the mother heard how she had come by her

3	

3	3	great riches, she thought she should like her ugly, lazy daughter to go

3	

3	3	and try her fortune. So she made the sister go and sit by the well

3	

3	3	and spin, and the girl pricked her finger and thrust her hand into a

3	

3	3	thorn-bush, so that she might drop some blood on to the spindle; then

3	

3	3	she threw it into the well, and jumped in herself.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Like her sister she awoke in the beautiful meadow, and walked over it

3	

3	3	till she came to the oven. ‘Take us out, take us out, or alas! we shall

3	

3	3	be burnt to a cinder; we were baked through long ago,’ cried the loaves

3	

3	3	as before. But the lazy girl answered, ‘Do you think I am going to dirty

3	

3	3	my hands for you?’ and walked on.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Presently she came to the apple-tree. ‘Shake me, shake me, I pray; my

3	

3	3	apples, one and all, are ripe,’ it cried. But she only answered, ‘A nice

3	

3	3	thing to ask me to do, one of the apples might fall on my head,’ and

3	

3	3	passed on.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	At last she came to Mother Holle’s house, and as she had heard all about

3	

3	3	the large teeth from her sister, she was not afraid of them, and engaged

3	

3	3	herself without delay to the old woman.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The first day she was very obedient and industrious, and exerted herself

3	

3	3	to please Mother Holle, for she thought of the gold she should get in

3	

3	3	return. The next day, however, she began to dawdle over her work, and

3	

3	3	the third day she was more idle still; then she began to lie in bed in

3	

3	3	the mornings and refused to get up. Worse still, she neglected to

3	

3	3	make the old woman’s bed properly, and forgot to shake it so that the

3	

3	3	feathers might fly about. So Mother Holle very soon got tired of her,

3	

3	3	and told her she might go. The lazy girl was delighted at this, and

3	

3	3	thought to herself, ‘The gold will soon be mine.’ Mother Holle led her,

3	

3	3	as she had led her sister, to the broad gateway; but as she was passing

3	

3	3	through, instead of the shower of gold, a great bucketful of pitch came

3	

3	3	pouring over her.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘That is in return for your services,’ said the old woman, and she shut

3	

3	3	the gate.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So the lazy girl had to go home covered with pitch, and the cock on the

3	

3	3	well called out as she saw her:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Cock-a-doodle-doo!

3	

3	3	  Your dirty daughter’s come back to you.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But, try what she would, she could not get the pitch off and it stuck to

3	

3	3	her as long as she lived.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	LITTLE RED-CAP [LITTLE RED RIDING HOOD]

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Once upon a time there was a dear little girl who was loved by everyone

3	

3	3	who looked at her, but most of all by her grandmother, and there was

3	

3	3	nothing that she would not have given to the child. Once she gave her a

3	

3	3	little cap of red velvet, which suited her so well that she would never

3	

3	3	wear anything else; so she was always called ‘Little Red-Cap.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	One day her mother said to her: ‘Come, Little Red-Cap, here is a piece

3	

3	3	of cake and a bottle of wine; take them to your grandmother, she is ill

3	

3	3	and weak, and they will do her good. Set out before it gets hot, and

3	

3	3	when you are going, walk nicely and quietly and do not run off the path,

3	

3	3	or you may fall and break the bottle, and then your grandmother will

3	

3	3	get nothing; and when you go into her room, don’t forget to say, “Good

3	

3	3	morning”, and don’t peep into every corner before you do it.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘I will take great care,’ said Little Red-Cap to her mother, and gave

3	

3	3	her hand on it.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The grandmother lived out in the wood, half a league from the village,

3	

3	3	and just as Little Red-Cap entered the wood, a wolf met her. Red-Cap

3	

3	3	did not know what a wicked creature he was, and was not at all afraid of

3	

3	3	him.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Good day, Little Red-Cap,’ said he.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Thank you kindly, wolf.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Whither away so early, Little Red-Cap?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘To my grandmother’s.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘What have you got in your apron?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Cake and wine; yesterday was baking-day, so poor sick grandmother is to

3	

3	3	have something good, to make her stronger.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Where does your grandmother live, Little Red-Cap?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘A good quarter of a league farther on in the wood; her house stands

3	

3	3	under the three large oak-trees, the nut-trees are just below; you

3	

3	3	surely must know it,’ replied Little Red-Cap.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The wolf thought to himself: ‘What a tender young creature! what a nice

3	

3	3	plump mouthful--she will be better to eat than the old woman. I must

3	

3	3	act craftily, so as to catch both.’ So he walked for a short time by

3	

3	3	the side of Little Red-Cap, and then he said: ‘See, Little Red-Cap, how

3	

3	3	pretty the flowers are about here--why do you not look round? I believe,

3	

3	3	too, that you do not hear how sweetly the little birds are singing; you

3	

3	3	walk gravely along as if you were going to school, while everything else

3	

3	3	out here in the wood is merry.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Little Red-Cap raised her eyes, and when she saw the sunbeams dancing

3	

3	3	here and there through the trees, and pretty flowers growing everywhere,

3	

3	3	she thought: ‘Suppose I take grandmother a fresh nosegay; that would

3	

3	3	please her too. It is so early in the day that I shall still get there

3	

3	3	in good time’; and so she ran from the path into the wood to look for

3	

3	3	flowers. And whenever she had picked one, she fancied that she saw a

3	

3	3	still prettier one farther on, and ran after it, and so got deeper and

3	

3	3	deeper into the wood.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Meanwhile the wolf ran straight to the grandmother’s house and knocked

3	

3	3	at the door.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Who is there?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Little Red-Cap,’ replied the wolf. ‘She is bringing cake and wine; open

3	

3	3	the door.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Lift the latch,’ called out the grandmother, ‘I am too weak, and cannot

3	

3	3	get up.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The wolf lifted the latch, the door sprang open, and without saying a

3	

3	3	word he went straight to the grandmother’s bed, and devoured her. Then

3	

3	3	he put on her clothes, dressed himself in her cap laid himself in bed

3	

3	3	and drew the curtains.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Little Red-Cap, however, had been running about picking flowers,

3	

3	3	and when she had gathered so many that she could carry no more, she

3	

3	3	remembered her grandmother, and set out on the way to her.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	She was surprised to find the cottage-door standing open, and when she

3	

3	3	went into the room, she had such a strange feeling that she said to

3	

3	3	herself: ‘Oh dear! how uneasy I feel today, and at other times I like

3	

3	3	being with grandmother so much.’ She called out: ‘Good morning,’ but

3	

3	3	received no answer; so she went to the bed and drew back the curtains.

3	

3	3	There lay her grandmother with her cap pulled far over her face, and

3	

3	3	looking very strange.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Oh! grandmother,’ she said, ‘what big ears you have!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘The better to hear you with, my child,’ was the reply.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘But, grandmother, what big eyes you have!’ she said.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘The better to see you with, my dear.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘But, grandmother, what large hands you have!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘The better to hug you with.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Oh! but, grandmother, what a terrible big mouth you have!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘The better to eat you with!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And scarcely had the wolf said this, than with one bound he was out of

3	

3	3	bed and swallowed up Red-Cap.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the wolf had appeased his appetite, he lay down again in the bed,

3	

3	3	fell asleep and began to snore very loud. The huntsman was just passing

3	

3	3	the house, and thought to himself: ‘How the old woman is snoring! I must

3	

3	3	just see if she wants anything.’ So he went into the room, and when he

3	

3	3	came to the bed, he saw that the wolf was lying in it. ‘Do I find you

3	

3	3	here, you old sinner!’ said he. ‘I have long sought you!’ Then just as

3	

3	3	he was going to fire at him, it occurred to him that the wolf might have

3	

3	3	devoured the grandmother, and that she might still be saved, so he did

3	

3	3	not fire, but took a pair of scissors, and began to cut open the stomach

3	

3	3	of the sleeping wolf. When he had made two snips, he saw the little

3	

3	3	Red-Cap shining, and then he made two snips more, and the little girl

3	

3	3	sprang out, crying: ‘Ah, how frightened I have been! How dark it was

3	

3	3	inside the wolf’; and after that the aged grandmother came out alive

3	

3	3	also, but scarcely able to breathe. Red-Cap, however, quickly fetched

3	

3	3	great stones with which they filled the wolf’s belly, and when he awoke,

3	

3	3	he wanted to run away, but the stones were so heavy that he collapsed at

3	

3	3	once, and fell dead.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then all three were delighted. The huntsman drew off the wolf’s skin and

3	

3	3	went home with it; the grandmother ate the cake and drank the wine which

3	

3	3	Red-Cap had brought, and revived, but Red-Cap thought to herself: ‘As

3	

3	3	long as I live, I will never by myself leave the path, to run into the

3	

3	3	wood, when my mother has forbidden me to do so.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	It also related that once when Red-Cap was again taking cakes to the old

3	

3	3	grandmother, another wolf spoke to her, and tried to entice her from the

3	

3	3	path. Red-Cap, however, was on her guard, and went straight forward on

3	

3	3	her way, and told her grandmother that she had met the wolf, and that he

3	

3	3	had said ‘good morning’ to her, but with such a wicked look in his eyes,

3	

3	3	that if they had not been on the public road she was certain he would

3	

3	3	have eaten her up. ‘Well,’ said the grandmother, ‘we will shut the door,

3	

3	3	that he may not come in.’ Soon afterwards the wolf knocked, and cried:

3	

3	3	‘Open the door, grandmother, I am Little Red-Cap, and am bringing you

3	

3	3	some cakes.’ But they did not speak, or open the door, so the grey-beard

3	

3	3	stole twice or thrice round the house, and at last jumped on the roof,

3	

3	3	intending to wait until Red-Cap went home in the evening, and then to

3	

3	3	steal after her and devour her in the darkness. But the grandmother

3	

3	3	saw what was in his thoughts. In front of the house was a great stone

3	

3	3	trough, so she said to the child: ‘Take the pail, Red-Cap; I made some

3	

3	3	sausages yesterday, so carry the water in which I boiled them to the

3	

3	3	trough.’ Red-Cap carried until the great trough was quite full. Then the

3	

3	3	smell of the sausages reached the wolf, and he sniffed and peeped down,

3	

3	3	and at last stretched out his neck so far that he could no longer keep

3	

3	3	his footing and began to slip, and slipped down from the roof straight

3	

3	3	into the great trough, and was drowned. But Red-Cap went joyously home,

3	

3	3	and no one ever did anything to harm her again.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE ROBBER BRIDEGROOM

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once a miller who had one beautiful daughter, and as she was

3	

3	3	grown up, he was anxious that she should be well married and provided

3	

3	3	for. He said to himself, ‘I will give her to the first suitable man who

3	

3	3	comes and asks for her hand.’ Not long after a suitor appeared, and as

3	

3	3	he appeared to be very rich and the miller could see nothing in him with

3	

3	3	which to find fault, he betrothed his daughter to him. But the girl did

3	

3	3	not care for the man as a girl ought to care for her betrothed husband.

3	

3	3	She did not feel that she could trust him, and she could not look at him

3	

3	3	nor think of him without an inward shudder. One day he said to her, ‘You

3	

3	3	have not yet paid me a visit, although we have been betrothed for some

3	

3	3	time.’ ‘I do not know where your house is,’ she answered. ‘My house is

3	

3	3	out there in the dark forest,’ he said. She tried to excuse herself by

3	

3	3	saying that she would not be able to find the way thither. Her betrothed

3	

3	3	only replied, ‘You must come and see me next Sunday; I have already

3	

3	3	invited guests for that day, and that you may not mistake the way, I

3	

3	3	will strew ashes along the path.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When Sunday came, and it was time for the girl to start, a feeling of

3	

3	3	dread came over her which she could not explain, and that she might

3	

3	3	be able to find her path again, she filled her pockets with peas and

3	

3	3	lentils to sprinkle on the ground as she went along. On reaching the

3	

3	3	entrance to the forest she found the path strewed with ashes, and these

3	

3	3	she followed, throwing down some peas on either side of her at every

3	

3	3	step she took. She walked the whole day until she came to the deepest,

3	

3	3	darkest part of the forest. There she saw a lonely house, looking so

3	

3	3	grim and mysterious, that it did not please her at all. She stepped

3	

3	3	inside, but not a soul was to be seen, and a great silence reigned

3	

3	3	throughout. Suddenly a voice cried:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Turn back, turn back, young maiden fair,

3	

3	3	  Linger not in this murderers’ lair.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The girl looked up and saw that the voice came from a bird hanging in a

3	

3	3	cage on the wall. Again it cried:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Turn back, turn back, young maiden fair,

3	

3	3	  Linger not in this murderers’ lair.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The girl passed on, going from room to room of the house, but they were

3	

3	3	all empty, and still she saw no one. At last she came to the cellar,

3	

3	3	and there sat a very, very old woman, who could not keep her head from

3	

3	3	shaking. ‘Can you tell me,’ asked the girl, ‘if my betrothed husband

3	

3	3	lives here?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Ah, you poor child,’ answered the old woman, ‘what a place for you to

3	

3	3	come to! This is a murderers’ den. You think yourself a promised bride,

3	

3	3	and that your marriage will soon take place, but it is with death that

3	

3	3	you will keep your marriage feast. Look, do you see that large cauldron

3	

3	3	of water which I am obliged to keep on the fire! As soon as they have

3	

3	3	you in their power they will kill you without mercy, and cook and eat

3	

3	3	you, for they are eaters of men. If I did not take pity on you and save

3	

3	3	you, you would be lost.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Thereupon the old woman led her behind a large cask, which quite hid her

3	

3	3	from view. ‘Keep as still as a mouse,’ she said; ‘do not move or speak,

3	

3	3	or it will be all over with you. Tonight, when the robbers are

3	

3	3	all asleep, we will flee together. I have long been waiting for an

3	

3	3	opportunity to escape.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The words were hardly out of her mouth when the godless crew returned,

3	

3	3	dragging another young girl along with them. They were all drunk, and

3	

3	3	paid no heed to her cries and lamentations. They gave her wine to drink,

3	

3	3	three glasses full, one of white wine, one of red, and one of yellow,

3	

3	3	and with that her heart gave way and she died. Then they tore off her

3	

3	3	dainty clothing, laid her on a table, and cut her beautiful body into

3	

3	3	pieces, and sprinkled salt upon it.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The poor betrothed girl crouched trembling and shuddering behind the

3	

3	3	cask, for she saw what a terrible fate had been intended for her by

3	

3	3	the robbers. One of them now noticed a gold ring still remaining on

3	

3	3	the little finger of the murdered girl, and as he could not draw it off

3	

3	3	easily, he took a hatchet and cut off the finger; but the finger sprang

3	

3	3	into the air, and fell behind the cask into the lap of the girl who was

3	

3	3	hiding there. The robber took a light and began looking for it, but he

3	

3	3	could not find it. ‘Have you looked behind the large cask?’ said one of

3	

3	3	the others. But the old woman called out, ‘Come and eat your suppers,

3	

3	3	and let the thing be till tomorrow; the finger won’t run away.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘The old woman is right,’ said the robbers, and they ceased looking for

3	

3	3	the finger and sat down.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The old woman then mixed a sleeping draught with their wine, and before

3	

3	3	long they were all lying on the floor of the cellar, fast asleep and

3	

3	3	snoring. As soon as the girl was assured of this, she came from behind

3	

3	3	the cask. She was obliged to step over the bodies of the sleepers, who

3	

3	3	were lying close together, and every moment she was filled with renewed

3	

3	3	dread lest she should awaken them. But God helped her, so that she

3	

3	3	passed safely over them, and then she and the old woman went upstairs,

3	

3	3	opened the door, and hastened as fast as they could from the murderers’

3	

3	3	den. They found the ashes scattered by the wind, but the peas and

3	

3	3	lentils had sprouted, and grown sufficiently above the ground, to guide

3	

3	3	them in the moonlight along the path. All night long they walked, and it

3	

3	3	was morning before they reached the mill. Then the girl told her father

3	

3	3	all that had happened.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The day came that had been fixed for the marriage. The bridegroom

3	

3	3	arrived and also a large company of guests, for the miller had taken

3	

3	3	care to invite all his friends and relations. As they sat at the feast,

3	

3	3	each guest in turn was asked to tell a tale; the bride sat still and did

3	

3	3	not say a word.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘And you, my love,’ said the bridegroom, turning to her, ‘is there no

3	

3	3	tale you know? Tell us something.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘I will tell you a dream, then,’ said the bride. ‘I went alone through a

3	

3	3	forest and came at last to a house; not a soul could I find within, but

3	

3	3	a bird that was hanging in a cage on the wall cried:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Turn back, turn back, young maiden fair,

3	

3	3	  Linger not in this murderers’ lair.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	and again a second time it said these words.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘My darling, this is only a dream.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘I went on through the house from room to room, but they were all empty,

3	

3	3	and everything was so grim and mysterious. At last I went down to the

3	

3	3	cellar, and there sat a very, very old woman, who could not keep her

3	

3	3	head still. I asked her if my betrothed lived here, and she answered,

3	

3	3	“Ah, you poor child, you are come to a murderers’ den; your betrothed

3	

3	3	does indeed live here, but he will kill you without mercy and afterwards

3	

3	3	cook and eat you.”’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘My darling, this is only a dream.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘The old woman hid me behind a large cask, and scarcely had she done

3	

3	3	this when the robbers returned home, dragging a young girl along with

3	

3	3	them. They gave her three kinds of wine to drink, white, red, and

3	

3	3	yellow, and with that she died.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘My darling, this is only a dream.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Then they tore off her dainty clothing, and cut her beautiful body into

3	

3	3	pieces and sprinkled salt upon it.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘My darling, this is only a dream.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘And one of the robbers saw that there was a gold ring still left on her

3	

3	3	finger, and as it was difficult to draw off, he took a hatchet and cut

3	

3	3	off her finger; but the finger sprang into the air and fell behind the

3	

3	3	great cask into my lap. And here is the finger with the ring.’ And

3	

3	3	with these words the bride drew forth the finger and shewed it to the

3	

3	3	assembled guests.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The bridegroom, who during this recital had grown deadly pale, up and

3	

3	3	tried to escape, but the guests seized him and held him fast. They

3	

3	3	delivered him up to justice, and he and all his murderous band were

3	

3	3	condemned to death for their wicked deeds.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	TOM THUMB

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	A poor woodman sat in his cottage one night, smoking his pipe by the

3	

3	3	fireside, while his wife sat by his side spinning. ‘How lonely it is,

3	

3	3	wife,’ said he, as he puffed out a long curl of smoke, ‘for you and me

3	

3	3	to sit here by ourselves, without any children to play about and amuse

3	

3	3	us while other people seem so happy and merry with their children!’

3	

3	3	‘What you say is very true,’ said the wife, sighing, and turning round

3	

3	3	her wheel; ‘how happy should I be if I had but one child! If it were

3	

3	3	ever so small--nay, if it were no bigger than my thumb--I should be very

3	

3	3	happy, and love it dearly.’ Now--odd as you may think it--it came to

3	

3	3	pass that this good woman’s wish was fulfilled, just in the very way she

3	

3	3	had wished it; for, not long afterwards, she had a little boy, who was

3	

3	3	quite healthy and strong, but was not much bigger than my thumb. So

3	

3	3	they said, ‘Well, we cannot say we have not got what we wished for, and,

3	

3	3	little as he is, we will love him dearly.’ And they called him Thomas

3	

3	3	Thumb.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	They gave him plenty of food, yet for all they could do he never grew

3	

3	3	bigger, but kept just the same size as he had been when he was born.

3	

3	3	Still, his eyes were sharp and sparkling, and he soon showed himself to

3	

3	3	be a clever little fellow, who always knew well what he was about.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	One day, as the woodman was getting ready to go into the wood to cut

3	

3	3	fuel, he said, ‘I wish I had someone to bring the cart after me, for I

3	

3	3	want to make haste.’ ‘Oh, father,’ cried Tom, ‘I will take care of that;

3	

3	3	the cart shall be in the wood by the time you want it.’ Then the woodman

3	

3	3	laughed, and said, ‘How can that be? you cannot reach up to the horse’s

3	

3	3	bridle.’ ‘Never mind that, father,’ said Tom; ‘if my mother will only

3	

3	3	harness the horse, I will get into his ear and tell him which way to

3	

3	3	go.’ ‘Well,’ said the father, ‘we will try for once.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the time came the mother harnessed the horse to the cart, and put

3	

3	3	Tom into his ear; and as he sat there the little man told the beast how

3	

3	3	to go, crying out, ‘Go on!’ and ‘Stop!’ as he wanted: and thus the horse

3	

3	3	went on just as well as if the woodman had driven it himself into the

3	

3	3	wood. It happened that as the horse was going a little too fast, and Tom

3	

3	3	was calling out, ‘Gently! gently!’ two strangers came up. ‘What an odd

3	

3	3	thing that is!’ said one: ‘there is a cart going along, and I hear a

3	

3	3	carter talking to the horse, but yet I can see no one.’ ‘That is queer,

3	

3	3	indeed,’ said the other; ‘let us follow the cart, and see where it

3	

3	3	goes.’ So they went on into the wood, till at last they came to the

3	

3	3	place where the woodman was. Then Tom Thumb, seeing his father, cried

3	

3	3	out, ‘See, father, here I am with the cart, all right and safe! now take

3	

3	3	me down!’ So his father took hold of the horse with one hand, and with

3	

3	3	the other took his son out of the horse’s ear, and put him down upon a

3	

3	3	straw, where he sat as merry as you please.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The two strangers were all this time looking on, and did not know what

3	

3	3	to say for wonder. At last one took the other aside, and said, ‘That

3	

3	3	little urchin will make our fortune, if we can get him, and carry him

3	

3	3	about from town to town as a show; we must buy him.’ So they went up to

3	

3	3	the woodman, and asked him what he would take for the little man. ‘He

3	

3	3	will be better off,’ said they, ‘with us than with you.’ ‘I won’t sell

3	

3	3	him at all,’ said the father; ‘my own flesh and blood is dearer to me

3	

3	3	than all the silver and gold in the world.’ But Tom, hearing of the

3	

3	3	bargain they wanted to make, crept up his father’s coat to his shoulder

3	

3	3	and whispered in his ear, ‘Take the money, father, and let them have me;

3	

3	3	I’ll soon come back to you.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So the woodman at last said he would sell Tom to the strangers for a

3	

3	3	large piece of gold, and they paid the price. ‘Where would you like to

3	

3	3	sit?’ said one of them. ‘Oh, put me on the rim of your hat; that will be

3	

3	3	a nice gallery for me; I can walk about there and see the country as we

3	

3	3	go along.’ So they did as he wished; and when Tom had taken leave of his

3	

3	3	father they took him away with them.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	They journeyed on till it began to be dusky, and then the little man

3	

3	3	said, ‘Let me get down, I’m tired.’ So the man took off his hat, and

3	

3	3	put him down on a clod of earth, in a ploughed field by the side of the

3	

3	3	road. But Tom ran about amongst the furrows, and at last slipped into

3	

3	3	an old mouse-hole. ‘Good night, my masters!’ said he, ‘I’m off! mind and

3	

3	3	look sharp after me the next time.’ Then they ran at once to the place,

3	

3	3	and poked the ends of their sticks into the mouse-hole, but all in vain;

3	

3	3	Tom only crawled farther and farther in; and at last it became quite

3	

3	3	dark, so that they were forced to go their way without their prize, as

3	

3	3	sulky as could be.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When Tom found they were gone, he came out of his hiding-place. ‘What

3	

3	3	dangerous walking it is,’ said he, ‘in this ploughed field! If I were to

3	

3	3	fall from one of these great clods, I should undoubtedly break my neck.’

3	

3	3	At last, by good luck, he found a large empty snail-shell. ‘This is

3	

3	3	lucky,’ said he, ‘I can sleep here very well’; and in he crept.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Just as he was falling asleep, he heard two men passing by, chatting

3	

3	3	together; and one said to the other, ‘How can we rob that rich parson’s

3	

3	3	house of his silver and gold?’ ‘I’ll tell you!’ cried Tom. ‘What noise

3	

3	3	was that?’ said the thief, frightened; ‘I’m sure I heard someone speak.’

3	

3	3	They stood still listening, and Tom said, ‘Take me with you, and I’ll

3	

3	3	soon show you how to get the parson’s money.’ ‘But where are you?’ said

3	

3	3	they. ‘Look about on the ground,’ answered he, ‘and listen where the

3	

3	3	sound comes from.’ At last the thieves found him out, and lifted him

3	

3	3	up in their hands. ‘You little urchin!’ they said, ‘what can you do for

3	

3	3	us?’ ‘Why, I can get between the iron window-bars of the parson’s house,

3	

3	3	and throw you out whatever you want.’ ‘That’s a good thought,’ said the

3	

3	3	thieves; ‘come along, we shall see what you can do.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When they came to the parson’s house, Tom slipped through the

3	

3	3	window-bars into the room, and then called out as loud as he could bawl,

3	

3	3	‘Will you have all that is here?’ At this the thieves were frightened,

3	

3	3	and said, ‘Softly, softly! Speak low, that you may not awaken anybody.’

3	

3	3	But Tom seemed as if he did not understand them, and bawled out again,

3	

3	3	‘How much will you have? Shall I throw it all out?’ Now the cook lay in

3	

3	3	the next room; and hearing a noise she raised herself up in her bed and

3	

3	3	listened. Meantime the thieves were frightened, and ran off a little

3	

3	3	way; but at last they plucked up their hearts, and said, ‘The little

3	

3	3	urchin is only trying to make fools of us.’ So they came back and

3	

3	3	whispered softly to him, saying, ‘Now let us have no more of your

3	

3	3	roguish jokes; but throw us out some of the money.’ Then Tom called out

3	

3	3	as loud as he could, ‘Very well! hold your hands! here it comes.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The cook heard this quite plain, so she sprang out of bed, and ran to

3	

3	3	open the door. The thieves ran off as if a wolf was at their tails: and

3	

3	3	the maid, having groped about and found nothing, went away for a light.

3	

3	3	By the time she came back, Tom had slipped off into the barn; and when

3	

3	3	she had looked about and searched every hole and corner, and found

3	

3	3	nobody, she went to bed, thinking she must have been dreaming with her

3	

3	3	eyes open.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The little man crawled about in the hay-loft, and at last found a snug

3	

3	3	place to finish his night’s rest in; so he laid himself down, meaning

3	

3	3	to sleep till daylight, and then find his way home to his father and

3	

3	3	mother. But alas! how woefully he was undone! what crosses and sorrows

3	

3	3	happen to us all in this world! The cook got up early, before daybreak,

3	

3	3	to feed the cows; and going straight to the hay-loft, carried away

3	

3	3	a large bundle of hay, with the little man in the middle of it, fast

3	

3	3	asleep. He still, however, slept on, and did not awake till he found

3	

3	3	himself in the mouth of the cow; for the cook had put the hay into the

3	

3	3	cow’s rick, and the cow had taken Tom up in a mouthful of it. ‘Good

3	

3	3	lack-a-day!’ said he, ‘how came I to tumble into the mill?’ But he soon

3	

3	3	found out where he really was; and was forced to have all his wits about

3	

3	3	him, that he might not get between the cow’s teeth, and so be crushed to

3	

3	3	death. At last down he went into her stomach. ‘It is rather dark,’ said

3	

3	3	he; ‘they forgot to build windows in this room to let the sun in; a

3	

3	3	candle would be no bad thing.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Though he made the best of his bad luck, he did not like his quarters at

3	

3	3	all; and the worst of it was, that more and more hay was always coming

3	

3	3	down, and the space left for him became smaller and smaller. At last he

3	

3	3	cried out as loud as he could, ‘Don’t bring me any more hay! Don’t bring

3	

3	3	me any more hay!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The maid happened to be just then milking the cow; and hearing someone

3	

3	3	speak, but seeing nobody, and yet being quite sure it was the same voice

3	

3	3	that she had heard in the night, she was so frightened that she fell off

3	

3	3	her stool, and overset the milk-pail. As soon as she could pick herself

3	

3	3	up out of the dirt, she ran off as fast as she could to her master the

3	

3	3	parson, and said, ‘Sir, sir, the cow is talking!’ But the parson

3	

3	3	said, ‘Woman, thou art surely mad!’ However, he went with her into the

3	

3	3	cow-house, to try and see what was the matter.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Scarcely had they set foot on the threshold, when Tom called out, ‘Don’t

3	

3	3	bring me any more hay!’ Then the parson himself was frightened; and

3	

3	3	thinking the cow was surely bewitched, told his man to kill her on the

3	

3	3	spot. So the cow was killed, and cut up; and the stomach, in which Tom

3	

3	3	lay, was thrown out upon a dunghill.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Tom soon set himself to work to get out, which was not a very easy

3	

3	3	task; but at last, just as he had made room to get his head out, fresh

3	

3	3	ill-luck befell him. A hungry wolf sprang out, and swallowed up the

3	

3	3	whole stomach, with Tom in it, at one gulp, and ran away.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Tom, however, was still not disheartened; and thinking the wolf would

3	

3	3	not dislike having some chat with him as he was going along, he called

3	

3	3	out, ‘My good friend, I can show you a famous treat.’ ‘Where’s that?’

3	

3	3	said the wolf. ‘In such and such a house,’ said Tom, describing his own

3	

3	3	father’s house. ‘You can crawl through the drain into the kitchen and

3	

3	3	then into the pantry, and there you will find cakes, ham, beef, cold

3	

3	3	chicken, roast pig, apple-dumplings, and everything that your heart can

3	

3	3	wish.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The wolf did not want to be asked twice; so that very night he went to

3	

3	3	the house and crawled through the drain into the kitchen, and then into

3	

3	3	the pantry, and ate and drank there to his heart’s content. As soon as

3	

3	3	he had had enough he wanted to get away; but he had eaten so much that

3	

3	3	he could not go out by the same way he came in.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	This was just what Tom had reckoned upon; and now he began to set up a

3	

3	3	great shout, making all the noise he could. ‘Will you be easy?’ said the

3	

3	3	wolf; ‘you’ll awaken everybody in the house if you make such a clatter.’

3	

3	3	‘What’s that to me?’ said the little man; ‘you have had your frolic, now

3	

3	3	I’ve a mind to be merry myself’; and he began, singing and shouting as

3	

3	3	loud as he could.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The woodman and his wife, being awakened by the noise, peeped through

3	

3	3	a crack in the door; but when they saw a wolf was there, you may well

3	

3	3	suppose that they were sadly frightened; and the woodman ran for his

3	

3	3	axe, and gave his wife a scythe. ‘Do you stay behind,’ said the woodman,

3	

3	3	‘and when I have knocked him on the head you must rip him up with the

3	

3	3	scythe.’ Tom heard all this, and cried out, ‘Father, father! I am here,

3	

3	3	the wolf has swallowed me.’ And his father said, ‘Heaven be praised! we

3	

3	3	have found our dear child again’; and he told his wife not to use the

3	

3	3	scythe for fear she should hurt him. Then he aimed a great blow, and

3	

3	3	struck the wolf on the head, and killed him on the spot! and when he was

3	

3	3	dead they cut open his body, and set Tommy free. ‘Ah!’ said the father,

3	

3	3	‘what fears we have had for you!’ ‘Yes, father,’ answered he; ‘I have

3	

3	3	travelled all over the world, I think, in one way or other, since we

3	

3	3	parted; and now I am very glad to come home and get fresh air again.’

3	

3	3	‘Why, where have you been?’ said his father. ‘I have been in a

3	

3	3	mouse-hole--and in a snail-shell--and down a cow’s throat--and in the

3	

3	3	wolf’s belly; and yet here I am again, safe and sound.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Well,’ said they, ‘you are come back, and we will not sell you again

3	

3	3	for all the riches in the world.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then they hugged and kissed their dear little son, and gave him plenty

3	

3	3	to eat and drink, for he was very hungry; and then they fetched new

3	

3	3	clothes for him, for his old ones had been quite spoiled on his journey.

3	

3	3	So Master Thumb stayed at home with his father and mother, in peace; for

3	

3	3	though he had been so great a traveller, and had done and seen so many

3	

3	3	fine things, and was fond enough of telling the whole story, he always

3	

3	3	agreed that, after all, there’s no place like HOME!

3	

3	3	

3

3	3	RUMPELSTILTSKIN

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	By the side of a wood, in a country a long way off, ran a fine stream

3	

3	3	of water; and upon the stream there stood a mill. The miller’s house was

3	

3	3	close by, and the miller, you must know, had a very beautiful daughter.

3	

3	3	She was, moreover, very shrewd and clever; and the miller was so proud

3	

3	3	of her, that he one day told the king of the land, who used to come and

3	

3	3	hunt in the wood, that his daughter could spin gold out of straw. Now

3	

3	3	this king was very fond of money; and when he heard the miller’s boast

3	

3	3	his greediness was raised, and he sent for the girl to be brought before

3	

3	3	him. Then he led her to a chamber in his palace where there was a great

3	

3	3	heap of straw, and gave her a spinning-wheel, and said, ‘All this must

3	

3	3	be spun into gold before morning, as you love your life.’ It was in vain

3	

3	3	that the poor maiden said that it was only a silly boast of her father,

3	

3	3	for that she could do no such thing as spin straw into gold: the chamber

3	

3	3	door was locked, and she was left alone.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	She sat down in one corner of the room, and began to bewail her hard

3	

3	3	fate; when on a sudden the door opened, and a droll-looking little man

3	

3	3	hobbled in, and said, ‘Good morrow to you, my good lass; what are you

3	

3	3	weeping for?’ ‘Alas!’ said she, ‘I must spin this straw into gold, and

3	

3	3	I know not how.’ ‘What will you give me,’ said the hobgoblin, ‘to do it

3	

3	3	for you?’ ‘My necklace,’ replied the maiden. He took her at her word,

3	

3	3	and sat himself down to the wheel, and whistled and sang:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Round about, round about,

3	

3	3	    Lo and behold!

3	

3	3	  Reel away, reel away,

3	

3	3	    Straw into gold!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And round about the wheel went merrily; the work was quickly done, and

3	

3	3	the straw was all spun into gold.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the king came and saw this, he was greatly astonished and pleased;

3	

3	3	but his heart grew still more greedy of gain, and he shut up the poor

3	

3	3	miller’s daughter again with a fresh task. Then she knew not what to do,

3	

3	3	and sat down once more to weep; but the dwarf soon opened the door, and

3	

3	3	said, ‘What will you give me to do your task?’ ‘The ring on my finger,’

3	

3	3	said she. So her little friend took the ring, and began to work at the

3	

3	3	wheel again, and whistled and sang:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Round about, round about,

3	

3	3	    Lo and behold!

3	

3	3	  Reel away, reel away,

3	

3	3	    Straw into gold!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	till, long before morning, all was done again.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The king was greatly delighted to see all this glittering treasure;

3	

3	3	but still he had not enough: so he took the miller’s daughter to a yet

3	

3	3	larger heap, and said, ‘All this must be spun tonight; and if it is,

3	

3	3	you shall be my queen.’ As soon as she was alone that dwarf came in, and

3	

3	3	said, ‘What will you give me to spin gold for you this third time?’

3	

3	3	‘I have nothing left,’ said she. ‘Then say you will give me,’ said

3	

3	3	the little man, ‘the first little child that you may have when you are

3	

3	3	queen.’ ‘That may never be,’ thought the miller’s daughter: and as she

3	

3	3	knew no other way to get her task done, she said she would do what he

3	

3	3	asked. Round went the wheel again to the old song, and the manikin once

3	

3	3	more spun the heap into gold. The king came in the morning, and, finding

3	

3	3	all he wanted, was forced to keep his word; so he married the miller’s

3	

3	3	daughter, and she really became queen.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	At the birth of her first little child she was very glad, and forgot the

3	

3	3	dwarf, and what she had said. But one day he came into her room, where

3	

3	3	she was sitting playing with her baby, and put her in mind of it. Then

3	

3	3	she grieved sorely at her misfortune, and said she would give him all

3	

3	3	the wealth of the kingdom if he would let her off, but in vain; till at

3	

3	3	last her tears softened him, and he said, ‘I will give you three days’

3	

3	3	grace, and if during that time you tell me my name, you shall keep your

3	

3	3	child.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Now the queen lay awake all night, thinking of all the odd names that

3	

3	3	she had ever heard; and she sent messengers all over the land to find

3	

3	3	out new ones. The next day the little man came, and she began with

3	

3	3	TIMOTHY, ICHABOD, BENJAMIN, JEREMIAH, and all the names she could

3	

3	3	remember; but to all and each of them he said, ‘Madam, that is not my

3	

3	3	name.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The second day she began with all the comical names she could hear of,

3	

3	3	BANDY-LEGS, HUNCHBACK, CROOK-SHANKS, and so on; but the little gentleman

3	

3	3	still said to every one of them, ‘Madam, that is not my name.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The third day one of the messengers came back, and said, ‘I have

3	

3	3	travelled two days without hearing of any other names; but yesterday, as

3	

3	3	I was climbing a high hill, among the trees of the forest where the fox

3	

3	3	and the hare bid each other good night, I saw a little hut; and before

3	

3	3	the hut burnt a fire; and round about the fire a funny little dwarf was

3	

3	3	dancing upon one leg, and singing:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 “Merrily the feast I’ll make.

3	

3	3	  Today I’ll brew, tomorrow bake;

3	

3	3	  Merrily I’ll dance and sing,

3	

3	3	  For next day will a stranger bring.

3	

3	3	  Little does my lady dream

3	

3	3	  Rumpelstiltskin is my name!”

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the queen heard this she jumped for joy, and as soon as her little

3	

3	3	friend came she sat down upon her throne, and called all her court round

3	

3	3	to enjoy the fun; and the nurse stood by her side with the baby in her

3	

3	3	arms, as if it was quite ready to be given up. Then the little man began

3	

3	3	to chuckle at the thought of having the poor child, to take home with

3	

3	3	him to his hut in the woods; and he cried out, ‘Now, lady, what is my

3	

3	3	name?’ ‘Is it JOHN?’ asked she. ‘No, madam!’ ‘Is it TOM?’ ‘No, madam!’

3	

3	3	‘Is it JEMMY?’ ‘It is not.’ ‘Can your name be RUMPELSTILTSKIN?’ said the

3	

3	3	lady slyly. ‘Some witch told you that!--some witch told you that!’ cried

3	

3	3	the little man, and dashed his right foot in a rage so deep into the

3	

3	3	floor, that he was forced to lay hold of it with both hands to pull it

3	

3	3	out.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then he made the best of his way off, while the nurse laughed and the

3
