3	3	baby crowed; and all the court jeered at him for having had so much

3	

3	3	trouble for nothing, and said, ‘We wish you a very good morning, and a

3	

3	3	merry feast, Mr RUMPLESTILTSKIN!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	CLEVER GRETEL

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once a cook named Gretel, who wore shoes with red heels, and

3	

3	3	when she walked out with them on, she turned herself this way and that,

3	

3	3	was quite happy and thought: ‘You certainly are a pretty girl!’ And when

3	

3	3	she came home she drank, in her gladness of heart, a draught of wine,

3	

3	3	and as wine excites a desire to eat, she tasted the best of whatever she

3	

3	3	was cooking until she was satisfied, and said: ‘The cook must know what

3	

3	3	the food is like.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	It came to pass that the master one day said to her: ‘Gretel, there is a

3	

3	3	guest coming this evening; prepare me two fowls very daintily.’ ‘I will

3	

3	3	see to it, master,’ answered Gretel. She killed two fowls, scalded them,

3	

3	3	plucked them, put them on the spit, and towards evening set them before

3	

3	3	the fire, that they might roast. The fowls began to turn brown, and were

3	

3	3	nearly ready, but the guest had not yet arrived. Then Gretel called out

3	

3	3	to her master: ‘If the guest does not come, I must take the fowls away

3	

3	3	from the fire, but it will be a sin and a shame if they are not eaten

3	

3	3	the moment they are at their juiciest.’ The master said: ‘I will run

3	

3	3	myself, and fetch the guest.’ When the master had turned his back,

3	

3	3	Gretel laid the spit with the fowls on one side, and thought: ‘Standing

3	

3	3	so long by the fire there, makes one sweat and thirsty; who knows

3	

3	3	when they will come? Meanwhile, I will run into the cellar, and take a

3	

3	3	drink.’ She ran down, set a jug, said: ‘God bless it for you, Gretel,’

3	

3	3	and took a good drink, and thought that wine should flow on, and should

3	

3	3	not be interrupted, and took yet another hearty draught.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then she went and put the fowls down again to the fire, basted them,

3	

3	3	and drove the spit merrily round. But as the roast meat smelt so good,

3	

3	3	Gretel thought: ‘Something might be wrong, it ought to be tasted!’

3	

3	3	She touched it with her finger, and said: ‘Ah! how good fowls are! It

3	

3	3	certainly is a sin and a shame that they are not eaten at the right

3	

3	3	time!’ She ran to the window, to see if the master was not coming with

3	

3	3	his guest, but she saw no one, and went back to the fowls and thought:

3	

3	3	‘One of the wings is burning! I had better take it off and eat it.’

3	

3	3	So she cut it off, ate it, and enjoyed it, and when she had done, she

3	

3	3	thought: ‘The other must go down too, or else master will observe that

3	

3	3	something is missing.’ When the two wings were eaten, she went and

3	

3	3	looked for her master, and did not see him. It suddenly occurred to

3	

3	3	her: ‘Who knows? They are perhaps not coming at all, and have turned in

3	

3	3	somewhere.’ Then she said: ‘Well, Gretel, enjoy yourself, one fowl has

3	

3	3	been cut into, take another drink, and eat it up entirely; when it is

3	

3	3	eaten you will have some peace, why should God’s good gifts be spoilt?’

3	

3	3	So she ran into the cellar again, took an enormous drink and ate up the

3	

3	3	one chicken in great glee. When one of the chickens was swallowed down,

3	

3	3	and still her master did not come, Gretel looked at the other and said:

3	

3	3	‘What one is, the other should be likewise, the two go together; what’s

3	

3	3	right for the one is right for the other; I think if I were to take

3	

3	3	another draught it would do me no harm.’ So she took another hearty

3	

3	3	drink, and let the second chicken follow the first.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	While she was making the most of it, her master came and cried: ‘Hurry

3	

3	3	up, Gretel, the guest is coming directly after me!’ ‘Yes, sir, I will

3	

3	3	soon serve up,’ answered Gretel. Meantime the master looked to see that

3	

3	3	the table was properly laid, and took the great knife, wherewith he was

3	

3	3	going to carve the chickens, and sharpened it on the steps. Presently

3	

3	3	the guest came, and knocked politely and courteously at the house-door.

3	

3	3	Gretel ran, and looked to see who was there, and when she saw the guest,

3	

3	3	she put her finger to her lips and said: ‘Hush! hush! go away as quickly

3	

3	3	as you can, if my master catches you it will be the worse for you; he

3	

3	3	certainly did ask you to supper, but his intention is to cut off your

3	

3	3	two ears. Just listen how he is sharpening the knife for it!’ The guest

3	

3	3	heard the sharpening, and hurried down the steps again as fast as he

3	

3	3	could. Gretel was not idle; she ran screaming to her master, and cried:

3	

3	3	‘You have invited a fine guest!’ ‘Why, Gretel? What do you mean by

3	

3	3	that?’ ‘Yes,’ said she, ‘he has taken the chickens which I was just

3	

3	3	going to serve up, off the dish, and has run away with them!’ ‘That’s a

3	

3	3	nice trick!’ said her master, and lamented the fine chickens. ‘If he had

3	

3	3	but left me one, so that something remained for me to eat.’ He called to

3	

3	3	him to stop, but the guest pretended not to hear. Then he ran after him

3	

3	3	with the knife still in his hand, crying: ‘Just one, just one,’ meaning

3	

3	3	that the guest should leave him just one chicken, and not take both. The

3	

3	3	guest, however, thought no otherwise than that he was to give up one of

3	

3	3	his ears, and ran as if fire were burning under him, in order to take

3	

3	3	them both with him.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE OLD MAN AND HIS GRANDSON

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once a very old man, whose eyes had become dim, his ears dull

3	

3	3	of hearing, his knees trembled, and when he sat at table he could hardly

3	

3	3	hold the spoon, and spilt the broth upon the table-cloth or let it run

3	

3	3	out of his mouth. His son and his son’s wife were disgusted at this, so

3	

3	3	the old grandfather at last had to sit in the corner behind the stove,

3	

3	3	and they gave him his food in an earthenware bowl, and not even enough

3	

3	3	of it. And he used to look towards the table with his eyes full of

3	

3	3	tears. Once, too, his trembling hands could not hold the bowl, and it

3	

3	3	fell to the ground and broke. The young wife scolded him, but he said

3	

3	3	nothing and only sighed. Then they brought him a wooden bowl for a few

3	

3	3	half-pence, out of which he had to eat.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	They were once sitting thus when the little grandson of four years old

3	

3	3	began to gather together some bits of wood upon the ground. ‘What are

3	

3	3	you doing there?’ asked the father. ‘I am making a little trough,’

3	

3	3	answered the child, ‘for father and mother to eat out of when I am big.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The man and his wife looked at each other for a while, and presently

3	

3	3	began to cry. Then they took the old grandfather to the table, and

3	

3	3	henceforth always let him eat with them, and likewise said nothing if he

3	

3	3	did spill a little of anything.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE LITTLE PEASANT

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was a certain village wherein no one lived but really rich

3	

3	3	peasants, and just one poor one, whom they called the little peasant. He

3	

3	3	had not even so much as a cow, and still less money to buy one, and

3	

3	3	yet he and his wife did so wish to have one. One day he said to her:

3	

3	3	‘Listen, I have a good idea, there is our gossip the carpenter, he shall

3	

3	3	make us a wooden calf, and paint it brown, so that it looks like any

3	

3	3	other, and in time it will certainly get big and be a cow.’ the woman

3	

3	3	also liked the idea, and their gossip the carpenter cut and planed

3	

3	3	the calf, and painted it as it ought to be, and made it with its head

3	

3	3	hanging down as if it were eating.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Next morning when the cows were being driven out, the little peasant

3	

3	3	called the cow-herd in and said: ‘Look, I have a little calf there,

3	

3	3	but it is still small and has to be carried.’ The cow-herd said: ‘All

3	

3	3	right,’ and took it in his arms and carried it to the pasture, and set

3	

3	3	it among the grass. The little calf always remained standing like one

3	

3	3	which was eating, and the cow-herd said: ‘It will soon run by itself,

3	

3	3	just look how it eats already!’ At night when he was going to drive the

3	

3	3	herd home again, he said to the calf: ‘If you can stand there and eat

3	

3	3	your fill, you can also go on your four legs; I don’t care to drag you

3	

3	3	home again in my arms.’ But the little peasant stood at his door, and

3	

3	3	waited for his little calf, and when the cow-herd drove the cows through

3	

3	3	the village, and the calf was missing, he inquired where it was. The

3	

3	3	cow-herd answered: ‘It is still standing out there eating. It would not

3	

3	3	stop and come with us.’ But the little peasant said: ‘Oh, but I must

3	

3	3	have my beast back again.’ Then they went back to the meadow together,

3	

3	3	but someone had stolen the calf, and it was gone. The cow-herd said: ‘It

3	

3	3	must have run away.’ The peasant, however, said: ‘Don’t tell me

3	

3	3	that,’ and led the cow-herd before the mayor, who for his carelessness

3	

3	3	condemned him to give the peasant a cow for the calf which had run away.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And now the little peasant and his wife had the cow for which they had

3	

3	3	so long wished, and they were heartily glad, but they had no food for

3	

3	3	it, and could give it nothing to eat, so it soon had to be killed. They

3	

3	3	salted the flesh, and the peasant went into the town and wanted to sell

3	

3	3	the skin there, so that he might buy a new calf with the proceeds. On

3	

3	3	the way he passed by a mill, and there sat a raven with broken wings,

3	

3	3	and out of pity he took him and wrapped him in the skin. But as the

3	

3	3	weather grew so bad and there was a storm of rain and wind, he could

3	

3	3	go no farther, and turned back to the mill and begged for shelter. The

3	

3	3	miller’s wife was alone in the house, and said to the peasant: ‘Lay

3	

3	3	yourself on the straw there,’ and gave him a slice of bread and cheese.

3	

3	3	The peasant ate it, and lay down with his skin beside him, and the woman

3	

3	3	thought: ‘He is tired and has gone to sleep.’ In the meantime came the

3	

3	3	parson; the miller’s wife received him well, and said: ‘My husband is

3	

3	3	out, so we will have a feast.’ The peasant listened, and when he heard

3	

3	3	them talk about feasting he was vexed that he had been forced to make

3	

3	3	shift with a slice of bread and cheese. Then the woman served up four

3	

3	3	different things, roast meat, salad, cakes, and wine.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Just as they were about to sit down and eat, there was a knocking

3	

3	3	outside. The woman said: ‘Oh, heavens! It is my husband!’ she quickly

3	

3	3	hid the roast meat inside the tiled stove, the wine under the pillow,

3	

3	3	the salad on the bed, the cakes under it, and the parson in the closet

3	

3	3	on the porch. Then she opened the door for her husband, and said: ‘Thank

3	

3	3	heaven, you are back again! There is such a storm, it looks as if the

3	

3	3	world were coming to an end.’ The miller saw the peasant lying on the

3	

3	3	straw, and asked, ‘What is that fellow doing there?’ ‘Ah,’ said the

3	

3	3	wife, ‘the poor knave came in the storm and rain, and begged for

3	

3	3	shelter, so I gave him a bit of bread and cheese, and showed him where

3	

3	3	the straw was.’ The man said: ‘I have no objection, but be quick and get

3	

3	3	me something to eat.’ The woman said: ‘But I have nothing but bread and

3	

3	3	cheese.’ ‘I am contented with anything,’ replied the husband, ‘so far as

3	

3	3	I am concerned, bread and cheese will do,’ and looked at the peasant and

3	

3	3	said: ‘Come and eat some more with me.’ The peasant did not require to

3	

3	3	be invited twice, but got up and ate. After this the miller saw the skin

3	

3	3	in which the raven was, lying on the ground, and asked: ‘What have you

3	

3	3	there?’ The peasant answered: ‘I have a soothsayer inside it.’ ‘Can

3	

3	3	he foretell anything to me?’ said the miller. ‘Why not?’ answered

3	

3	3	the peasant: ‘but he only says four things, and the fifth he keeps to

3	

3	3	himself.’ The miller was curious, and said: ‘Let him foretell something

3	

3	3	for once.’ Then the peasant pinched the raven’s head, so that he croaked

3	

3	3	and made a noise like krr, krr. The miller said: ‘What did he say?’ The

3	

3	3	peasant answered: ‘In the first place, he says that there is some wine

3	

3	3	hidden under the pillow.’ ‘Bless me!’ cried the miller, and went there

3	

3	3	and found the wine. ‘Now go on,’ said he. The peasant made the raven

3	

3	3	croak again, and said: ‘In the second place, he says that there is some

3	

3	3	roast meat in the tiled stove.’ ‘Upon my word!’ cried the miller, and

3	

3	3	went thither, and found the roast meat. The peasant made the raven

3	

3	3	prophesy still more, and said: ‘Thirdly, he says that there is some

3	

3	3	salad on the bed.’ ‘That would be a fine thing!’ cried the miller, and

3	

3	3	went there and found the salad. At last the peasant pinched the raven

3	

3	3	once more till he croaked, and said: ‘Fourthly, he says that there

3	

3	3	are some cakes under the bed.’ ‘That would be a fine thing!’ cried the

3	

3	3	miller, and looked there, and found the cakes.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And now the two sat down to the table together, but the miller’s wife

3	

3	3	was frightened to death, and went to bed and took all the keys with

3	

3	3	her. The miller would have liked much to know the fifth, but the little

3	

3	3	peasant said: ‘First, we will quickly eat the four things, for the fifth

3	

3	3	is something bad.’ So they ate, and after that they bargained how much

3	

3	3	the miller was to give for the fifth prophecy, until they agreed on

3	

3	3	three hundred talers. Then the peasant once more pinched the raven’s

3	

3	3	head till he croaked loudly. The miller asked: ‘What did he say?’ The

3	

3	3	peasant replied: ‘He says that the Devil is hiding outside there in

3	

3	3	the closet on the porch.’ The miller said: ‘The Devil must go out,’ and

3	

3	3	opened the house-door; then the woman was forced to give up the keys,

3	

3	3	and the peasant unlocked the closet. The parson ran out as fast as he

3	

3	3	could, and the miller said: ‘It was true; I saw the black rascal with my

3	

3	3	own eyes.’ The peasant, however, made off next morning by daybreak with

3	

3	3	the three hundred talers.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	At home the small peasant gradually launched out; he built a beautiful

3	

3	3	house, and the peasants said: ‘The small peasant has certainly been to

3	

3	3	the place where golden snow falls, and people carry the gold home in

3	

3	3	shovels.’ Then the small peasant was brought before the mayor, and

3	

3	3	bidden to say from whence his wealth came. He answered: ‘I sold my cow’s

3	

3	3	skin in the town, for three hundred talers.’ When the peasants heard

3	

3	3	that, they too wished to enjoy this great profit, and ran home, killed

3	

3	3	all their cows, and stripped off their skins in order to sell them in

3	

3	3	the town to the greatest advantage. The mayor, however, said: ‘But my

3	

3	3	servant must go first.’ When she came to the merchant in the town, he

3	

3	3	did not give her more than two talers for a skin, and when the others

3	

3	3	came, he did not give them so much, and said: ‘What can I do with all

3	

3	3	these skins?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the peasants were vexed that the small peasant should have thus

3	

3	3	outwitted them, wanted to take vengeance on him, and accused him of this

3	

3	3	treachery before the mayor. The innocent little peasant was unanimously

3	

3	3	sentenced to death, and was to be rolled into the water, in a barrel

3	

3	3	pierced full of holes. He was led forth, and a priest was brought who

3	

3	3	was to say a mass for his soul. The others were all obliged to retire to

3	

3	3	a distance, and when the peasant looked at the priest, he recognized the

3	

3	3	man who had been with the miller’s wife. He said to him: ‘I set you free

3	

3	3	from the closet, set me free from the barrel.’ At this same moment up

3	

3	3	came, with a flock of sheep, the very shepherd whom the peasant knew had

3	

3	3	long been wishing to be mayor, so he cried with all his might: ‘No, I

3	

3	3	will not do it; if the whole world insists on it, I will not do it!’ The

3	

3	3	shepherd hearing that, came up to him, and asked: ‘What are you about?

3	

3	3	What is it that you will not do?’ The peasant said: ‘They want to make

3	

3	3	me mayor, if I will but put myself in the barrel, but I will not do it.’

3	

3	3	The shepherd said: ‘If nothing more than that is needful in order to be

3	

3	3	mayor, I would get into the barrel at once.’ The peasant said: ‘If you

3	

3	3	will get in, you will be mayor.’ The shepherd was willing, and got in,

3	

3	3	and the peasant shut the top down on him; then he took the shepherd’s

3	

3	3	flock for himself, and drove it away. The parson went to the crowd,

3	

3	3	and declared that the mass had been said. Then they came and rolled the

3	

3	3	barrel towards the water. When the barrel began to roll, the shepherd

3	

3	3	cried: ‘I am quite willing to be mayor.’ They believed no otherwise than

3	

3	3	that it was the peasant who was saying this, and answered: ‘That is

3	

3	3	what we intend, but first you shall look about you a little down below

3	

3	3	there,’ and they rolled the barrel down into the water.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	After that the peasants went home, and as they were entering the

3	

3	3	village, the small peasant also came quietly in, driving a flock of

3	

3	3	sheep and looking quite contented. Then the peasants were astonished,

3	

3	3	and said: ‘Peasant, from whence do you come? Have you come out of the

3	

3	3	water?’ ‘Yes, truly,’ replied the peasant, ‘I sank deep, deep down,

3	

3	3	until at last I got to the bottom; I pushed the bottom out of the

3	

3	3	barrel, and crept out, and there were pretty meadows on which a number

3	

3	3	of lambs were feeding, and from thence I brought this flock away with

3	

3	3	me.’ Said the peasants: ‘Are there any more there?’ ‘Oh, yes,’ said he,

3	

3	3	‘more than I could want.’ Then the peasants made up their minds that

3	

3	3	they too would fetch some sheep for themselves, a flock apiece, but the

3	

3	3	mayor said: ‘I come first.’ So they went to the water together, and just

3	

3	3	then there were some of the small fleecy clouds in the blue sky, which

3	

3	3	are called little lambs, and they were reflected in the water, whereupon

3	

3	3	the peasants cried: ‘We already see the sheep down below!’ The mayor

3	

3	3	pressed forward and said: ‘I will go down first, and look about me, and

3	

3	3	if things promise well I’ll call you.’ So he jumped in; splash! went

3	

3	3	the water; it sounded as if he were calling them, and the whole crowd

3	

3	3	plunged in after him as one man. Then the entire village was dead, and

3	

3	3	the small peasant, as sole heir, became a rich man.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	FREDERICK AND CATHERINE

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once a man called Frederick: he had a wife whose name was

3	

3	3	Catherine, and they had not long been married. One day Frederick said.

3	

3	3	‘Kate! I am going to work in the fields; when I come back I shall be

3	

3	3	hungry so let me have something nice cooked, and a good draught of ale.’

3	

3	3	‘Very well,’ said she, ‘it shall all be ready.’ When dinner-time drew

3	

3	3	nigh, Catherine took a nice steak, which was all the meat she had, and

3	

3	3	put it on the fire to fry. The steak soon began to look brown, and to

3	

3	3	crackle in the pan; and Catherine stood by with a fork and turned it:

3	

3	3	then she said to herself, ‘The steak is almost ready, I may as well go

3	

3	3	to the cellar for the ale.’ So she left the pan on the fire and took a

3	

3	3	large jug and went into the cellar and tapped the ale cask. The beer ran

3	

3	3	into the jug and Catherine stood looking on. At last it popped into her

3	

3	3	head, ‘The dog is not shut up--he may be running away with the steak;

3	

3	3	that’s well thought of.’ So up she ran from the cellar; and sure enough

3	

3	3	the rascally cur had got the steak in his mouth, and was making off with

3	

3	3	it.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Away ran Catherine, and away ran the dog across the field: but he ran

3	

3	3	faster than she, and stuck close to the steak. ‘It’s all gone, and “what

3	

3	3	can’t be cured must be endured”,’ said Catherine. So she turned round;

3	

3	3	and as she had run a good way and was tired, she walked home leisurely

3	

3	3	to cool herself.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Now all this time the ale was running too, for Catherine had not turned

3	

3	3	the cock; and when the jug was full the liquor ran upon the floor till

3	

3	3	the cask was empty. When she got to the cellar stairs she saw what had

3	

3	3	happened. ‘My stars!’ said she, ‘what shall I do to keep Frederick from

3	

3	3	seeing all this slopping about?’ So she thought a while; and at last

3	

3	3	remembered that there was a sack of fine meal bought at the last fair,

3	

3	3	and that if she sprinkled this over the floor it would suck up the ale

3	

3	3	nicely. ‘What a lucky thing,’ said she, ‘that we kept that meal! we have

3	

3	3	now a good use for it.’ So away she went for it: but she managed to set

3	

3	3	it down just upon the great jug full of beer, and upset it; and thus

3	

3	3	all the ale that had been saved was set swimming on the floor also. ‘Ah!

3	

3	3	well,’ said she, ‘when one goes another may as well follow.’ Then she

3	

3	3	strewed the meal all about the cellar, and was quite pleased with her

3	

3	3	cleverness, and said, ‘How very neat and clean it looks!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	At noon Frederick came home. ‘Now, wife,’ cried he, ‘what have you for

3	

3	3	dinner?’ ‘O Frederick!’ answered she, ‘I was cooking you a steak; but

3	

3	3	while I went down to draw the ale, the dog ran away with it; and while

3	

3	3	I ran after him, the ale ran out; and when I went to dry up the ale

3	

3	3	with the sack of meal that we got at the fair, I upset the jug: but the

3	

3	3	cellar is now quite dry, and looks so clean!’ ‘Kate, Kate,’ said he,

3	

3	3	‘how could you do all this?’ Why did you leave the steak to fry, and the

3	

3	3	ale to run, and then spoil all the meal?’ ‘Why, Frederick,’ said she, ‘I

3	

3	3	did not know I was doing wrong; you should have told me before.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The husband thought to himself, ‘If my wife manages matters thus, I must

3	

3	3	look sharp myself.’ Now he had a good deal of gold in the house: so he

3	

3	3	said to Catherine, ‘What pretty yellow buttons these are! I shall put

3	

3	3	them into a box and bury them in the garden; but take care that you

3	

3	3	never go near or meddle with them.’ ‘No, Frederick,’ said she, ‘that

3	

3	3	I never will.’ As soon as he was gone, there came by some pedlars with

3	

3	3	earthenware plates and dishes, and they asked her whether she would buy.

3	

3	3	‘Oh dear me, I should like to buy very much, but I have no money: if

3	

3	3	you had any use for yellow buttons, I might deal with you.’ ‘Yellow

3	

3	3	buttons!’ said they: ‘let us have a look at them.’ ‘Go into the garden

3	

3	3	and dig where I tell you, and you will find the yellow buttons: I dare

3	

3	3	not go myself.’ So the rogues went: and when they found what these

3	

3	3	yellow buttons were, they took them all away, and left her plenty of

3	

3	3	plates and dishes. Then she set them all about the house for a show:

3	

3	3	and when Frederick came back, he cried out, ‘Kate, what have you been

3	

3	3	doing?’ ‘See,’ said she, ‘I have bought all these with your yellow

3	

3	3	buttons: but I did not touch them myself; the pedlars went themselves

3	

3	3	and dug them up.’ ‘Wife, wife,’ said Frederick, ‘what a pretty piece of

3	

3	3	work you have made! those yellow buttons were all my money: how came you

3	

3	3	to do such a thing?’ ‘Why,’ answered she, ‘I did not know there was any

3	

3	3	harm in it; you should have told me.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Catherine stood musing for a while, and at last said to her husband,

3	

3	3	‘Hark ye, Frederick, we will soon get the gold back: let us run after

3	

3	3	the thieves.’ ‘Well, we will try,’ answered he; ‘but take some butter

3	

3	3	and cheese with you, that we may have something to eat by the way.’

3	

3	3	‘Very well,’ said she; and they set out: and as Frederick walked the

3	

3	3	fastest, he left his wife some way behind. ‘It does not matter,’ thought

3	

3	3	she: ‘when we turn back, I shall be so much nearer home than he.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Presently she came to the top of a hill, down the side of which there

3	

3	3	was a road so narrow that the cart wheels always chafed the trees

3	

3	3	on each side as they passed. ‘Ah, see now,’ said she, ‘how they have

3	

3	3	bruised and wounded those poor trees; they will never get well.’ So she

3	

3	3	took pity on them, and made use of the butter to grease them all, so

3	

3	3	that the wheels might not hurt them so much. While she was doing this

3	

3	3	kind office one of her cheeses fell out of the basket, and rolled down

3	

3	3	the hill. Catherine looked, but could not see where it had gone; so she

3	

3	3	said, ‘Well, I suppose the other will go the same way and find you; he

3	

3	3	has younger legs than I have.’ Then she rolled the other cheese after

3	

3	3	it; and away it went, nobody knows where, down the hill. But she said

3	

3	3	she supposed that they knew the road, and would follow her, and she

3	

3	3	could not stay there all day waiting for them.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	At last she overtook Frederick, who desired her to give him something to

3	

3	3	eat. Then she gave him the dry bread. ‘Where are the butter and cheese?’

3	

3	3	said he. ‘Oh!’ answered she, ‘I used the butter to grease those poor

3	

3	3	trees that the wheels chafed so: and one of the cheeses ran away so I

3	

3	3	sent the other after it to find it, and I suppose they are both on

3	

3	3	the road together somewhere.’ ‘What a goose you are to do such silly

3	

3	3	things!’ said the husband. ‘How can you say so?’ said she; ‘I am sure

3	

3	3	you never told me not.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	They ate the dry bread together; and Frederick said, ‘Kate, I hope you

3	

3	3	locked the door safe when you came away.’ ‘No,’ answered she, ‘you did

3	

3	3	not tell me.’ ‘Then go home, and do it now before we go any farther,’

3	

3	3	said Frederick, ‘and bring with you something to eat.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Catherine did as he told her, and thought to herself by the way,

3	

3	3	‘Frederick wants something to eat; but I don’t think he is very fond of

3	

3	3	butter and cheese: I’ll bring him a bag of fine nuts, and the vinegar,

3	

3	3	for I have often seen him take some.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When she reached home, she bolted the back door, but the front door she

3	

3	3	took off the hinges, and said, ‘Frederick told me to lock the door, but

3	

3	3	surely it can nowhere be so safe if I take it with me.’ So she took

3	

3	3	her time by the way; and when she overtook her husband she cried

3	

3	3	out, ‘There, Frederick, there is the door itself, you may watch it as

3	

3	3	carefully as you please.’ ‘Alas! alas!’ said he, ‘what a clever wife I

3	

3	3	have! I sent you to make the house fast, and you take the door away, so

3	

3	3	that everybody may go in and out as they please--however, as you have

3	

3	3	brought the door, you shall carry it about with you for your pains.’

3	

3	3	‘Very well,’ answered she, ‘I’ll carry the door; but I’ll not carry the

3	

3	3	nuts and vinegar bottle also--that would be too much of a load; so if

3	

3	3	you please, I’ll fasten them to the door.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Frederick of course made no objection to that plan, and they set off

3	

3	3	into the wood to look for the thieves; but they could not find them: and

3	

3	3	when it grew dark, they climbed up into a tree to spend the night there.

3	

3	3	Scarcely were they up, than who should come by but the very rogues they

3	

3	3	were looking for. They were in truth great rascals, and belonged to that

3	

3	3	class of people who find things before they are lost; they were tired;

3	

3	3	so they sat down and made a fire under the very tree where Frederick and

3	

3	3	Catherine were. Frederick slipped down on the other side, and picked up

3	

3	3	some stones. Then he climbed up again, and tried to hit the thieves on

3	

3	3	the head with them: but they only said, ‘It must be near morning, for

3	

3	3	the wind shakes the fir-apples down.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Catherine, who had the door on her shoulder, began to be very tired;

3	

3	3	but she thought it was the nuts upon it that were so heavy: so she said

3	

3	3	softly, ‘Frederick, I must let the nuts go.’ ‘No,’ answered he, ‘not

3	

3	3	now, they will discover us.’ ‘I can’t help that: they must go.’ ‘Well,

3	

3	3	then, make haste and throw them down, if you will.’ Then away rattled

3	

3	3	the nuts down among the boughs and one of the thieves cried, ‘Bless me,

3	

3	3	it is hailing.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	A little while after, Catherine thought the door was still very heavy:

3	

3	3	so she whispered to Frederick, ‘I must throw the vinegar down.’ ‘Pray

3	

3	3	don’t,’ answered he, ‘it will discover us.’ ‘I can’t help that,’ said

3	

3	3	she, ‘go it must.’ So she poured all the vinegar down; and the thieves

3	

3	3	said, ‘What a heavy dew there is!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	At last it popped into Catherine’s head that it was the door itself that

3	

3	3	was so heavy all the time: so she whispered, ‘Frederick, I must throw

3	

3	3	the door down soon.’ But he begged and prayed her not to do so, for he

3	

3	3	was sure it would betray them. ‘Here goes, however,’ said she: and down

3	

3	3	went the door with such a clatter upon the thieves, that they cried

3	

3	3	out ‘Murder!’ and not knowing what was coming, ran away as fast as they

3	

3	3	could, and left all the gold. So when Frederick and Catherine came down,

3	

3	3	there they found all their money safe and sound.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	SWEETHEART ROLAND

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once upon a time a woman who was a real witch and had two

3	

3	3	daughters, one ugly and wicked, and this one she loved because she was

3	

3	3	her own daughter, and one beautiful and good, and this one she hated,

3	

3	3	because she was her stepdaughter. The stepdaughter once had a pretty

3	

3	3	apron, which the other fancied so much that she became envious, and

3	

3	3	told her mother that she must and would have that apron. ‘Be quiet, my

3	

3	3	child,’ said the old woman, ‘and you shall have it. Your stepsister has

3	

3	3	long deserved death; tonight when she is asleep I will come and cut her

3	

3	3	head off. Only be careful that you are at the far side of the bed, and

3	

3	3	push her well to the front.’ It would have been all over with the poor

3	

3	3	girl if she had not just then been standing in a corner, and heard

3	

3	3	everything. All day long she dared not go out of doors, and when bedtime

3	

3	3	had come, the witch’s daughter got into bed first, so as to lie at the

3	

3	3	far side, but when she was asleep, the other pushed her gently to the

3	

3	3	front, and took for herself the place at the back, close by the wall. In

3	

3	3	the night, the old woman came creeping in, she held an axe in her right

3	

3	3	hand, and felt with her left to see if anyone were lying at the outside,

3	

3	3	and then she grasped the axe with both hands, and cut her own child’s

3	

3	3	head off.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When she had gone away, the girl got up and went to her sweetheart, who

3	

3	3	was called Roland, and knocked at his door. When he came out, she said

3	

3	3	to him: ‘Listen, dearest Roland, we must fly in all haste; my stepmother

3	

3	3	wanted to kill me, but has struck her own child. When daylight comes,

3	

3	3	and she sees what she has done, we shall be lost.’ ‘But,’ said Roland,

3	

3	3	‘I counsel you first to take away her magic wand, or we cannot escape

3	

3	3	if she pursues us.’ The maiden fetched the magic wand, and she took the

3	

3	3	dead girl’s head and dropped three drops of blood on the ground, one in

3	

3	3	front of the bed, one in the kitchen, and one on the stairs. Then she

3	

3	3	hurried away with her lover.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the old witch got up next morning, she called her daughter, and

3	

3	3	wanted to give her the apron, but she did not come. Then the witch

3	

3	3	cried: ‘Where are you?’ ‘Here, on the stairs, I am sweeping,’ answered

3	

3	3	the first drop of blood. The old woman went out, but saw no one on the

3	

3	3	stairs, and cried again: ‘Where are you?’ ‘Here in the kitchen, I am

3	

3	3	warming myself,’ cried the second drop of blood. She went into the

3	

3	3	kitchen, but found no one. Then she cried again: ‘Where are you?’ ‘Ah,

3	

3	3	here in the bed, I am sleeping,’ cried the third drop of blood. She went

3	

3	3	into the room to the bed. What did she see there? Her own child,

3	

3	3	whose head she had cut off, bathed in her blood. The witch fell into

3	

3	3	a passion, sprang to the window, and as she could look forth quite far

3	

3	3	into the world, she perceived her stepdaughter hurrying away with her

3	

3	3	sweetheart Roland. ‘That shall not help you,’ cried she, ‘even if you

3	

3	3	have got a long way off, you shall still not escape me.’ She put on her

3	

3	3	many-league boots, in which she covered an hour’s walk at every step,

3	

3	3	and it was not long before she overtook them. The girl, however, when

3	

3	3	she saw the old woman striding towards her, changed, with her magic

3	

3	3	wand, her sweetheart Roland into a lake, and herself into a duck

3	

3	3	swimming in the middle of it. The witch placed herself on the shore,

3	

3	3	threw breadcrumbs in, and went to endless trouble to entice the duck;

3	

3	3	but the duck did not let herself be enticed, and the old woman had to

3	

3	3	go home at night as she had come. At this the girl and her sweetheart

3	

3	3	Roland resumed their natural shapes again, and they walked on the whole

3	

3	3	night until daybreak. Then the maiden changed herself into a beautiful

3	

3	3	flower which stood in the midst of a briar hedge, and her sweetheart

3	

3	3	Roland into a fiddler. It was not long before the witch came striding up

3	

3	3	towards them, and said to the musician: ‘Dear musician, may I pluck that

3	

3	3	beautiful flower for myself?’ ‘Oh, yes,’ he replied, ‘I will play to

3	

3	3	you while you do it.’ As she was hastily creeping into the hedge and was

3	

3	3	just going to pluck the flower, knowing perfectly well who the flower

3	

3	3	was, he began to play, and whether she would or not, she was forced

3	

3	3	to dance, for it was a magical dance. The faster he played, the more

3	

3	3	violent springs was she forced to make, and the thorns tore her clothes

3	

3	3	from her body, and pricked her and wounded her till she bled, and as he

3	

3	3	did not stop, she had to dance till she lay dead on the ground.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	As they were now set free, Roland said: ‘Now I will go to my father and

3	

3	3	arrange for the wedding.’ ‘Then in the meantime I will stay here and

3	

3	3	wait for you,’ said the girl, ‘and that no one may recognize me, I will

3	

3	3	change myself into a red stone landmark.’ Then Roland went away, and the

3	

3	3	girl stood like a red landmark in the field and waited for her beloved.

3	

3	3	But when Roland got home, he fell into the snares of another, who so

3	

3	3	fascinated him that he forgot the maiden. The poor girl remained there a

3	

3	3	long time, but at length, as he did not return at all, she was sad, and

3	

3	3	changed herself into a flower, and thought: ‘Someone will surely come

3	

3	3	this way, and trample me down.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	It befell, however, that a shepherd kept his sheep in the field and saw

3	

3	3	the flower, and as it was so pretty, plucked it, took it with him, and

3	

3	3	laid it away in his chest. From that time forth, strange things happened

3	

3	3	in the shepherd’s house. When he arose in the morning, all the work was

3	

3	3	already done, the room was swept, the table and benches cleaned, the

3	

3	3	fire in the hearth was lighted, and the water was fetched, and at noon,

3	

3	3	when he came home, the table was laid, and a good dinner served. He

3	

3	3	could not conceive how this came to pass, for he never saw a human being

3	

3	3	in his house, and no one could have concealed himself in it. He was

3	

3	3	certainly pleased with this good attendance, but still at last he was so

3	

3	3	afraid that he went to a wise woman and asked for her advice. The wise

3	

3	3	woman said: ‘There is some enchantment behind it, listen very early some

3	

3	3	morning if anything is moving in the room, and if you see anything, no

3	

3	3	matter what it is, throw a white cloth over it, and then the magic will

3	

3	3	be stopped.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The shepherd did as she bade him, and next morning just as day dawned,

3	

3	3	he saw the chest open, and the flower come out. Swiftly he

3	

3	3	sprang towards it, and threw a white cloth over it. Instantly the

3	

3	3	transformation came to an end, and a beautiful girl stood before him,

3	

3	3	who admitted to him that she had been the flower, and that up to this

3	

3	3	time she had attended to his house-keeping. She told him her story,

3	

3	3	and as she pleased him he asked her if she would marry him, but she

3	

3	3	answered: ‘No,’ for she wanted to remain faithful to her sweetheart

3	

3	3	Roland, although he had deserted her. Nevertheless, she promised not to

3	

3	3	go away, but to continue keeping house for the shepherd.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And now the time drew near when Roland’s wedding was to be celebrated,

3	

3	3	and then, according to an old custom in the country, it was announced

3	

3	3	that all the girls were to be present at it, and sing in honour of the

3	

3	3	bridal pair. When the faithful maiden heard of this, she grew so sad

3	

3	3	that she thought her heart would break, and she would not go thither,

3	

3	3	but the other girls came and took her. When it came to her turn to sing,

3	

3	3	she stepped back, until at last she was the only one left, and then she

3	

3	3	could not refuse. But when she began her song, and it reached Roland’s

3	

3	3	ears, he sprang up and cried: ‘I know the voice, that is the true

3	

3	3	bride, I will have no other!’ Everything he had forgotten, and which had

3	

3	3	vanished from his mind, had suddenly come home again to his heart. Then

3	

3	3	the faithful maiden held her wedding with her sweetheart Roland, and

3	

3	3	grief came to an end and joy began.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	SNOWDROP

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	It was the middle of winter, when the broad flakes of snow were falling

3	

3	3	around, that the queen of a country many thousand miles off sat working

3	

3	3	at her window. The frame of the window was made of fine black ebony, and

3	

3	3	as she sat looking out upon the snow, she pricked her finger, and three

3	

3	3	drops of blood fell upon it. Then she gazed thoughtfully upon the red

3	

3	3	drops that sprinkled the white snow, and said, ‘Would that my little

3	

3	3	daughter may be as white as that snow, as red as that blood, and as

3	

3	3	black as this ebony windowframe!’ And so the little girl really did grow

3	

3	3	up; her skin was as white as snow, her cheeks as rosy as the blood, and

3	

3	3	her hair as black as ebony; and she was called Snowdrop.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But this queen died; and the king soon married another wife, who became

3	

3	3	queen, and was very beautiful, but so vain that she could not bear

3	

3	3	to think that anyone could be handsomer than she was. She had a fairy

3	

3	3	looking-glass, to which she used to go, and then she would gaze upon

3	

3	3	herself in it, and say:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Tell me, glass, tell me true!

3	

3	3	  Of all the ladies in the land,

3	

3	3	  Who is fairest, tell me, who?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And the glass had always answered:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Thou, queen, art the fairest in all the land.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But Snowdrop grew more and more beautiful; and when she was seven years

3	

3	3	old she was as bright as the day, and fairer than the queen herself.

3	

3	3	Then the glass one day answered the queen, when she went to look in it

3	

3	3	as usual:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Thou, queen, art fair, and beauteous to see,

3	

3	3	  But Snowdrop is lovelier far than thee!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When she heard this she turned pale with rage and envy, and called to

3	

3	3	one of her servants, and said, ‘Take Snowdrop away into the wide wood,

3	

3	3	that I may never see her any more.’ Then the servant led her away; but

3	

3	3	his heart melted when Snowdrop begged him to spare her life, and he

3	

3	3	said, ‘I will not hurt you, thou pretty child.’ So he left her by

3	

3	3	herself; and though he thought it most likely that the wild beasts would

3	

3	3	tear her in pieces, he felt as if a great weight were taken off his

3	

3	3	heart when he had made up his mind not to kill her but to leave her to

3	

3	3	her fate, with the chance of someone finding and saving her.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then poor Snowdrop wandered along through the wood in great fear; and

3	

3	3	the wild beasts roared about her, but none did her any harm. In the

3	

3	3	evening she came to a cottage among the hills, and went in to rest, for

3	

3	3	her little feet would carry her no further. Everything was spruce and

3	

3	3	neat in the cottage: on the table was spread a white cloth, and there

3	

3	3	were seven little plates, seven little loaves, and seven little glasses

3	

3	3	with wine in them; and seven knives and forks laid in order; and by

3	

3	3	the wall stood seven little beds. As she was very hungry, she picked

3	

3	3	a little piece of each loaf and drank a very little wine out of each

3	

3	3	glass; and after that she thought she would lie down and rest. So she

3	

3	3	tried all the little beds; but one was too long, and another was too

3	

3	3	short, till at last the seventh suited her: and there she laid herself

3	

3	3	down and went to sleep.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	By and by in came the masters of the cottage. Now they were seven little

3	

3	3	dwarfs, that lived among the mountains, and dug and searched for gold.

3	

3	3	They lighted up their seven lamps, and saw at once that all was not

3	

3	3	right. The first said, ‘Who has been sitting on my stool?’ The second,

3	

3	3	‘Who has been eating off my plate?’ The third, ‘Who has been picking my

3	

3	3	bread?’ The fourth, ‘Who has been meddling with my spoon?’ The fifth,

3	

3	3	‘Who has been handling my fork?’ The sixth, ‘Who has been cutting with

3	

3	3	my knife?’ The seventh, ‘Who has been drinking my wine?’ Then the first

3	

3	3	looked round and said, ‘Who has been lying on my bed?’ And the rest came

3	

3	3	running to him, and everyone cried out that somebody had been upon his

3	

3	3	bed. But the seventh saw Snowdrop, and called all his brethren to come

3	

3	3	and see her; and they cried out with wonder and astonishment and brought

3	

3	3	their lamps to look at her, and said, ‘Good heavens! what a lovely child

3	

3	3	she is!’ And they were very glad to see her, and took care not to wake

3	

3	3	her; and the seventh dwarf slept an hour with each of the other dwarfs

3	

3	3	in turn, till the night was gone.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	In the morning Snowdrop told them all her story; and they pitied her,

3	

3	3	and said if she would keep all things in order, and cook and wash and

3	

3	3	knit and spin for them, she might stay where she was, and they would

3	

3	3	take good care of her. Then they went out all day long to their work,

3	

3	3	seeking for gold and silver in the mountains: but Snowdrop was left at

3	

3	3	home; and they warned her, and said, ‘The queen will soon find out where

3	

3	3	you are, so take care and let no one in.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But the queen, now that she thought Snowdrop was dead, believed that she

3	

3	3	must be the handsomest lady in the land; and she went to her glass and

3	

3	3	said:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Tell me, glass, tell me true!

3	

3	3	  Of all the ladies in the land,

3	

3	3	  Who is fairest, tell me, who?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And the glass answered:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Thou, queen, art the fairest in all this land:

3	

3	3	  But over the hills, in the greenwood shade,

3	

3	3	  Where the seven dwarfs their dwelling have made,

3	

3	3	  There Snowdrop is hiding her head; and she

3	

3	3	  Is lovelier far, O queen! than thee.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the queen was very much frightened; for she knew that the glass

3	

3	3	always spoke the truth, and was sure that the servant had betrayed her.

3	

3	3	And she could not bear to think that anyone lived who was more beautiful

3	

3	3	than she was; so she dressed herself up as an old pedlar, and went

3	

3	3	her way over the hills, to the place where the dwarfs dwelt. Then she

3	

3	3	knocked at the door, and cried, ‘Fine wares to sell!’ Snowdrop looked

3	

3	3	out at the window, and said, ‘Good day, good woman! what have you to

3	

3	3	sell?’ ‘Good wares, fine wares,’ said she; ‘laces and bobbins of all

3	

3	3	colours.’ ‘I will let the old lady in; she seems to be a very good

3	

3	3	sort of body,’ thought Snowdrop, as she ran down and unbolted the door.

3	

3	3	‘Bless me!’ said the old woman, ‘how badly your stays are laced! Let me

3	

3	3	lace them up with one of my nice new laces.’ Snowdrop did not dream of

3	

3	3	any mischief; so she stood before the old woman; but she set to work

3	

3	3	so nimbly, and pulled the lace so tight, that Snowdrop’s breath was

3	

3	3	stopped, and she fell down as if she were dead. ‘There’s an end to all

3	

3	3	thy beauty,’ said the spiteful queen, and went away home.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	In the evening the seven dwarfs came home; and I need not say how

3	

3	3	grieved they were to see their faithful Snowdrop stretched out upon the

3	

3	3	ground, as if she was quite dead. However, they lifted her up, and when

3	

3	3	they found what ailed her, they cut the lace; and in a little time she

3	

3	3	began to breathe, and very soon came to life again. Then they said, ‘The

3	

3	3	old woman was the queen herself; take care another time, and let no one

3	

3	3	in when we are away.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the queen got home, she went straight to her glass, and spoke to it

3	

3	3	as before; but to her great grief it still said:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Thou, queen, art the fairest in all this land:

3	

3	3	  But over the hills, in the greenwood shade,

3	

3	3	  Where the seven dwarfs their dwelling have made,

3	

3	3	  There Snowdrop is hiding her head; and she

3	

3	3	  Is lovelier far, O queen! than thee.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the blood ran cold in her heart with spite and malice, to see that

3	

3	3	Snowdrop still lived; and she dressed herself up again, but in quite

3	

3	3	another dress from the one she wore before, and took with her a poisoned

3	

3	3	comb. When she reached the dwarfs’ cottage, she knocked at the door, and

3	

3	3	cried, ‘Fine wares to sell!’ But Snowdrop said, ‘I dare not let anyone

3	

3	3	in.’ Then the queen said, ‘Only look at my beautiful combs!’ and gave

3	

3	3	her the poisoned one. And it looked so pretty, that she took it up and

3	

3	3	put it into her hair to try it; but the moment it touched her head,

3	

3	3	the poison was so powerful that she fell down senseless. ‘There you may

3	

3	3	lie,’ said the queen, and went her way. But by good luck the dwarfs

3	

3	3	came in very early that evening; and when they saw Snowdrop lying on

3	

3	3	the ground, they thought what had happened, and soon found the poisoned

3	

3	3	comb. And when they took it away she got well, and told them all that

3	

3	3	had passed; and they warned her once more not to open the door to

3	

3	3	anyone.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Meantime the queen went home to her glass, and shook with rage when she

3	

3	3	read the very same answer as before; and she said, ‘Snowdrop shall die,

3	

3	3	if it cost me my life.’ So she went by herself into her chamber, and got

3	

3	3	ready a poisoned apple: the outside looked very rosy and tempting, but

3	

3	3	whoever tasted it was sure to die. Then she dressed herself up as a

3	

3	3	peasant’s wife, and travelled over the hills to the dwarfs’ cottage,

3	

3	3	and knocked at the door; but Snowdrop put her head out of the window and

3	

3	3	said, ‘I dare not let anyone in, for the dwarfs have told me not.’ ‘Do

3	

3	3	as you please,’ said the old woman, ‘but at any rate take this pretty

3	

3	3	apple; I will give it you.’ ‘No,’ said Snowdrop, ‘I dare not take it.’

3	

3	3	‘You silly girl!’ answered the other, ‘what are you afraid of? Do you

3	

3	3	think it is poisoned? Come! do you eat one part, and I will eat the

3	

3	3	other.’ Now the apple was so made up that one side was good, though the

3	

3	3	other side was poisoned. Then Snowdrop was much tempted to taste, for

3	

3	3	the apple looked so very nice; and when she saw the old woman eat, she

3	

3	3	could wait no longer. But she had scarcely put the piece into her mouth,

3	

3	3	when she fell down dead upon the ground. ‘This time nothing will save

3	

3	3	thee,’ said the queen; and she went home to her glass, and at last it

3	

3	3	said:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Thou, queen, art the fairest of all the fair.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And then her wicked heart was glad, and as happy as such a heart could

3	

3	3	be.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When evening came, and the dwarfs had gone home, they found Snowdrop

3	

3	3	lying on the ground: no breath came from her lips, and they were afraid

3	

3	3	that she was quite dead. They lifted her up, and combed her hair, and

3	

3	3	washed her face with wine and water; but all was in vain, for the little

3	

3	3	girl seemed quite dead. So they laid her down upon a bier, and all seven

3	

3	3	watched and bewailed her three whole days; and then they thought they

3	

3	3	would bury her: but her cheeks were still rosy; and her face looked just

3	

3	3	as it did while she was alive; so they said, ‘We will never bury her in

3	

3	3	the cold ground.’ And they made a coffin of glass, so that they might

3	

3	3	still look at her, and wrote upon it in golden letters what her name

3	

3	3	was, and that she was a king’s daughter. And the coffin was set among

3	

3	3	the hills, and one of the dwarfs always sat by it and watched. And the

3	

3	3	birds of the air came too, and bemoaned Snowdrop; and first of all came

3	

3	3	an owl, and then a raven, and at last a dove, and sat by her side.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And thus Snowdrop lay for a long, long time, and still only looked as

3	

3	3	though she was asleep; for she was even now as white as snow, and as red

3	

3	3	as blood, and as black as ebony. At last a prince came and called at the

3	

3	3	dwarfs’ house; and he saw Snowdrop, and read what was written in golden

3	

3	3	letters. Then he offered the dwarfs money, and prayed and besought them

3	

3	3	to let him take her away; but they said, ‘We will not part with her for

3	

3	3	all the gold in the world.’ At last, however, they had pity on him, and

3	

3	3	gave him the coffin; but the moment he lifted it up to carry it home

3	

3	3	with him, the piece of apple fell from between her lips, and Snowdrop

3	

3	3	awoke, and said, ‘Where am I?’ And the prince said, ‘Thou art quite safe

3	

3	3	with me.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then he told her all that had happened, and said, ‘I love you far better

3	

3	3	than all the world; so come with me to my father’s palace, and you shall

3	

3	3	be my wife.’ And Snowdrop consented, and went home with the prince;

3	

3	3	and everything was got ready with great pomp and splendour for their

3	

3	3	wedding.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	To the feast was asked, among the rest, Snowdrop’s old enemy the queen;

3	

3	3	and as she was dressing herself in fine rich clothes, she looked in the

3	

3	3	glass and said:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Tell me, glass, tell me true!

3	

3	3	  Of all the ladies in the land,

3	

3	3	  Who is fairest, tell me, who?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And the glass answered:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Thou, lady, art loveliest here, I ween;

3	

3	3	  But lovelier far is the new-made queen.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When she heard this she started with rage; but her envy and curiosity

3	

3	3	were so great, that she could not help setting out to see the bride. And

3	

3	3	when she got there, and saw that it was no other than Snowdrop, who, as

3	

3	3	she thought, had been dead a long while, she choked with rage, and fell

3	

3	3	down and died: but Snowdrop and the prince lived and reigned happily

3	

3	3	over that land many, many years; and sometimes they went up into the

3	

3	3	mountains, and paid a visit to the little dwarfs, who had been so kind

3	

3	3	to Snowdrop in her time of need.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE PINK

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once upon a time a queen to whom God had given no children.

3	

3	3	Every morning she went into the garden and prayed to God in heaven to

3	

3	3	bestow on her a son or a daughter. Then an angel from heaven came to her

3	

3	3	and said: ‘Be at rest, you shall have a son with the power of wishing,

3	

3	3	so that whatsoever in the world he wishes for, that shall he have.’ Then

3	

3	3	she went to the king, and told him the joyful tidings, and when the time

3	

3	3	was come she gave birth to a son, and the king was filled with gladness.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Every morning she went with the child to the garden where the wild

3	

3	3	beasts were kept, and washed herself there in a clear stream. It

3	

3	3	happened once when the child was a little older, that it was lying in

3	

3	3	her arms and she fell asleep. Then came the old cook, who knew that the

3	

3	3	child had the power of wishing, and stole it away, and he took a hen,

3	

3	3	and cut it in pieces, and dropped some of its blood on the queen’s apron

3	

3	3	and on her dress. Then he carried the child away to a secret place,

3	

3	3	where a nurse was obliged to suckle it, and he ran to the king and

3	

3	3	accused the queen of having allowed her child to be taken from her by

3	

3	3	the wild beasts. When the king saw the blood on her apron, he believed

3	

3	3	this, fell into such a passion that he ordered a high tower to be built,

3	

3	3	in which neither sun nor moon could be seen and had his wife put into

3	

3	3	it, and walled up. Here she was to stay for seven years without meat

3	

3	3	or drink, and die of hunger. But God sent two angels from heaven in the

3	

3	3	shape of white doves, which flew to her twice a day, and carried her

3	

3	3	food until the seven years were over.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The cook, however, thought to himself: ‘If the child has the power of

3	

3	3	wishing, and I am here, he might very easily get me into trouble.’ So

3	

3	3	he left the palace and went to the boy, who was already big enough to

3	

3	3	speak, and said to him: ‘Wish for a beautiful palace for yourself with

3	

3	3	a garden, and all else that pertains to it.’ Scarcely were the words out

3	

3	3	of the boy’s mouth, when everything was there that he had wished for.

3	

3	3	After a while the cook said to him: ‘It is not well for you to be so

3	

3	3	alone, wish for a pretty girl as a companion.’ Then the king’s son

3	

3	3	wished for one, and she immediately stood before him, and was more

3	

3	3	beautiful than any painter could have painted her. The two played

3	

3	3	together, and loved each other with all their hearts, and the old cook

3	

3	3	went out hunting like a nobleman. The thought occurred to him, however,

3	

3	3	that the king’s son might some day wish to be with his father, and thus

3	

3	3	bring him into great peril. So he went out and took the maiden aside,

3	

3	3	and said: ‘Tonight when the boy is asleep, go to his bed and plunge this

3	

3	3	knife into his heart, and bring me his heart and tongue, and if you do

3	

3	3	not do it, you shall lose your life.’ Thereupon he went away, and when

3	

3	3	he returned next day she had not done it, and said: ‘Why should I shed

3	

3	3	the blood of an innocent boy who has never harmed anyone?’ The cook once

3	

3	3	more said: ‘If you do not do it, it shall cost you your own life.’ When

3	

3	3	he had gone away, she had a little hind brought to her, and ordered her

3	

3	3	to be killed, and took her heart and tongue, and laid them on a plate,

3	

3	3	and when she saw the old man coming, she said to the boy: ‘Lie down in

3	

3	3	your bed, and draw the clothes over you.’ Then the wicked wretch came in

3	

3	3	and said: ‘Where are the boy’s heart and tongue?’ The girl reached the

3	

3	3	plate to him, but the king’s son threw off the quilt, and said: ‘You old

3	

3	3	sinner, why did you want to kill me? Now will I pronounce thy sentence.

3	

3	3	You shall become a black poodle and have a gold collar round your neck,

3	

3	3	and shall eat burning coals, till the flames burst forth from your

3	

3	3	throat.’ And when he had spoken these words, the old man was changed

3	

3	3	into a poodle dog, and had a gold collar round his neck, and the cooks

3	

3	3	were ordered to bring up some live coals, and these he ate, until the

3	

3	3	flames broke forth from his throat. The king’s son remained there a

3	

3	3	short while longer, and he thought of his mother, and wondered if she

3	

3	3	were still alive. At length he said to the maiden: ‘I will go home to my

3	

3	3	own country; if you will go with me, I will provide for you.’ ‘Ah,’

3	

3	3	she replied, ‘the way is so long, and what shall I do in a strange land

3	

3	3	where I am unknown?’ As she did not seem quite willing, and as they

3	

3	3	could not be parted from each other, he wished that she might be changed

3	

3	3	into a beautiful pink, and took her with him. Then he went away to his

3	

3	3	own country, and the poodle had to run after him. He went to the tower

3	

3	3	in which his mother was confined, and as it was so high, he wished for

3	

3	3	a ladder which would reach up to the very top. Then he mounted up and

3	

3	3	looked inside, and cried: ‘Beloved mother, Lady Queen, are you still

3	

3	3	alive, or are you dead?’ She answered: ‘I have just eaten, and am still

3	

3	3	satisfied,’ for she thought the angels were there. Said he: ‘I am your

3	

3	3	dear son, whom the wild beasts were said to have torn from your arms;

3	

3	3	but I am alive still, and will soon set you free.’ Then he descended

3	

3	3	again, and went to his father, and caused himself to be announced as a

3	

3	3	strange huntsman, and asked if he could offer him service. The king said

3	

3	3	yes, if he was skilful and could get game for him, he should come to

3	

3	3	him, but that deer had never taken up their quarters in any part of the

3	

3	3	district or country. Then the huntsman promised to procure as much game

3	

3	3	for him as he could possibly use at the royal table. So he summoned all

3	

3	3	the huntsmen together, and bade them go out into the forest with him.

3	

3	3	And he went with them and made them form a great circle, open at one end

3	

3	3	where he stationed himself, and began to wish. Two hundred deer and more

3	

3	3	came running inside the circle at once, and the huntsmen shot them.

3	

3	3	Then they were all placed on sixty country carts, and driven home to the

3	

3	3	king, and for once he was able to deck his table with game, after having

3	

3	3	had none at all for years.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Now the king felt great joy at this, and commanded that his entire

3	

3	3	household should eat with him next day, and made a great feast. When

3	

3	3	they were all assembled together, he said to the huntsman: ‘As you are

3	

3	3	so clever, you shall sit by me.’ He replied: ‘Lord King, your majesty

3	

3	3	must excuse me, I am a poor huntsman.’ But the king insisted on it,

3	

3	3	and said: ‘You shall sit by me,’ until he did it. Whilst he was sitting

3	

3	3	there, he thought of his dearest mother, and wished that one of the

3	

3	3	king’s principal servants would begin to speak of her, and would ask how

3	

3	3	it was faring with the queen in the tower, and if she were alive still,

3	

3	3	or had perished. Hardly had he formed the wish than the marshal began,

3	

3	3	and said: ‘Your majesty, we live joyously here, but how is the queen

3	

3	3	living in the tower? Is she still alive, or has she died?’ But the king

3	

3	3	replied: ‘She let my dear son be torn to pieces by wild beasts; I will

3	

3	3	not have her named.’ Then the huntsman arose and said: ‘Gracious lord

3	

3	3	father she is alive still, and I am her son, and I was not carried away

3	

3	3	by wild beasts, but by that wretch the old cook, who tore me from her

3	

3	3	arms when she was asleep, and sprinkled her apron with the blood of a

3	

3	3	chicken.’ Thereupon he took the dog with the golden collar, and said:

3	

3	3	‘That is the wretch!’ and caused live coals to be brought, and these the

3	

3	3	dog was compelled to devour before the sight of all, until flames burst

3	

3	3	forth from its throat. On this the huntsman asked the king if he would

3	

3	3	like to see the dog in his true shape, and wished him back into the form

3	

3	3	of the cook, in the which he stood immediately, with his white apron,

3	

3	3	and his knife by his side. When the king saw him he fell into a passion,

3	

3	3	and ordered him to be cast into the deepest dungeon. Then the huntsman

3	

3	3	spoke further and said: ‘Father, will you see the maiden who brought me

3	

3	3	up so tenderly and who was afterwards to murder me, but did not do it,

3	

3	3	though her own life depended on it?’ The king replied: ‘Yes, I would

3	

3	3	like to see her.’ The son said: ‘Most gracious father, I will show her

3	

3	3	to you in the form of a beautiful flower,’ and he thrust his hand into

3	

3	3	his pocket and brought forth the pink, and placed it on the royal table,

3	

3	3	and it was so beautiful that the king had never seen one to equal it.

3	

3	3	Then the son said: ‘Now will I show her to you in her own form,’ and

3	

3	3	wished that she might become a maiden, and she stood there looking so

3	

3	3	beautiful that no painter could have made her look more so.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And the king sent two waiting-maids and two attendants into the tower,

3	

3	3	to fetch the queen and bring her to the royal table. But when she was

3	

3	3	led in she ate nothing, and said: ‘The gracious and merciful God who has

3	

3	3	supported me in the tower, will soon set me free.’ She lived three days

3	

3	3	more, and then died happily, and when she was buried, the two white

3	

3	3	doves which had brought her food to the tower, and were angels of

3	

3	3	heaven, followed her body and seated themselves on her grave. The aged

3	

3	3	king ordered the cook to be torn in four pieces, but grief consumed the

3	

3	3	king’s own heart, and he soon died. His son married the beautiful maiden

3	

3	3	whom he had brought with him as a flower in his pocket, and whether they

3	

3	3	are still alive or not, is known to God.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	CLEVER ELSIE

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once a man who had a daughter who was called Clever Elsie. And

3	

3	3	when she had grown up her father said: ‘We will get her married.’ ‘Yes,’

3	

3	3	said the mother, ‘if only someone would come who would have her.’ At

3	

3	3	length a man came from a distance and wooed her, who was called Hans;

3	

3	3	but he stipulated that Clever Elsie should be really smart. ‘Oh,’ said

3	

3	3	the father, ‘she has plenty of good sense’; and the mother said: ‘Oh,

3	

3	3	she can see the wind coming up the street, and hear the flies coughing.’

3	

3	3	‘Well,’ said Hans, ‘if she is not really smart, I won’t have her.’ When

3	

3	3	they were sitting at dinner and had eaten, the mother said: ‘Elsie, go

3	

3	3	into the cellar and fetch some beer.’ Then Clever Elsie took the pitcher

3	

3	3	from the wall, went into the cellar, and tapped the lid briskly as she

3	

3	3	went, so that the time might not appear long. When she was below she

3	

3	3	fetched herself a chair, and set it before the barrel so that she had

3	

3	3	no need to stoop, and did not hurt her back or do herself any unexpected

3	

3	3	injury. Then she placed the can before her, and turned the tap, and

3	

3	3	while the beer was running she would not let her eyes be idle, but

3	

3	3	looked up at the wall, and after much peering here and there, saw a

3	

3	3	pick-axe exactly above her, which the masons had accidentally left

3	

3	3	there.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then Clever Elsie began to weep and said: ‘If I get Hans, and we have

3	

3	3	a child, and he grows big, and we send him into the cellar here to draw

3	

3	3	beer, then the pick-axe will fall on his head and kill him.’ Then she

3	

3	3	sat and wept and screamed with all the strength of her body, over the

3	

3	3	misfortune which lay before her. Those upstairs waited for the drink,

3	

3	3	but Clever Elsie still did not come. Then the woman said to the servant:

3	

3	3	‘Just go down into the cellar and see where Elsie is.’ The maid went and

3	

3	3	found her sitting in front of the barrel, screaming loudly. ‘Elsie why

3	

3	3	do you weep?’ asked the maid. ‘Ah,’ she answered, ‘have I not reason to

3	

3	3	weep? If I get Hans, and we have a child, and he grows big, and has to

3	

3	3	draw beer here, the pick-axe will perhaps fall on his head, and kill

3	

3	3	him.’ Then said the maid: ‘What a clever Elsie we have!’ and sat down

3	

3	3	beside her and began loudly to weep over the misfortune. After a while,

3	

3	3	as the maid did not come back, and those upstairs were thirsty for the

3	

3	3	beer, the man said to the boy: ‘Just go down into the cellar and see

3	

3	3	where Elsie and the girl are.’ The boy went down, and there sat Clever

3	

3	3	Elsie and the girl both weeping together. Then he asked: ‘Why are you

3	

3	3	weeping?’ ‘Ah,’ said Elsie, ‘have I not reason to weep? If I get Hans,

3	

3	3	and we have a child, and he grows big, and has to draw beer here, the

3	

3	3	pick-axe will fall on his head and kill him.’ Then said the boy: ‘What

3	

3	3	a clever Elsie we have!’ and sat down by her, and likewise began to

3	

3	3	howl loudly. Upstairs they waited for the boy, but as he still did not

3	

3	3	return, the man said to the woman: ‘Just go down into the cellar and see

3	

3	3	where Elsie is!’ The woman went down, and found all three in the midst

3	

3	3	of their lamentations, and inquired what was the cause; then Elsie told

3	

3	3	her also that her future child was to be killed by the pick-axe, when it

3	

3	3	grew big and had to draw beer, and the pick-axe fell down. Then said the

3	

3	3	mother likewise: ‘What a clever Elsie we have!’ and sat down and wept

3	

3	3	with them. The man upstairs waited a short time, but as his wife did not

3	

3	3	come back and his thirst grew ever greater, he said: ‘I must go into the

3	

3	3	cellar myself and see where Elsie is.’ But when he got into the cellar,

3	

3	3	and they were all sitting together crying, and he heard the reason, and

3	

3	3	that Elsie’s child was the cause, and the Elsie might perhaps bring one

3	

3	3	into the world some day, and that he might be killed by the pick-axe, if

3	

3	3	he should happen to be sitting beneath it, drawing beer just at the very

3	

3	3	time when it fell down, he cried: ‘Oh, what a clever Elsie!’ and sat

3	

3	3	down, and likewise wept with them. The bridegroom stayed upstairs alone

3	

3	3	for a long time; then as no one would come back he thought: ‘They must be

3	

3	3	waiting for me below: I too must go there and see what they are about.’

3	

3	3	When he got down, the five of them were sitting screaming and lamenting

3	

3	3	quite piteously, each out-doing the other. ‘What misfortune has happened

3	

3	3	then?’ asked he. ‘Ah, dear Hans,’ said Elsie, ‘if we marry each other

3	

3	3	and have a child, and he is big, and we perhaps send him here to draw

3	

3	3	something to drink, then the pick-axe which has been left up there might

3	

3	3	dash his brains out if it were to fall down, so have we not reason to

3	

3	3	weep?’ ‘Come,’ said Hans, ‘more understanding than that is not needed

3	

3	3	for my household, as you are such a clever Elsie, I will have you,’ and

3	

3	3	seized her hand, took her upstairs with him, and married her.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	After Hans had had her some time, he said: ‘Wife, I am going out to work

3	

3	3	and earn some money for us; go into the field and cut the corn that we

3	

3	3	may have some bread.’ ‘Yes, dear Hans, I will do that.’ After Hans had

3	

3	3	gone away, she cooked herself some good broth and took it into the field

3	

3	3	with her. When she came to the field she said to herself: ‘What shall I

3	

3	3	do; shall I cut first, or shall I eat first? Oh, I will eat first.’ Then

3	

3	3	she drank her cup of broth and when she was fully satisfied, she once

3	

3	3	more said: ‘What shall I do? Shall I cut first, or shall I sleep first?

3	

3	3	I will sleep first.’ Then she lay down among the corn and fell asleep.

3	

3	3	Hans had been at home for a long time, but Elsie did not come; then said

3	

3	3	he: ‘What a clever Elsie I have; she is so industrious that she does not

3	

3	3	even come home to eat.’ But when evening came and she still stayed away,

3	

3	3	Hans went out to see what she had cut, but nothing was cut, and she

3	

3	3	was lying among the corn asleep. Then Hans hastened home and brought

3	

3	3	a fowler’s net with little bells and hung it round about her, and she

3	

3	3	still went on sleeping. Then he ran home, shut the house-door, and sat

3	

3	3	down in his chair and worked. At length, when it was quite dark, Clever

3	

3	3	Elsie awoke and when she got up there was a jingling all round about

3	

3	3	her, and the bells rang at each step which she took. Then she was

3	

3	3	alarmed, and became uncertain whether she really was Clever Elsie or

3	

3	3	not, and said: ‘Is it I, or is it not I?’ But she knew not what answer

3	

3	3	to make to this, and stood for a time in doubt; at length she thought:

3	

3	3	‘I will go home and ask if it be I, or if it be not I, they will be sure

3	

3	3	to know.’ She ran to the door of her own house, but it was shut; then

3	

3	3	she knocked at the window and cried: ‘Hans, is Elsie within?’ ‘Yes,’

3	

3	3	answered Hans, ‘she is within.’ Hereupon she was terrified, and said:

3	

3	3	‘Ah, heavens! Then it is not I,’ and went to another door; but when the

3	

3	3	people heard the jingling of the bells they would not open it, and she

3	

3	3	could get in nowhere. Then she ran out of the village, and no one has

3	

3	3	seen her since.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE MISER IN THE BUSH

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	A farmer had a faithful and diligent servant, who had worked hard for

3	

3	3	him three years, without having been paid any wages. At last it came

3	

3	3	into the man’s head that he would not go on thus without pay any longer;

3	

3	3	so he went to his master, and said, ‘I have worked hard for you a long

3	

3	3	time, I will trust to you to give me what I deserve to have for my

3	

3	3	trouble.’ The farmer was a sad miser, and knew that his man was very

3	

3	3	simple-hearted; so he took out threepence, and gave him for every year’s

3	

3	3	service a penny. The poor fellow thought it was a great deal of money to

3	

3	3	have, and said to himself, ‘Why should I work hard, and live here on bad

3	

3	3	fare any longer? I can now travel into the wide world, and make myself

3	

3	3	merry.’ With that he put his money into his purse, and set out, roaming

3	

3	3	over hill and valley.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	As he jogged along over the fields, singing and dancing, a little dwarf

3	

3	3	met him, and asked him what made him so merry. ‘Why, what should make

3	

3	3	me down-hearted?’ said he; ‘I am sound in health and rich in purse, what

3	

3	3	should I care for? I have saved up my three years’ earnings and have it

3	

3	3	all safe in my pocket.’ ‘How much may it come to?’ said the little man.

3	

3	3	‘Full threepence,’ replied the countryman. ‘I wish you would give them

3	

3	3	to me,’ said the other; ‘I am very poor.’ Then the man pitied him, and

3	

3	3	gave him all he had; and the little dwarf said in return, ‘As you have

3	

3	3	such a kind honest heart, I will grant you three wishes--one for every

3	

3	3	penny; so choose whatever you like.’ Then the countryman rejoiced at

3	

3	3	his good luck, and said, ‘I like many things better than money: first, I

3	

3	3	will have a bow that will bring down everything I shoot at; secondly,

3	

3	3	a fiddle that will set everyone dancing that hears me play upon it; and

3	

3	3	thirdly, I should like that everyone should grant what I ask.’ The dwarf

3	

3	3	said he should have his three wishes; so he gave him the bow and fiddle,

3	

3	3	and went his way.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Our honest friend journeyed on his way too; and if he was merry before,

3	

3	3	he was now ten times more so. He had not gone far before he met an old

3	

3	3	miser: close by them stood a tree, and on the topmost twig sat a thrush

3	

3	3	singing away most joyfully. ‘Oh, what a pretty bird!’ said the miser; ‘I

3	

3	3	would give a great deal of money to have such a one.’ ‘If that’s all,’

3	

3	3	said the countryman, ‘I will soon bring it down.’ Then he took up his

3	

3	3	bow, and down fell the thrush into the bushes at the foot of the tree.

3	

3	3	The miser crept into the bush to find it; but directly he had got into

3	

3	3	the middle, his companion took up his fiddle and played away, and the

3	

3	3	miser began to dance and spring about, capering higher and higher in

3	

3	3	the air. The thorns soon began to tear his clothes till they all hung

3	

3	3	in rags about him, and he himself was all scratched and wounded, so that

3	

3	3	the blood ran down. ‘Oh, for heaven’s sake!’ cried the miser, ‘Master!

3	

3	3	master! pray let the fiddle alone. What have I done to deserve this?’

3	

3	3	‘Thou hast shaved many a poor soul close enough,’ said the other; ‘thou

3	

3	3	art only meeting thy reward’: so he played up another tune. Then the

3	

3	3	miser began to beg and promise, and offered money for his liberty; but

3	

3	3	he did not come up to the musician’s price for some time, and he danced

3	

3	3	him along brisker and brisker, and the miser bid higher and higher, till

3	

3	3	at last he offered a round hundred of florins that he had in his purse,

3	

3	3	and had just gained by cheating some poor fellow. When the countryman

3	

3	3	saw so much money, he said, ‘I will agree to your proposal.’ So he took

3	

3	3	the purse, put up his fiddle, and travelled on very pleased with his

3	

3	3	bargain.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Meanwhile the miser crept out of the bush half-naked and in a piteous

3	

3	3	plight, and began to ponder how he should take his revenge, and serve

3	

3	3	his late companion some trick. At last he went to the judge, and

3	

3	3	complained that a rascal had robbed him of his money, and beaten him

3	

3	3	into the bargain; and that the fellow who did it carried a bow at his

3	

3	3	back and a fiddle hung round his neck. Then the judge sent out his

3	

3	3	officers to bring up the accused wherever they should find him; and he

3	

3	3	was soon caught and brought up to be tried.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The miser began to tell his tale, and said he had been robbed of

3	

3	3	his money. ‘No, you gave it me for playing a tune to you.’ said the

3	

3	3	countryman; but the judge told him that was not likely, and cut the

3	

3	3	matter short by ordering him off to the gallows.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So away he was taken; but as he stood on the steps he said, ‘My Lord

3	

3	3	Judge, grant me one last request.’ ‘Anything but thy life,’ replied the

3	

3	3	other. ‘No,’ said he, ‘I do not ask my life; only to let me play upon

3	

3	3	my fiddle for the last time.’ The miser cried out, ‘Oh, no! no! for

3	

3	3	heaven’s sake don’t listen to him! don’t listen to him!’ But the judge

3	

3	3	said, ‘It is only this once, he will soon have done.’ The fact was, he

3	

3	3	could not refuse the request, on account of the dwarf’s third gift.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the miser said, ‘Bind me fast, bind me fast, for pity’s sake.’ But

3	

3	3	the countryman seized his fiddle, and struck up a tune, and at the first

3	

3	3	note judge, clerks, and jailer were in motion; all began capering, and

3	

3	3	no one could hold the miser. At the second note the hangman let his

3	

3	3	prisoner go, and danced also, and by the time he had played the first

3	

3	3	bar of the tune, all were dancing together--judge, court, and miser, and

3	

3	3	all the people who had followed to look on. At first the thing was merry

3	

3	3	and pleasant enough; but when it had gone on a while, and there seemed

3	

3	3	to be no end of playing or dancing, they began to cry out, and beg him

3	

3	3	to leave off; but he stopped not a whit the more for their entreaties,

3	

3	3	till the judge not only gave him his life, but promised to return him

3	

3	3	the hundred florins.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then he called to the miser, and said, ‘Tell us now, you vagabond, where

3	

3	3	you got that gold, or I shall play on for your amusement only,’ ‘I stole

3	

3	3	it,’ said the miser in the presence of all the people; ‘I acknowledge

3
