3	3	that I stole it, and that you earned it fairly.’ Then the countryman

3	

3	3	stopped his fiddle, and left the miser to take his place at the gallows.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	ASHPUTTEL

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The wife of a rich man fell sick; and when she felt that her end drew

3	

3	3	nigh, she called her only daughter to her bed-side, and said, ‘Always be

3	

3	3	a good girl, and I will look down from heaven and watch over you.’ Soon

3	

3	3	afterwards she shut her eyes and died, and was buried in the garden;

3	

3	3	and the little girl went every day to her grave and wept, and was always

3	

3	3	good and kind to all about her. And the snow fell and spread a beautiful

3	

3	3	white covering over the grave; but by the time the spring came, and the

3	

3	3	sun had melted it away again, her father had married another wife. This

3	

3	3	new wife had two daughters of her own, that she brought home with her;

3	

3	3	they were fair in face but foul at heart, and it was now a sorry time

3	

3	3	for the poor little girl. ‘What does the good-for-nothing want in the

3	

3	3	parlour?’ said they; ‘they who would eat bread should first earn it;

3	

3	3	away with the kitchen-maid!’ Then they took away her fine clothes, and

3	

3	3	gave her an old grey frock to put on, and laughed at her, and turned her

3	

3	3	into the kitchen.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There she was forced to do hard work; to rise early before daylight, to

3	

3	3	bring the water, to make the fire, to cook and to wash. Besides that,

3	

3	3	the sisters plagued her in all sorts of ways, and laughed at her. In the

3	

3	3	evening when she was tired, she had no bed to lie down on, but was made

3	

3	3	to lie by the hearth among the ashes; and as this, of course, made her

3	

3	3	always dusty and dirty, they called her Ashputtel.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	It happened once that the father was going to the fair, and asked his

3	

3	3	wife’s daughters what he should bring them. ‘Fine clothes,’ said the

3	

3	3	first; ‘Pearls and diamonds,’ cried the second. ‘Now, child,’ said he

3	

3	3	to his own daughter, ‘what will you have?’ ‘The first twig, dear

3	

3	3	father, that brushes against your hat when you turn your face to come

3	

3	3	homewards,’ said she. Then he bought for the first two the fine clothes

3	

3	3	and pearls and diamonds they had asked for: and on his way home, as he

3	

3	3	rode through a green copse, a hazel twig brushed against him, and almost

3	

3	3	pushed off his hat: so he broke it off and brought it away; and when he

3	

3	3	got home he gave it to his daughter. Then she took it, and went to

3	

3	3	her mother’s grave and planted it there; and cried so much that it was

3	

3	3	watered with her tears; and there it grew and became a fine tree. Three

3	

3	3	times every day she went to it and cried; and soon a little bird came

3	

3	3	and built its nest upon the tree, and talked with her, and watched over

3	

3	3	her, and brought her whatever she wished for.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Now it happened that the king of that land held a feast, which was to

3	

3	3	last three days; and out of those who came to it his son was to choose

3	

3	3	a bride for himself. Ashputtel’s two sisters were asked to come; so they

3	

3	3	called her up, and said, ‘Now, comb our hair, brush our shoes, and tie

3	

3	3	our sashes for us, for we are going to dance at the king’s feast.’

3	

3	3	Then she did as she was told; but when all was done she could not help

3	

3	3	crying, for she thought to herself, she should so have liked to have

3	

3	3	gone with them to the ball; and at last she begged her mother very hard

3	

3	3	to let her go. ‘You, Ashputtel!’ said she; ‘you who have nothing to

3	

3	3	wear, no clothes at all, and who cannot even dance--you want to go to

3	

3	3	the ball? And when she kept on begging, she said at last, to get rid of

3	

3	3	her, ‘I will throw this dishful of peas into the ash-heap, and if in

3	

3	3	two hours’ time you have picked them all out, you shall go to the feast

3	

3	3	too.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then she threw the peas down among the ashes, but the little maiden ran

3	

3	3	out at the back door into the garden, and cried out:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Hither, hither, through the sky,

3	

3	3	  Turtle-doves and linnets, fly!

3	

3	3	  Blackbird, thrush, and chaffinch gay,

3	

3	3	  Hither, hither, haste away!

3	

3	3	  One and all come help me, quick!

3	

3	3	  Haste ye, haste ye!--pick, pick, pick!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then first came two white doves, flying in at the kitchen window; next

3	

3	3	came two turtle-doves; and after them came all the little birds under

3	

3	3	heaven, chirping and fluttering in: and they flew down into the ashes.

3	

3	3	And the little doves stooped their heads down and set to work, pick,

3	

3	3	pick, pick; and then the others began to pick, pick, pick: and among

3	

3	3	them all they soon picked out all the good grain, and put it into a dish

3	

3	3	but left the ashes. Long before the end of the hour the work was quite

3	

3	3	done, and all flew out again at the windows.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then Ashputtel brought the dish to her mother, overjoyed at the thought

3	

3	3	that now she should go to the ball. But the mother said, ‘No, no! you

3	

3	3	slut, you have no clothes, and cannot dance; you shall not go.’ And when

3	

3	3	Ashputtel begged very hard to go, she said, ‘If you can in one hour’s

3	

3	3	time pick two of those dishes of peas out of the ashes, you shall go

3	

3	3	too.’ And thus she thought she should at least get rid of her. So she

3	

3	3	shook two dishes of peas into the ashes.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But the little maiden went out into the garden at the back of the house,

3	

3	3	and cried out as before:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Hither, hither, through the sky,

3	

3	3	  Turtle-doves and linnets, fly!

3	

3	3	  Blackbird, thrush, and chaffinch gay,

3	

3	3	  Hither, hither, haste away!

3	

3	3	  One and all come help me, quick!

3	

3	3	  Haste ye, haste ye!--pick, pick, pick!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then first came two white doves in at the kitchen window; next came two

3	

3	3	turtle-doves; and after them came all the little birds under heaven,

3	

3	3	chirping and hopping about. And they flew down into the ashes; and the

3	

3	3	little doves put their heads down and set to work, pick, pick, pick; and

3	

3	3	then the others began pick, pick, pick; and they put all the good grain

3	

3	3	into the dishes, and left all the ashes. Before half an hour’s time all

3	

3	3	was done, and out they flew again. And then Ashputtel took the dishes to

3	

3	3	her mother, rejoicing to think that she should now go to the ball.

3	

3	3	But her mother said, ‘It is all of no use, you cannot go; you have no

3	

3	3	clothes, and cannot dance, and you would only put us to shame’: and off

3	

3	3	she went with her two daughters to the ball.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Now when all were gone, and nobody left at home, Ashputtel went

3	

3	3	sorrowfully and sat down under the hazel-tree, and cried out:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Shake, shake, hazel-tree,

3	

3	3	  Gold and silver over me!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then her friend the bird flew out of the tree, and brought a gold and

3	

3	3	silver dress for her, and slippers of spangled silk; and she put them

3	

3	3	on, and followed her sisters to the feast. But they did not know her,

3	

3	3	and thought it must be some strange princess, she looked so fine and

3	

3	3	beautiful in her rich clothes; and they never once thought of Ashputtel,

3	

3	3	taking it for granted that she was safe at home in the dirt.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The king’s son soon came up to her, and took her by the hand and danced

3	

3	3	with her, and no one else: and he never left her hand; but when anyone

3	

3	3	else came to ask her to dance, he said, ‘This lady is dancing with me.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Thus they danced till a late hour of the night; and then she wanted to

3	

3	3	go home: and the king’s son said, ‘I shall go and take care of you to

3	

3	3	your home’; for he wanted to see where the beautiful maiden lived. But

3	

3	3	she slipped away from him, unawares, and ran off towards home; and as

3	

3	3	the prince followed her, she jumped up into the pigeon-house and shut

3	

3	3	the door. Then he waited till her father came home, and told him that

3	

3	3	the unknown maiden, who had been at the feast, had hid herself in the

3	

3	3	pigeon-house. But when they had broken open the door they found no one

3	

3	3	within; and as they came back into the house, Ashputtel was lying, as

3	

3	3	she always did, in her dirty frock by the ashes, and her dim little

3	

3	3	lamp was burning in the chimney. For she had run as quickly as she could

3	

3	3	through the pigeon-house and on to the hazel-tree, and had there taken

3	

3	3	off her beautiful clothes, and put them beneath the tree, that the bird

3	

3	3	might carry them away, and had lain down again amid the ashes in her

3	

3	3	little grey frock.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The next day when the feast was again held, and her father, mother, and

3	

3	3	sisters were gone, Ashputtel went to the hazel-tree, and said:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Shake, shake, hazel-tree,

3	

3	3	  Gold and silver over me!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And the bird came and brought a still finer dress than the one she

3	

3	3	had worn the day before. And when she came in it to the ball, everyone

3	

3	3	wondered at her beauty: but the king’s son, who was waiting for her,

3	

3	3	took her by the hand, and danced with her; and when anyone asked her to

3	

3	3	dance, he said as before, ‘This lady is dancing with me.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When night came she wanted to go home; and the king’s son followed here

3	

3	3	as before, that he might see into what house she went: but she sprang

3	

3	3	away from him all at once into the garden behind her father’s house.

3	

3	3	In this garden stood a fine large pear-tree full of ripe fruit; and

3	

3	3	Ashputtel, not knowing where to hide herself, jumped up into it without

3	

3	3	being seen. Then the king’s son lost sight of her, and could not find

3	

3	3	out where she was gone, but waited till her father came home, and said

3	

3	3	to him, ‘The unknown lady who danced with me has slipped away, and I

3	

3	3	think she must have sprung into the pear-tree.’ The father thought to

3	

3	3	himself, ‘Can it be Ashputtel?’ So he had an axe brought; and they cut

3	

3	3	down the tree, but found no one upon it. And when they came back into

3	

3	3	the kitchen, there lay Ashputtel among the ashes; for she had slipped

3	

3	3	down on the other side of the tree, and carried her beautiful clothes

3	

3	3	back to the bird at the hazel-tree, and then put on her little grey

3	

3	3	frock.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The third day, when her father and mother and sisters were gone, she

3	

3	3	went again into the garden, and said:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Shake, shake, hazel-tree,

3	

3	3	  Gold and silver over me!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then her kind friend the bird brought a dress still finer than the

3	

3	3	former one, and slippers which were all of gold: so that when she came

3	

3	3	to the feast no one knew what to say, for wonder at her beauty: and the

3	

3	3	king’s son danced with nobody but her; and when anyone else asked her to

3	

3	3	dance, he said, ‘This lady is _my_ partner, sir.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When night came she wanted to go home; and the king’s son would go with

3	

3	3	her, and said to himself, ‘I will not lose her this time’; but, however,

3	

3	3	she again slipped away from him, though in such a hurry that she dropped

3	

3	3	her left golden slipper upon the stairs.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The prince took the shoe, and went the next day to the king his father,

3	

3	3	and said, ‘I will take for my wife the lady that this golden slipper

3	

3	3	fits.’ Then both the sisters were overjoyed to hear it; for they

3	

3	3	had beautiful feet, and had no doubt that they could wear the golden

3	

3	3	slipper. The eldest went first into the room where the slipper was, and

3	

3	3	wanted to try it on, and the mother stood by. But her great toe could

3	

3	3	not go into it, and the shoe was altogether much too small for her. Then

3	

3	3	the mother gave her a knife, and said, ‘Never mind, cut it off; when you

3	

3	3	are queen you will not care about toes; you will not want to walk.’ So

3	

3	3	the silly girl cut off her great toe, and thus squeezed on the shoe,

3	

3	3	and went to the king’s son. Then he took her for his bride, and set her

3	

3	3	beside him on his horse, and rode away with her homewards.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But on their way home they had to pass by the hazel-tree that Ashputtel

3	

3	3	had planted; and on the branch sat a little dove singing:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Back again! back again! look to the shoe!

3	

3	3	  The shoe is too small, and not made for you!

3	

3	3	  Prince! prince! look again for thy bride,

3	

3	3	  For she’s not the true one that sits by thy side.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the prince got down and looked at her foot; and he saw, by the

3	

3	3	blood that streamed from it, what a trick she had played him. So he

3	

3	3	turned his horse round, and brought the false bride back to her home,

3	

3	3	and said, ‘This is not the right bride; let the other sister try and put

3	

3	3	on the slipper.’ Then she went into the room and got her foot into the

3	

3	3	shoe, all but the heel, which was too large. But her mother squeezed it

3	

3	3	in till the blood came, and took her to the king’s son: and he set her

3	

3	3	as his bride by his side on his horse, and rode away with her.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But when they came to the hazel-tree the little dove sat there still,

3	

3	3	and sang:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Back again! back again! look to the shoe!

3	

3	3	  The shoe is too small, and not made for you!

3	

3	3	  Prince! prince! look again for thy bride,

3	

3	3	  For she’s not the true one that sits by thy side.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then he looked down, and saw that the blood streamed so much from the

3	

3	3	shoe, that her white stockings were quite red. So he turned his horse

3	

3	3	and brought her also back again. ‘This is not the true bride,’ said he

3	

3	3	to the father; ‘have you no other daughters?’ ‘No,’ said he; ‘there is

3	

3	3	only a little dirty Ashputtel here, the child of my first wife; I am

3	

3	3	sure she cannot be the bride.’ The prince told him to send her. But the

3	

3	3	mother said, ‘No, no, she is much too dirty; she will not dare to show

3	

3	3	herself.’ However, the prince would have her come; and she first washed

3	

3	3	her face and hands, and then went in and curtsied to him, and he reached

3	

3	3	her the golden slipper. Then she took her clumsy shoe off her left foot,

3	

3	3	and put on the golden slipper; and it fitted her as if it had been made

3	

3	3	for her. And when he drew near and looked at her face he knew her, and

3	

3	3	said, ‘This is the right bride.’ But the mother and both the sisters

3	

3	3	were frightened, and turned pale with anger as he took Ashputtel on his

3	

3	3	horse, and rode away with her. And when they came to the hazel-tree, the

3	

3	3	white dove sang:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Home! home! look at the shoe!

3	

3	3	  Princess! the shoe was made for you!

3	

3	3	  Prince! prince! take home thy bride,

3	

3	3	  For she is the true one that sits by thy side!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And when the dove had done its song, it came flying, and perched upon

3	

3	3	her right shoulder, and so went home with her.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE WHITE SNAKE

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	A long time ago there lived a king who was famed for his wisdom through

3	

3	3	all the land. Nothing was hidden from him, and it seemed as if news of

3	

3	3	the most secret things was brought to him through the air. But he had a

3	

3	3	strange custom; every day after dinner, when the table was cleared,

3	

3	3	and no one else was present, a trusty servant had to bring him one more

3	

3	3	dish. It was covered, however, and even the servant did not know what

3	

3	3	was in it, neither did anyone know, for the king never took off the

3	

3	3	cover to eat of it until he was quite alone.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	This had gone on for a long time, when one day the servant, who took

3	

3	3	away the dish, was overcome with such curiosity that he could not help

3	

3	3	carrying the dish into his room. When he had carefully locked the door,

3	

3	3	he lifted up the cover, and saw a white snake lying on the dish. But

3	

3	3	when he saw it he could not deny himself the pleasure of tasting it,

3	

3	3	so he cut of a little bit and put it into his mouth. No sooner had it

3	

3	3	touched his tongue than he heard a strange whispering of little voices

3	

3	3	outside his window. He went and listened, and then noticed that it was

3	

3	3	the sparrows who were chattering together, and telling one another of

3	

3	3	all kinds of things which they had seen in the fields and woods. Eating

3	

3	3	the snake had given him power of understanding the language of animals.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Now it so happened that on this very day the queen lost her most

3	

3	3	beautiful ring, and suspicion of having stolen it fell upon this trusty

3	

3	3	servant, who was allowed to go everywhere. The king ordered the man to

3	

3	3	be brought before him, and threatened with angry words that unless he

3	

3	3	could before the morrow point out the thief, he himself should be looked

3	

3	3	upon as guilty and executed. In vain he declared his innocence; he was

3	

3	3	dismissed with no better answer.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	In his trouble and fear he went down into the courtyard and took thought

3	

3	3	how to help himself out of his trouble. Now some ducks were sitting

3	

3	3	together quietly by a brook and taking their rest; and, whilst they

3	

3	3	were making their feathers smooth with their bills, they were having a

3	

3	3	confidential conversation together. The servant stood by and listened.

3	

3	3	They were telling one another of all the places where they had been

3	

3	3	waddling about all the morning, and what good food they had found; and

3	

3	3	one said in a pitiful tone: ‘Something lies heavy on my stomach; as

3	

3	3	I was eating in haste I swallowed a ring which lay under the queen’s

3	

3	3	window.’ The servant at once seized her by the neck, carried her to the

3	

3	3	kitchen, and said to the cook: ‘Here is a fine duck; pray, kill her.’

3	

3	3	‘Yes,’ said the cook, and weighed her in his hand; ‘she has spared

3	

3	3	no trouble to fatten herself, and has been waiting to be roasted long

3	

3	3	enough.’ So he cut off her head, and as she was being dressed for the

3	

3	3	spit, the queen’s ring was found inside her.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The servant could now easily prove his innocence; and the king, to make

3	

3	3	amends for the wrong, allowed him to ask a favour, and promised him

3	

3	3	the best place in the court that he could wish for. The servant refused

3	

3	3	everything, and only asked for a horse and some money for travelling, as

3	

3	3	he had a mind to see the world and go about a little. When his request

3	

3	3	was granted he set out on his way, and one day came to a pond, where he

3	

3	3	saw three fishes caught in the reeds and gasping for water. Now, though

3	

3	3	it is said that fishes are dumb, he heard them lamenting that they must

3	

3	3	perish so miserably, and, as he had a kind heart, he got off his

3	

3	3	horse and put the three prisoners back into the water. They leapt with

3	

3	3	delight, put out their heads, and cried to him: ‘We will remember you

3	

3	3	and repay you for saving us!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	He rode on, and after a while it seemed to him that he heard a voice in

3	

3	3	the sand at his feet. He listened, and heard an ant-king complain: ‘Why

3	

3	3	cannot folks, with their clumsy beasts, keep off our bodies? That stupid

3	

3	3	horse, with his heavy hoofs, has been treading down my people without

3	

3	3	mercy!’ So he turned on to a side path and the ant-king cried out to

3	

3	3	him: ‘We will remember you--one good turn deserves another!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The path led him into a wood, and there he saw two old ravens standing

3	

3	3	by their nest, and throwing out their young ones. ‘Out with you, you

3	

3	3	idle, good-for-nothing creatures!’ cried they; ‘we cannot find food for

3	

3	3	you any longer; you are big enough, and can provide for yourselves.’

3	

3	3	But the poor young ravens lay upon the ground, flapping their wings, and

3	

3	3	crying: ‘Oh, what helpless chicks we are! We must shift for ourselves,

3	

3	3	and yet we cannot fly! What can we do, but lie here and starve?’ So the

3	

3	3	good young fellow alighted and killed his horse with his sword, and gave

3	

3	3	it to them for food. Then they came hopping up to it, satisfied their

3	

3	3	hunger, and cried: ‘We will remember you--one good turn deserves

3	

3	3	another!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And now he had to use his own legs, and when he had walked a long

3	

3	3	way, he came to a large city. There was a great noise and crowd in

3	

3	3	the streets, and a man rode up on horseback, crying aloud: ‘The king’s

3	

3	3	daughter wants a husband; but whoever seeks her hand must perform a hard

3	

3	3	task, and if he does not succeed he will forfeit his life.’ Many had

3	

3	3	already made the attempt, but in vain; nevertheless when the youth

3	

3	3	saw the king’s daughter he was so overcome by her great beauty that he

3	

3	3	forgot all danger, went before the king, and declared himself a suitor.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So he was led out to the sea, and a gold ring was thrown into it, before

3	

3	3	his eyes; then the king ordered him to fetch this ring up from the

3	

3	3	bottom of the sea, and added: ‘If you come up again without it you will

3	

3	3	be thrown in again and again until you perish amid the waves.’ All the

3	

3	3	people grieved for the handsome youth; then they went away, leaving him

3	

3	3	alone by the sea.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	He stood on the shore and considered what he should do, when suddenly

3	

3	3	he saw three fishes come swimming towards him, and they were the very

3	

3	3	fishes whose lives he had saved. The one in the middle held a mussel in

3	

3	3	its mouth, which it laid on the shore at the youth’s feet, and when he

3	

3	3	had taken it up and opened it, there lay the gold ring in the shell.

3	

3	3	Full of joy he took it to the king and expected that he would grant him

3	

3	3	the promised reward.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But when the proud princess perceived that he was not her equal in

3	

3	3	birth, she scorned him, and required him first to perform another

3	

3	3	task. She went down into the garden and strewed with her own hands ten

3	

3	3	sacksful of millet-seed on the grass; then she said: ‘Tomorrow morning

3	

3	3	before sunrise these must be picked up, and not a single grain be

3	

3	3	wanting.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The youth sat down in the garden and considered how it might be possible

3	

3	3	to perform this task, but he could think of nothing, and there he sat

3	

3	3	sorrowfully awaiting the break of day, when he should be led to death.

3	

3	3	But as soon as the first rays of the sun shone into the garden he saw

3	

3	3	all the ten sacks standing side by side, quite full, and not a single

3	

3	3	grain was missing. The ant-king had come in the night with thousands

3	

3	3	and thousands of ants, and the grateful creatures had by great industry

3	

3	3	picked up all the millet-seed and gathered them into the sacks.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Presently the king’s daughter herself came down into the garden, and was

3	

3	3	amazed to see that the young man had done the task she had given him.

3	

3	3	But she could not yet conquer her proud heart, and said: ‘Although he

3	

3	3	has performed both the tasks, he shall not be my husband until he had

3	

3	3	brought me an apple from the Tree of Life.’ The youth did not know where

3	

3	3	the Tree of Life stood, but he set out, and would have gone on for ever,

3	

3	3	as long as his legs would carry him, though he had no hope of finding

3	

3	3	it. After he had wandered through three kingdoms, he came one evening to

3	

3	3	a wood, and lay down under a tree to sleep. But he heard a rustling in

3	

3	3	the branches, and a golden apple fell into his hand. At the same time

3	

3	3	three ravens flew down to him, perched themselves upon his knee, and

3	

3	3	said: ‘We are the three young ravens whom you saved from starving; when

3	

3	3	we had grown big, and heard that you were seeking the Golden Apple,

3	

3	3	we flew over the sea to the end of the world, where the Tree of Life

3	

3	3	stands, and have brought you the apple.’ The youth, full of joy, set out

3	

3	3	homewards, and took the Golden Apple to the king’s beautiful daughter,

3	

3	3	who had now no more excuses left to make. They cut the Apple of Life in

3	

3	3	two and ate it together; and then her heart became full of love for him,

3	

3	3	and they lived in undisturbed happiness to a great age.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE WOLF AND THE SEVEN LITTLE KIDS

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once upon a time an old goat who had seven little kids, and

3	

3	3	loved them with all the love of a mother for her children. One day she

3	

3	3	wanted to go into the forest and fetch some food. So she called all

3	

3	3	seven to her and said: ‘Dear children, I have to go into the forest,

3	

3	3	be on your guard against the wolf; if he comes in, he will devour you

3	

3	3	all--skin, hair, and everything. The wretch often disguises himself, but

3	

3	3	you will know him at once by his rough voice and his black feet.’ The

3	

3	3	kids said: ‘Dear mother, we will take good care of ourselves; you may go

3	

3	3	away without any anxiety.’ Then the old one bleated, and went on her way

3	

3	3	with an easy mind.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	It was not long before someone knocked at the house-door and called:

3	

3	3	‘Open the door, dear children; your mother is here, and has brought

3	

3	3	something back with her for each of you.’ But the little kids knew that

3	

3	3	it was the wolf, by the rough voice. ‘We will not open the door,’ cried

3	

3	3	they, ‘you are not our mother. She has a soft, pleasant voice, but

3	

3	3	your voice is rough; you are the wolf!’ Then the wolf went away to a

3	

3	3	shopkeeper and bought himself a great lump of chalk, ate this and made

3	

3	3	his voice soft with it. Then he came back, knocked at the door of the

3	

3	3	house, and called: ‘Open the door, dear children, your mother is here

3	

3	3	and has brought something back with her for each of you.’ But the wolf

3	

3	3	had laid his black paws against the window, and the children saw them

3	

3	3	and cried: ‘We will not open the door, our mother has not black feet

3	

3	3	like you: you are the wolf!’ Then the wolf ran to a baker and said: ‘I

3	

3	3	have hurt my feet, rub some dough over them for me.’ And when the baker

3	

3	3	had rubbed his feet over, he ran to the miller and said: ‘Strew some

3	

3	3	white meal over my feet for me.’ The miller thought to himself: ‘The

3	

3	3	wolf wants to deceive someone,’ and refused; but the wolf said: ‘If you

3	

3	3	will not do it, I will devour you.’ Then the miller was afraid, and made

3	

3	3	his paws white for him. Truly, this is the way of mankind.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So now the wretch went for the third time to the house-door, knocked at

3	

3	3	it and said: ‘Open the door for me, children, your dear little mother

3	

3	3	has come home, and has brought every one of you something back from the

3	

3	3	forest with her.’ The little kids cried: ‘First show us your paws that

3	

3	3	we may know if you are our dear little mother.’ Then he put his paws

3	

3	3	in through the window and when the kids saw that they were white, they

3	

3	3	believed that all he said was true, and opened the door. But who should

3	

3	3	come in but the wolf! They were terrified and wanted to hide themselves.

3	

3	3	One sprang under the table, the second into the bed, the third into the

3	

3	3	stove, the fourth into the kitchen, the fifth into the cupboard, the

3	

3	3	sixth under the washing-bowl, and the seventh into the clock-case. But

3	

3	3	the wolf found them all, and used no great ceremony; one after the

3	

3	3	other he swallowed them down his throat. The youngest, who was in

3	

3	3	the clock-case, was the only one he did not find. When the wolf had

3	

3	3	satisfied his appetite he took himself off, laid himself down under a

3	

3	3	tree in the green meadow outside, and began to sleep. Soon afterwards

3	

3	3	the old goat came home again from the forest. Ah! what a sight she saw

3	

3	3	there! The house-door stood wide open. The table, chairs, and benches

3	

3	3	were thrown down, the washing-bowl lay broken to pieces, and the quilts

3	

3	3	and pillows were pulled off the bed. She sought her children, but they

3	

3	3	were nowhere to be found. She called them one after another by name, but

3	

3	3	no one answered. At last, when she came to the youngest, a soft voice

3	

3	3	cried: ‘Dear mother, I am in the clock-case.’ She took the kid out, and

3	

3	3	it told her that the wolf had come and had eaten all the others. Then

3	

3	3	you may imagine how she wept over her poor children.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	At length in her grief she went out, and the youngest kid ran with her.

3	

3	3	When they came to the meadow, there lay the wolf by the tree and snored

3	

3	3	so loud that the branches shook. She looked at him on every side and

3	

3	3	saw that something was moving and struggling in his gorged belly. ‘Ah,

3	

3	3	heavens,’ she said, ‘is it possible that my poor children whom he has

3	

3	3	swallowed down for his supper, can be still alive?’ Then the kid had to

3	

3	3	run home and fetch scissors, and a needle and thread, and the goat cut

3	

3	3	open the monster’s stomach, and hardly had she made one cut, than one

3	

3	3	little kid thrust its head out, and when she had cut farther, all six

3	

3	3	sprang out one after another, and were all still alive, and had suffered

3	

3	3	no injury whatever, for in his greediness the monster had swallowed them

3	

3	3	down whole. What rejoicing there was! They embraced their dear mother,

3	

3	3	and jumped like a tailor at his wedding. The mother, however, said: ‘Now

3	

3	3	go and look for some big stones, and we will fill the wicked beast’s

3	

3	3	stomach with them while he is still asleep.’ Then the seven kids dragged

3	

3	3	the stones thither with all speed, and put as many of them into this

3	

3	3	stomach as they could get in; and the mother sewed him up again in the

3	

3	3	greatest haste, so that he was not aware of anything and never once

3	

3	3	stirred.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the wolf at length had had his fill of sleep, he got on his legs,

3	

3	3	and as the stones in his stomach made him very thirsty, he wanted to

3	

3	3	go to a well to drink. But when he began to walk and to move about, the

3	

3	3	stones in his stomach knocked against each other and rattled. Then cried

3	

3	3	he:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘What rumbles and tumbles

3	

3	3	  Against my poor bones?

3	

3	3	  I thought ‘twas six kids,

3	

3	3	  But it feels like big stones.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And when he got to the well and stooped over the water to drink, the

3	

3	3	heavy stones made him fall in, and he drowned miserably. When the seven

3	

3	3	kids saw that, they came running to the spot and cried aloud: ‘The wolf

3	

3	3	is dead! The wolf is dead!’ and danced for joy round about the well with

3	

3	3	their mother.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE QUEEN BEE

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Two kings’ sons once upon a time went into the world to seek their

3	

3	3	fortunes; but they soon fell into a wasteful foolish way of living, so

3	

3	3	that they could not return home again. Then their brother, who was a

3	

3	3	little insignificant dwarf, went out to seek for his brothers: but when

3	

3	3	he had found them they only laughed at him, to think that he, who was so

3	

3	3	young and simple, should try to travel through the world, when they, who

3	

3	3	were so much wiser, had been unable to get on. However, they all set

3	

3	3	out on their journey together, and came at last to an ant-hill. The two

3	

3	3	elder brothers would have pulled it down, in order to see how the poor

3	

3	3	ants in their fright would run about and carry off their eggs. But the

3	

3	3	little dwarf said, ‘Let the poor things enjoy themselves, I will not

3	

3	3	suffer you to trouble them.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So on they went, and came to a lake where many many ducks were swimming

3	

3	3	about. The two brothers wanted to catch two, and roast them. But the

3	

3	3	dwarf said, ‘Let the poor things enjoy themselves, you shall not kill

3	

3	3	them.’ Next they came to a bees’-nest in a hollow tree, and there was

3	

3	3	so much honey that it ran down the trunk; and the two brothers wanted to

3	

3	3	light a fire under the tree and kill the bees, so as to get their honey.

3	

3	3	But the dwarf held them back, and said, ‘Let the pretty insects enjoy

3	

3	3	themselves, I cannot let you burn them.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	At length the three brothers came to a castle: and as they passed by the

3	

3	3	stables they saw fine horses standing there, but all were of marble, and

3	

3	3	no man was to be seen. Then they went through all the rooms, till they

3	

3	3	came to a door on which were three locks: but in the middle of the door

3	

3	3	was a wicket, so that they could look into the next room. There they saw

3	

3	3	a little grey old man sitting at a table; and they called to him once or

3	

3	3	twice, but he did not hear: however, they called a third time, and then

3	

3	3	he rose and came out to them.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	He said nothing, but took hold of them and led them to a beautiful

3	

3	3	table covered with all sorts of good things: and when they had eaten and

3	

3	3	drunk, he showed each of them to a bed-chamber.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The next morning he came to the eldest and took him to a marble table,

3	

3	3	where there were three tablets, containing an account of the means by

3	

3	3	which the castle might be disenchanted. The first tablet said: ‘In the

3	

3	3	wood, under the moss, lie the thousand pearls belonging to the king’s

3	

3	3	daughter; they must all be found: and if one be missing by set of sun,

3	

3	3	he who seeks them will be turned into marble.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The eldest brother set out, and sought for the pearls the whole day:

3	

3	3	but the evening came, and he had not found the first hundred: so he was

3	

3	3	turned into stone as the tablet had foretold.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The next day the second brother undertook the task; but he succeeded no

3	

3	3	better than the first; for he could only find the second hundred of the

3	

3	3	pearls; and therefore he too was turned into stone.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	At last came the little dwarf’s turn; and he looked in the moss; but it

3	

3	3	was so hard to find the pearls, and the job was so tiresome!--so he sat

3	

3	3	down upon a stone and cried. And as he sat there, the king of the ants

3	

3	3	(whose life he had saved) came to help him, with five thousand ants; and

3	

3	3	it was not long before they had found all the pearls and laid them in a

3	

3	3	heap.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The second tablet said: ‘The key of the princess’s bed-chamber must be

3	

3	3	fished up out of the lake.’ And as the dwarf came to the brink of it,

3	

3	3	he saw the two ducks whose lives he had saved swimming about; and they

3	

3	3	dived down and soon brought in the key from the bottom.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The third task was the hardest. It was to choose out the youngest and

3	

3	3	the best of the king’s three daughters. Now they were all beautiful, and

3	

3	3	all exactly alike: but he was told that the eldest had eaten a piece of

3	

3	3	sugar, the next some sweet syrup, and the youngest a spoonful of honey;

3	

3	3	so he was to guess which it was that had eaten the honey.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then came the queen of the bees, who had been saved by the little dwarf

3	

3	3	from the fire, and she tried the lips of all three; but at last she sat

3	

3	3	upon the lips of the one that had eaten the honey: and so the dwarf knew

3	

3	3	which was the youngest. Thus the spell was broken, and all who had been

3	

3	3	turned into stones awoke, and took their proper forms. And the dwarf

3	

3	3	married the youngest and the best of the princesses, and was king after

3	

3	3	her father’s death; but his two brothers married the other two sisters.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE ELVES AND THE SHOEMAKER

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once a shoemaker, who worked very hard and was very honest:

3	

3	3	but still he could not earn enough to live upon; and at last all he

3	

3	3	had in the world was gone, save just leather enough to make one pair of

3	

3	3	shoes.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then he cut his leather out, all ready to make up the next day, meaning

3	

3	3	to rise early in the morning to his work. His conscience was clear and

3	

3	3	his heart light amidst all his troubles; so he went peaceably to bed,

3	

3	3	left all his cares to Heaven, and soon fell asleep. In the morning after

3	

3	3	he had said his prayers, he sat himself down to his work; when, to his

3	

3	3	great wonder, there stood the shoes all ready made, upon the table. The

3	

3	3	good man knew not what to say or think at such an odd thing happening.

3	

3	3	He looked at the workmanship; there was not one false stitch in the

3	

3	3	whole job; all was so neat and true, that it was quite a masterpiece.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The same day a customer came in, and the shoes suited him so well that

3	

3	3	he willingly paid a price higher than usual for them; and the poor

3	

3	3	shoemaker, with the money, bought leather enough to make two pairs more.

3	

3	3	In the evening he cut out the work, and went to bed early, that he might

3	

3	3	get up and begin betimes next day; but he was saved all the trouble, for

3	

3	3	when he got up in the morning the work was done ready to his hand. Soon

3	

3	3	in came buyers, who paid him handsomely for his goods, so that he bought

3	

3	3	leather enough for four pair more. He cut out the work again overnight

3	

3	3	and found it done in the morning, as before; and so it went on for some

3	

3	3	time: what was got ready in the evening was always done by daybreak, and

3	

3	3	the good man soon became thriving and well off again.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	One evening, about Christmas-time, as he and his wife were sitting over

3	

3	3	the fire chatting together, he said to her, ‘I should like to sit up and

3	

3	3	watch tonight, that we may see who it is that comes and does my work for

3	

3	3	me.’ The wife liked the thought; so they left a light burning, and hid

3	

3	3	themselves in a corner of the room, behind a curtain that was hung up

3	

3	3	there, and watched what would happen.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	As soon as it was midnight, there came in two little naked dwarfs; and

3	

3	3	they sat themselves upon the shoemaker’s bench, took up all the work

3	

3	3	that was cut out, and began to ply with their little fingers, stitching

3	

3	3	and rapping and tapping away at such a rate, that the shoemaker was all

3	

3	3	wonder, and could not take his eyes off them. And on they went, till the

3	

3	3	job was quite done, and the shoes stood ready for use upon the table.

3	

3	3	This was long before daybreak; and then they bustled away as quick as

3	

3	3	lightning.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The next day the wife said to the shoemaker. ‘These little wights have

3	

3	3	made us rich, and we ought to be thankful to them, and do them a good

3	

3	3	turn if we can. I am quite sorry to see them run about as they do; and

3	

3	3	indeed it is not very decent, for they have nothing upon their backs to

3	

3	3	keep off the cold. I’ll tell you what, I will make each of them a shirt,

3	

3	3	and a coat and waistcoat, and a pair of pantaloons into the bargain; and

3	

3	3	do you make each of them a little pair of shoes.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The thought pleased the good cobbler very much; and one evening, when

3	

3	3	all the things were ready, they laid them on the table, instead of the

3	

3	3	work that they used to cut out, and then went and hid themselves, to

3	

3	3	watch what the little elves would do.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	About midnight in they came, dancing and skipping, hopped round the

3	

3	3	room, and then went to sit down to their work as usual; but when they

3	

3	3	saw the clothes lying for them, they laughed and chuckled, and seemed

3	

3	3	mightily delighted.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then they dressed themselves in the twinkling of an eye, and danced and

3	

3	3	capered and sprang about, as merry as could be; till at last they danced

3	

3	3	out at the door, and away over the green.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The good couple saw them no more; but everything went well with them

3	

3	3	from that time forward, as long as they lived.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE JUNIPER-TREE

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Long, long ago, some two thousand years or so, there lived a rich

3	

3	3	man with a good and beautiful wife. They loved each other dearly, but

3	

3	3	sorrowed much that they had no children. So greatly did they desire

3	

3	3	to have one, that the wife prayed for it day and night, but still they

3	

3	3	remained childless.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	In front of the house there was a court, in which grew a juniper-tree.

3	

3	3	One winter’s day the wife stood under the tree to peel some apples, and

3	

3	3	as she was peeling them, she cut her finger, and the blood fell on the

3	

3	3	snow. ‘Ah,’ sighed the woman heavily, ‘if I had but a child, as red as

3	

3	3	blood and as white as snow,’ and as she spoke the words, her heart grew

3	

3	3	light within her, and it seemed to her that her wish was granted, and

3	

3	3	she returned to the house feeling glad and comforted. A month passed,

3	

3	3	and the snow had all disappeared; then another month went by, and all

3	

3	3	the earth was green. So the months followed one another, and first the

3	

3	3	trees budded in the woods, and soon the green branches grew thickly

3	

3	3	intertwined, and then the blossoms began to fall. Once again the wife

3	

3	3	stood under the juniper-tree, and it was so full of sweet scent that her

3	

3	3	heart leaped for joy, and she was so overcome with her happiness, that

3	

3	3	she fell on her knees. Presently the fruit became round and firm, and

3	

3	3	she was glad and at peace; but when they were fully ripe she picked the

3	

3	3	berries and ate eagerly of them, and then she grew sad and ill. A little

3	

3	3	while later she called her husband, and said to him, weeping. ‘If I

3	

3	3	die, bury me under the juniper-tree.’ Then she felt comforted and happy

3	

3	3	again, and before another month had passed she had a little child, and

3	

3	3	when she saw that it was as white as snow and as red as blood, her joy

3	

3	3	was so great that she died.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Her husband buried her under the juniper-tree, and wept bitterly for

3	

3	3	her. By degrees, however, his sorrow grew less, and although at times he

3	

3	3	still grieved over his loss, he was able to go about as usual, and later

3	

3	3	on he married again.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	He now had a little daughter born to him; the child of his first wife

3	

3	3	was a boy, who was as red as blood and as white as snow. The mother

3	

3	3	loved her daughter very much, and when she looked at her and then looked

3	

3	3	at the boy, it pierced her heart to think that he would always stand in

3	

3	3	the way of her own child, and she was continually thinking how she could

3	

3	3	get the whole of the property for her. This evil thought took possession

3	

3	3	of her more and more, and made her behave very unkindly to the boy. She

3	

3	3	drove him from place to place with cuffings and buffetings, so that the

3	

3	3	poor child went about in fear, and had no peace from the time he left

3	

3	3	school to the time he went back.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	One day the little daughter came running to her mother in the

3	

3	3	store-room, and said, ‘Mother, give me an apple.’ ‘Yes, my child,’ said

3	

3	3	the wife, and she gave her a beautiful apple out of the chest; the chest

3	

3	3	had a very heavy lid and a large iron lock.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Mother,’ said the little daughter again, ‘may not brother have one

3	

3	3	too?’ The mother was angry at this, but she answered, ‘Yes, when he

3	

3	3	comes out of school.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Just then she looked out of the window and saw him coming, and it seemed

3	

3	3	as if an evil spirit entered into her, for she snatched the apple out

3	

3	3	of her little daughter’s hand, and said, ‘You shall not have one before

3	

3	3	your brother.’ She threw the apple into the chest and shut it to. The

3	

3	3	little boy now came in, and the evil spirit in the wife made her say

3	

3	3	kindly to him, ‘My son, will you have an apple?’ but she gave him a

3	

3	3	wicked look. ‘Mother,’ said the boy, ‘how dreadful you look! Yes, give

3	

3	3	me an apple.’ The thought came to her that she would kill him. ‘Come

3	

3	3	with me,’ she said, and she lifted up the lid of the chest; ‘take one

3	

3	3	out for yourself.’ And as he bent over to do so, the evil spirit urged

3	

3	3	her, and crash! down went the lid, and off went the little boy’s head.

3	

3	3	Then she was overwhelmed with fear at the thought of what she had done.

3	

3	3	‘If only I can prevent anyone knowing that I did it,’ she thought. So

3	

3	3	she went upstairs to her room, and took a white handkerchief out of

3	

3	3	her top drawer; then she set the boy’s head again on his shoulders, and

3	

3	3	bound it with the handkerchief so that nothing could be seen, and placed

3	

3	3	him on a chair by the door with an apple in his hand.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Soon after this, little Marleen came up to her mother who was stirring

3	

3	3	a pot of boiling water over the fire, and said, ‘Mother, brother is

3	

3	3	sitting by the door with an apple in his hand, and he looks so pale;

3	

3	3	and when I asked him to give me the apple, he did not answer, and that

3	

3	3	frightened me.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Go to him again,’ said her mother, ‘and if he does not answer, give him

3	

3	3	a box on the ear.’ So little Marleen went, and said, ‘Brother, give me

3	

3	3	that apple,’ but he did not say a word; then she gave him a box on the

3	

3	3	ear, and his head rolled off. She was so terrified at this, that she ran

3	

3	3	crying and screaming to her mother. ‘Oh!’ she said, ‘I have knocked off

3	

3	3	brother’s head,’ and then she wept and wept, and nothing would stop her.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘What have you done!’ said her mother, ‘but no one must know about it,

3	

3	3	so you must keep silence; what is done can’t be undone; we will make

3	

3	3	him into puddings.’ And she took the little boy and cut him up, made him

3	

3	3	into puddings, and put him in the pot. But Marleen stood looking on,

3	

3	3	and wept and wept, and her tears fell into the pot, so that there was no

3	

3	3	need of salt.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Presently the father came home and sat down to his dinner; he asked,

3	

3	3	‘Where is my son?’ The mother said nothing, but gave him a large dish of

3	

3	3	black pudding, and Marleen still wept without ceasing.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The father again asked, ‘Where is my son?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Oh,’ answered the wife, ‘he is gone into the country to his mother’s

3	

3	3	great uncle; he is going to stay there some time.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘What has he gone there for, and he never even said goodbye to me!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Well, he likes being there, and he told me he should be away quite six

3	

3	3	weeks; he is well looked after there.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘I feel very unhappy about it,’ said the husband, ‘in case it should not

3	

3	3	be all right, and he ought to have said goodbye to me.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	With this he went on with his dinner, and said, ‘Little Marleen, why do

3	

3	3	you weep? Brother will soon be back.’ Then he asked his wife for more

3	

3	3	pudding, and as he ate, he threw the bones under the table.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Little Marleen went upstairs and took her best silk handkerchief out of

3	

3	3	her bottom drawer, and in it she wrapped all the bones from under the

3	

3	3	table and carried them outside, and all the time she did nothing but

3	

3	3	weep. Then she laid them in the green grass under the juniper-tree, and

3	

3	3	she had no sooner done so, then all her sadness seemed to leave her,

3	

3	3	and she wept no more. And now the juniper-tree began to move, and the

3	

3	3	branches waved backwards and forwards, first away from one another, and

3	

3	3	then together again, as it might be someone clapping their hands for

3	

3	3	joy. After this a mist came round the tree, and in the midst of it there

3	

3	3	was a burning as of fire, and out of the fire there flew a beautiful

3	

3	3	bird, that rose high into the air, singing magnificently, and when it

3	

3	3	could no more be seen, the juniper-tree stood there as before, and the

3	

3	3	silk handkerchief and the bones were gone.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Little Marleen now felt as lighthearted and happy as if her brother were

3	

3	3	still alive, and she went back to the house and sat down cheerfully to

3	

3	3	the table and ate.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The bird flew away and alighted on the house of a goldsmith and began to

3	

3	3	sing:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘My mother killed her little son;

3	

3	3	  My father grieved when I was gone;

3	

3	3	  My sister loved me best of all;

3	

3	3	  She laid her kerchief over me,

3	

3	3	  And took my bones that they might lie

3	

3	3	  Underneath the juniper-tree

3	

3	3	  Kywitt, Kywitt, what a beautiful bird am I!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The goldsmith was in his workshop making a gold chain, when he heard the

3	

3	3	song of the bird on his roof. He thought it so beautiful that he got

3	

3	3	up and ran out, and as he crossed the threshold he lost one of his

3	

3	3	slippers. But he ran on into the middle of the street, with a slipper on

3	

3	3	one foot and a sock on the other; he still had on his apron, and still

3	

3	3	held the gold chain and the pincers in his hands, and so he stood gazing

3	

3	3	up at the bird, while the sun came shining brightly down on the street.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Bird,’ he said, ‘how beautifully you sing! Sing me that song again.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Nay,’ said the bird, ‘I do not sing twice for nothing. Give that gold

3	

3	3	chain, and I will sing it you again.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Here is the chain, take it,’ said the goldsmith. ‘Only sing me that

3	

3	3	again.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The bird flew down and took the gold chain in his right claw, and then

3	

3	3	he alighted again in front of the goldsmith and sang:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘My mother killed her little son;

3	

3	3	  My father grieved when I was gone;

3	

3	3	  My sister loved me best of all;

3	

3	3	  She laid her kerchief over me,

3	

3	3	  And took my bones that they might lie

3	

3	3	  Underneath the juniper-tree

3	

3	3	  Kywitt, Kywitt, what a beautiful bird am I!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then he flew away, and settled on the roof of a shoemaker’s house and

3	

3	3	sang:

3	

3	3

3	3	 ‘My mother killed her little son;

3	

3	3	  My father grieved when I was gone;

3	

3	3	  My sister loved me best of all;

3	

3	3	  She laid her kerchief over me,

3	

3	3	  And took my bones that they might lie

3	

3	3	  Underneath the juniper-tree

3	3	The shoemaker heard him, and he jumped up and ran out in his

3	

3	3	shirt-sleeves, and stood looking up at the bird on the roof with his

3	

3	3	hand over his eyes to keep himself from being blinded by the sun.

3	3

3	3	‘Bird,’ he said, ‘how beautifully you sing!’ Then he called through the

3	

3	3	and hear how beautifully it sings.’ Then he called his daughter and the

3	

3	3	children, then the apprentices, girls and boys, and they all ran up the

3	

3	3	street to look at the bird, and saw how splendid it was with its red

3	

3	3	and green feathers, and its neck like burnished gold, and eyes like two

3	

3	3	bright stars in its head.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Bird,’ said the shoemaker, ‘sing me that song again.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Nay,’ answered the bird, ‘I do not sing twice for nothing; you must

3	

3	3	give me something.’

3	

3	3

3	3	‘Wife,’ said the man, ‘go into the garret; on the upper shelf you will

3	

3	3	see a pair of red shoes; bring them to me.’ The wife went in and fetched

3	

3	3	the shoes.

3	

3	3	

3

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The bird flew down and took the red shoes in his left claw, and then he

3	

3	3	went back to the roof and sang:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘My mother killed her little son;

3	

3	3	  My father grieved when I was gone;

3	

3	3	  My sister loved me best of all;

3	

3	3	  She laid her kerchief over me,

3	

3	3	  And took my bones that they might lie

3	

3	3	  Underneath the juniper-tree

3	

3	3	  Kywitt, Kywitt, what a beautiful bird am I!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When he had finished, he flew away. He had the chain in his right claw

3	

3	3	and the shoes in his left, and he flew right away to a mill, and the

3	

3	3	mill went ‘Click clack, click clack, click clack.’ Inside the mill were

3	

3	3	twenty of the miller’s men hewing a stone, and as they went ‘Hick hack,

3	

3	3	hick hack, hick hack,’ the mill went ‘Click clack, click clack, click

3	

3	3	clack.’

3	

3	3

3	

3	3

3	3

3	3	now there were only eight at work,

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	  Underneath

3	

3	3

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	  the juniper-tree.

3	

3	3

3	3	And now only one,

3	

3	3

3	3	  Kywitt, Kywitt, what a beautiful bird am I!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	then he looked up and the last one had left off work.

3	3

3	3	‘Bird,’ he said, ‘what a beautiful song that is you sing! Let me hear it

3	

3	3	too; sing it again.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Nay,’ answered the bird, ‘I do not sing twice for nothing; give me that

3	

3	3	millstone, and I will sing it again.’

3	3

3	3	‘If it belonged to me alone,’ said the man, ‘you should have it.’

3	3

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The bird came down, and all the twenty millers set to and lifted up the

3	

3	3	stone with a beam; then the bird put his head through the hole and took

3	

3	3	the stone round his neck like a collar, and flew back with it to the

3	

3	3	tree and sang--

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘My mother killed her little son;

3	

3	3	  My father grieved when I was gone;

3	

3	3	  My sister loved me best of all;

3	

3	3	  She laid her kerchief over me,

3	

3	3	  And took my bones that they might lie

3	

3	3	  Underneath the juniper-tree

3	

3	3	  Kywitt, Kywitt, what a beautiful bird am I!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And when he had finished his song, he spread his wings, and with the

3	

3	3	chain in his right claw, the shoes in his left, and the millstone round

3	

3	3	his neck, he flew right away to his father’s house.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The father, the mother, and little Marleen were having their dinner.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘How lighthearted I feel,’ said the father, ‘so pleased and cheerful.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘And I,’ said the mother, ‘I feel so uneasy, as if a heavy thunderstorm

3	

3	3	were coming.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But little Marleen sat and wept and wept.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the bird came flying towards the house and settled on the roof.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘I do feel so happy,’ said the father, ‘and how beautifully the sun

3	

3	3	shines; I feel just as if I were going to see an old friend again.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Ah!’ said the wife, ‘and I am so full of distress and uneasiness that

3	

3	3	my teeth chatter, and I feel as if there were a fire in my veins,’ and

3	

3	3	she tore open her dress; and all the while little Marleen sat in the

3	

3	3	corner and wept, and the plate on her knees was wet with her tears.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The bird now flew to the juniper-tree and began singing:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘My mother killed her little son;

3	

3	3

3	3	the mother shut her eyes and her ears, that she might see and hear

3	3

3	3	magnificently; and how warm and bright the sun is, and what a delicious

3	3	man’s neck, so that it fitted him exactly.

3	3

3	3	given me this beautiful gold chain, and looks so beautiful himself.’

3	

3	3

3	3	my misery, for I feel as if the world were coming to an end.’

3	3

3	3	down on her head, and she was crushed to death.

3	3

3	3	The father and little Marleen heard the sound and ran out, but they only

3	

3	3	saw mist and flame and fire rising from the spot, and when these had

3	

3	3	passed, there stood the little brother, and he took the father and

3	

3	3	little Marleen by the hand; then they all three rejoiced, and went

3	

3	3	inside together and sat down to their dinners and ate.

3	3

3	3

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE TURNIP

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There were two brothers who were both soldiers; the one was rich and

3	

3	3	the other poor. The poor man thought he would try to better himself; so,

3	

3	3	pulling off his red coat, he became a gardener, and dug his ground well,

3	

3	3	and sowed turnips.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the seed came up, there was one plant bigger than all the rest; and

3	

3	3	it kept getting larger and larger, and seemed as if it would never cease

3	

3	3	growing; so that it might have been called the prince of turnips for

3	

3	3	there never was such a one seen before, and never will again. At last it

3	

3	3	was so big that it filled a cart, and two oxen could hardly draw it; and

3	

3	3	the gardener knew not what in the world to do with it, nor whether it

3	

3	3	would be a blessing or a curse to him. One day he said to himself, ‘What

3	

3	3	shall I do with it? if I sell it, it will bring no more than another;

3	

3	3	and for eating, the little turnips are better than this; the best thing

3	

3	3	perhaps is to carry it and give it to the king as a mark of respect.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then he yoked his oxen, and drew the turnip to the court, and gave it

3	

3	3	to the king. ‘What a wonderful thing!’ said the king; ‘I have seen many

3	

3	3	strange things, but such a monster as this I never saw. Where did you

3	

3	3	get the seed? or is it only your good luck? If so, you are a true child

3	

3	3	of fortune.’ ‘Ah, no!’ answered the gardener, ‘I am no child of fortune;

3	

3	3	I am a poor soldier, who never could get enough to live upon; so I

3	

3	3	laid aside my red coat, and set to work, tilling the ground. I have a

3	

3	3	brother, who is rich, and your majesty knows him well, and all the world

3	

3	3	knows him; but because I am poor, everybody forgets me.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The king then took pity on him, and said, ‘You shall be poor no

3	

3	3	longer. I will give you so much that you shall be even richer than your

3	

3	3	brother.’ Then he gave him gold and lands and flocks, and made him so

3	

3	3	rich that his brother’s fortune could not at all be compared with his.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the brother heard of all this, and how a turnip had made the

3	

3	3	gardener so rich, he envied him sorely, and bethought himself how he

3	

3	3	could contrive to get the same good fortune for himself. However, he

3	

3	3	determined to manage more cleverly than his brother, and got together a

3	

3	3	rich present of gold and fine horses for the king; and thought he must

3	

3	3	have a much larger gift in return; for if his brother had received so

3	

3	3	much for only a turnip, what must his present be worth?

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The king took the gift very graciously, and said he knew not what to

3	

3	3	give in return more valuable and wonderful than the great turnip; so

3	

3	3	the soldier was forced to put it into a cart, and drag it home with him.

3	

3	3	When he reached home, he knew not upon whom to vent his rage and spite;

3	

3	3	and at length wicked thoughts came into his head, and he resolved to

3	

3	3	kill his brother.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So he hired some villains to murder him; and having shown them where to

3	

3	3	lie in ambush, he went to his brother, and said, ‘Dear brother, I have

3	

3	3	found a hidden treasure; let us go and dig it up, and share it between

3	

3	3	us.’ The other had no suspicions of his roguery: so they went out

3	

3	3	together, and as they were travelling along, the murderers rushed out

3	

3	3	upon him, bound him, and were going to hang him on a tree.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But whilst they were getting all ready, they heard the trampling of a

3	

3	3	horse at a distance, which so frightened them that they pushed their

3	

3	3	prisoner neck and shoulders together into a sack, and swung him up by a

3	

3	3	cord to the tree, where they left him dangling, and ran away. Meantime

3	

3	3	he worked and worked away, till he made a hole large enough to put out

3	

3	3	his head.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the horseman came up, he proved to be a student, a merry fellow,

3	

3	3	who was journeying along on his nag, and singing as he went. As soon as

3	

3	3	the man in the sack saw him passing under the tree, he cried out, ‘Good

3	

3	3	morning! good morning to thee, my friend!’ The student looked about

3	

3	3	everywhere; and seeing no one, and not knowing where the voice came

3	

3	3	from, cried out, ‘Who calls me?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the man in the tree answered, ‘Lift up thine eyes, for behold here

3	

3	3	I sit in the sack of wisdom; here have I, in a short time, learned great

3	

3	3	and wondrous things. Compared to this seat, all the learning of the

3	

3	3	schools is as empty air. A little longer, and I shall know all that man

3	

3	3	can know, and shall come forth wiser than the wisest of mankind. Here

3	

3	3	I discern the signs and motions of the heavens and the stars; the laws

3	

3	3	that control the winds; the number of the sands on the seashore; the

3	

3	3	healing of the sick; the virtues of all simples, of birds, and of

3	

3	3	precious stones. Wert thou but once here, my friend, though wouldst feel

3	

3	3	and own the power of knowledge.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The student listened to all this and wondered much; at last he said,

3	

3	3	‘Blessed be the day and hour when I found you; cannot you contrive to

3	

3	3	let me into the sack for a little while?’ Then the other answered, as if

3	

3	3	very unwillingly, ‘A little space I may allow thee to sit here, if thou

3	

3	3	wilt reward me well and entreat me kindly; but thou must tarry yet an

3	

3	3	hour below, till I have learnt some little matters that are yet unknown

3	

3	3	to me.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So the student sat himself down and waited a while; but the time hung

3	

3	3	heavy upon him, and he begged earnestly that he might ascend forthwith,

3	

3	3	for his thirst for knowledge was great. Then the other pretended to give

3	

3	3	way, and said, ‘Thou must let the sack of wisdom descend, by untying

3	

3	3	yonder cord, and then thou shalt enter.’ So the student let him down,

3	

3	3	opened the sack, and set him free. ‘Now then,’ cried he, ‘let me ascend

3	

3	3	quickly.’ As he began to put himself into the sack heels first, ‘Wait a

3	

3	3	while,’ said the gardener, ‘that is not the way.’ Then he pushed him

3	

3	3	in head first, tied up the sack, and soon swung up the searcher after

3	

3	3	wisdom dangling in the air. ‘How is it with thee, friend?’ said he,

3	

3	3	‘dost thou not feel that wisdom comes unto thee? Rest there in peace,

3	

3	3	till thou art a wiser man than thou wert.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So saying, he trotted off on the student’s nag, and left the poor fellow

3	

3	3	to gather wisdom till somebody should come and let him down.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	CLEVER HANS

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The mother of Hans said: ‘Whither away, Hans?’ Hans answered: ‘To

3	

3	3	Gretel.’ ‘Behave well, Hans.’ ‘Oh, I’ll behave well. Goodbye, mother.’

3	

3	3	‘Goodbye, Hans.’ Hans comes to Gretel. ‘Good day, Gretel.’ ‘Good day,

3	

3	3	Hans. What do you bring that is good?’ ‘I bring nothing, I want to have

3	

3	3	something given me.’ Gretel presents Hans with a needle, Hans says:

3	

3	3	‘Goodbye, Gretel.’ ‘Goodbye, Hans.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Hans takes the needle, sticks it into a hay-cart, and follows the cart

3	

3	3	home. ‘Good evening, mother.’ ‘Good evening, Hans. Where have you been?’

3	

3	3	‘With Gretel.’ ‘What did you take her?’ ‘Took nothing; had something

3	

3	3	given me.’ ‘What did Gretel give you?’ ‘Gave me a needle.’ ‘Where is the

3	

3	3	needle, Hans?’ ‘Stuck in the hay-cart.’ ‘That was ill done, Hans. You

3	

3	3	should have stuck the needle in your sleeve.’ ‘Never mind, I’ll do

3	

3	3	better next time.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Whither away, Hans?’ ‘To Gretel, mother.’ ‘Behave well, Hans.’ ‘Oh,

3	

3	3	I’ll behave well. Goodbye, mother.’ ‘Goodbye, Hans.’ Hans comes to

3	

3	3	Gretel. ‘Good day, Gretel.’ ‘Good day, Hans. What do you bring that is

3	

3	3	good?’ ‘I bring nothing. I want to have something given to me.’ Gretel

3	

3	3	presents Hans with a knife. ‘Goodbye, Gretel.’ ‘Goodbye, Hans.’ Hans

3	

3	3	takes the knife, sticks it in his sleeve, and goes home. ‘Good evening,

3	

3	3	mother.’ ‘Good evening, Hans. Where have you been?’ ‘With Gretel.’ What

3	

3	3	did you take her?’ ‘Took her nothing, she gave me something.’ ‘What did

3	

3	3	Gretel give you?’ ‘Gave me a knife.’ ‘Where is the knife, Hans?’ ‘Stuck

3	

3	3	in my sleeve.’ ‘That’s ill done, Hans, you should have put the knife in

3	

3	3	your pocket.’ ‘Never mind, will do better next time.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Whither away, Hans?’ ‘To Gretel, mother.’ ‘Behave well, Hans.’ ‘Oh,

3	

3	3	I’ll behave well. Goodbye, mother.’ ‘Goodbye, Hans.’ Hans comes to

3	

3	3	Gretel. ‘Good day, Gretel.’ ‘Good day, Hans. What good thing do you

3	

3	3	bring?’ ‘I bring nothing, I want something given me.’ Gretel presents

3	

3	3	Hans with a young goat. ‘Goodbye, Gretel.’ ‘Goodbye, Hans.’ Hans takes

3	

3	3	the goat, ties its legs, and puts it in his pocket. When he gets home it

3	

3	3	is suffocated. ‘Good evening, mother.’ ‘Good evening, Hans. Where have

3	

3	3	you been?’ ‘With Gretel.’ ‘What did you take her?’ ‘Took nothing, she

3	

3	3	gave me something.’ ‘What did Gretel give you?’ ‘She gave me a goat.’

3	

3	3	‘Where is the goat, Hans?’ ‘Put it in my pocket.’ ‘That was ill done,

3	

3	3	Hans, you should have put a rope round the goat’s neck.’ ‘Never mind,

3	

3	3	will do better next time.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Whither away, Hans?’ ‘To Gretel, mother.’ ‘Behave well, Hans.’ ‘Oh,

3	

3	3	I’ll behave well. Goodbye, mother.’ ‘Goodbye, Hans.’ Hans comes to

3	

3	3	Gretel. ‘Good day, Gretel.’ ‘Good day, Hans. What good thing do you

3	

3	3	bring?’ ‘I bring nothing, I want something given me.’ Gretel presents

3	

3	3	Hans with a piece of bacon. ‘Goodbye, Gretel.’ ‘Goodbye, Hans.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Hans takes the bacon, ties it to a rope, and drags it away behind him.

3	

3	3	The dogs come and devour the bacon. When he gets home, he has the rope

3	

3	3	in his hand, and there is no longer anything hanging on to it. ‘Good

3	

3	3	evening, mother.’ ‘Good evening, Hans. Where have you been?’ ‘With

3	

3	3	Gretel.’ ‘What did you take her?’ ‘I took her nothing, she gave me

3	

3	3	something.’ ‘What did Gretel give you?’ ‘Gave me a bit of bacon.’ ‘Where

3	

3	3	is the bacon, Hans?’ ‘I tied it to a rope, brought it home, dogs took

3	

3	3	it.’ ‘That was ill done, Hans, you should have carried the bacon on your

3	

3	3	head.’ ‘Never mind, will do better next time.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Whither away, Hans?’ ‘To Gretel, mother.’ ‘Behave well, Hans.’ ‘I’ll

3	

3	3	behave well. Goodbye, mother.’ ‘Goodbye, Hans.’ Hans comes to Gretel.

3	

3	3	‘Good day, Gretel.’ ‘Good day, Hans, What good thing do you bring?’ ‘I

3	

3	3	bring nothing, but would have something given.’ Gretel presents Hans

3	

3	3	with a calf. ‘Goodbye, Gretel.’ ‘Goodbye, Hans.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Hans takes the calf, puts it on his head, and the calf kicks his face.

3	

3	3	‘Good evening, mother.’ ‘Good evening, Hans. Where have you been?’ ‘With

3	

3	3	Gretel.’ ‘What did you take her?’ ‘I took nothing, but had something

3	

3	3	given me.’ ‘What did Gretel give you?’ ‘A calf.’ ‘Where have you the

3	

3	3	calf, Hans?’ ‘I set it on my head and it kicked my face.’ ‘That was

3	

3	3	ill done, Hans, you should have led the calf, and put it in the stall.’

3	

3	3	‘Never mind, will do better next time.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Whither away, Hans?’ ‘To Gretel, mother.’ ‘Behave well, Hans.’ ‘I’ll

3	

3	3	behave well. Goodbye, mother.’ ‘Goodbye, Hans.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Hans comes to Gretel. ‘Good day, Gretel.’ ‘Good day, Hans. What good

3	

3	3	thing do you bring?’ ‘I bring nothing, but would have something given.’

3	

3	3	Gretel says to Hans: ‘I will go with you.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Hans takes Gretel, ties her to a rope, leads her to the rack, and binds

3	

3	3	her fast. Then Hans goes to his mother. ‘Good evening, mother.’ ‘Good

3	

3	3	evening, Hans. Where have you been?’ ‘With Gretel.’ ‘What did you take

3	

3	3	her?’ ‘I took her nothing.’ ‘What did Gretel give you?’ ‘She gave me

3	

3	3	nothing, she came with me.’ ‘Where have you left Gretel?’ ‘I led her by

3	

3	3	the rope, tied her to the rack, and scattered some grass for her.’ ‘That

3	

3	3	was ill done, Hans, you should have cast friendly eyes on her.’ ‘Never

3	

3	3	mind, will do better.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Hans went into the stable, cut out all the calves’ and sheep’s eyes,

3	

3	3	and threw them in Gretel’s face. Then Gretel became angry, tore herself

3	

3	3	loose and ran away, and was no longer the bride of Hans.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE THREE LANGUAGES

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	An aged count once lived in Switzerland, who had an only son, but he

3	

3	3	was stupid, and could learn nothing. Then said the father: ‘Hark you,

3	

3	3	my son, try as I will I can get nothing into your head. You must go from

3	

3	3	hence, I will give you into the care of a celebrated master, who shall

3	

3	3	see what he can do with you.’ The youth was sent into a strange town,

3	

3	3	and remained a whole year with the master. At the end of this time,

3	

3	3	he came home again, and his father asked: ‘Now, my son, what have you

3	

3	3	learnt?’ ‘Father, I have learnt what the dogs say when they bark.’ ‘Lord

3	

3	3	have mercy on us!’ cried the father; ‘is that all you have learnt? I

3	

3	3	will send you into another town, to another master.’ The youth was taken

3	

3	3	thither, and stayed a year with this master likewise. When he came back

3	

3	3	the father again asked: ‘My son, what have you learnt?’ He answered:

3	

3	3	‘Father, I have learnt what the birds say.’ Then the father fell into a

3	

3	3	rage and said: ‘Oh, you lost man, you have spent the precious time and

3	

3	3	learnt nothing; are you not ashamed to appear before my eyes? I will

3	

3	3	send you to a third master, but if you learn nothing this time also, I

3	

3	3	will no longer be your father.’ The youth remained a whole year with the

3	

3	3	third master also, and when he came home again, and his father inquired:

3	

3	3	‘My son, what have you learnt?’ he answered: ‘Dear father, I have this

3	

3	3	year learnt what the frogs croak.’ Then the father fell into the most

3	

3	3	furious anger, sprang up, called his people thither, and said: ‘This man

3	

3	3	is no longer my son, I drive him forth, and command you to take him

3	

3	3	out into the forest, and kill him.’ They took him forth, but when they

3	

3	3	should have killed him, they could not do it for pity, and let him go,

3	

3	3	and they cut the eyes and tongue out of a deer that they might carry

3	

3	3	them to the old man as a token.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The youth wandered on, and after some time came to a fortress where he

3	

3	3	begged for a night’s lodging. ‘Yes,’ said the lord of the castle, ‘if

3	

3	3	you will pass the night down there in the old tower, go thither; but I

3	

3	3	warn you, it is at the peril of your life, for it is full of wild dogs,

3	

3	3	which bark and howl without stopping, and at certain hours a man has to

3	

3	3	be given to them, whom they at once devour.’ The whole district was in

3	

3	3	sorrow and dismay because of them, and yet no one could do anything to

3	

3	3	stop this. The youth, however, was without fear, and said: ‘Just let me

3	

3	3	go down to the barking dogs, and give me something that I can throw to

3	

3	3	them; they will do nothing to harm me.’ As he himself would have it so,

3	

3	3	they gave him some food for the wild animals, and led him down to the

3	

3	3	tower. When he went inside, the dogs did not bark at him, but wagged

3	

3	3	their tails quite amicably around him, ate what he set before them, and

3	

3	3	did not hurt one hair of his head. Next morning, to the astonishment of

3	

3	3	everyone, he came out again safe and unharmed, and said to the lord of

3	

3	3	the castle: ‘The dogs have revealed to me, in their own language, why

3	

3	3	they dwell there, and bring evil on the land. They are bewitched, and

3	

3	3	are obliged to watch over a great treasure which is below in the tower,

3	

3	3	and they can have no rest until it is taken away, and I have likewise

3	

3	3	learnt, from their discourse, how that is to be done.’ Then all who

3	

3	3	heard this rejoiced, and the lord of the castle said he would adopt him

3	

3	3	as a son if he accomplished it successfully. He went down again, and

3	

3	3	as he knew what he had to do, he did it thoroughly, and brought a chest

3	

3	3	full of gold out with him. The howling of the wild dogs was henceforth

3	

3	3	heard no more; they had disappeared, and the country was freed from the

3	

3	3	trouble.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	After some time he took it in his head that he would travel to Rome. On

3	

3	3	the way he passed by a marsh, in which a number of frogs were sitting

3	

3	3	croaking. He listened to them, and when he became aware of what they

3	

3	3	were saying, he grew very thoughtful and sad. At last he arrived in

3	

3	3	Rome, where the Pope had just died, and there was great doubt among

3	

3	3	the cardinals as to whom they should appoint as his successor. They at

3	

3	3	length agreed that the person should be chosen as pope who should be

3	

3	3	distinguished by some divine and miraculous token. And just as that was

3	

3	3	decided on, the young count entered into the church, and suddenly two

3	

3	3	snow-white doves flew on his shoulders and remained sitting there. The

3	

3	3	ecclesiastics recognized therein the token from above, and asked him on

3	

3	3	the spot if he would be pope. He was undecided, and knew not if he were

3	

3	3	worthy of this, but the doves counselled him to do it, and at length he

3	

3	3	said yes. Then was he anointed and consecrated, and thus was fulfilled

3	

3	3	what he had heard from the frogs on his way, which had so affected him,

3	

3	3	that he was to be his Holiness the Pope. Then he had to sing a mass, and

3	

3	3	did not know one word of it, but the two doves sat continually on his

3	

3	3	shoulders, and said it all in his ear.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE FOX AND THE CAT

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	It happened that the cat met the fox in a forest, and as she thought to

3	

3	3	herself: ‘He is clever and full of experience, and much esteemed in the

3	

3	3	world,’ she spoke to him in a friendly way. ‘Good day, dear Mr Fox,

3	

3	3	how are you? How is all with you? How are you getting on in these hard

3	

3	3	times?’ The fox, full of all kinds of arrogance, looked at the cat from

3	

3	3	head to foot, and for a long time did not know whether he would give

3	

3	3	any answer or not. At last he said: ‘Oh, you wretched beard-cleaner, you

3	

3	3	piebald fool, you hungry mouse-hunter, what can you be thinking of? Have

3	

3	3	you the cheek to ask how I am getting on? What have you learnt? How

3	

3	3	many arts do you understand?’ ‘I understand but one,’ replied the

3	

3	3	cat, modestly. ‘What art is that?’ asked the fox. ‘When the hounds are

3	

3	3	following me, I can spring into a tree and save myself.’ ‘Is that all?’

3	

3	3	said the fox. ‘I am master of a hundred arts, and have into the bargain

3	

3	3	a sackful of cunning. You make me sorry for you; come with me, I will

3	

3	3	teach you how people get away from the hounds.’ Just then came a hunter

3	

3	3	with four dogs. The cat sprang nimbly up a tree, and sat down at the top

3	

3	3	of it, where the branches and foliage quite concealed her. ‘Open your

3	

3	3	sack, Mr Fox, open your sack,’ cried the cat to him, but the dogs had

3	

3	3	already seized him, and were holding him fast. ‘Ah, Mr Fox,’ cried the

3	

3	3	cat. ‘You with your hundred arts are left in the lurch! Had you been

3	

3	3	able to climb like me, you would not have lost your life.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE FOUR CLEVER BROTHERS

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Dear children,’ said a poor man to his four sons, ‘I have nothing to

3	

3	3	give you; you must go out into the wide world and try your luck. Begin

3	

3	3	by learning some craft or another, and see how you can get on.’ So the

3	

3	3	four brothers took their walking-sticks in their hands, and their little

3	

3	3	bundles on their shoulders, and after bidding their father goodbye, went

3	

3	3	all out at the gate together. When they had got on some way they came

3	

3	3	to four crossways, each leading to a different country. Then the eldest

3	

3	3	said, ‘Here we must part; but this day four years we will come back

3	

3	3	to this spot, and in the meantime each must try what he can do for

3	

3	3	himself.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So each brother went his way; and as the eldest was hastening on a man

3	

3	3	met him, and asked him where he was going, and what he wanted. ‘I am
