3	

3	3	going to try my luck in the world, and should like to begin by learning

3	

3	3	some art or trade,’ answered he. ‘Then,’ said the man, ‘go with me, and

3	

3	3	I will teach you to become the cunningest thief that ever was.’ ‘No,’

3	

3	3	said the other, ‘that is not an honest calling, and what can one look

3	

3	3	to earn by it in the end but the gallows?’ ‘Oh!’ said the man, ‘you need

3	

3	3	not fear the gallows; for I will only teach you to steal what will be

3	

3	3	fair game: I meddle with nothing but what no one else can get or care

3	

3	3	anything about, and where no one can find you out.’ So the young man

3	

3	3	agreed to follow his trade, and he soon showed himself so clever, that

3	

3	3	nothing could escape him that he had once set his mind upon.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The second brother also met a man, who, when he found out what he was

3	

3	3	setting out upon, asked him what craft he meant to follow. ‘I do not

3	

3	3	know yet,’ said he. ‘Then come with me, and be a star-gazer. It is a

3	

3	3	noble art, for nothing can be hidden from you, when once you understand

3	

3	3	the stars.’ The plan pleased him much, and he soon became such a skilful

3	

3	3	star-gazer, that when he had served out his time, and wanted to leave

3	

3	3	his master, he gave him a glass, and said, ‘With this you can see all

3	

3	3	that is passing in the sky and on earth, and nothing can be hidden from

3	

3	3	you.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The third brother met a huntsman, who took him with him, and taught him

3	

3	3	so well all that belonged to hunting, that he became very clever in the

3	

3	3	craft of the woods; and when he left his master he gave him a bow, and

3	

3	3	said, ‘Whatever you shoot at with this bow you will be sure to hit.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The youngest brother likewise met a man who asked him what he wished to

3	

3	3	do. ‘Would not you like,’ said he, ‘to be a tailor?’ ‘Oh, no!’ said

3	

3	3	the young man; ‘sitting cross-legged from morning to night, working

3	

3	3	backwards and forwards with a needle and goose, will never suit me.’

3	

3	3	‘Oh!’ answered the man, ‘that is not my sort of tailoring; come with me,

3	

3	3	and you will learn quite another kind of craft from that.’ Not knowing

3	

3	3	what better to do, he came into the plan, and learnt tailoring from the

3	

3	3	beginning; and when he left his master, he gave him a needle, and said,

3	

3	3	‘You can sew anything with this, be it as soft as an egg or as hard as

3	

3	3	steel; and the joint will be so fine that no seam will be seen.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	After the space of four years, at the time agreed upon, the four

3	

3	3	brothers met at the four cross-roads; and having welcomed each other,

3	

3	3	set off towards their father’s home, where they told him all that had

3	

3	3	happened to them, and how each had learned some craft.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then, one day, as they were sitting before the house under a very high

3	

3	3	tree, the father said, ‘I should like to try what each of you can do in

3	

3	3	this way.’ So he looked up, and said to the second son, ‘At the top of

3	

3	3	this tree there is a chaffinch’s nest; tell me how many eggs there are

3	

3	3	in it.’ The star-gazer took his glass, looked up, and said, ‘Five.’

3	

3	3	‘Now,’ said the father to the eldest son, ‘take away the eggs without

3	

3	3	letting the bird that is sitting upon them and hatching them know

3	

3	3	anything of what you are doing.’ So the cunning thief climbed up the

3	

3	3	tree, and brought away to his father the five eggs from under the bird;

3	

3	3	and it never saw or felt what he was doing, but kept sitting on at its

3	

3	3	ease. Then the father took the eggs, and put one on each corner of the

3	

3	3	table, and the fifth in the middle, and said to the huntsman, ‘Cut all

3	

3	3	the eggs in two pieces at one shot.’ The huntsman took up his bow, and

3	

3	3	at one shot struck all the five eggs as his father wished.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Now comes your turn,’ said he to the young tailor; ‘sew the eggs and

3	

3	3	the young birds in them together again, so neatly that the shot shall

3	

3	3	have done them no harm.’ Then the tailor took his needle, and sewed the

3	

3	3	eggs as he was told; and when he had done, the thief was sent to take

3	

3	3	them back to the nest, and put them under the bird without its knowing

3	

3	3	it. Then she went on sitting, and hatched them: and in a few days they

3	

3	3	crawled out, and had only a little red streak across their necks, where

3	

3	3	the tailor had sewn them together.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Well done, sons!’ said the old man; ‘you have made good use of your

3	

3	3	time, and learnt something worth the knowing; but I am sure I do not

3	

3	3	know which ought to have the prize. Oh, that a time might soon come for

3	

3	3	you to turn your skill to some account!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Not long after this there was a great bustle in the country; for the

3	

3	3	king’s daughter had been carried off by a mighty dragon, and the king

3	

3	3	mourned over his loss day and night, and made it known that whoever

3	

3	3	brought her back to him should have her for a wife. Then the four

3	

3	3	brothers said to each other, ‘Here is a chance for us; let us try

3	

3	3	what we can do.’ And they agreed to see whether they could not set the

3	

3	3	princess free. ‘I will soon find out where she is, however,’ said the

3	

3	3	star-gazer, as he looked through his glass; and he soon cried out, ‘I

3	

3	3	see her afar off, sitting upon a rock in the sea, and I can spy the

3	

3	3	dragon close by, guarding her.’ Then he went to the king, and asked for

3	

3	3	a ship for himself and his brothers; and they sailed together over the

3	

3	3	sea, till they came to the right place. There they found the princess

3	

3	3	sitting, as the star-gazer had said, on the rock; and the dragon was

3	

3	3	lying asleep, with his head upon her lap. ‘I dare not shoot at him,’

3	

3	3	said the huntsman, ‘for I should kill the beautiful young lady also.’

3	

3	3	‘Then I will try my skill,’ said the thief, and went and stole her away

3	

3	3	from under the dragon, so quietly and gently that the beast did not know

3	

3	3	it, but went on snoring.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then away they hastened with her full of joy in their boat towards the

3	

3	3	ship; but soon came the dragon roaring behind them through the air; for

3	

3	3	he awoke and missed the princess. But when he got over the boat, and

3	

3	3	wanted to pounce upon them and carry off the princess, the huntsman took

3	

3	3	up his bow and shot him straight through the heart so that he fell down

3	

3	3	dead. They were still not safe; for he was such a great beast that in

3	

3	3	his fall he overset the boat, and they had to swim in the open sea

3	

3	3	upon a few planks. So the tailor took his needle, and with a few large

3	

3	3	stitches put some of the planks together; and he sat down upon these,

3	

3	3	and sailed about and gathered up all pieces of the boat; and then tacked

3	

3	3	them together so quickly that the boat was soon ready, and they then

3	

3	3	reached the ship and got home safe.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When they had brought home the princess to her father, there was great

3	

3	3	rejoicing; and he said to the four brothers, ‘One of you shall marry

3	

3	3	her, but you must settle amongst yourselves which it is to be.’ Then

3	

3	3	there arose a quarrel between them; and the star-gazer said, ‘If I had

3	

3	3	not found the princess out, all your skill would have been of no use;

3	

3	3	therefore she ought to be mine.’ ‘Your seeing her would have been of

3	

3	3	no use,’ said the thief, ‘if I had not taken her away from the dragon;

3	

3	3	therefore she ought to be mine.’ ‘No, she is mine,’ said the huntsman;

3	

3	3	‘for if I had not killed the dragon, he would, after all, have torn you

3	

3	3	and the princess into pieces.’ ‘And if I had not sewn the boat together

3	

3	3	again,’ said the tailor, ‘you would all have been drowned, therefore she

3	

3	3	is mine.’ Then the king put in a word, and said, ‘Each of you is right;

3	

3	3	and as all cannot have the young lady, the best way is for neither of

3	

3	3	you to have her: for the truth is, there is somebody she likes a great

3	

3	3	deal better. But to make up for your loss, I will give each of you, as a

3	

3	3	reward for his skill, half a kingdom.’ So the brothers agreed that this

3	

3	3	plan would be much better than either quarrelling or marrying a lady who

3	

3	3	had no mind to have them. And the king then gave to each half a kingdom,

3	

3	3	as he had said; and they lived very happily the rest of their days, and

3	

3	3	took good care of their father; and somebody took better care of the

3	

3	3	young lady, than to let either the dragon or one of the craftsmen have

3	

3	3	her again.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	LILY AND THE LION

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	A merchant, who had three daughters, was once setting out upon a

3	

3	3	journey; but before he went he asked each daughter what gift he should

3	

3	3	bring back for her. The eldest wished for pearls; the second for jewels;

3	

3	3	but the third, who was called Lily, said, ‘Dear father, bring me a

3	

3	3	rose.’ Now it was no easy task to find a rose, for it was the middle

3	

3	3	of winter; yet as she was his prettiest daughter, and was very fond of

3	

3	3	flowers, her father said he would try what he could do. So he kissed all

3	

3	3	three, and bid them goodbye.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And when the time came for him to go home, he had bought pearls and

3	

3	3	jewels for the two eldest, but he had sought everywhere in vain for the

3	

3	3	rose; and when he went into any garden and asked for such a thing, the

3	

3	3	people laughed at him, and asked him whether he thought roses grew in

3	

3	3	snow. This grieved him very much, for Lily was his dearest child; and as

3	

3	3	he was journeying home, thinking what he should bring her, he came to a

3	

3	3	fine castle; and around the castle was a garden, in one half of which it

3	

3	3	seemed to be summer-time and in the other half winter. On one side the

3	

3	3	finest flowers were in full bloom, and on the other everything looked

3	

3	3	dreary and buried in the snow. ‘A lucky hit!’ said he, as he called to

3	

3	3	his servant, and told him to go to a beautiful bed of roses that was

3	

3	3	there, and bring him away one of the finest flowers.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	This done, they were riding away well pleased, when up sprang a fierce

3	

3	3	lion, and roared out, ‘Whoever has stolen my roses shall be eaten up

3	

3	3	alive!’ Then the man said, ‘I knew not that the garden belonged to you;

3	

3	3	can nothing save my life?’ ‘No!’ said the lion, ‘nothing, unless you

3	

3	3	undertake to give me whatever meets you on your return home; if you

3	

3	3	agree to this, I will give you your life, and the rose too for your

3	

3	3	daughter.’ But the man was unwilling to do so and said, ‘It may be my

3	

3	3	youngest daughter, who loves me most, and always runs to meet me when

3	

3	3	I go home.’ Then the servant was greatly frightened, and said, ‘It may

3	

3	3	perhaps be only a cat or a dog.’ And at last the man yielded with a

3	

3	3	heavy heart, and took the rose; and said he would give the lion whatever

3	

3	3	should meet him first on his return.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And as he came near home, it was Lily, his youngest and dearest

3	

3	3	daughter, that met him; she came running, and kissed him, and welcomed

3	

3	3	him home; and when she saw that he had brought her the rose, she was

3	

3	3	still more glad. But her father began to be very sorrowful, and to weep,

3	

3	3	saying, ‘Alas, my dearest child! I have bought this flower at a high

3	

3	3	price, for I have said I would give you to a wild lion; and when he has

3	

3	3	you, he will tear you in pieces, and eat you.’ Then he told her all that

3	

3	3	had happened, and said she should not go, let what would happen.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But she comforted him, and said, ‘Dear father, the word you have given

3	

3	3	must be kept; I will go to the lion, and soothe him: perhaps he will let

3	

3	3	me come safe home again.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The next morning she asked the way she was to go, and took leave of her

3	

3	3	father, and went forth with a bold heart into the wood. But the lion was

3	

3	3	an enchanted prince. By day he and all his court were lions, but in the

3	

3	3	evening they took their right forms again. And when Lily came to the

3	

3	3	castle, he welcomed her so courteously that she agreed to marry him. The

3	

3	3	wedding-feast was held, and they lived happily together a long time. The

3	

3	3	prince was only to be seen as soon as evening came, and then he held his

3	

3	3	court; but every morning he left his bride, and went away by himself,

3	

3	3	she knew not whither, till the night came again.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	After some time he said to her, ‘Tomorrow there will be a great feast in

3	

3	3	your father’s house, for your eldest sister is to be married; and if

3	

3	3	you wish to go and visit her my lions shall lead you thither.’ Then she

3	

3	3	rejoiced much at the thoughts of seeing her father once more, and set

3	

3	3	out with the lions; and everyone was overjoyed to see her, for they had

3	

3	3	thought her dead long since. But she told them how happy she was, and

3	

3	3	stayed till the feast was over, and then went back to the wood.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Her second sister was soon after married, and when Lily was asked to

3	

3	3	go to the wedding, she said to the prince, ‘I will not go alone this

3	

3	3	time--you must go with me.’ But he would not, and said that it would be

3	

3	3	a very hazardous thing; for if the least ray of the torch-light should

3	

3	3	fall upon him his enchantment would become still worse, for he should be

3	

3	3	changed into a dove, and be forced to wander about the world for seven

3	

3	3	long years. However, she gave him no rest, and said she would take care

3	

3	3	no light should fall upon him. So at last they set out together, and

3	

3	3	took with them their little child; and she chose a large hall with thick

3	

3	3	walls for him to sit in while the wedding-torches were lighted; but,

3	

3	3	unluckily, no one saw that there was a crack in the door. Then the

3	

3	3	wedding was held with great pomp, but as the train came from the church,

3	

3	3	and passed with the torches before the hall, a very small ray of light

3	

3	3	fell upon the prince. In a moment he disappeared, and when his wife came

3	

3	3	in and looked for him, she found only a white dove; and it said to her,

3	

3	3	‘Seven years must I fly up and down over the face of the earth, but

3	

3	3	every now and then I will let fall a white feather, that will show you

3	

3	3	the way I am going; follow it, and at last you may overtake and set me

3	

3	3	free.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	This said, he flew out at the door, and poor Lily followed; and every

3	

3	3	now and then a white feather fell, and showed her the way she was to

3	

3	3	journey. Thus she went roving on through the wide world, and looked

3	

3	3	neither to the right hand nor to the left, nor took any rest, for seven

3	

3	3	years. Then she began to be glad, and thought to herself that the time

3	

3	3	was fast coming when all her troubles should end; yet repose was still

3	

3	3	far off, for one day as she was travelling on she missed the white

3	

3	3	feather, and when she lifted up her eyes she could nowhere see the dove.

3	

3	3	‘Now,’ thought she to herself, ‘no aid of man can be of use to me.’ So

3	

3	3	she went to the sun and said, ‘Thou shinest everywhere, on the hill’s

3	

3	3	top and the valley’s depth--hast thou anywhere seen my white dove?’

3	

3	3	‘No,’ said the sun, ‘I have not seen it; but I will give thee a

3	

3	3	casket--open it when thy hour of need comes.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So she thanked the sun, and went on her way till eventide; and when

3	

3	3	the moon arose, she cried unto it, and said, ‘Thou shinest through the

3	

3	3	night, over field and grove--hast thou nowhere seen my white dove?’

3	

3	3	‘No,’ said the moon, ‘I cannot help thee but I will give thee an

3	

3	3	egg--break it when need comes.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then she thanked the moon, and went on till the night-wind blew; and she

3	

3	3	raised up her voice to it, and said, ‘Thou blowest through every tree

3	

3	3	and under every leaf--hast thou not seen my white dove?’ ‘No,’ said the

3	

3	3	night-wind, ‘but I will ask three other winds; perhaps they have seen

3	

3	3	it.’ Then the east wind and the west wind came, and said they too had

3	

3	3	not seen it, but the south wind said, ‘I have seen the white dove--he

3	

3	3	has fled to the Red Sea, and is changed once more into a lion, for the

3	

3	3	seven years are passed away, and there he is fighting with a dragon;

3	

3	3	and the dragon is an enchanted princess, who seeks to separate him from

3	

3	3	you.’ Then the night-wind said, ‘I will give thee counsel. Go to the

3	

3	3	Red Sea; on the right shore stand many rods--count them, and when thou

3	

3	3	comest to the eleventh, break it off, and smite the dragon with it; and

3	

3	3	so the lion will have the victory, and both of them will appear to you

3	

3	3	in their own forms. Then look round and thou wilt see a griffin, winged

3	

3	3	like bird, sitting by the Red Sea; jump on to his back with thy beloved

3	

3	3	one as quickly as possible, and he will carry you over the waters to

3	

3	3	your home. I will also give thee this nut,’ continued the night-wind.

3	

3	3	‘When you are half-way over, throw it down, and out of the waters will

3	

3	3	immediately spring up a high nut-tree on which the griffin will be able

3	

3	3	to rest, otherwise he would not have the strength to bear you the whole

3	

3	3	way; if, therefore, thou dost forget to throw down the nut, he will let

3	

3	3	you both fall into the sea.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So our poor wanderer went forth, and found all as the night-wind had

3	

3	3	said; and she plucked the eleventh rod, and smote the dragon, and the

3	

3	3	lion forthwith became a prince, and the dragon a princess again. But

3	

3	3	no sooner was the princess released from the spell, than she seized

3	

3	3	the prince by the arm and sprang on to the griffin’s back, and went off

3	

3	3	carrying the prince away with her.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Thus the unhappy traveller was again forsaken and forlorn; but she

3	

3	3	took heart and said, ‘As far as the wind blows, and so long as the cock

3	

3	3	crows, I will journey on, till I find him once again.’ She went on for

3	

3	3	a long, long way, till at length she came to the castle whither the

3	

3	3	princess had carried the prince; and there was a feast got ready, and

3	

3	3	she heard that the wedding was about to be held. ‘Heaven aid me now!’

3	

3	3	said she; and she took the casket that the sun had given her, and found

3	

3	3	that within it lay a dress as dazzling as the sun itself. So she put it

3	

3	3	on, and went into the palace, and all the people gazed upon her; and

3	

3	3	the dress pleased the bride so much that she asked whether it was to be

3	

3	3	sold. ‘Not for gold and silver.’ said she, ‘but for flesh and blood.’

3	

3	3	The princess asked what she meant, and she said, ‘Let me speak with the

3	

3	3	bridegroom this night in his chamber, and I will give thee the dress.’

3	

3	3	At last the princess agreed, but she told her chamberlain to give the

3	

3	3	prince a sleeping draught, that he might not hear or see her. When

3	

3	3	evening came, and the prince had fallen asleep, she was led into

3	

3	3	his chamber, and she sat herself down at his feet, and said: ‘I have

3	

3	3	followed thee seven years. I have been to the sun, the moon, and the

3	

3	3	night-wind, to seek thee, and at last I have helped thee to overcome

3	

3	3	the dragon. Wilt thou then forget me quite?’ But the prince all the time

3	

3	3	slept so soundly, that her voice only passed over him, and seemed like

3	

3	3	the whistling of the wind among the fir-trees.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then poor Lily was led away, and forced to give up the golden dress; and

3	

3	3	when she saw that there was no help for her, she went out into a meadow,

3	

3	3	and sat herself down and wept. But as she sat she bethought herself of

3	

3	3	the egg that the moon had given her; and when she broke it, there ran

3	

3	3	out a hen and twelve chickens of pure gold, that played about, and then

3	

3	3	nestled under the old one’s wings, so as to form the most beautiful

3	

3	3	sight in the world. And she rose up and drove them before her, till the

3	

3	3	bride saw them from her window, and was so pleased that she came forth

3	

3	3	and asked her if she would sell the brood. ‘Not for gold or silver, but

3	

3	3	for flesh and blood: let me again this evening speak with the bridegroom

3	

3	3	in his chamber, and I will give thee the whole brood.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the princess thought to betray her as before, and agreed to

3	

3	3	what she asked: but when the prince went to his chamber he asked

3	

3	3	the chamberlain why the wind had whistled so in the night. And the

3	

3	3	chamberlain told him all--how he had given him a sleeping draught, and

3	

3	3	how a poor maiden had come and spoken to him in his chamber, and was

3	

3	3	to come again that night. Then the prince took care to throw away the

3	

3	3	sleeping draught; and when Lily came and began again to tell him what

3	

3	3	woes had befallen her, and how faithful and true to him she had been,

3	

3	3	he knew his beloved wife’s voice, and sprang up, and said, ‘You have

3	

3	3	awakened me as from a dream, for the strange princess had thrown a spell

3	

3	3	around me, so that I had altogether forgotten you; but Heaven hath sent

3	

3	3	you to me in a lucky hour.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And they stole away out of the palace by night unawares, and seated

3	

3	3	themselves on the griffin, who flew back with them over the Red Sea.

3	

3	3	When they were half-way across Lily let the nut fall into the water,

3	

3	3	and immediately a large nut-tree arose from the sea, whereon the griffin

3	

3	3	rested for a while, and then carried them safely home. There they found

3	

3	3	their child, now grown up to be comely and fair; and after all their

3	

3	3	troubles they lived happily together to the end of their days.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE FOX AND THE HORSE

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	A farmer had a horse that had been an excellent faithful servant to

3	

3	3	him: but he was now grown too old to work; so the farmer would give him

3	

3	3	nothing more to eat, and said, ‘I want you no longer, so take yourself

3	

3	3	off out of my stable; I shall not take you back again until you are

3	

3	3	stronger than a lion.’ Then he opened the door and turned him adrift.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The poor horse was very melancholy, and wandered up and down in the

3	

3	3	wood, seeking some little shelter from the cold wind and rain. Presently

3	

3	3	a fox met him: ‘What’s the matter, my friend?’ said he, ‘why do you hang

3	

3	3	down your head and look so lonely and woe-begone?’ ‘Ah!’ replied the

3	

3	3	horse, ‘justice and avarice never dwell in one house; my master has

3	

3	3	forgotten all that I have done for him so many years, and because I

3	

3	3	can no longer work he has turned me adrift, and says unless I become

3	

3	3	stronger than a lion he will not take me back again; what chance can I

3	

3	3	have of that? he knows I have none, or he would not talk so.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	However, the fox bid him be of good cheer, and said, ‘I will help you;

3	

3	3	lie down there, stretch yourself out quite stiff, and pretend to be

3	

3	3	dead.’ The horse did as he was told, and the fox went straight to the

3	

3	3	lion who lived in a cave close by, and said to him, ‘A little way off

3	

3	3	lies a dead horse; come with me and you may make an excellent meal of

3	

3	3	his carcase.’ The lion was greatly pleased, and set off immediately; and

3	

3	3	when they came to the horse, the fox said, ‘You will not be able to eat

3	

3	3	him comfortably here; I’ll tell you what--I will tie you fast to

3	

3	3	his tail, and then you can draw him to your den, and eat him at your

3	

3	3	leisure.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	This advice pleased the lion, so he laid himself down quietly for the

3	

3	3	fox to make him fast to the horse. But the fox managed to tie his legs

3	

3	3	together and bound all so hard and fast that with all his strength he

3	

3	3	could not set himself free. When the work was done, the fox clapped the

3	

3	3	horse on the shoulder, and said, ‘Jip! Dobbin! Jip!’ Then up he sprang,

3	

3	3	and moved off, dragging the lion behind him. The beast began to roar

3	

3	3	and bellow, till all the birds of the wood flew away for fright; but the

3	

3	3	horse let him sing on, and made his way quietly over the fields to his

3	

3	3	master’s house.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Here he is, master,’ said he, ‘I have got the better of him’: and when

3	

3	3	the farmer saw his old servant, his heart relented, and he said. ‘Thou

3	

3	3	shalt stay in thy stable and be well taken care of.’ And so the poor old

3	

3	3	horse had plenty to eat, and lived--till he died.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE BLUE LIGHT

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once upon a time a soldier who for many years had served the

3	

3	3	king faithfully, but when the war came to an end could serve no longer

3	

3	3	because of the many wounds which he had received. The king said to him:

3	

3	3	‘You may return to your home, I need you no longer, and you will not

3	

3	3	receive any more money, for he only receives wages who renders me

3	

3	3	service for them.’ Then the soldier did not know how to earn a living,

3	

3	3	went away greatly troubled, and walked the whole day, until in the

3	

3	3	evening he entered a forest. When darkness came on, he saw a light,

3	

3	3	which he went up to, and came to a house wherein lived a witch. ‘Do give

3	

3	3	me one night’s lodging, and a little to eat and drink,’ said he to

3	

3	3	her, ‘or I shall starve.’ ‘Oho!’ she answered, ‘who gives anything to a

3	

3	3	run-away soldier? Yet will I be compassionate, and take you in, if you

3	

3	3	will do what I wish.’ ‘What do you wish?’ said the soldier. ‘That you

3	

3	3	should dig all round my garden for me, tomorrow.’ The soldier consented,

3	

3	3	and next day laboured with all his strength, but could not finish it by

3	

3	3	the evening. ‘I see well enough,’ said the witch, ‘that you can do no

3	

3	3	more today, but I will keep you yet another night, in payment for

3	

3	3	which you must tomorrow chop me a load of wood, and chop it small.’ The

3	

3	3	soldier spent the whole day in doing it, and in the evening the witch

3	

3	3	proposed that he should stay one night more. ‘Tomorrow, you shall only

3	

3	3	do me a very trifling piece of work. Behind my house, there is an old

3	

3	3	dry well, into which my light has fallen, it burns blue, and never goes

3	

3	3	out, and you shall bring it up again.’ Next day the old woman took him

3	

3	3	to the well, and let him down in a basket. He found the blue light, and

3	

3	3	made her a signal to draw him up again. She did draw him up, but when he

3	

3	3	came near the edge, she stretched down her hand and wanted to take the

3	

3	3	blue light away from him. ‘No,’ said he, perceiving her evil intention,

3	

3	3	‘I will not give you the light until I am standing with both feet upon

3	

3	3	the ground.’ The witch fell into a passion, let him fall again into the

3	

3	3	well, and went away.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The poor soldier fell without injury on the moist ground, and the blue

3	

3	3	light went on burning, but of what use was that to him? He saw very well

3	

3	3	that he could not escape death. He sat for a while very sorrowfully,

3	

3	3	then suddenly he felt in his pocket and found his tobacco pipe, which

3	

3	3	was still half full. ‘This shall be my last pleasure,’ thought he,

3	

3	3	pulled it out, lit it at the blue light and began to smoke. When the

3	

3	3	smoke had circled about the cavern, suddenly a little black dwarf stood

3	

3	3	before him, and said: ‘Lord, what are your commands?’ ‘What my commands

3	

3	3	are?’ replied the soldier, quite astonished. ‘I must do everything you

3	

3	3	bid me,’ said the little man. ‘Good,’ said the soldier; ‘then in the

3	

3	3	first place help me out of this well.’ The little man took him by the

3	

3	3	hand, and led him through an underground passage, but he did not forget

3	

3	3	to take the blue light with him. On the way the dwarf showed him the

3	

3	3	treasures which the witch had collected and hidden there, and the

3	

3	3	soldier took as much gold as he could carry. When he was above, he said

3	

3	3	to the little man: ‘Now go and bind the old witch, and carry her before

3	

3	3	the judge.’ In a short time she came by like the wind, riding on a wild

3	

3	3	tom-cat and screaming frightfully. Nor was it long before the little man

3	

3	3	reappeared. ‘It is all done,’ said he, ‘and the witch is already hanging

3	

3	3	on the gallows. What further commands has my lord?’ inquired the dwarf.

3	

3	3	‘At this moment, none,’ answered the soldier; ‘you can return home, only

3	

3	3	be at hand immediately, if I summon you.’ ‘Nothing more is needed than

3	

3	3	that you should light your pipe at the blue light, and I will appear

3	

3	3	before you at once.’ Thereupon he vanished from his sight.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The soldier returned to the town from which he came. He went to the

3	

3	3	best inn, ordered himself handsome clothes, and then bade the landlord

3	

3	3	furnish him a room as handsome as possible. When it was ready and the

3	

3	3	soldier had taken possession of it, he summoned the little black manikin

3	

3	3	and said: ‘I have served the king faithfully, but he has dismissed me,

3	

3	3	and left me to hunger, and now I want to take my revenge.’ ‘What am I to

3	

3	3	do?’ asked the little man. ‘Late at night, when the king’s daughter is

3	

3	3	in bed, bring her here in her sleep, she shall do servant’s work for

3	

3	3	me.’ The manikin said: ‘That is an easy thing for me to do, but a very

3	

3	3	dangerous thing for you, for if it is discovered, you will fare ill.’

3	

3	3	When twelve o’clock had struck, the door sprang open, and the manikin

3	

3	3	carried in the princess. ‘Aha! are you there?’ cried the soldier, ‘get

3	

3	3	to your work at once! Fetch the broom and sweep the chamber.’ When

3	

3	3	she had done this, he ordered her to come to his chair, and then he

3	

3	3	stretched out his feet and said: ‘Pull off my boots,’ and then he

3	

3	3	threw them in her face, and made her pick them up again, and clean

3	

3	3	and brighten them. She, however, did everything he bade her, without

3	

3	3	opposition, silently and with half-shut eyes. When the first cock

3	

3	3	crowed, the manikin carried her back to the royal palace, and laid her

3	

3	3	in her bed.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Next morning when the princess arose she went to her father, and told

3	

3	3	him that she had had a very strange dream. ‘I was carried through the

3	

3	3	streets with the rapidity of lightning,’ said she, ‘and taken into a

3	

3	3	soldier’s room, and I had to wait upon him like a servant, sweep his

3	

3	3	room, clean his boots, and do all kinds of menial work. It was only a

3	

3	3	dream, and yet I am just as tired as if I really had done everything.’

3	

3	3	‘The dream may have been true,’ said the king. ‘I will give you a piece

3	

3	3	of advice. Fill your pocket full of peas, and make a small hole in the

3	

3	3	pocket, and then if you are carried away again, they will fall out and

3	

3	3	leave a track in the streets.’ But unseen by the king, the manikin was

3	

3	3	standing beside him when he said that, and heard all. At night when

3	

3	3	the sleeping princess was again carried through the streets, some peas

3	

3	3	certainly did fall out of her pocket, but they made no track, for the

3	

3	3	crafty manikin had just before scattered peas in every street there

3	

3	3	was. And again the princess was compelled to do servant’s work until

3	

3	3	cock-crow.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Next morning the king sent his people out to seek the track, but it was

3	

3	3	all in vain, for in every street poor children were sitting, picking up

3	

3	3	peas, and saying: ‘It must have rained peas, last night.’ ‘We must think

3	

3	3	of something else,’ said the king; ‘keep your shoes on when you go to

3	

3	3	bed, and before you come back from the place where you are taken, hide

3	

3	3	one of them there, I will soon contrive to find it.’ The black manikin

3	

3	3	heard this plot, and at night when the soldier again ordered him to

3	

3	3	bring the princess, revealed it to him, and told him that he knew of no

3	

3	3	expedient to counteract this stratagem, and that if the shoe were found

3	

3	3	in the soldier’s house it would go badly with him. ‘Do what I bid you,’

3	

3	3	replied the soldier, and again this third night the princess was obliged

3	

3	3	to work like a servant, but before she went away, she hid her shoe under

3	

3	3	the bed.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Next morning the king had the entire town searched for his daughter’s

3	

3	3	shoe. It was found at the soldier’s, and the soldier himself, who at the

3	

3	3	entreaty of the dwarf had gone outside the gate, was soon brought back,

3	

3	3	and thrown into prison. In his flight he had forgotten the most valuable

3	

3	3	things he had, the blue light and the gold, and had only one ducat in

3	

3	3	his pocket. And now loaded with chains, he was standing at the window of

3	

3	3	his dungeon, when he chanced to see one of his comrades passing by. The

3	

3	3	soldier tapped at the pane of glass, and when this man came up, said to

3	

3	3	him: ‘Be so kind as to fetch me the small bundle I have left lying in

3	

3	3	the inn, and I will give you a ducat for doing it.’ His comrade ran

3	

3	3	thither and brought him what he wanted. As soon as the soldier was alone

3	

3	3	again, he lighted his pipe and summoned the black manikin. ‘Have no

3	

3	3	fear,’ said the latter to his master. ‘Go wheresoever they take you, and

3	

3	3	let them do what they will, only take the blue light with you.’ Next day

3	

3	3	the soldier was tried, and though he had done nothing wicked, the judge

3	

3	3	condemned him to death. When he was led forth to die, he begged a last

3	

3	3	favour of the king. ‘What is it?’ asked the king. ‘That I may smoke one

3	

3	3	more pipe on my way.’ ‘You may smoke three,’ answered the king, ‘but do

3	

3	3	not imagine that I will spare your life.’ Then the soldier pulled out

3	

3	3	his pipe and lighted it at the blue light, and as soon as a few wreaths

3	

3	3	of smoke had ascended, the manikin was there with a small cudgel in his

3	

3	3	hand, and said: ‘What does my lord command?’ ‘Strike down to earth that

3	

3	3	false judge there, and his constable, and spare not the king who has

3	

3	3	treated me so ill.’ Then the manikin fell on them like lightning,

3	

3	3	darting this way and that way, and whosoever was so much as touched by

3	

3	3	his cudgel fell to earth, and did not venture to stir again. The king

3	

3	3	was terrified; he threw himself on the soldier’s mercy, and merely to

3	

3	3	be allowed to live at all, gave him his kingdom for his own, and his

3	

3	3	daughter to wife.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE RAVEN

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once a queen who had a little daughter, still too young to run

3	

3	3	alone. One day the child was very troublesome, and the mother could not

3	

3	3	quiet it, do what she would. She grew impatient, and seeing the ravens

3	

3	3	flying round the castle, she opened the window, and said: ‘I wish you

3	

3	3	were a raven and would fly away, then I should have a little peace.’

3	

3	3	Scarcely were the words out of her mouth, when the child in her arms was

3	

3	3	turned into a raven, and flew away from her through the open window. The

3	

3	3	bird took its flight to a dark wood and remained there for a long time,

3	

3	3	and meanwhile the parents could hear nothing of their child.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Long after this, a man was making his way through the wood when he heard

3	

3	3	a raven calling, and he followed the sound of the voice. As he drew

3	

3	3	near, the raven said, ‘I am by birth a king’s daughter, but am now under

3	

3	3	the spell of some enchantment; you can, however, set me free.’ ‘What

3	

3	3	am I to do?’ he asked. She replied, ‘Go farther into the wood until you

3	

3	3	come to a house, wherein lives an old woman; she will offer you food and

3	

3	3	drink, but you must not take of either; if you do, you will fall into

3	

3	3	a deep sleep, and will not be able to help me. In the garden behind the

3	

3	3	house is a large tan-heap, and on that you must stand and watch for me.

3	

3	3	I shall drive there in my carriage at two o’clock in the afternoon for

3	

3	3	three successive days; the first day it will be drawn by four white, the

3	

3	3	second by four chestnut, and the last by four black horses; but if you

3	

3	3	fail to keep awake and I find you sleeping, I shall not be set free.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The man promised to do all that she wished, but the raven said, ‘Alas! I

3	

3	3	know even now that you will take something from the woman and be unable

3	

3	3	to save me.’ The man assured her again that he would on no account touch

3	

3	3	a thing to eat or drink.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When he came to the house and went inside, the old woman met him, and

3	

3	3	said, ‘Poor man! how tired you are! Come in and rest and let me give you

3	

3	3	something to eat and drink.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘No,’ answered the man, ‘I will neither eat not drink.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But she would not leave him alone, and urged him saying, ‘If you will

3	

3	3	not eat anything, at least you might take a draught of wine; one drink

3	

3	3	counts for nothing,’ and at last he allowed himself to be persuaded, and

3	

3	3	drank.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	As it drew towards the appointed hour, he went outside into the garden

3	

3	3	and mounted the tan-heap to await the raven. Suddenly a feeling of

3	

3	3	fatigue came over him, and unable to resist it, he lay down for a little

3	

3	3	while, fully determined, however, to keep awake; but in another minute

3	

3	3	his eyes closed of their own accord, and he fell into such a deep sleep,

3	

3	3	that all the noises in the world would not have awakened him. At two

3	

3	3	o’clock the raven came driving along, drawn by her four white horses;

3	

3	3	but even before she reached the spot, she said to herself, sighing, ‘I

3	

3	3	know he has fallen asleep.’ When she entered the garden, there she found

3	

3	3	him as she had feared, lying on the tan-heap, fast asleep. She got out

3	

3	3	of her carriage and went to him; she called him and shook him, but it

3	

3	3	was all in vain, he still continued sleeping.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The next day at noon, the old woman came to him again with food and

3	

3	3	drink which he at first refused. At last, overcome by her persistent

3	

3	3	entreaties that he would take something, he lifted the glass and drank

3	

3	3	again.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Towards two o’clock he went into the garden and on to the tan-heap to

3	

3	3	watch for the raven. He had not been there long before he began to feel

3	

3	3	so tired that his limbs seemed hardly able to support him, and he could

3	

3	3	not stand upright any longer; so again he lay down and fell fast asleep.

3	

3	3	As the raven drove along her four chestnut horses, she said sorrowfully

3	

3	3	to herself, ‘I know he has fallen asleep.’ She went as before to look

3	

3	3	for him, but he slept, and it was impossible to awaken him.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The following day the old woman said to him, ‘What is this? You are not

3	

3	3	eating or drinking anything, do you want to kill yourself?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	He answered, ‘I may not and will not either eat or drink.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But she put down the dish of food and the glass of wine in front of him,

3	

3	3	and when he smelt the wine, he was unable to resist the temptation, and

3	

3	3	took a deep draught.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the hour came round again he went as usual on to the tan-heap in

3	

3	3	the garden to await the king’s daughter, but he felt even more overcome

3	

3	3	with weariness than on the two previous days, and throwing himself down,

3	

3	3	he slept like a log. At two o’clock the raven could be seen approaching,

3	

3	3	and this time her coachman and everything about her, as well as her

3	

3	3	horses, were black.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	She was sadder than ever as she drove along, and said mournfully, ‘I

3	

3	3	know he has fallen asleep, and will not be able to set me free.’ She

3	

3	3	found him sleeping heavily, and all her efforts to awaken him were of no

3	

3	3	avail. Then she placed beside him a loaf, and some meat, and a flask

3	

3	3	of wine, of such a kind, that however much he took of them, they would

3	

3	3	never grow less. After that she drew a gold ring, on which her name was

3	

3	3	engraved, off her finger, and put it upon one of his. Finally, she laid

3	

3	3	a letter near him, in which, after giving him particulars of the food

3	

3	3	and drink she had left for him, she finished with the following words:

3	

3	3	‘I see that as long as you remain here you will never be able to set me

3	

3	3	free; if, however, you still wish to do so, come to the golden castle

3	

3	3	of Stromberg; this is well within your power to accomplish.’ She then

3	

3	3	returned to her carriage and drove to the golden castle of Stromberg.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the man awoke and found that he had been sleeping, he was grieved

3	

3	3	at heart, and said, ‘She has no doubt been here and driven away again,

3	

3	3	and it is now too late for me to save her.’ Then his eyes fell on the

3	

3	3	things which were lying beside him; he read the letter, and knew from it

3	

3	3	all that had happened. He rose up without delay, eager to start on his

3	

3	3	way and to reach the castle of Stromberg, but he had no idea in which

3	

3	3	direction he ought to go. He travelled about a long time in search of it

3	

3	3	and came at last to a dark forest, through which he went on walking for

3	

3	3	fourteen days and still could not find a way out. Once more the night

3	

3	3	came on, and worn out he lay down under a bush and fell asleep. Again

3	

3	3	the next day he pursued his way through the forest, and that evening,

3	

3	3	thinking to rest again, he lay down as before, but he heard such a

3	

3	3	howling and wailing that he found it impossible to sleep. He waited till

3	

3	3	it was darker and people had begun to light up their houses, and then

3	

3	3	seeing a little glimmer ahead of him, he went towards it.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	He found that the light came from a house which looked smaller than

3	

3	3	it really was, from the contrast of its height with that of an immense

3	

3	3	giant who stood in front of it. He thought to himself, ‘If the giant

3	

3	3	sees me going in, my life will not be worth much.’ However, after a

3	

3	3	while he summoned up courage and went forward. When the giant saw him,

3	

3	3	he called out, ‘It is lucky for that you have come, for I have not had

3	

3	3	anything to eat for a long time. I can have you now for my supper.’ ‘I

3	

3	3	would rather you let that alone,’ said the man, ‘for I do not willingly

3	

3	3	give myself up to be eaten; if you are wanting food I have enough to

3	

3	3	satisfy your hunger.’ ‘If that is so,’ replied the giant, ‘I will leave

3	

3	3	you in peace; I only thought of eating you because I had nothing else.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So they went indoors together and sat down, and the man brought out the

3	

3	3	bread, meat, and wine, which although he had eaten and drunk of them,

3	

3	3	were still unconsumed. The giant was pleased with the good cheer, and

3	

3	3	ate and drank to his heart’s content. When he had finished his supper

3	

3	3	the man asked him if he could direct him to the castle of Stromberg.

3	

3	3	The giant said, ‘I will look on my map; on it are marked all the towns,

3	

3	3	villages, and houses.’ So he fetched his map, and looked for the castle,

3	

3	3	but could not find it. ‘Never mind,’ he said, ‘I have larger maps

3	

3	3	upstairs in the cupboard, we will look on those,’ but they searched in

3	

3	3	vain, for the castle was not marked even on these. The man now thought

3	

3	3	he should like to continue his journey, but the giant begged him to

3	

3	3	remain for a day or two longer until the return of his brother, who was

3	

3	3	away in search of provisions. When the brother came home, they asked him

3	

3	3	about the castle of Stromberg, and he told them he would look on his own

3	

3	3	maps as soon as he had eaten and appeased his hunger. Accordingly, when

3	

3	3	he had finished his supper, they all went up together to his room and

3	

3	3	looked through his maps, but the castle was not to be found. Then he

3	

3	3	fetched other older maps, and they went on looking for the castle until

3	

3	3	at last they found it, but it was many thousand miles away. ‘How shall I

3	

3	3	be able to get there?’ asked the man. ‘I have two hours to spare,’ said

3	

3	3	the giant, ‘and I will carry you into the neighbourhood of the castle; I

3	

3	3	must then return to look after the child who is in our care.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The giant, thereupon, carried the man to within about a hundred leagues

3	

3	3	of the castle, where he left him, saying, ‘You will be able to walk the

3	

3	3	remainder of the way yourself.’ The man journeyed on day and night

3	

3	3	till he reached the golden castle of Stromberg. He found it situated,

3	

3	3	however, on a glass mountain, and looking up from the foot he saw the

3	

3	3	enchanted maiden drive round her castle and then go inside. He was

3	

3	3	overjoyed to see her, and longed to get to the top of the mountain, but

3	

3	3	the sides were so slippery that every time he attempted to climb he

3	

3	3	fell back again. When he saw that it was impossible to reach her, he was

3	

3	3	greatly grieved, and said to himself, ‘I will remain here and wait for

3	

3	3	her,’ so he built himself a little hut, and there he sat and watched for

3	

3	3	a whole year, and every day he saw the king’s daughter driving round her

3	

3	3	castle, but still was unable to get nearer to her.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Looking out from his hut one day he saw three robbers fighting and he

3	

3	3	called out to them, ‘God be with you.’ They stopped when they heard the

3	

3	3	call, but looking round and seeing nobody, they went on again with their

3	

3	3	fighting, which now became more furious. ‘God be with you,’ he cried

3	

3	3	again, and again they paused and looked about, but seeing no one went

3	

3	3	back to their fighting. A third time he called out, ‘God be with you,’

3	

3	3	and then thinking he should like to know the cause of dispute between

3	

3	3	the three men, he went out and asked them why they were fighting so

3	

3	3	angrily with one another. One of them said that he had found a stick,

3	

3	3	and that he had but to strike it against any door through which he

3	

3	3	wished to pass, and it immediately flew open. Another told him that he

3	

3	3	had found a cloak which rendered its wearer invisible; and the third had

3	

3	3	caught a horse which would carry its rider over any obstacle, and even

3	

3	3	up the glass mountain. They had been unable to decide whether they

3	

3	3	would keep together and have the things in common, or whether they would

3	

3	3	separate. On hearing this, the man said, ‘I will give you something in

3	

3	3	exchange for those three things; not money, for that I have not got,

3	

3	3	but something that is of far more value. I must first, however, prove

3	

3	3	whether all you have told me about your three things is true.’ The

3	

3	3	robbers, therefore, made him get on the horse, and handed him the stick

3	

3	3	and the cloak, and when he had put this round him he was no longer

3	

3	3	visible. Then he fell upon them with the stick and beat them one after

3	

3	3	another, crying, ‘There, you idle vagabonds, you have got what you

3	

3	3	deserve; are you satisfied now!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	After this he rode up the glass mountain. When he reached the gate of

3	

3	3	the castle, he found it closed, but he gave it a blow with his stick,

3	

3	3	and it flew wide open at once and he passed through. He mounted the

3	

3	3	steps and entered the room where the maiden was sitting, with a golden

3	

3	3	goblet full of wine in front of her. She could not see him for he still

3	

3	3	wore his cloak. He took the ring which she had given him off his finger,

3	

3	3	and threw it into the goblet, so that it rang as it touched the bottom.

3	

3	3	‘That is my own ring,’ she exclaimed, ‘and if that is so the man must

3	

3	3	also be here who is coming to set me free.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	She sought for him about the castle, but could find him nowhere.

3	

3	3	Meanwhile he had gone outside again and mounted his horse and thrown off

3	

3	3	the cloak. When therefore she came to the castle gate she saw him, and

3	

3	3	cried aloud for joy. Then he dismounted and took her in his arms; and

3	

3	3	she kissed him, and said, ‘Now you have indeed set me free, and tomorrow

3	

3	3	we will celebrate our marriage.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE GOLDEN GOOSE

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was a man who had three sons, the youngest of whom was called

3	

3	3	Dummling,[*] and was despised, mocked, and sneered at on every occasion.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	It happened that the eldest wanted to go into the forest to hew wood,

3	

3	3	and before he went his mother gave him a beautiful sweet cake and a

3	

3	3	bottle of wine in order that he might not suffer from hunger or thirst.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When he entered the forest he met a little grey-haired old man who bade

3	

3	3	him good day, and said: ‘Do give me a piece of cake out of your pocket,

3	

3	3	and let me have a draught of your wine; I am so hungry and thirsty.’ But

3	

3	3	the clever son answered: ‘If I give you my cake and wine, I shall have

3	

3	3	none for myself; be off with you,’ and he left the little man standing

3	

3	3	and went on.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But when he began to hew down a tree, it was not long before he made a

3	

3	3	false stroke, and the axe cut him in the arm, so that he had to go home

3	

3	3	and have it bound up. And this was the little grey man’s doing.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	After this the second son went into the forest, and his mother gave him,

3	

3	3	like the eldest, a cake and a bottle of wine. The little old grey man

3	

3	3	met him likewise, and asked him for a piece of cake and a drink of wine.

3	

3	3	But the second son, too, said sensibly enough: ‘What I give you will be

3	

3	3	taken away from myself; be off!’ and he left the little man standing and

3	

3	3	went on. His punishment, however, was not delayed; when he had made a

3	

3	3	few blows at the tree he struck himself in the leg, so that he had to be

3	

3	3	carried home.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then Dummling said: ‘Father, do let me go and cut wood.’ The father

3	

3	3	answered: ‘Your brothers have hurt themselves with it, leave it alone,

3	

3	3	you do not understand anything about it.’ But Dummling begged so long

3	

3	3	that at last he said: ‘Just go then, you will get wiser by hurting

3	

3	3	yourself.’ His mother gave him a cake made with water and baked in the

3	

3	3	cinders, and with it a bottle of sour beer.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When he came to the forest the little old grey man met him likewise,

3	

3	3	and greeting him, said: ‘Give me a piece of your cake and a drink out

3	

3	3	of your bottle; I am so hungry and thirsty.’ Dummling answered: ‘I have

3	

3	3	only cinder-cake and sour beer; if that pleases you, we will sit

3	

3	3	down and eat.’ So they sat down, and when Dummling pulled out his

3	

3	3	cinder-cake, it was a fine sweet cake, and the sour beer had become good

3	

3	3	wine. So they ate and drank, and after that the little man said: ‘Since

3	

3	3	you have a good heart, and are willing to divide what you have, I will

3	

3	3	give you good luck. There stands an old tree, cut it down, and you will

3	

3	3	find something at the roots.’ Then the little man took leave of him.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Dummling went and cut down the tree, and when it fell there was a goose

3	

3	3	sitting in the roots with feathers of pure gold. He lifted her up, and

3	

3	3	taking her with him, went to an inn where he thought he would stay the

3	

3	3	night. Now the host had three daughters, who saw the goose and were

3	

3	3	curious to know what such a wonderful bird might be, and would have

3	

3	3	liked to have one of its golden feathers.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The eldest thought: ‘I shall soon find an opportunity of pulling out a

3	

3	3	feather,’ and as soon as Dummling had gone out she seized the goose by

3	

3	3	the wing, but her finger and hand remained sticking fast to it.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The second came soon afterwards, thinking only of how she might get a

3	

3	3	feather for herself, but she had scarcely touched her sister than she

3	

3	3	was held fast.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	At last the third also came with the like intent, and the others

3	

3	3	screamed out: ‘Keep away; for goodness’ sake keep away!’ But she did

3	

3	3	not understand why she was to keep away. ‘The others are there,’ she

3	

3	3	thought, ‘I may as well be there too,’ and ran to them; but as soon as

3	

3	3	she had touched her sister, she remained sticking fast to her. So they

3	

3	3	had to spend the night with the goose.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The next morning Dummling took the goose under his arm and set out,

3	

3	3	without troubling himself about the three girls who were hanging on to

3	

3	3	it. They were obliged to run after him continually, now left, now right,

3	

3	3	wherever his legs took him.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	In the middle of the fields the parson met them, and when he saw the

3	

3	3	procession he said: ‘For shame, you good-for-nothing girls, why are you

3	

3	3	running across the fields after this young man? Is that seemly?’ At the

3	

3	3	same time he seized the youngest by the hand in order to pull her away,

3	

3	3	but as soon as he touched her he likewise stuck fast, and was himself

3	

3	3	obliged to run behind.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Before long the sexton came by and saw his master, the parson, running

3	

3	3	behind three girls. He was astonished at this and called out: ‘Hi!

3	

3	3	your reverence, whither away so quickly? Do not forget that we have a

3	

3	3	christening today!’ and running after him he took him by the sleeve, but

3	

3	3	was also held fast to it.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Whilst the five were trotting thus one behind the other, two labourers

3	

3	3	came with their hoes from the fields; the parson called out to them

3	

3	3	and begged that they would set him and the sexton free. But they had

3	

3	3	scarcely touched the sexton when they were held fast, and now there were

3	

3	3	seven of them running behind Dummling and the goose.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Soon afterwards he came to a city, where a king ruled who had a daughter

3	

3	3	who was so serious that no one could make her laugh. So he had put forth

3	

3	3	a decree that whosoever should be able to make her laugh should marry

3	

3	3	her. When Dummling heard this, he went with his goose and all her train

3	

3	3	before the king’s daughter, and as soon as she saw the seven people

3	

3	3	running on and on, one behind the other, she began to laugh quite

3	

3	3	loudly, and as if she would never stop. Thereupon Dummling asked to have

3	

3	3	her for his wife; but the king did not like the son-in-law, and made all

3	

3	3	manner of excuses and said he must first produce a man who could drink

3	

3	3	a cellarful of wine. Dummling thought of the little grey man, who could

3	

3	3	certainly help him; so he went into the forest, and in the same place

3	

3	3	where he had felled the tree, he saw a man sitting, who had a very

3	

3	3	sorrowful face. Dummling asked him what he was taking to heart so

3	

3	3	sorely, and he answered: ‘I have such a great thirst and cannot quench

3	

3	3	it; cold water I cannot stand, a barrel of wine I have just emptied, but

3	

3	3	that to me is like a drop on a hot stone!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘There, I can help you,’ said Dummling, ‘just come with me and you shall

3	

3	3	be satisfied.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	He led him into the king’s cellar, and the man bent over the huge

3	

3	3	barrels, and drank and drank till his loins hurt, and before the day was

3	

3	3	out he had emptied all the barrels. Then Dummling asked once more

3	

3	3	for his bride, but the king was vexed that such an ugly fellow, whom

3	

3	3	everyone called Dummling, should take away his daughter, and he made a

3	

3	3	new condition; he must first find a man who could eat a whole mountain

3	

3	3	of bread. Dummling did not think long, but went straight into the

3	

3	3	forest, where in the same place there sat a man who was tying up his

3	

3	3	body with a strap, and making an awful face, and saying: ‘I have eaten a

3	

3	3	whole ovenful of rolls, but what good is that when one has such a hunger

3	

3	3	as I? My stomach remains empty, and I must tie myself up if I am not to

3	

3	3	die of hunger.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	At this Dummling was glad, and said: ‘Get up and come with me; you shall

3	

3	3	eat yourself full.’ He led him to the king’s palace where all the

3	

3	3	flour in the whole Kingdom was collected, and from it he caused a huge

3	

3	3	mountain of bread to be baked. The man from the forest stood before it,

3	

3	3	began to eat, and by the end of one day the whole mountain had vanished.

3	

3	3	Then Dummling for the third time asked for his bride; but the king again

3	

3	3	sought a way out, and ordered a ship which could sail on land and on

3	

3	3	water. ‘As soon as you come sailing back in it,’ said he, ‘you shall

3	

3	3	have my daughter for wife.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Dummling went straight into the forest, and there sat the little grey

3	

3	3	man to whom he had given his cake. When he heard what Dummling wanted,

3	

3	3	he said: ‘Since you have given me to eat and to drink, I will give you

3	

3	3	the ship; and I do all this because you once were kind to me.’ Then he

3	

3	3	gave him the ship which could sail on land and water, and when the king

3	

3	3	saw that, he could no longer prevent him from having his daughter. The

3	

3	3	wedding was celebrated, and after the king’s death, Dummling inherited

3	

3	3	his kingdom and lived for a long time contentedly with his wife.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	     [*] Simpleton

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE WATER OF LIFE

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Long before you or I were born, there reigned, in a country a great way

3	

3	3	off, a king who had three sons. This king once fell very ill--so ill

3	

3	3	that nobody thought he could live. His sons were very much grieved

3	

3	3	at their father’s sickness; and as they were walking together very

3	

3	3	mournfully in the garden of the palace, a little old man met them and

3	

3	3	asked what was the matter. They told him that their father was very ill,

3	

3	3	and that they were afraid nothing could save him. ‘I know what would,’

3	

3	3	said the little old man; ‘it is the Water of Life. If he could have a

3	

3	3	draught of it he would be well again; but it is very hard to get.’ Then

3	

3	3	the eldest son said, ‘I will soon find it’: and he went to the sick

3	

3	3	king, and begged that he might go in search of the Water of Life, as

3	

3	3	it was the only thing that could save him. ‘No,’ said the king. ‘I had

3	

3	3	rather die than place you in such great danger as you must meet with in

3	

3	3	your journey.’ But he begged so hard that the king let him go; and the

3	

3	3	prince thought to himself, ‘If I bring my father this water, he will

3	

3	3	make me sole heir to his kingdom.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then he set out: and when he had gone on his way some time he came to a

3	

3	3	deep valley, overhung with rocks and woods; and as he looked around, he

3	

3	3	saw standing above him on one of the rocks a little ugly dwarf, with a

3	

3	3	sugarloaf cap and a scarlet cloak; and the dwarf called to him and said,

3	

3	3	‘Prince, whither so fast?’ ‘What is that to thee, you ugly imp?’ said

3	

3	3	the prince haughtily, and rode on.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But the dwarf was enraged at his behaviour, and laid a fairy spell

3	

3	3	of ill-luck upon him; so that as he rode on the mountain pass became

3	

3	3	narrower and narrower, and at last the way was so straitened that he

3	

3	3	could not go to step forward: and when he thought to have turned his

3	

3	3	horse round and go back the way he came, he heard a loud laugh ringing

3	

3	3	round him, and found that the path was closed behind him, so that he was

3	

3	3	shut in all round. He next tried to get off his horse and make his way

3	

3	3	on foot, but again the laugh rang in his ears, and he found himself

3	

3	3	unable to move a step, and thus he was forced to abide spellbound.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Meantime the old king was lingering on in daily hope of his son’s

3	

3	3	return, till at last the second son said, ‘Father, I will go in search

3	

3	3	of the Water of Life.’ For he thought to himself, ‘My brother is surely

3	

3	3	dead, and the kingdom will fall to me if I find the water.’ The king was

3	

3	3	at first very unwilling to let him go, but at last yielded to his wish.

3	

3	3	So he set out and followed the same road which his brother had done,

3	

3	3	and met with the same elf, who stopped him at the same spot in the

3	

3	3	mountains, saying, as before, ‘Prince, prince, whither so fast?’ ‘Mind

3	

3	3	your own affairs, busybody!’ said the prince scornfully, and rode on.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But the dwarf put the same spell upon him as he put on his elder

3	

3	3	brother, and he, too, was at last obliged to take up his abode in the

3	

3	3	heart of the mountains. Thus it is with proud silly people, who think

3	

3	3	themselves above everyone else, and are too proud to ask or take advice.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the second prince had thus been gone a long time, the youngest son

3	

3	3	said he would go and search for the Water of Life, and trusted he should

3	

3	3	soon be able to make his father well again. So he set out, and the dwarf

3	

3	3	met him too at the same spot in the valley, among the mountains, and

3	

3	3	said, ‘Prince, whither so fast?’ And the prince said, ‘I am going in

3	

3	3	search of the Water of Life, because my father is ill, and like to die:

3	

3	3	can you help me? Pray be kind, and aid me if you can!’ ‘Do you know

3	

3	3	where it is to be found?’ asked the dwarf. ‘No,’ said the prince, ‘I do

3	

3	3	not. Pray tell me if you know.’ ‘Then as you have spoken to me kindly,

3	

3	3	and are wise enough to seek for advice, I will tell you how and where to

3	

3	3	go. The water you seek springs from a well in an enchanted castle; and,

3	

3	3	that you may be able to reach it in safety, I will give you an iron wand

3	

3	3	and two little loaves of bread; strike the iron door of the castle three

3	

3	3	times with the wand, and it will open: two hungry lions will be lying

3	

3	3	down inside gaping for their prey, but if you throw them the bread they

3	

3	3	will let you pass; then hasten on to the well, and take some of the

3	

3	3	Water of Life before the clock strikes twelve; for if you tarry longer

3	

3	3	the door will shut upon you for ever.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the prince thanked his little friend with the scarlet cloak for his

3	

3	3	friendly aid, and took the wand and the bread, and went travelling on

3	

3	3	and on, over sea and over land, till he came to his journey’s end, and

3	

3	3	found everything to be as the dwarf had told him. The door flew open at

3	

3	3	the third stroke of the wand, and when the lions were quieted he went on

3	

3	3	through the castle and came at length to a beautiful hall. Around it he

3	

3	3	saw several knights sitting in a trance; then he pulled off their rings

3	

3	3	and put them on his own fingers. In another room he saw on a table a

3	

3	3	sword and a loaf of bread, which he also took. Further on he came to a

3	

3	3	room where a beautiful young lady sat upon a couch; and she welcomed him

3	

3	3	joyfully, and said, if he would set her free from the spell that bound

3	

3	3	her, the kingdom should be his, if he would come back in a year and

3	

3	3	marry her. Then she told him that the well that held the Water of Life

3	

3	3	was in the palace gardens; and bade him make haste, and draw what he

3	

3	3	wanted before the clock struck twelve.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	He walked on; and as he walked through beautiful gardens he came to a

3	

3	3	delightful shady spot in which stood a couch; and he thought to himself,

3	

3	3	as he felt tired, that he would rest himself for a while, and gaze on

3	

3	3	the lovely scenes around him. So he laid himself down, and sleep

3	

3	3	fell upon him unawares, so that he did not wake up till the clock was

3	

3	3	striking a quarter to twelve. Then he sprang from the couch dreadfully

3	

3	3	frightened, ran to the well, filled a cup that was standing by him full

3	

3	3	of water, and hastened to get away in time. Just as he was going out of

3	

3	3	the iron door it struck twelve, and the door fell so quickly upon him

3	

3	3	that it snapped off a piece of his heel.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When he found himself safe, he was overjoyed to think that he had got

3	

3	3	the Water of Life; and as he was going on his way homewards, he passed

3	

3	3	by the little dwarf, who, when he saw the sword and the loaf, said, ‘You

3	

3	3	have made a noble prize; with the sword you can at a blow slay whole

3	

3	3	armies, and the bread will never fail you.’ Then the prince thought

3	

3	3	to himself, ‘I cannot go home to my father without my brothers’; so he

3	

3	3	said, ‘My dear friend, cannot you tell me where my two brothers are, who

3	

3	3	set out in search of the Water of Life before me, and never came back?’

3	

3	3	‘I have shut them up by a charm between two mountains,’ said the dwarf,

3	

3	3	‘because they were proud and ill-behaved, and scorned to ask advice.’

3	

3	3	The prince begged so hard for his brothers, that the dwarf at last set

3	

3	3	them free, though unwillingly, saying, ‘Beware of them, for they have

3	

3	3	bad hearts.’ Their brother, however, was greatly rejoiced to see them,

3	

3	3	and told them all that had happened to him; how he had found the Water

3	

3	3	of Life, and had taken a cup full of it; and how he had set a beautiful

3	

3	3	princess free from a spell that bound her; and how she had engaged to

3	

3	3	wait a whole year, and then to marry him, and to give him the kingdom.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then they all three rode on together, and on their way home came to a

3	

3	3	country that was laid waste by war and a dreadful famine, so that it was

3	

3	3	feared all must die for want. But the prince gave the king of the land

3	

3	3	the bread, and all his kingdom ate of it. And he lent the king the

3	

3	3	wonderful sword, and he slew the enemy’s army with it; and thus the

3	

3	3	kingdom was once more in peace and plenty. In the same manner he

3	

3	3	befriended two other countries through which they passed on their way.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When they came to the sea, they got into a ship and during their voyage

3	

3	3	the two eldest said to themselves, ‘Our brother has got the water which

3	

3	3	we could not find, therefore our father will forsake us and give him the

3	

3	3	kingdom, which is our right’; so they were full of envy and revenge, and

3	

3	3	agreed together how they could ruin him. Then they waited till he was

3	

3	3	fast asleep, and poured the Water of Life out of the cup, and took it

3	

3	3	for themselves, giving him bitter sea-water instead.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When they came to their journey’s end, the youngest son brought his cup

3	

3	3	to the sick king, that he might drink and be healed. Scarcely, however,

3	

3	3	had he tasted the bitter sea-water when he became worse even than he was

3	

3	3	before; and then both the elder sons came in, and blamed the youngest

3	

3	3	for what they had done; and said that he wanted to poison their father,

3	

3	3	but that they had found the Water of Life, and had brought it with them.

3	

3	3	He no sooner began to drink of what they brought him, than he felt his

3	

3	3	sickness leave him, and was as strong and well as in his younger days.

3	

3	3	Then they went to their brother, and laughed at him, and said, ‘Well,

3	

3	3	brother, you found the Water of Life, did you? You have had the trouble

3	

3	3	and we shall have the reward. Pray, with all your cleverness, why did

3	

3	3	not you manage to keep your eyes open? Next year one of us will take

3	

3	3	away your beautiful princess, if you do not take care. You had better

3	

3	3	say nothing about this to our father, for he does not believe a word you

3	

3	3	say; and if you tell tales, you shall lose your life into the bargain:

3	

3	3	but be quiet, and we will let you off.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The old king was still very angry with his youngest son, and thought

3	

3	3	that he really meant to have taken away his life; so he called his court

3	

3	3	together, and asked what should be done, and all agreed that he ought to

3	

3	3	be put to death. The prince knew nothing of what was going on, till one

3	

3	3	day, when the king’s chief huntsmen went a-hunting with him, and they

3	

3	3	were alone in the wood together, the huntsman looked so sorrowful that

3	

3	3	the prince said, ‘My friend, what is the matter with you?’ ‘I cannot and

3	

3	3	dare not tell you,’ said he. But the prince begged very hard, and said,

3	

3	3	‘Only tell me what it is, and do not think I shall be angry, for I will

3	

3	3	forgive you.’ ‘Alas!’ said the huntsman; ‘the king has ordered me to

3	

3	3	shoot you.’ The prince started at this, and said, ‘Let me live, and I

3	

3	3	will change dresses with you; you shall take my royal coat to show to my

3	

3	3	father, and do you give me your shabby one.’ ‘With all my heart,’ said

3	

3	3	the huntsman; ‘I am sure I shall be glad to save you, for I could not

3	

3	3	have shot you.’ Then he took the prince’s coat, and gave him the shabby

3	

3	3	one, and went away through the wood.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Some time after, three grand embassies came to the old king’s court,

3	

3	3	with rich gifts of gold and precious stones for his youngest son; now

3	

3	3	all these were sent from the three kings to whom he had lent his sword

3	

3	3	and loaf of bread, in order to rid them of their enemy and feed their

3	

3	3	people. This touched the old king’s heart, and he thought his son might

3	

3	3	still be guiltless, and said to his court, ‘O that my son were still

3	

3	3	alive! how it grieves me that I had him killed!’ ‘He is still alive,’

3	

3	3	said the huntsman; ‘and I am glad that I had pity on him, but let him

3	

3	3	go in peace, and brought home his royal coat.’ At this the king was

3	

3	3	overwhelmed with joy, and made it known throughout all his kingdom, that

3	

3	3	if his son would come back to his court he would forgive him.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Meanwhile the princess was eagerly waiting till her deliverer should

3	

3	3	come back; and had a road made leading up to her palace all of shining

3	

3	3	gold; and told her courtiers that whoever came on horseback, and rode

3	

3	3	straight up to the gate upon it, was her true lover; and that they must

3	

3	3	let him in: but whoever rode on one side of it, they must be sure was

3	

3	3	not the right one; and that they must send him away at once.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The time soon came, when the eldest brother thought that he would make

3	

3	3	haste to go to the princess, and say that he was the one who had set

3	

3	3	her free, and that he should have her for his wife, and the kingdom with

3	

3	3	her. As he came before the palace and saw the golden road, he stopped to

3	

3	3	look at it, and he thought to himself, ‘It is a pity to ride upon this

3	

3	3	beautiful road’; so he turned aside and rode on the right-hand side of

3	

3	3	it. But when he came to the gate, the guards, who had seen the road

3	

3	3	he took, said to him, he could not be what he said he was, and must go

3	

3	3	about his business.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The second prince set out soon afterwards on the same errand; and when

3	

3	3	he came to the golden road, and his horse had set one foot upon it,

3	

3	3	he stopped to look at it, and thought it very beautiful, and said to

3	

3	3	himself, ‘What a pity it is that anything should tread here!’ Then he

3	

3	3	too turned aside and rode on the left side of it. But when he came to

3	

3	3	the gate the guards said he was not the true prince, and that he too

3	

3	3	must go away about his business; and away he went.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Now when the full year was come round, the third brother left the forest

3	

3	3	in which he had lain hid for fear of his father’s anger, and set out in

3	

3	3	search of his betrothed bride. So he journeyed on, thinking of her all

3	

3	3	the way, and rode so quickly that he did not even see what the road was

3	

3	3	made of, but went with his horse straight over it; and as he came to the

3	

3	3	gate it flew open, and the princess welcomed him with joy, and said

3	

3	3	he was her deliverer, and should now be her husband and lord of the

3	

3	3	kingdom. When the first joy at their meeting was over, the princess told

3	

3	3	him she had heard of his father having forgiven him, and of his wish to

3	

3	3	have him home again: so, before his wedding with the princess, he went

3	

3	3	to visit his father, taking her with him. Then he told him everything;

3	

3	3	how his brothers had cheated and robbed him, and yet that he had borne

3	

3	3	all those wrongs for the love of his father. And the old king was very

3	

3	3	angry, and wanted to punish his wicked sons; but they made their escape,

3	

3	3	and got into a ship and sailed away over the wide sea, and where they

3	

3	3	went to nobody knew and nobody cared.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And now the old king gathered together his court, and asked all his

3	

3	3	kingdom to come and celebrate the wedding of his son and the princess.

3	

3	3	And young and old, noble and squire, gentle and simple, came at once

3	

3	3	on the summons; and among the rest came the friendly dwarf, with the

3	

3	3	sugarloaf hat, and a new scarlet cloak.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	  And the wedding was held, and the merry bells run.

3	

3	3	  And all the good people they danced and they sung,

3	

3	3	  And feasted and frolick’d I can’t tell how long.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE TWELVE HUNTSMEN

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once a king’s son who had a bride whom he loved very much. And

3	

3	3	when he was sitting beside her and very happy, news came that his father

3	

3	3	lay sick unto death, and desired to see him once again before his end.

3	

3	3	Then he said to his beloved: ‘I must now go and leave you, I give you

3	

3	3	a ring as a remembrance of me. When I am king, I will return and fetch

3	

3	3	you.’ So he rode away, and when he reached his father, the latter was

3	

3	3	dangerously ill, and near his death. He said to him: ‘Dear son, I wished

3	

3	3	to see you once again before my end, promise me to marry as I wish,’ and

3	

3	3	he named a certain king’s daughter who was to be his wife. The son was

3	

3	3	in such trouble that he did not think what he was doing, and said: ‘Yes,

3	

3	3	dear father, your will shall be done,’ and thereupon the king shut his

3	

3	3	eyes, and died.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When therefore the son had been proclaimed king, and the time of

3	

3	3	mourning was over, he was forced to keep the promise which he had given

3	

3	3	his father, and caused the king’s daughter to be asked in marriage, and

3	

3	3	she was promised to him. His first betrothed heard of this, and fretted

3	

3	3	so much about his faithfulness that she nearly died. Then her father

3	

3	3	said to her: ‘Dearest child, why are you so sad? You shall have

3	

3	3	whatsoever you will.’ She thought for a moment and said: ‘Dear father,

3	

3	3	I wish for eleven girls exactly like myself in face, figure, and size.’

3	

3	3	The father said: ‘If it be possible, your desire shall be fulfilled,’

3	

3	3	and he caused a search to be made in his whole kingdom, until eleven

3	

3	3	young maidens were found who exactly resembled his daughter in face,

3	

3	3	figure, and size.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When they came to the king’s daughter, she had twelve suits of

3	

3	3	huntsmen’s clothes made, all alike, and the eleven maidens had to put

3	

3	3	on the huntsmen’s clothes, and she herself put on the twelfth suit.

3	

3	3	Thereupon she took her leave of her father, and rode away with them,

3	

3	3	and rode to the court of her former betrothed, whom she loved so dearly.

3	

3	3	Then she asked if he required any huntsmen, and if he would take all of

3	

3	3	them into his service. The king looked at her and did not know her, but

3	

3	3	as they were such handsome fellows, he said: ‘Yes,’ and that he would

3	

3	3	willingly take them, and now they were the king’s twelve huntsmen.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The king, however, had a lion which was a wondrous animal, for he knew

3	

3	3	all concealed and secret things. It came to pass that one evening he

3	

3	3	said to the king: ‘You think you have twelve huntsmen?’ ‘Yes,’ said the

3
