3	3	king, ‘they are twelve huntsmen.’ The lion continued: ‘You are mistaken,

3	

3	3	they are twelve girls.’ The king said: ‘That cannot be true! How

3	

3	3	will you prove that to me?’ ‘Oh, just let some peas be strewn in the

3	

3	3	ante-chamber,’ answered the lion, ‘and then you will soon see. Men have

3	

3	3	a firm step, and when they walk over peas none of them stir, but girls

3	

3	3	trip and skip, and drag their feet, and the peas roll about.’ The king

3	

3	3	was well pleased with the counsel, and caused the peas to be strewn.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was, however, a servant of the king’s who favoured the huntsmen,

3	

3	3	and when he heard that they were going to be put to this test he went to

3	

3	3	them and repeated everything, and said: ‘The lion wants to make the king

3	

3	3	believe that you are girls.’ Then the king’s daughter thanked him, and

3	

3	3	said to her maidens: ‘Show some strength, and step firmly on the peas.’

3	

3	3	So next morning when the king had the twelve huntsmen called before

3	

3	3	him, and they came into the ante-chamber where the peas were lying, they

3	

3	3	stepped so firmly on them, and had such a strong, sure walk, that not

3	

3	3	one of the peas either rolled or stirred. Then they went away again,

3	

3	3	and the king said to the lion: ‘You have lied to me, they walk just like

3	

3	3	men.’ The lion said: ‘They have been informed that they were going to

3	

3	3	be put to the test, and have assumed some strength. Just let twelve

3	

3	3	spinning-wheels be brought into the ante-chamber, and they will go to

3	

3	3	them and be pleased with them, and that is what no man would do.’

3	

3	3	The king liked the advice, and had the spinning-wheels placed in the

3	

3	3	ante-chamber.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But the servant, who was well disposed to the huntsmen, went to them,

3	

3	3	and disclosed the project. So when they were alone the king’s daughter

3	

3	3	said to her eleven girls: ‘Show some constraint, and do not look round

3	

3	3	at the spinning-wheels.’ And next morning when the king had his twelve

3	

3	3	huntsmen summoned, they went through the ante-chamber, and never once

3	

3	3	looked at the spinning-wheels. Then the king again said to the lion:

3	

3	3	‘You have deceived me, they are men, for they have not looked at the

3	

3	3	spinning-wheels.’ The lion replied: ‘They have restrained themselves.’

3	

3	3	The king, however, would no longer believe the lion.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The twelve huntsmen always followed the king to the chase, and his

3	

3	3	liking for them continually increased. Now it came to pass that

3	

3	3	once when they were out hunting, news came that the king’s bride was

3	

3	3	approaching. When the true bride heard that, it hurt her so much that

3	

3	3	her heart was almost broken, and she fell fainting to the ground. The

3	

3	3	king thought something had happened to his dear huntsman, ran up to him,

3	

3	3	wanted to help him, and drew his glove off. Then he saw the ring which

3	

3	3	he had given to his first bride, and when he looked in her face he

3	

3	3	recognized her. Then his heart was so touched that he kissed her, and

3	

3	3	when she opened her eyes he said: ‘You are mine, and I am yours, and

3	

3	3	no one in the world can alter that.’ He sent a messenger to the other

3	

3	3	bride, and entreated her to return to her own kingdom, for he had a wife

3	

3	3	already, and someone who had just found an old key did not require a new

3	

3	3	one. Thereupon the wedding was celebrated, and the lion was again taken

3	

3	3	into favour, because, after all, he had told the truth.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE KING OF THE GOLDEN MOUNTAIN

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once a merchant who had only one child, a son, that was very

3	

3	3	young, and barely able to run alone. He had two richly laden ships then

3	

3	3	making a voyage upon the seas, in which he had embarked all his wealth,

3	

3	3	in the hope of making great gains, when the news came that both were

3	

3	3	lost. Thus from being a rich man he became all at once so very poor that

3	

3	3	nothing was left to him but one small plot of land; and there he often

3	

3	3	went in an evening to take his walk, and ease his mind of a little of

3	

3	3	his trouble.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	One day, as he was roaming along in a brown study, thinking with no

3	

3	3	great comfort on what he had been and what he now was, and was like

3	

3	3	to be, all on a sudden there stood before him a little, rough-looking,

3	

3	3	black dwarf. ‘Prithee, friend, why so sorrowful?’ said he to the

3	

3	3	merchant; ‘what is it you take so deeply to heart?’ ‘If you would do me

3	

3	3	any good I would willingly tell you,’ said the merchant. ‘Who knows but

3	

3	3	I may?’ said the little man: ‘tell me what ails you, and perhaps you

3	

3	3	will find I may be of some use.’ Then the merchant told him how all his

3	

3	3	wealth was gone to the bottom of the sea, and how he had nothing left

3	

3	3	but that little plot of land. ‘Oh, trouble not yourself about that,’

3	

3	3	said the dwarf; ‘only undertake to bring me here, twelve years hence,

3	

3	3	whatever meets you first on your going home, and I will give you as much

3	

3	3	as you please.’ The merchant thought this was no great thing to ask;

3	

3	3	that it would most likely be his dog or his cat, or something of that

3	

3	3	sort, but forgot his little boy Heinel; so he agreed to the bargain, and

3	

3	3	signed and sealed the bond to do what was asked of him.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But as he drew near home, his little boy was so glad to see him that he

3	

3	3	crept behind him, and laid fast hold of his legs, and looked up in

3	

3	3	his face and laughed. Then the father started, trembling with fear and

3	

3	3	horror, and saw what it was that he had bound himself to do; but as no

3	

3	3	gold was come, he made himself easy by thinking that it was only a joke

3	

3	3	that the dwarf was playing him, and that, at any rate, when the money

3	

3	3	came, he should see the bearer, and would not take it in.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	About a month afterwards he went upstairs into a lumber-room to look

3	

3	3	for some old iron, that he might sell it and raise a little money; and

3	

3	3	there, instead of his iron, he saw a large pile of gold lying on the

3	

3	3	floor. At the sight of this he was overjoyed, and forgetting all about

3	

3	3	his son, went into trade again, and became a richer merchant than

3	

3	3	before.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Meantime little Heinel grew up, and as the end of the twelve years drew

3	

3	3	near the merchant began to call to mind his bond, and became very sad

3	

3	3	and thoughtful; so that care and sorrow were written upon his face. The

3	

3	3	boy one day asked what was the matter, but his father would not tell for

3	

3	3	some time; at last, however, he said that he had, without knowing it,

3	

3	3	sold him for gold to a little, ugly-looking, black dwarf, and that the

3	

3	3	twelve years were coming round when he must keep his word. Then Heinel

3	

3	3	said, ‘Father, give yourself very little trouble about that; I shall be

3	

3	3	too much for the little man.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the time came, the father and son went out together to the place

3	

3	3	agreed upon: and the son drew a circle on the ground, and set himself

3	

3	3	and his father in the middle of it. The little black dwarf soon came,

3	

3	3	and walked round and round about the circle, but could not find any way

3	

3	3	to get into it, and he either could not, or dared not, jump over it. At

3	

3	3	last the boy said to him. ‘Have you anything to say to us, my friend, or

3	

3	3	what do you want?’ Now Heinel had found a friend in a good fairy, that

3	

3	3	was fond of him, and had told him what to do; for this fairy knew what

3	

3	3	good luck was in store for him. ‘Have you brought me what you said you

3	

3	3	would?’ said the dwarf to the merchant. The old man held his tongue, but

3	

3	3	Heinel said again, ‘What do you want here?’ The dwarf said, ‘I come to

3	

3	3	talk with your father, not with you.’ ‘You have cheated and taken in my

3	

3	3	father,’ said the son; ‘pray give him up his bond at once.’ ‘Fair and

3	

3	3	softly,’ said the little old man; ‘right is right; I have paid my money,

3	

3	3	and your father has had it, and spent it; so be so good as to let me

3	

3	3	have what I paid it for.’ ‘You must have my consent to that first,’ said

3	

3	3	Heinel, ‘so please to step in here, and let us talk it over.’ The old

3	

3	3	man grinned, and showed his teeth, as if he should have been very glad

3	

3	3	to get into the circle if he could. Then at last, after a long talk,

3	

3	3	they came to terms. Heinel agreed that his father must give him up, and

3	

3	3	that so far the dwarf should have his way: but, on the other hand, the

3	

3	3	fairy had told Heinel what fortune was in store for him, if he followed

3	

3	3	his own course; and he did not choose to be given up to his hump-backed

3	

3	3	friend, who seemed so anxious for his company.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So, to make a sort of drawn battle of the matter, it was settled that

3	

3	3	Heinel should be put into an open boat, that lay on the sea-shore hard

3	

3	3	by; that the father should push him off with his own hand, and that he

3	

3	3	should thus be set adrift, and left to the bad or good luck of wind and

3	

3	3	weather. Then he took leave of his father, and set himself in the boat,

3	

3	3	but before it got far off a wave struck it, and it fell with one side

3	

3	3	low in the water, so the merchant thought that poor Heinel was lost, and

3	

3	3	went home very sorrowful, while the dwarf went his way, thinking that at

3	

3	3	any rate he had had his revenge.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The boat, however, did not sink, for the good fairy took care of her

3	

3	3	friend, and soon raised the boat up again, and it went safely on. The

3	

3	3	young man sat safe within, till at length it ran ashore upon an unknown

3	

3	3	land. As he jumped upon the shore he saw before him a beautiful castle

3	

3	3	but empty and dreary within, for it was enchanted. ‘Here,’ said he to

3	

3	3	himself, ‘must I find the prize the good fairy told me of.’ So he once

3	

3	3	more searched the whole palace through, till at last he found a white

3	

3	3	snake, lying coiled up on a cushion in one of the chambers.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Now the white snake was an enchanted princess; and she was very glad

3	

3	3	to see him, and said, ‘Are you at last come to set me free? Twelve

3	

3	3	long years have I waited here for the fairy to bring you hither as she

3	

3	3	promised, for you alone can save me. This night twelve men will come:

3	

3	3	their faces will be black, and they will be dressed in chain armour.

3	

3	3	They will ask what you do here, but give no answer; and let them do

3	

3	3	what they will--beat, whip, pinch, prick, or torment you--bear all; only

3	

3	3	speak not a word, and at twelve o’clock they must go away. The second

3	

3	3	night twelve others will come: and the third night twenty-four, who

3	

3	3	will even cut off your head; but at the twelfth hour of that night their

3	

3	3	power is gone, and I shall be free, and will come and bring you the

3	

3	3	Water of Life, and will wash you with it, and bring you back to life

3	

3	3	and health.’ And all came to pass as she had said; Heinel bore all, and

3	

3	3	spoke not a word; and the third night the princess came, and fell on his

3	

3	3	neck and kissed him. Joy and gladness burst forth throughout the castle,

3	

3	3	the wedding was celebrated, and he was crowned king of the Golden

3	

3	3	Mountain.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	They lived together very happily, and the queen had a son. And thus

3	

3	3	eight years had passed over their heads, when the king thought of his

3	

3	3	father; and he began to long to see him once again. But the queen was

3	

3	3	against his going, and said, ‘I know well that misfortunes will come

3	

3	3	upon us if you go.’ However, he gave her no rest till she agreed. At his

3	

3	3	going away she gave him a wishing-ring, and said, ‘Take this ring, and

3	

3	3	put it on your finger; whatever you wish it will bring you; only promise

3	

3	3	never to make use of it to bring me hence to your father’s house.’ Then

3	

3	3	he said he would do what she asked, and put the ring on his finger, and

3	

3	3	wished himself near the town where his father lived.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Heinel found himself at the gates in a moment; but the guards would

3	

3	3	not let him go in, because he was so strangely clad. So he went up to a

3	

3	3	neighbouring hill, where a shepherd dwelt, and borrowed his old frock,

3	

3	3	and thus passed unknown into the town. When he came to his father’s

3	

3	3	house, he said he was his son; but the merchant would not believe him,

3	

3	3	and said he had had but one son, his poor Heinel, who he knew was long

3	

3	3	since dead: and as he was only dressed like a poor shepherd, he would

3	

3	3	not even give him anything to eat. The king, however, still vowed that

3	

3	3	he was his son, and said, ‘Is there no mark by which you would know me

3	

3	3	if I am really your son?’ ‘Yes,’ said his mother, ‘our Heinel had a mark

3	

3	3	like a raspberry on his right arm.’ Then he showed them the mark, and

3	

3	3	they knew that what he had said was true.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	He next told them how he was king of the Golden Mountain, and was

3	

3	3	married to a princess, and had a son seven years old. But the merchant

3	

3	3	said, ‘that can never be true; he must be a fine king truly who travels

3	

3	3	about in a shepherd’s frock!’ At this the son was vexed; and forgetting

3	

3	3	his word, turned his ring, and wished for his queen and son. In an

3	

3	3	instant they stood before him; but the queen wept, and said he had

3	

3	3	broken his word, and bad luck would follow. He did all he could to

3	

3	3	soothe her, and she at last seemed to be appeased; but she was not so in

3	

3	3	truth, and was only thinking how she should punish him.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	One day he took her to walk with him out of the town, and showed her

3	

3	3	the spot where the boat was set adrift upon the wide waters. Then he sat

3	

3	3	himself down, and said, ‘I am very much tired; sit by me, I will rest my

3	

3	3	head in your lap, and sleep a while.’ As soon as he had fallen asleep,

3	

3	3	however, she drew the ring from his finger, and crept softly away, and

3	

3	3	wished herself and her son at home in their kingdom. And when he awoke

3	

3	3	he found himself alone, and saw that the ring was gone from his finger.

3	

3	3	‘I can never go back to my father’s house,’ said he; ‘they would say I

3	

3	3	am a sorcerer: I will journey forth into the world, till I come again to

3	

3	3	my kingdom.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So saying he set out and travelled till he came to a hill, where three

3	

3	3	giants were sharing their father’s goods; and as they saw him pass they

3	

3	3	cried out and said, ‘Little men have sharp wits; he shall part the goods

3	

3	3	between us.’ Now there was a sword that cut off an enemy’s head whenever

3	

3	3	the wearer gave the words, ‘Heads off!’; a cloak that made the owner

3	

3	3	invisible, or gave him any form he pleased; and a pair of boots that

3	

3	3	carried the wearer wherever he wished. Heinel said they must first let

3	

3	3	him try these wonderful things, then he might know how to set a value

3	

3	3	upon them. Then they gave him the cloak, and he wished himself a fly,

3	

3	3	and in a moment he was a fly. ‘The cloak is very well,’ said he: ‘now

3	

3	3	give me the sword.’ ‘No,’ said they; ‘not unless you undertake not to

3	

3	3	say, “Heads off!” for if you do we are all dead men.’ So they gave it

3	

3	3	him, charging him to try it on a tree. He next asked for the boots also;

3	

3	3	and the moment he had all three in his power, he wished himself at

3	

3	3	the Golden Mountain; and there he was at once. So the giants were left

3	

3	3	behind with no goods to share or quarrel about.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	As Heinel came near his castle he heard the sound of merry music; and

3	

3	3	the people around told him that his queen was about to marry another

3	

3	3	husband. Then he threw his cloak around him, and passed through the

3	

3	3	castle hall, and placed himself by the side of the queen, where no one

3	

3	3	saw him. But when anything to eat was put upon her plate, he took it

3	

3	3	away and ate it himself; and when a glass of wine was handed to her, he

3	

3	3	took it and drank it; and thus, though they kept on giving her meat and

3	

3	3	drink, her plate and cup were always empty.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Upon this, fear and remorse came over her, and she went into her chamber

3	

3	3	alone, and sat there weeping; and he followed her there. ‘Alas!’ said

3	

3	3	she to herself, ‘was I not once set free? Why then does this enchantment

3	

3	3	still seem to bind me?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘False and fickle one!’ said he. ‘One indeed came who set thee free, and

3	

3	3	he is now near thee again; but how have you used him? Ought he to

3	

3	3	have had such treatment from thee?’ Then he went out and sent away the

3	

3	3	company, and said the wedding was at an end, for that he was come back

3	

3	3	to the kingdom. But the princes, peers, and great men mocked at him.

3	

3	3	However, he would enter into no parley with them, but only asked them

3	

3	3	if they would go in peace or not. Then they turned upon him and tried

3	

3	3	to seize him; but he drew his sword. ‘Heads Off!’ cried he; and with the

3	

3	3	word the traitors’ heads fell before him, and Heinel was once more king

3	

3	3	of the Golden Mountain.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	DOCTOR KNOWALL

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once upon a time a poor peasant called Crabb, who drove with

3	

3	3	two oxen a load of wood to the town, and sold it to a doctor for two

3	

3	3	talers. When the money was being counted out to him, it so happened that

3	

3	3	the doctor was sitting at table, and when the peasant saw how well he

3	

3	3	ate and drank, his heart desired what he saw, and would willingly

3	

3	3	have been a doctor too. So he remained standing a while, and at length

3	

3	3	inquired if he too could not be a doctor. ‘Oh, yes,’ said the doctor,

3	

3	3	‘that is soon managed.’ ‘What must I do?’ asked the peasant. ‘In the

3	

3	3	first place buy yourself an A B C book of the kind which has a cock on

3	

3	3	the frontispiece; in the second, turn your cart and your two oxen into

3	

3	3	money, and get yourself some clothes, and whatsoever else pertains to

3	

3	3	medicine; thirdly, have a sign painted for yourself with the words: “I

3	

3	3	am Doctor Knowall,” and have that nailed up above your house-door.’ The

3	

3	3	peasant did everything that he had been told to do. When he had doctored

3	

3	3	people awhile, but not long, a rich and great lord had some money

3	

3	3	stolen. Then he was told about Doctor Knowall who lived in such and such

3	

3	3	a village, and must know what had become of the money. So the lord had

3	

3	3	the horses harnessed to his carriage, drove out to the village, and

3	

3	3	asked Crabb if he were Doctor Knowall. Yes, he was, he said. Then he was

3	

3	3	to go with him and bring back the stolen money. ‘Oh, yes, but Grete, my

3	

3	3	wife, must go too.’ The lord was willing, and let both of them have a

3	

3	3	seat in the carriage, and they all drove away together. When they came

3	

3	3	to the nobleman’s castle, the table was spread, and Crabb was told to

3	

3	3	sit down and eat. ‘Yes, but my wife, Grete, too,’ said he, and he seated

3	

3	3	himself with her at the table. And when the first servant came with a

3	

3	3	dish of delicate fare, the peasant nudged his wife, and said: ‘Grete,

3	

3	3	that was the first,’ meaning that was the servant who brought the first

3	

3	3	dish. The servant, however, thought he intended by that to say: ‘That is

3	

3	3	the first thief,’ and as he actually was so, he was terrified, and said

3	

3	3	to his comrade outside: ‘The doctor knows all: we shall fare ill, he

3	

3	3	said I was the first.’ The second did not want to go in at all, but was

3	

3	3	forced. So when he went in with his dish, the peasant nudged his wife,

3	

3	3	and said: ‘Grete, that is the second.’ This servant was equally alarmed,

3	

3	3	and he got out as fast as he could. The third fared no better, for the

3	

3	3	peasant again said: ‘Grete, that is the third.’ The fourth had to carry

3	

3	3	in a dish that was covered, and the lord told the doctor that he was to

3	

3	3	show his skill, and guess what was beneath the cover. Actually, there

3	

3	3	were crabs. The doctor looked at the dish, had no idea what to say, and

3	

3	3	cried: ‘Ah, poor Crabb.’ When the lord heard that, he cried: ‘There! he

3	

3	3	knows it; he must also know who has the money!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	On this the servants looked terribly uneasy, and made a sign to the

3	

3	3	doctor that they wished him to step outside for a moment. When therefore

3	

3	3	he went out, all four of them confessed to him that they had stolen

3	

3	3	the money, and said that they would willingly restore it and give him a

3	

3	3	heavy sum into the bargain, if he would not denounce them, for if he

3	

3	3	did they would be hanged. They led him to the spot where the money was

3	

3	3	concealed. With this the doctor was satisfied, and returned to the hall,

3	

3	3	sat down to the table, and said: ‘My lord, now will I search in my book

3	

3	3	where the gold is hidden.’ The fifth servant, however, crept into the

3	

3	3	stove to hear if the doctor knew still more. But the doctor sat still

3	

3	3	and opened his A B C book, turned the pages backwards and forwards, and

3	

3	3	looked for the cock. As he could not find it immediately he said: ‘I

3	

3	3	know you are there, so you had better come out!’ Then the fellow in the

3	

3	3	stove thought that the doctor meant him, and full of terror, sprang out,

3	

3	3	crying: ‘That man knows everything!’ Then Doctor Knowall showed the lord

3	

3	3	where the money was, but did not say who had stolen it, and received

3	

3	3	from both sides much money in reward, and became a renowned man.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE SEVEN RAVENS

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once a man who had seven sons, and last of all one daughter.

3	

3	3	Although the little girl was very pretty, she was so weak and small that

3	

3	3	they thought she could not live; but they said she should at once be

3	

3	3	christened.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	So the father sent one of his sons in haste to the spring to get some

3	

3	3	water, but the other six ran with him. Each wanted to be first at

3	

3	3	drawing the water, and so they were in such a hurry that all let their

3	

3	3	pitchers fall into the well, and they stood very foolishly looking at

3	

3	3	one another, and did not know what to do, for none dared go home. In the

3	

3	3	meantime the father was uneasy, and could not tell what made the

3	

3	3	young men stay so long. ‘Surely,’ said he, ‘the whole seven must have

3	

3	3	forgotten themselves over some game of play’; and when he had waited

3	

3	3	still longer and they yet did not come, he flew into a rage and wished

3	

3	3	them all turned into ravens. Scarcely had he spoken these words when he

3	

3	3	heard a croaking over his head, and looked up and saw seven ravens as

3	

3	3	black as coal flying round and round. Sorry as he was to see his wish

3	

3	3	so fulfilled, he did not know how what was done could be undone, and

3	

3	3	comforted himself as well as he could for the loss of his seven sons

3	

3	3	with his dear little daughter, who soon became stronger and every day

3	

3	3	more beautiful.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	For a long time she did not know that she had ever had any brothers; for

3	

3	3	her father and mother took care not to speak of them before her: but one

3	

3	3	day by chance she heard the people about her speak of them. ‘Yes,’ said

3	

3	3	they, ‘she is beautiful indeed, but still ‘tis a pity that her brothers

3	

3	3	should have been lost for her sake.’ Then she was much grieved, and went

3	

3	3	to her father and mother, and asked if she had any brothers, and what

3	

3	3	had become of them. So they dared no longer hide the truth from her, but

3	

3	3	said it was the will of Heaven, and that her birth was only the innocent

3	

3	3	cause of it; but the little girl mourned sadly about it every day, and

3	

3	3	thought herself bound to do all she could to bring her brothers back;

3	

3	3	and she had neither rest nor ease, till at length one day she stole

3	

3	3	away, and set out into the wide world to find her brothers, wherever

3	

3	3	they might be, and free them, whatever it might cost her.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	She took nothing with her but a little ring which her father and mother

3	

3	3	had given her, a loaf of bread in case she should be hungry, a little

3	

3	3	pitcher of water in case she should be thirsty, and a little stool

3	

3	3	to rest upon when she should be weary. Thus she went on and on, and

3	

3	3	journeyed till she came to the world’s end; then she came to the sun,

3	

3	3	but the sun looked much too hot and fiery; so she ran away quickly to

3	

3	3	the moon, but the moon was cold and chilly, and said, ‘I smell flesh

3	

3	3	and blood this way!’ so she took herself away in a hurry and came to the

3	

3	3	stars, and the stars were friendly and kind to her, and each star sat

3	

3	3	upon his own little stool; but the morning star rose up and gave her a

3	

3	3	little piece of wood, and said, ‘If you have not this little piece of

3	

3	3	wood, you cannot unlock the castle that stands on the glass-mountain,

3	

3	3	and there your brothers live.’ The little girl took the piece of wood,

3	

3	3	rolled it up in a little cloth, and went on again until she came to the

3	

3	3	glass-mountain, and found the door shut. Then she felt for the little

3	

3	3	piece of wood; but when she unwrapped the cloth it was not there, and

3	

3	3	she saw she had lost the gift of the good stars. What was to be done?

3	

3	3	She wanted to save her brothers, and had no key of the castle of the

3	

3	3	glass-mountain; so this faithful little sister took a knife out of her

3	

3	3	pocket and cut off her little finger, that was just the size of the

3	

3	3	piece of wood she had lost, and put it in the door and opened it.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	As she went in, a little dwarf came up to her, and said, ‘What are you

3	

3	3	seeking for?’ ‘I seek for my brothers, the seven ravens,’ answered she.

3	

3	3	Then the dwarf said, ‘My masters are not at home; but if you will wait

3	

3	3	till they come, pray step in.’ Now the little dwarf was getting their

3	

3	3	dinner ready, and he brought their food upon seven little plates, and

3	

3	3	their drink in seven little glasses, and set them upon the table, and

3	

3	3	out of each little plate their sister ate a small piece, and out of each

3	

3	3	little glass she drank a small drop; but she let the ring that she had

3	

3	3	brought with her fall into the last glass.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	On a sudden she heard a fluttering and croaking in the air, and the

3	

3	3	dwarf said, ‘Here come my masters.’ When they came in, they wanted to

3	

3	3	eat and drink, and looked for their little plates and glasses. Then said

3	

3	3	one after the other,

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Who has eaten from my little plate? And who has been drinking out of my

3	

3	3	little glass?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Caw! Caw! well I ween

3	

3	3	  Mortal lips have this way been.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the seventh came to the bottom of his glass, and found there the

3	

3	3	ring, he looked at it, and knew that it was his father’s and mother’s,

3	

3	3	and said, ‘O that our little sister would but come! then we should be

3	

3	3	free.’ When the little girl heard this (for she stood behind the door

3	

3	3	all the time and listened), she ran forward, and in an instant all

3	

3	3	the ravens took their right form again; and all hugged and kissed each

3	

3	3	other, and went merrily home.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE WEDDING OF MRS FOX

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	FIRST STORY

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once upon a time an old fox with nine tails, who believed that

3	

3	3	his wife was not faithful to him, and wished to put her to the test. He

3	

3	3	stretched himself out under the bench, did not move a limb, and behaved

3	

3	3	as if he were stone dead. Mrs Fox went up to her room, shut herself in,

3	

3	3	and her maid, Miss Cat, sat by the fire, and did the cooking. When it

3	

3	3	became known that the old fox was dead, suitors presented themselves.

3	

3	3	The maid heard someone standing at the house-door, knocking. She went

3	

3	3	and opened it, and it was a young fox, who said:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘What may you be about, Miss Cat?

3	

3	3	  Do you sleep or do you wake?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	She answered:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘I am not sleeping, I am waking,

3	

3	3	  Would you know what I am making?

3	

3	3	  I am boiling warm beer with butter,

3	

3	3	  Will you be my guest for supper?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘No, thank you, miss,’ said the fox, ‘what is Mrs Fox doing?’ The maid

3	

3	3	replied:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘She is sitting in her room,

3	

3	3	  Moaning in her gloom,

3	

3	3	  Weeping her little eyes quite red,

3	

3	3	  Because old Mr Fox is dead.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Do just tell her, miss, that a young fox is here, who would like to woo

3	

3	3	her.’ ‘Certainly, young sir.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	  The cat goes up the stairs trip, trap,

3	

3	3	  The door she knocks at tap, tap, tap,

3	

3	3	 ‘Mistress Fox, are you inside?’

3	

3	3	 ‘Oh, yes, my little cat,’ she cried.

3	

3	3	 ‘A wooer he stands at the door out there.’

3	

3	3	 ‘What does he look like, my dear?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Has he nine as beautiful tails as the late Mr Fox?’ ‘Oh, no,’ answered

3	

3	3	the cat, ‘he has only one.’ ‘Then I will not have him.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Miss Cat went downstairs and sent the wooer away. Soon afterwards there

3	

3	3	was another knock, and another fox was at the door who wished to woo Mrs

3	

3	3	Fox. He had two tails, but he did not fare better than the first. After

3	

3	3	this still more came, each with one tail more than the other, but they

3	

3	3	were all turned away, until at last one came who had nine tails, like

3	

3	3	old Mr Fox. When the widow heard that, she said joyfully to the cat:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Now open the gates and doors all wide,

3	

3	3	  And carry old Mr Fox outside.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But just as the wedding was going to be solemnized, old Mr Fox stirred

3	

3	3	under the bench, and cudgelled all the rabble, and drove them and Mrs

3	

3	3	Fox out of the house.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	SECOND STORY

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When old Mr Fox was dead, the wolf came as a suitor, and knocked at the

3	

3	3	door, and the cat who was servant to Mrs Fox, opened it for him. The

3	

3	3	wolf greeted her, and said:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Good day, Mrs Cat of Kehrewit,

3	

3	3	  How comes it that alone you sit?

3	

3	3	  What are you making good?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The cat replied:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘In milk I’m breaking bread so sweet,

3	

3	3	  Will you be my guest, and eat?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘No, thank you, Mrs Cat,’ answered the wolf. ‘Is Mrs Fox not at home?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The cat said:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘She sits upstairs in her room,

3	

3	3	  Bewailing her sorrowful doom,

3	

3	3	  Bewailing her trouble so sore,

3	

3	3	  For old Mr Fox is no more.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The wolf answered:

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘If she’s in want of a husband now,

3	

3	3	  Then will it please her to step below?’

3	

3	3	  The cat runs quickly up the stair,

3	

3	3	  And lets her tail fly here and there,

3	

3	3	  Until she comes to the parlour door.

3	

3	3	  With her five gold rings at the door she knocks:

3	

3	3	 ‘Are you within, good Mistress Fox?

3	

3	3	  If you’re in want of a husband now,

3	

3	3	  Then will it please you to step below?

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Mrs Fox asked: ‘Has the gentleman red stockings on, and has he a pointed

3	

3	3	mouth?’ ‘No,’ answered the cat. ‘Then he won’t do for me.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the wolf was gone, came a dog, a stag, a hare, a bear, a lion, and

3	

3	3	all the beasts of the forest, one after the other. But one of the good

3	

3	3	qualities which old Mr Fox had possessed, was always lacking, and the

3	

3	3	cat had continually to send the suitors away. At length came a young

3	

3	3	fox. Then Mrs Fox said: ‘Has the gentleman red stockings on, and has a

3	

3	3	little pointed mouth?’ ‘Yes,’ said the cat, ‘he has.’ ‘Then let him come

3	

3	3	upstairs,’ said Mrs Fox, and ordered the servant to prepare the wedding

3	

3	3	feast.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	 ‘Sweep me the room as clean as you can,

3	

3	3	  Up with the window, fling out my old man!

3	

3	3	  For many a fine fat mouse he brought,

3	

3	3	  Yet of his wife he never thought,

3	

3	3	  But ate up every one he caught.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the wedding was solemnized with young Mr Fox, and there was much

3	

3	3	rejoicing and dancing; and if they have not left off, they are dancing

3	

3	3	still.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE SALAD

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	As a merry young huntsman was once going briskly along through a wood,

3	

3	3	there came up a little old woman, and said to him, ‘Good day, good day;

3	

3	3	you seem merry enough, but I am hungry and thirsty; do pray give me

3	

3	3	something to eat.’ The huntsman took pity on her, and put his hand in

3	

3	3	his pocket and gave her what he had. Then he wanted to go his way; but

3	

3	3	she took hold of him, and said, ‘Listen, my friend, to what I am going

3	

3	3	to tell you; I will reward you for your kindness; go your way, and after

3	

3	3	a little time you will come to a tree where you will see nine birds

3	

3	3	sitting on a cloak. Shoot into the midst of them, and one will fall down

3	

3	3	dead: the cloak will fall too; take it, it is a wishing-cloak, and when

3	

3	3	you wear it you will find yourself at any place where you may wish to

3	

3	3	be. Cut open the dead bird, take out its heart and keep it, and you will

3	

3	3	find a piece of gold under your pillow every morning when you rise. It

3	

3	3	is the bird’s heart that will bring you this good luck.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The huntsman thanked her, and thought to himself, ‘If all this does

3	

3	3	happen, it will be a fine thing for me.’ When he had gone a hundred

3	

3	3	steps or so, he heard a screaming and chirping in the branches over him,

3	

3	3	and looked up and saw a flock of birds pulling a cloak with their bills

3	

3	3	and feet; screaming, fighting, and tugging at each other as if

3	

3	3	each wished to have it himself. ‘Well,’ said the huntsman, ‘this is

3	

3	3	wonderful; this happens just as the old woman said’; then he shot into

3	

3	3	the midst of them so that their feathers flew all about. Off went the

3	

3	3	flock chattering away; but one fell down dead, and the cloak with it.

3	

3	3	Then the huntsman did as the old woman told him, cut open the bird, took

3	

3	3	out the heart, and carried the cloak home with him.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The next morning when he awoke he lifted up his pillow, and there lay

3	

3	3	the piece of gold glittering underneath; the same happened next day, and

3	

3	3	indeed every day when he arose. He heaped up a great deal of gold, and

3	

3	3	at last thought to himself, ‘Of what use is this gold to me whilst I am

3	

3	3	at home? I will go out into the world and look about me.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then he took leave of his friends, and hung his bag and bow about his

3	

3	3	neck, and went his way. It so happened that his road one day led through

3	

3	3	a thick wood, at the end of which was a large castle in a green meadow,

3	

3	3	and at one of the windows stood an old woman with a very beautiful young

3	

3	3	lady by her side looking about them. Now the old woman was a witch, and

3	

3	3	said to the young lady, ‘There is a young man coming out of the wood who

3	

3	3	carries a wonderful prize; we must get it away from him, my dear child,

3	

3	3	for it is more fit for us than for him. He has a bird’s heart that

3	

3	3	brings a piece of gold under his pillow every morning.’ Meantime the

3	

3	3	huntsman came nearer and looked at the lady, and said to himself, ‘I

3	

3	3	have been travelling so long that I should like to go into this castle

3	

3	3	and rest myself, for I have money enough to pay for anything I want’;

3	

3	3	but the real reason was, that he wanted to see more of the beautiful

3	

3	3	lady. Then he went into the house, and was welcomed kindly; and it was

3	

3	3	not long before he was so much in love that he thought of nothing else

3	

3	3	but looking at the lady’s eyes, and doing everything that she wished.

3	

3	3	Then the old woman said, ‘Now is the time for getting the bird’s heart.’

3	

3	3	So the lady stole it away, and he never found any more gold under his

3	

3	3	pillow, for it lay now under the young lady’s, and the old woman took it

3	

3	3	away every morning; but he was so much in love that he never missed his

3	

3	3	prize.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Well,’ said the old witch, ‘we have got the bird’s heart, but not the

3	

3	3	wishing-cloak yet, and that we must also get.’ ‘Let us leave him that,’

3	

3	3	said the young lady; ‘he has already lost his wealth.’ Then the witch

3	

3	3	was very angry, and said, ‘Such a cloak is a very rare and wonderful

3	

3	3	thing, and I must and will have it.’ So she did as the old woman told

3	

3	3	her, and set herself at the window, and looked about the country and

3	

3	3	seemed very sorrowful; then the huntsman said, ‘What makes you so sad?’

3	

3	3	‘Alas! dear sir,’ said she, ‘yonder lies the granite rock where all the

3	

3	3	costly diamonds grow, and I want so much to go there, that whenever I

3	

3	3	think of it I cannot help being sorrowful, for who can reach it? only

3	

3	3	the birds and the flies--man cannot.’ ‘If that’s all your grief,’ said

3	

3	3	the huntsman, ‘I’ll take you there with all my heart’; so he drew her under

3	

3	3	his cloak, and the moment he wished to be on the granite mountain they

3	

3	3	were both there. The diamonds glittered so on all sides that they were

3	

3	3	delighted with the sight and picked up the finest. But the old witch

3	

3	3	made a deep sleep come upon him, and he said to the young lady, ‘Let us

3	

3	3	sit down and rest ourselves a little, I am so tired that I cannot stand

3	

3	3	any longer.’ So they sat down, and he laid his head in her lap and

3	

3	3	fell asleep; and whilst he was sleeping on she took the cloak from

3	

3	3	his shoulders, hung it on her own, picked up the diamonds, and wished

3	

3	3	herself home again.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When he awoke and found that his lady had tricked him, and left him

3	

3	3	alone on the wild rock, he said, ‘Alas! what roguery there is in the

3	

3	3	world!’ and there he sat in great grief and fear, not knowing what to

3	

3	3	do. Now this rock belonged to fierce giants who lived upon it; and as

3	

3	3	he saw three of them striding about, he thought to himself, ‘I can only

3	

3	3	save myself by feigning to be asleep’; so he laid himself down as if he

3	

3	3	were in a sound sleep. When the giants came up to him, the first pushed

3	

3	3	him with his foot, and said, ‘What worm is this that lies here curled

3	

3	3	up?’ ‘Tread upon him and kill him,’ said the second. ‘It’s not worth the

3	

3	3	trouble,’ said the third; ‘let him live, he’ll go climbing higher up the

3	

3	3	mountain, and some cloud will come rolling and carry him away.’ And they

3	

3	3	passed on. But the huntsman had heard all they said; and as soon as they

3	

3	3	were gone, he climbed to the top of the mountain, and when he had sat

3	

3	3	there a short time a cloud came rolling around him, and caught him in a

3	

3	3	whirlwind and bore him along for some time, till it settled in a garden,

3	

3	3	and he fell quite gently to the ground amongst the greens and cabbages.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then he looked around him, and said, ‘I wish I had something to eat, if

3	

3	3	not I shall be worse off than before; for here I see neither apples

3	

3	3	nor pears, nor any kind of fruits, nothing but vegetables.’ At last he

3	

3	3	thought to himself, ‘I can eat salad, it will refresh and strengthen

3	

3	3	me.’ So he picked out a fine head and ate of it; but scarcely had he

3	

3	3	swallowed two bites when he felt himself quite changed, and saw with

3	

3	3	horror that he was turned into an ass. However, he still felt very

3	

3	3	hungry, and the salad tasted very nice; so he ate on till he came

3	

3	3	to another kind of salad, and scarcely had he tasted it when he felt

3	

3	3	another change come over him, and soon saw that he was lucky enough to

3	

3	3	have found his old shape again.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then he laid himself down and slept off a little of his weariness; and

3	

3	3	when he awoke the next morning he broke off a head both of the good and

3	

3	3	the bad salad, and thought to himself, ‘This will help me to my fortune

3	

3	3	again, and enable me to pay off some folks for their treachery.’ So he

3	

3	3	went away to try and find the castle of his friends; and after wandering

3	

3	3	about a few days he luckily found it. Then he stained his face all over

3	

3	3	brown, so that even his mother would not have known him, and went into

3	

3	3	the castle and asked for a lodging; ‘I am so tired,’ said he, ‘that I

3	

3	3	can go no farther.’ ‘Countryman,’ said the witch, ‘who are you? and what

3	

3	3	is your business?’ ‘I am,’ said he, ‘a messenger sent by the king to

3	

3	3	find the finest salad that grows under the sun. I have been lucky

3	

3	3	enough to find it, and have brought it with me; but the heat of the sun

3	

3	3	scorches so that it begins to wither, and I don’t know that I can carry

3	

3	3	it farther.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the witch and the young lady heard of his beautiful salad, they

3	

3	3	longed to taste it, and said, ‘Dear countryman, let us just taste it.’

3	

3	3	‘To be sure,’ answered he; ‘I have two heads of it with me, and will

3	

3	3	give you one’; so he opened his bag and gave them the bad. Then the

3	

3	3	witch herself took it into the kitchen to be dressed; and when it was

3	

3	3	ready she could not wait till it was carried up, but took a few leaves

3	

3	3	immediately and put them in her mouth, and scarcely were they swallowed

3	

3	3	when she lost her own form and ran braying down into the court in the

3	

3	3	form of an ass. Now the servant-maid came into the kitchen, and seeing

3	

3	3	the salad ready, was going to carry it up; but on the way she too felt a

3	

3	3	wish to taste it as the old woman had done, and ate some leaves; so she

3	

3	3	also was turned into an ass and ran after the other, letting the dish

3	

3	3	with the salad fall on the ground. The messenger sat all this time with

3	

3	3	the beautiful young lady, and as nobody came with the salad and she

3	

3	3	longed to taste it, she said, ‘I don’t know where the salad can be.’

3	

3	3	Then he thought something must have happened, and said, ‘I will go

3	

3	3	into the kitchen and see.’ And as he went he saw two asses in the court

3	

3	3	running about, and the salad lying on the ground. ‘All right!’ said

3	

3	3	he; ‘those two have had their share.’ Then he took up the rest of

3	

3	3	the leaves, laid them on the dish and brought them to the young lady,

3	

3	3	saying, ‘I bring you the dish myself that you may not wait any longer.’

3	

3	3	So she ate of it, and like the others ran off into the court braying

3	

3	3	away.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the huntsman washed his face and went into the court that they

3	

3	3	might know him. ‘Now you shall be paid for your roguery,’ said he; and

3	

3	3	tied them all three to a rope and took them along with him till he

3	

3	3	came to a mill and knocked at the window. ‘What’s the matter?’ said the

3	

3	3	miller. ‘I have three tiresome beasts here,’ said the other; ‘if you

3	

3	3	will take them, give them food and room, and treat them as I tell you,

3	

3	3	I will pay you whatever you ask.’ ‘With all my heart,’ said the miller;

3	

3	3	‘but how shall I treat them?’ Then the huntsman said, ‘Give the old

3	

3	3	one stripes three times a day and hay once; give the next (who was

3	

3	3	the servant-maid) stripes once a day and hay three times; and give

3	

3	3	the youngest (who was the beautiful lady) hay three times a day and

3	

3	3	no stripes’: for he could not find it in his heart to have her beaten.

3	

3	3	After this he went back to the castle, where he found everything he

3	

3	3	wanted.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Some days after, the miller came to him and told him that the old ass

3	

3	3	was dead; ‘The other two,’ said he, ‘are alive and eat, but are so

3	

3	3	sorrowful that they cannot last long.’ Then the huntsman pitied them,

3	

3	3	and told the miller to drive them back to him, and when they came, he

3	

3	3	gave them some of the good salad to eat. And the beautiful young lady

3	

3	3	fell upon her knees before him, and said, ‘O dearest huntsman! forgive

3	

3	3	me all the ill I have done you; my mother forced me to it, it was

3	

3	3	against my will, for I always loved you very much. Your wishing-cloak

3	

3	3	hangs up in the closet, and as for the bird’s heart, I will give it you

3	

3	3	too.’ But he said, ‘Keep it, it will be just the same thing, for I mean

3	

3	3	to make you my wife.’ So they were married, and lived together very

3	

3	3	happily till they died.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	THE STORY OF THE YOUTH WHO WENT FORTH TO LEARN WHAT FEAR WAS

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	A certain father had two sons, the elder of who was smart and sensible,

3	

3	3	and could do everything, but the younger was stupid and could neither

3	

3	3	learn nor understand anything, and when people saw him they said:

3	

3	3	‘There’s a fellow who will give his father some trouble!’ When anything

3	

3	3	had to be done, it was always the elder who was forced to do it; but

3	

3	3	if his father bade him fetch anything when it was late, or in the

3	

3	3	night-time, and the way led through the churchyard, or any other dismal

3	

3	3	place, he answered: ‘Oh, no father, I’ll not go there, it makes me

3	

3	3	shudder!’ for he was afraid. Or when stories were told by the fire at

3	

3	3	night which made the flesh creep, the listeners sometimes said: ‘Oh,

3	

3	3	it makes us shudder!’ The younger sat in a corner and listened with

3	

3	3	the rest of them, and could not imagine what they could mean. ‘They are

3	

3	3	always saying: “It makes me shudder, it makes me shudder!” It does not

3	

3	3	make me shudder,’ thought he. ‘That, too, must be an art of which I

3	

3	3	understand nothing!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Now it came to pass that his father said to him one day: ‘Hearken to me,

3	

3	3	you fellow in the corner there, you are growing tall and strong, and you

3	

3	3	too must learn something by which you can earn your bread. Look how your

3	

3	3	brother works, but you do not even earn your salt.’ ‘Well, father,’ he

3	

3	3	replied, ‘I am quite willing to learn something--indeed, if it could but

3	

3	3	be managed, I should like to learn how to shudder. I don’t understand

3	

3	3	that at all yet.’ The elder brother smiled when he heard that, and

3	

3	3	thought to himself: ‘Goodness, what a blockhead that brother of mine is!

3	

3	3	He will never be good for anything as long as he lives! He who wants to

3	

3	3	be a sickle must bend himself betimes.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The father sighed, and answered him: ‘You shall soon learn what it is to

3	

3	3	shudder, but you will not earn your bread by that.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Soon after this the sexton came to the house on a visit, and the father

3	

3	3	bewailed his trouble, and told him how his younger son was so backward

3	

3	3	in every respect that he knew nothing and learnt nothing. ‘Just think,’

3	

3	3	said he, ‘when I asked him how he was going to earn his bread, he

3	

3	3	actually wanted to learn to shudder.’ ‘If that be all,’ replied the

3	

3	3	sexton, ‘he can learn that with me. Send him to me, and I will soon

3	

3	3	polish him.’ The father was glad to do it, for he thought: ‘It will

3	

3	3	train the boy a little.’ The sexton therefore took him into his house,

3	

3	3	and he had to ring the church bell. After a day or two, the sexton awoke

3	

3	3	him at midnight, and bade him arise and go up into the church tower and

3	

3	3	ring the bell. ‘You shall soon learn what shuddering is,’ thought he,

3	

3	3	and secretly went there before him; and when the boy was at the top of

3	

3	3	the tower and turned round, and was just going to take hold of the bell

3	

3	3	rope, he saw a white figure standing on the stairs opposite the sounding

3	

3	3	hole. ‘Who is there?’ cried he, but the figure made no reply, and did

3	

3	3	not move or stir. ‘Give an answer,’ cried the boy, ‘or take yourself

3	

3	3	off, you have no business here at night.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The sexton, however, remained standing motionless that the boy might

3	

3	3	think he was a ghost. The boy cried a second time: ‘What do you want

3	

3	3	here?--speak if you are an honest fellow, or I will throw you down the

3	

3	3	steps!’ The sexton thought: ‘He can’t mean to be as bad as his words,’

3	

3	3	uttered no sound and stood as if he were made of stone. Then the boy

3	

3	3	called to him for the third time, and as that was also to no purpose,

3	

3	3	he ran against him and pushed the ghost down the stairs, so that it fell

3	

3	3	down the ten steps and remained lying there in a corner. Thereupon he

3	

3	3	rang the bell, went home, and without saying a word went to bed, and

3	

3	3	fell asleep. The sexton’s wife waited a long time for her husband, but

3	

3	3	he did not come back. At length she became uneasy, and wakened the boy,

3	

3	3	and asked: ‘Do you know where my husband is? He climbed up the tower

3	

3	3	before you did.’ ‘No, I don’t know,’ replied the boy, ‘but someone was

3	

3	3	standing by the sounding hole on the other side of the steps, and as he

3	

3	3	would neither gave an answer nor go away, I took him for a scoundrel,

3	

3	3	and threw him downstairs. Just go there and you will see if it was he.

3	

3	3	I should be sorry if it were.’ The woman ran away and found her husband,

3	

3	3	who was lying moaning in the corner, and had broken his leg.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	She carried him down, and then with loud screams she hastened to the

3	

3	3	boy’s father, ‘Your boy,’ cried she, ‘has been the cause of a great

3	

3	3	misfortune! He has thrown my husband down the steps so that he broke his

3	

3	3	leg. Take the good-for-nothing fellow out of our house.’ The father was

3	

3	3	terrified, and ran thither and scolded the boy. ‘What wicked tricks

3	

3	3	are these?’ said he. ‘The devil must have put them into your head.’

3	

3	3	‘Father,’ he replied, ‘do listen to me. I am quite innocent. He was

3	

3	3	standing there by night like one intent on doing evil. I did not know

3	

3	3	who it was, and I entreated him three times either to speak or to go

3	

3	3	away.’ ‘Ah,’ said the father, ‘I have nothing but unhappiness with you.

3	

3	3	Go out of my sight. I will see you no more.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Yes, father, right willingly, wait only until it is day. Then will I

3	

3	3	go forth and learn how to shudder, and then I shall, at any rate,

3	

3	3	understand one art which will support me.’ ‘Learn what you will,’ spoke

3	

3	3	the father, ‘it is all the same to me. Here are fifty talers for you.

3	

3	3	Take these and go into the wide world, and tell no one from whence you

3	

3	3	come, and who is your father, for I have reason to be ashamed of you.’

3	

3	3	‘Yes, father, it shall be as you will. If you desire nothing more than

3	

3	3	that, I can easily keep it in mind.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the day dawned, therefore, the boy put his fifty talers into his

3	

3	3	pocket, and went forth on the great highway, and continually said to

3	

3	3	himself: ‘If I could but shudder! If I could but shudder!’ Then a man

3	

3	3	approached who heard this conversation which the youth was holding with

3	

3	3	himself, and when they had walked a little farther to where they could

3	

3	3	see the gallows, the man said to him: ‘Look, there is the tree where

3	

3	3	seven men have married the ropemaker’s daughter, and are now learning

3	

3	3	how to fly. Sit down beneath it, and wait till night comes, and you will

3	

3	3	soon learn how to shudder.’ ‘If that is all that is wanted,’ answered

3	

3	3	the youth, ‘it is easily done; but if I learn how to shudder as fast as

3	

3	3	that, you shall have my fifty talers. Just come back to me early in the

3	

3	3	morning.’ Then the youth went to the gallows, sat down beneath it, and

3	

3	3	waited till evening came. And as he was cold, he lighted himself a fire,

3	

3	3	but at midnight the wind blew so sharply that in spite of his fire, he

3	

3	3	could not get warm. And as the wind knocked the hanged men against each

3	

3	3	other, and they moved backwards and forwards, he thought to himself:

3	

3	3	‘If you shiver below by the fire, how those up above must freeze and

3	

3	3	suffer!’ And as he felt pity for them, he raised the ladder, and climbed

3	

3	3	up, unbound one of them after the other, and brought down all seven.

3	

3	3	Then he stoked the fire, blew it, and set them all round it to warm

3	

3	3	themselves. But they sat there and did not stir, and the fire caught

3	

3	3	their clothes. So he said: ‘Take care, or I will hang you up again.’ The

3	

3	3	dead men, however, did not hear, but were quite silent, and let their

3	

3	3	rags go on burning. At this he grew angry, and said: ‘If you will not

3	

3	3	take care, I cannot help you, I will not be burnt with you,’ and he hung

3	

3	3	them up again each in his turn. Then he sat down by his fire and fell

3	

3	3	asleep, and the next morning the man came to him and wanted to have

3	

3	3	the fifty talers, and said: ‘Well do you know how to shudder?’ ‘No,’

3	

3	3	answered he, ‘how should I know? Those fellows up there did not open

3	

3	3	their mouths, and were so stupid that they let the few old rags which

3	

3	3	they had on their bodies get burnt.’ Then the man saw that he would not

3	

3	3	get the fifty talers that day, and went away saying: ‘Such a youth has

3	

3	3	never come my way before.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The youth likewise went his way, and once more began to mutter to

3	

3	3	himself: ‘Ah, if I could but shudder! Ah, if I could but shudder!’ A

3	

3	3	waggoner who was striding behind him heard this and asked: ‘Who are

3	

3	3	you?’ ‘I don’t know,’ answered the youth. Then the waggoner asked: ‘From

3	

3	3	whence do you come?’ ‘I know not.’ ‘Who is your father?’ ‘That I may

3	

3	3	not tell you.’ ‘What is it that you are always muttering between your

3	

3	3	teeth?’ ‘Ah,’ replied the youth, ‘I do so wish I could shudder, but

3	

3	3	no one can teach me how.’ ‘Enough of your foolish chatter,’ said the

3	

3	3	waggoner. ‘Come, go with me, I will see about a place for you.’ The

3	

3	3	youth went with the waggoner, and in the evening they arrived at an inn

3	

3	3	where they wished to pass the night. Then at the entrance of the parlour

3	

3	3	the youth again said quite loudly: ‘If I could but shudder! If I could

3	

3	3	but shudder!’ The host who heard this, laughed and said: ‘If that is

3	

3	3	your desire, there ought to be a good opportunity for you here.’ ‘Ah,

3	

3	3	be silent,’ said the hostess, ‘so many prying persons have already lost

3	

3	3	their lives, it would be a pity and a shame if such beautiful eyes as

3	

3	3	these should never see the daylight again.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But the youth said: ‘However difficult it may be, I will learn it. For

3	

3	3	this purpose indeed have I journeyed forth.’ He let the host have

3	

3	3	no rest, until the latter told him, that not far from thence stood a

3	

3	3	haunted castle where anyone could very easily learn what shuddering was,

3	

3	3	if he would but watch in it for three nights. The king had promised that

3	

3	3	he who would venture should have his daughter to wife, and she was the

3	

3	3	most beautiful maiden the sun shone on. Likewise in the castle lay great

3	

3	3	treasures, which were guarded by evil spirits, and these treasures would

3	

3	3	then be freed, and would make a poor man rich enough. Already many men

3	

3	3	had gone into the castle, but as yet none had come out again. Then the

3	

3	3	youth went next morning to the king, and said: ‘If it be allowed, I will

3	

3	3	willingly watch three nights in the haunted castle.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The king looked at him, and as the youth pleased him, he said: ‘You may

3	

3	3	ask for three things to take into the castle with you, but they must

3	

3	3	be things without life.’ Then he answered: ‘Then I ask for a fire, a

3	

3	3	turning lathe, and a cutting-board with the knife.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The king had these things carried into the castle for him during the

3	

3	3	day. When night was drawing near, the youth went up and made himself

3	

3	3	a bright fire in one of the rooms, placed the cutting-board and knife

3	

3	3	beside it, and seated himself by the turning-lathe. ‘Ah, if I could

3	

3	3	but shudder!’ said he, ‘but I shall not learn it here either.’ Towards

3	

3	3	midnight he was about to poke his fire, and as he was blowing it,

3	

3	3	something cried suddenly from one corner: ‘Au, miau! how cold we are!’

3	

3	3	‘You fools!’ cried he, ‘what are you crying about? If you are cold, come

3	

3	3	and take a seat by the fire and warm yourselves.’ And when he had said

3	

3	3	that, two great black cats came with one tremendous leap and sat down

3	

3	3	on each side of him, and looked savagely at him with their fiery

3	

3	3	eyes. After a short time, when they had warmed themselves, they said:

3	

3	3	‘Comrade, shall we have a game of cards?’ ‘Why not?’ he replied, ‘but

3	

3	3	just show me your paws.’ Then they stretched out their claws. ‘Oh,’ said

3	

3	3	he, ‘what long nails you have! Wait, I must first cut them for you.’

3	

3	3	Thereupon he seized them by the throats, put them on the cutting-board

3	

3	3	and screwed their feet fast. ‘I have looked at your fingers,’ said he,

3	

3	3	‘and my fancy for card-playing has gone,’ and he struck them dead and

3	

3	3	threw them out into the water. But when he had made away with these two,

3	

3	3	and was about to sit down again by his fire, out from every hole and

3	

3	3	corner came black cats and black dogs with red-hot chains, and more

3	

3	3	and more of them came until he could no longer move, and they yelled

3	

3	3	horribly, and got on his fire, pulled it to pieces, and tried to put

3	

3	3	it out. He watched them for a while quietly, but at last when they were

3	

3	3	going too far, he seized his cutting-knife, and cried: ‘Away with you,

3	

3	3	vermin,’ and began to cut them down. Some of them ran away, the others

3	

3	3	he killed, and threw out into the fish-pond. When he came back he fanned

3	

3	3	the embers of his fire again and warmed himself. And as he thus sat, his

3	

3	3	eyes would keep open no longer, and he felt a desire to sleep. Then he

3	

3	3	looked round and saw a great bed in the corner. ‘That is the very thing

3	

3	3	for me,’ said he, and got into it. When he was just going to shut his

3	

3	3	eyes, however, the bed began to move of its own accord, and went over

3	

3	3	the whole of the castle. ‘That’s right,’ said he, ‘but go faster.’ Then

3	

3	3	the bed rolled on as if six horses were harnessed to it, up and down,

3	

3	3	over thresholds and stairs, but suddenly hop, hop, it turned over upside

3	

3	3	down, and lay on him like a mountain. But he threw quilts and pillows up

3	

3	3	in the air, got out and said: ‘Now anyone who likes, may drive,’ and

3	

3	3	lay down by his fire, and slept till it was day. In the morning the king

3	

3	3	came, and when he saw him lying there on the ground, he thought the evil

3	

3	3	spirits had killed him and he was dead. Then said he: ‘After all it is a

3	

3	3	pity,--for so handsome a man.’ The youth heard it, got up, and said: ‘It

3	

3	3	has not come to that yet.’ Then the king was astonished, but very glad,

3	

3	3	and asked how he had fared. ‘Very well indeed,’ answered he; ‘one

3	

3	3	night is past, the two others will pass likewise.’ Then he went to the

3	

3	3	innkeeper, who opened his eyes very wide, and said: ‘I never expected to

3	

3	3	see you alive again! Have you learnt how to shudder yet?’ ‘No,’ said he,

3	

3	3	‘it is all in vain. If someone would but tell me!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The second night he again went up into the old castle, sat down by the

3	

3	3	fire, and once more began his old song: ‘If I could but shudder!’ When

3	

3	3	midnight came, an uproar and noise of tumbling about was heard; at

3	

3	3	first it was low, but it grew louder and louder. Then it was quiet for

3	

3	3	a while, and at length with a loud scream, half a man came down the

3	

3	3	chimney and fell before him. ‘Hullo!’ cried he, ‘another half belongs

3	

3	3	to this. This is not enough!’ Then the uproar began again, there was a

3	

3	3	roaring and howling, and the other half fell down likewise. ‘Wait,’ said

3	

3	3	he, ‘I will just stoke up the fire a little for you.’ When he had done

3	

3	3	that and looked round again, the two pieces were joined together, and a

3	

3	3	hideous man was sitting in his place. ‘That is no part of our bargain,’

3	

3	3	said the youth, ‘the bench is mine.’ The man wanted to push him away;

3	

3	3	the youth, however, would not allow that, but thrust him off with all

3	

3	3	his strength, and seated himself again in his own place. Then still more

3	

3	3	men fell down, one after the other; they brought nine dead men’s legs

3	

3	3	and two skulls, and set them up and played at nine-pins with them. The

3	

3	3	youth also wanted to play and said: ‘Listen you, can I join you?’ ‘Yes,

3	

3	3	if you have any money.’ ‘Money enough,’ replied he, ‘but your balls are

3	

3	3	not quite round.’ Then he took the skulls and put them in the lathe and

3	

3	3	turned them till they were round. ‘There, now they will roll better!’

3	

3	3	said he. ‘Hurrah! now we’ll have fun!’ He played with them and lost some

3	

3	3	of his money, but when it struck twelve, everything vanished from his

3	

3	3	sight. He lay down and quietly fell asleep. Next morning the king came

3	

3	3	to inquire after him. ‘How has it fared with you this time?’ asked he.

3	

3	3	‘I have been playing at nine-pins,’ he answered, ‘and have lost a couple

3	

3	3	of farthings.’ ‘Have you not shuddered then?’ ‘What?’ said he, ‘I have

3	

3	3	had a wonderful time! If I did but know what it was to shudder!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The third night he sat down again on his bench and said quite sadly:

3	

3	3	‘If I could but shudder.’ When it grew late, six tall men came in and

3	

3	3	brought a coffin. Then he said: ‘Ha, ha, that is certainly my little

3	

3	3	cousin, who died only a few days ago,’ and he beckoned with his finger,

3	

3	3	and cried: ‘Come, little cousin, come.’ They placed the coffin on the

3	

3	3	ground, but he went to it and took the lid off, and a dead man lay

3	

3	3	therein. He felt his face, but it was cold as ice. ‘Wait,’ said he, ‘I

3	

3	3	will warm you a little,’ and went to the fire and warmed his hand and

3	

3	3	laid it on the dead man’s face, but he remained cold. Then he took him

3	

3	3	out, and sat down by the fire and laid him on his breast and rubbed his

3	

3	3	arms that the blood might circulate again. As this also did no good, he

3	

3	3	thought to himself: ‘When two people lie in bed together, they warm each

3	

3	3	other,’ and carried him to the bed, covered him over and lay down by

3	

3	3	him. After a short time the dead man became warm too, and began to move.

3	

3	3	Then said the youth, ‘See, little cousin, have I not warmed you?’ The

3	

3	3	dead man, however, got up and cried: ‘Now will I strangle you.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘What!’ said he, ‘is that the way you thank me? You shall at once go

3	

3	3	into your coffin again,’ and he took him up, threw him into it, and shut

3	

3	3	the lid. Then came the six men and carried him away again. ‘I cannot

3	

3	3	manage to shudder,’ said he. ‘I shall never learn it here as long as I

3	

3	3	live.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then a man entered who was taller than all others, and looked terrible.

3	

3	3	He was old, however, and had a long white beard. ‘You wretch,’ cried he,

3	

3	3	‘you shall soon learn what it is to shudder, for you shall die.’ ‘Not so

3	

3	3	fast,’ replied the youth. ‘If I am to die, I shall have to have a say

3	

3	3	in it.’ ‘I will soon seize you,’ said the fiend. ‘Softly, softly, do not

3	

3	3	talk so big. I am as strong as you are, and perhaps even stronger.’

3	

3	3	‘We shall see,’ said the old man. ‘If you are stronger, I will let you

3	

3	3	go--come, we will try.’ Then he led him by dark passages to a smith’s

3	

3	3	forge, took an axe, and with one blow struck an anvil into the ground.

3	

3	3	‘I can do better than that,’ said the youth, and went to the other

3	

3	3	anvil. The old man placed himself near and wanted to look on, and his

3	

3	3	white beard hung down. Then the youth seized the axe, split the anvil

3	

3	3	with one blow, and in it caught the old man’s beard. ‘Now I have you,’

3	

3	3	said the youth. ‘Now it is your turn to die.’ Then he seized an iron bar

3	

3	3	and beat the old man till he moaned and entreated him to stop, when he

3	

3	3	would give him great riches. The youth drew out the axe and let him go.

3	

3	3	The old man led him back into the castle, and in a cellar showed him

3	

3	3	three chests full of gold. ‘Of these,’ said he, ‘one part is for the

3	

3	3	poor, the other for the king, the third yours.’ In the meantime it

3	

3	3	struck twelve, and the spirit disappeared, so that the youth stood in

3	

3	3	darkness. ‘I shall still be able to find my way out,’ said he, and felt

3	

3	3	about, found the way into the room, and slept there by his fire.

3	

3	3	Next morning the king came and said: ‘Now you must have learnt what

3	

3	3	shuddering is?’ ‘No,’ he answered; ‘what can it be? My dead cousin was

3	

3	3	here, and a bearded man came and showed me a great deal of money down

3	

3	3	below, but no one told me what it was to shudder.’ ‘Then,’ said the

3	

3	3	king, ‘you have saved the castle, and shall marry my daughter.’ ‘That

3	

3	3	is all very well,’ said he, ‘but still I do not know what it is to

3	

3	3	shudder!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the gold was brought up and the wedding celebrated; but howsoever

3	

3	3	much the young king loved his wife, and however happy he was, he still

3	

3	3	said always: ‘If I could but shudder--if I could but shudder.’ And this

3	

3	3	at last angered her. Her waiting-maid said: ‘I will find a cure for him;

3	

3	3	he shall soon learn what it is to shudder.’ She went out to the stream

3	

3	3	which flowed through the garden, and had a whole bucketful of gudgeons

3	

3	3	brought to her. At night when the young king was sleeping, his wife was

3	

3	3	to draw the clothes off him and empty the bucket full of cold water

3	

3	3	with the gudgeons in it over him, so that the little fishes would

3	

3	3	sprawl about him. Then he woke up and cried: ‘Oh, what makes me shudder

3	

3	3	so?--what makes me shudder so, dear wife? Ah! now I know what it is to

3	

3	3	shudder!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	KING GRISLY-BEARD

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	A great king of a land far away in the East had a daughter who was very

3	

3	3	beautiful, but so proud, and haughty, and conceited, that none of the

3	

3	3	princes who came to ask her in marriage was good enough for her, and she

3	

3	3	only made sport of them.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Once upon a time the king held a great feast, and asked thither all

3	

3	3	her suitors; and they all sat in a row, ranged according to their

3	

3	3	rank--kings, and princes, and dukes, and earls, and counts, and barons,

3	

3	3	and knights. Then the princess came in, and as she passed by them she

3	

3	3	had something spiteful to say to every one. The first was too fat: ‘He’s

3	

3	3	as round as a tub,’ said she. The next was too tall: ‘What a maypole!’

3	

3	3	said she. The next was too short: ‘What a dumpling!’ said she. The

3	

3	3	fourth was too pale, and she called him ‘Wallface.’ The fifth was too

3	

3	3	red, so she called him ‘Coxcomb.’ The sixth was not straight enough;

3	

3	3	so she said he was like a green stick, that had been laid to dry over

3	

3	3	a baker’s oven. And thus she had some joke to crack upon every one: but

3	

3	3	she laughed more than all at a good king who was there. ‘Look at

3	

3	3	him,’ said she; ‘his beard is like an old mop; he shall be called

3	

3	3	Grisly-beard.’ So the king got the nickname of Grisly-beard.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But the old king was very angry when he saw how his daughter behaved,

3	

3	3	and how she ill-treated all his guests; and he vowed that, willing or

3	

3	3	unwilling, she should marry the first man, be he prince or beggar, that

3	

3	3	came to the door.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Two days after there came by a travelling fiddler, who began to play

3	

3	3	under the window and beg alms; and when the king heard him, he said,

3	

3	3	‘Let him come in.’ So they brought in a dirty-looking fellow; and when

3	

3	3	he had sung before the king and the princess, he begged a boon. Then the

3	

3	3	king said, ‘You have sung so well, that I will give you my daughter for

3	

3	3	your wife.’ The princess begged and prayed; but the king said, ‘I have

3	

3	3	sworn to give you to the first comer, and I will keep my word.’ So words

3	

3	3	and tears were of no avail; the parson was sent for, and she was married

3	

3	3	to the fiddler. When this was over the king said, ‘Now get ready to

3	

3	3	go--you must not stay here--you must travel on with your husband.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the fiddler went his way, and took her with him, and they soon came

3	

3	3	to a great wood. ‘Pray,’ said she, ‘whose is this wood?’ ‘It belongs

3	

3	3	to King Grisly-beard,’ answered he; ‘hadst thou taken him, all had been

3	

3	3	thine.’ ‘Ah! unlucky wretch that I am!’ sighed she; ‘would that I had

3	

3	3	married King Grisly-beard!’ Next they came to some fine meadows. ‘Whose

3	

3	3	are these beautiful green meadows?’ said she. ‘They belong to King

3	

3	3	Grisly-beard, hadst thou taken him, they had all been thine.’ ‘Ah!

3	

3	3	unlucky wretch that I am!’ said she; ‘would that I had married King

3	

3	3	Grisly-beard!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then they came to a great city. ‘Whose is this noble city?’ said she.

3	

3	3	‘It belongs to King Grisly-beard; hadst thou taken him, it had all been

3	

3	3	thine.’ ‘Ah! wretch that I am!’ sighed she; ‘why did I not marry King

3	

3	3	Grisly-beard?’ ‘That is no business of mine,’ said the fiddler: ‘why

3	

3	3	should you wish for another husband? Am not I good enough for you?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	At last they came to a small cottage. ‘What a paltry place!’ said she;

3	

3	3	‘to whom does that little dirty hole belong?’ Then the fiddler said,

3	

3	3	‘That is your and my house, where we are to live.’ ‘Where are your

3	

3	3	servants?’ cried she. ‘What do we want with servants?’ said he; ‘you

3	

3	3	must do for yourself whatever is to be done. Now make the fire, and put

3	

3	3	on water and cook my supper, for I am very tired.’ But the princess knew

3	

3	3	nothing of making fires and cooking, and the fiddler was forced to help

3	

3	3	her. When they had eaten a very scanty meal they went to bed; but the

3	

3	3	fiddler called her up very early in the morning to clean the house. Thus

3	

3	3	they lived for two days: and when they had eaten up all there was in the

3	

3	3	cottage, the man said, ‘Wife, we can’t go on thus, spending money and

3	

3	3	earning nothing. You must learn to weave baskets.’ Then he went out and

3	

3	3	cut willows, and brought them home, and she began to weave; but it made

3	

3	3	her fingers very sore. ‘I see this work won’t do,’ said he: ‘try and

3	

3	3	spin; perhaps you will do that better.’ So she sat down and tried to

3	

3	3	spin; but the threads cut her tender fingers till the blood ran. ‘See

3	

3	3	now,’ said the fiddler, ‘you are good for nothing; you can do no work:

3	

3	3	what a bargain I have got! However, I’ll try and set up a trade in pots

3	

3	3	and pans, and you shall stand in the market and sell them.’ ‘Alas!’

3	

3	3	sighed she, ‘if any of my father’s court should pass by and see me

3	

3	3	standing in the market, how they will laugh at me!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But her husband did not care for that, and said she must work, if she

3	

3	3	did not wish to die of hunger. At first the trade went well; for many

3	

3	3	people, seeing such a beautiful woman, went to buy her wares, and paid

3	

3	3	their money without thinking of taking away the goods. They lived on

3	

3	3	this as long as it lasted; and then her husband bought a fresh lot of

3	

3	3	ware, and she sat herself down with it in the corner of the market; but

3	

3	3	a drunken soldier soon came by, and rode his horse against her stall,

3	

3	3	and broke all her goods into a thousand pieces. Then she began to cry,

3	

3	3	and knew not what to do. ‘Ah! what will become of me?’ said she; ‘what

3	

3	3	will my husband say?’ So she ran home and told him all. ‘Who would

3	

3	3	have thought you would have been so silly,’ said he, ‘as to put an

3	

3	3	earthenware stall in the corner of the market, where everybody passes?

3	

3	3	but let us have no more crying; I see you are not fit for this sort of

3	

3	3	work, so I have been to the king’s palace, and asked if they did not

3	

3	3	want a kitchen-maid; and they say they will take you, and there you will

3	

3	3	have plenty to eat.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Thus the princess became a kitchen-maid, and helped the cook to do all

3	

3	3	the dirtiest work; but she was allowed to carry home some of the meat

3	

3	3	that was left, and on this they lived.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	She had not been there long before she heard that the king’s eldest son

3	

3	3	was passing by, going to be married; and she went to one of the windows

3	

3	3	and looked out. Everything was ready, and all the pomp and brightness of

3	

3	3	the court was there. Then she bitterly grieved for the pride and folly

3	

3	3	which had brought her so low. And the servants gave her some of the rich

3	

3	3	meats, which she put into her basket to take home.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	All on a sudden, as she was going out, in came the king’s son in golden

3	

3	3	clothes; and when he saw a beautiful woman at the door, he took her

3	

3	3	by the hand, and said she should be his partner in the dance; but she

3	

3	3	trembled for fear, for she saw that it was King Grisly-beard, who was

3	

3	3	making sport of her. However, he kept fast hold, and led her in; and the

3	

3	3	cover of the basket came off, so that the meats in it fell about. Then

3	

3	3	everybody laughed and jeered at her; and she was so abashed, that she

3	

3	3	wished herself a thousand feet deep in the earth. She sprang to the

3	

3	3	door to run away; but on the steps King Grisly-beard overtook her, and

3	

3	3	brought her back and said, ‘Fear me not! I am the fiddler who has lived

3	

3	3	with you in the hut. I brought you there because I really loved you. I

3	

3	3	am also the soldier that overset your stall. I have done all this only

3	

3	3	to cure you of your silly pride, and to show you the folly of your

3	

3	3	ill-treatment of me. Now all is over: you have learnt wisdom, and it is

3	

3	3	time to hold our marriage feast.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the chamberlains came and brought her the most beautiful robes; and

3	

3	3	her father and his whole court were there already, and welcomed her home

3	

3	3	on her marriage. Joy was in every face and every heart. The feast was

3	

3	3	grand; they danced and sang; all were merry; and I only wish that you

3	

3	3	and I had been of the party.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	IRON HANS

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once upon a time a king who had a great forest near his

3	

3	3	palace, full of all kinds of wild animals. One day he sent out a

3	

3	3	huntsman to shoot him a roe, but he did not come back. ‘Perhaps some

3	

3	3	accident has befallen him,’ said the king, and the next day he sent out

3	

3	3	two more huntsmen who were to search for him, but they too stayed away.

3	

3	3	Then on the third day, he sent for all his huntsmen, and said: ‘Scour

3	

3	3	the whole forest through, and do not give up until you have found all

3	

3	3	three.’ But of these also, none came home again, none were seen again.

3	

3	3	From that time forth, no one would any longer venture into the forest,

3	

3	3	and it lay there in deep stillness and solitude, and nothing was seen

3	

3	3	of it, but sometimes an eagle or a hawk flying over it. This lasted for

3	

3	3	many years, when an unknown huntsman announced himself to the king as

3	

3	3	seeking a situation, and offered to go into the dangerous forest. The

3	

3	3	king, however, would not give his consent, and said: ‘It is not safe in

3	

3	3	there; I fear it would fare with you no better than with the others,

3	

3	3	and you would never come out again.’ The huntsman replied: ‘Lord, I will

3	

3	3	venture it at my own risk, of fear I know nothing.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The huntsman therefore betook himself with his dog to the forest. It was

3	

3	3	not long before the dog fell in with some game on the way, and wanted to

3
