3	3	pursue it; but hardly had the dog run two steps when it stood before a

3	

3	3	deep pool, could go no farther, and a naked arm stretched itself out of

3	

3	3	the water, seized it, and drew it under. When the huntsman saw that, he

3	

3	3	went back and fetched three men to come with buckets and bale out the

3	

3	3	water. When they could see to the bottom there lay a wild man whose body

3	

3	3	was brown like rusty iron, and whose hair hung over his face down to his

3	

3	3	knees. They bound him with cords, and led him away to the castle. There

3	

3	3	was great astonishment over the wild man; the king, however, had him put

3	

3	3	in an iron cage in his courtyard, and forbade the door to be opened

3	

3	3	on pain of death, and the queen herself was to take the key into her

3	

3	3	keeping. And from this time forth everyone could again go into the

3	

3	3	forest with safety.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The king had a son of eight years, who was once playing in the

3	

3	3	courtyard, and while he was playing, his golden ball fell into the cage.

3	

3	3	The boy ran thither and said: ‘Give me my ball out.’ ‘Not till you have

3	

3	3	opened the door for me,’ answered the man. ‘No,’ said the boy, ‘I will

3	

3	3	not do that; the king has forbidden it,’ and ran away. The next day he

3	

3	3	again went and asked for his ball; the wild man said: ‘Open my door,’

3	

3	3	but the boy would not. On the third day the king had ridden out hunting,

3	

3	3	and the boy went once more and said: ‘I cannot open the door even if I

3	

3	3	wished, for I have not the key.’ Then the wild man said: ‘It lies under

3	

3	3	your mother’s pillow, you can get it there.’ The boy, who wanted to have

3	

3	3	his ball back, cast all thought to the winds, and brought the key. The

3	

3	3	door opened with difficulty, and the boy pinched his fingers. When it

3	

3	3	was open the wild man stepped out, gave him the golden ball, and hurried

3	

3	3	away. The boy had become afraid; he called and cried after him: ‘Oh,

3	

3	3	wild man, do not go away, or I shall be beaten!’ The wild man turned

3	

3	3	back, took him up, set him on his shoulder, and went with hasty steps

3	

3	3	into the forest. When the king came home, he observed the empty cage,

3	

3	3	and asked the queen how that had happened. She knew nothing about it,

3	

3	3	and sought the key, but it was gone. She called the boy, but no one

3	

3	3	answered. The king sent out people to seek for him in the fields, but

3	

3	3	they did not find him. Then he could easily guess what had happened, and

3	

3	3	much grief reigned in the royal court.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the wild man had once more reached the dark forest, he took the boy

3	

3	3	down from his shoulder, and said to him: ‘You will never see your father

3	

3	3	and mother again, but I will keep you with me, for you have set me free,

3	

3	3	and I have compassion on you. If you do all I bid you, you shall fare

3	

3	3	well. Of treasure and gold have I enough, and more than anyone in the

3	

3	3	world.’ He made a bed of moss for the boy on which he slept, and the

3	

3	3	next morning the man took him to a well, and said: ‘Behold, the gold

3	

3	3	well is as bright and clear as crystal, you shall sit beside it, and

3	

3	3	take care that nothing falls into it, or it will be polluted. I will

3	

3	3	come every evening to see if you have obeyed my order.’ The boy placed

3	

3	3	himself by the brink of the well, and often saw a golden fish or a

3	

3	3	golden snake show itself therein, and took care that nothing fell in.

3	

3	3	As he was thus sitting, his finger hurt him so violently that he

3	

3	3	involuntarily put it in the water. He drew it quickly out again, but saw

3	

3	3	that it was quite gilded, and whatsoever pains he took to wash the gold

3	

3	3	off again, all was to no purpose. In the evening Iron Hans came back,

3	

3	3	looked at the boy, and said: ‘What has happened to the well?’ ‘Nothing

3	

3	3	nothing,’ he answered, and held his finger behind his back, that the

3	

3	3	man might not see it. But he said: ‘You have dipped your finger into

3	

3	3	the water, this time it may pass, but take care you do not again let

3	

3	3	anything go in.’ By daybreak the boy was already sitting by the well and

3	

3	3	watching it. His finger hurt him again and he passed it over his head,

3	

3	3	and then unhappily a hair fell down into the well. He took it quickly

3	

3	3	out, but it was already quite gilded. Iron Hans came, and already knew

3	

3	3	what had happened. ‘You have let a hair fall into the well,’ said he.

3	

3	3	‘I will allow you to watch by it once more, but if this happens for the

3	

3	3	third time then the well is polluted and you can no longer remain with

3	

3	3	me.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	On the third day, the boy sat by the well, and did not stir his finger,

3	

3	3	however much it hurt him. But the time was long to him, and he looked at

3	

3	3	the reflection of his face on the surface of the water. And as he

3	

3	3	still bent down more and more while he was doing so, and trying to look

3	

3	3	straight into the eyes, his long hair fell down from his shoulders into

3	

3	3	the water. He raised himself up quickly, but the whole of the hair of

3	

3	3	his head was already golden and shone like the sun. You can imagine how

3	

3	3	terrified the poor boy was! He took his pocket-handkerchief and tied it

3	

3	3	round his head, in order that the man might not see it. When he came he

3	

3	3	already knew everything, and said: ‘Take the handkerchief off.’ Then the

3	

3	3	golden hair streamed forth, and let the boy excuse himself as he might,

3	

3	3	it was of no use. ‘You have not stood the trial and can stay here no

3	

3	3	longer. Go forth into the world, there you will learn what poverty is.

3	

3	3	But as you have not a bad heart, and as I mean well by you, there is

3	

3	3	one thing I will grant you; if you fall into any difficulty, come to the

3	

3	3	forest and cry: “Iron Hans,” and then I will come and help you. My

3	

3	3	power is great, greater than you think, and I have gold and silver in

3	

3	3	abundance.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then the king’s son left the forest, and walked by beaten and unbeaten

3	

3	3	paths ever onwards until at length he reached a great city. There he

3	

3	3	looked for work, but could find none, and he learnt nothing by which he

3	

3	3	could help himself. At length he went to the palace, and asked if they

3	

3	3	would take him in. The people about court did not at all know what use

3	

3	3	they could make of him, but they liked him, and told him to stay. At

3	

3	3	length the cook took him into his service, and said he might carry wood

3	

3	3	and water, and rake the cinders together. Once when it so happened that

3	

3	3	no one else was at hand, the cook ordered him to carry the food to the

3	

3	3	royal table, but as he did not like to let his golden hair be seen, he

3	

3	3	kept his little cap on. Such a thing as that had never yet come under

3	

3	3	the king’s notice, and he said: ‘When you come to the royal table you

3	

3	3	must take your hat off.’ He answered: ‘Ah, Lord, I cannot; I have a bad

3	

3	3	sore place on my head.’ Then the king had the cook called before him

3	

3	3	and scolded him, and asked how he could take such a boy as that into his

3	

3	3	service; and that he was to send him away at once. The cook, however,

3	

3	3	had pity on him, and exchanged him for the gardener’s boy.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	And now the boy had to plant and water the garden, hoe and dig, and bear

3	

3	3	the wind and bad weather. Once in summer when he was working alone in

3	

3	3	the garden, the day was so warm he took his little cap off that the air

3	

3	3	might cool him. As the sun shone on his hair it glittered and flashed so

3	

3	3	that the rays fell into the bedroom of the king’s daughter, and up she

3	

3	3	sprang to see what that could be. Then she saw the boy, and cried to

3	

3	3	him: ‘Boy, bring me a wreath of flowers.’ He put his cap on with all

3	

3	3	haste, and gathered wild field-flowers and bound them together. When he

3	

3	3	was ascending the stairs with them, the gardener met him, and said: ‘How

3	

3	3	can you take the king’s daughter a garland of such common flowers? Go

3	

3	3	quickly, and get another, and seek out the prettiest and rarest.’ ‘Oh,

3	

3	3	no,’ replied the boy, ‘the wild ones have more scent, and will please

3	

3	3	her better.’ When he got into the room, the king’s daughter said: ‘Take

3	

3	3	your cap off, it is not seemly to keep it on in my presence.’ He again

3	

3	3	said: ‘I may not, I have a sore head.’ She, however, caught at his

3	

3	3	cap and pulled it off, and then his golden hair rolled down on his

3	

3	3	shoulders, and it was splendid to behold. He wanted to run out, but she

3	

3	3	held him by the arm, and gave him a handful of ducats. With these he

3	

3	3	departed, but he cared nothing for the gold pieces. He took them to the

3	

3	3	gardener, and said: ‘I present them to your children, they can play with

3	

3	3	them.’ The following day the king’s daughter again called to him that he

3	

3	3	was to bring her a wreath of field-flowers, and then he went in with it,

3	

3	3	she instantly snatched at his cap, and wanted to take it away from him,

3	

3	3	but he held it fast with both hands. She again gave him a handful of

3	

3	3	ducats, but he would not keep them, and gave them to the gardener for

3	

3	3	playthings for his children. On the third day things went just the

3	

3	3	same; she could not get his cap away from him, and he would not have her

3	

3	3	money.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Not long afterwards, the country was overrun by war. The king gathered

3	

3	3	together his people, and did not know whether or not he could offer any

3	

3	3	opposition to the enemy, who was superior in strength and had a mighty

3	

3	3	army. Then said the gardener’s boy: ‘I am grown up, and will go to the

3	

3	3	wars also, only give me a horse.’ The others laughed, and said: ‘Seek

3	

3	3	one for yourself when we are gone, we will leave one behind us in the

3	

3	3	stable for you.’ When they had gone forth, he went into the stable, and

3	

3	3	led the horse out; it was lame of one foot, and limped hobblety jib,

3	

3	3	hobblety jib; nevertheless he mounted it, and rode away to the dark

3	

3	3	forest. When he came to the outskirts, he called ‘Iron Hans’ three

3	

3	3	times so loudly that it echoed through the trees. Thereupon the wild man

3	

3	3	appeared immediately, and said: ‘What do you desire?’ ‘I want a strong

3	

3	3	steed, for I am going to the wars.’ ‘That you shall have, and still more

3	

3	3	than you ask for.’ Then the wild man went back into the forest, and it

3	

3	3	was not long before a stable-boy came out of it, who led a horse that

3	

3	3	snorted with its nostrils, and could hardly be restrained, and behind

3	

3	3	them followed a great troop of warriors entirely equipped in iron, and

3	

3	3	their swords flashed in the sun. The youth made over his three-legged

3	

3	3	horse to the stable-boy, mounted the other, and rode at the head of the

3	

3	3	soldiers. When he got near the battlefield a great part of the king’s

3	

3	3	men had already fallen, and little was wanting to make the rest give

3	

3	3	way. Then the youth galloped thither with his iron soldiers, broke like

3	

3	3	a hurricane over the enemy, and beat down all who opposed him. They

3	

3	3	began to flee, but the youth pursued, and never stopped, until there

3	

3	3	was not a single man left. Instead of returning to the king, however, he

3	

3	3	conducted his troop by byways back to the forest, and called forth Iron

3	

3	3	Hans. ‘What do you desire?’ asked the wild man. ‘Take back your horse

3	

3	3	and your troops, and give me my three-legged horse again.’ All that he

3	

3	3	asked was done, and soon he was riding on his three-legged horse. When

3	

3	3	the king returned to his palace, his daughter went to meet him, and

3	

3	3	wished him joy of his victory. ‘I am not the one who carried away the

3	

3	3	victory,’ said he, ‘but a strange knight who came to my assistance with

3	

3	3	his soldiers.’ The daughter wanted to hear who the strange knight was,

3	

3	3	but the king did not know, and said: ‘He followed the enemy, and I did

3	

3	3	not see him again.’ She inquired of the gardener where his boy was, but

3	

3	3	he smiled, and said: ‘He has just come home on his three-legged horse,

3	

3	3	and the others have been mocking him, and crying: “Here comes our

3	

3	3	hobblety jib back again!” They asked, too: “Under what hedge have you

3	

3	3	been lying sleeping all the time?” So he said: “I did the best of all,

3	

3	3	and it would have gone badly without me.” And then he was still more

3	

3	3	ridiculed.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The king said to his daughter: ‘I will proclaim a great feast that shall

3	

3	3	last for three days, and you shall throw a golden apple. Perhaps the

3	

3	3	unknown man will show himself.’ When the feast was announced, the youth

3	

3	3	went out to the forest, and called Iron Hans. ‘What do you desire?’

3	

3	3	asked he. ‘That I may catch the king’s daughter’s golden apple.’ ‘It is

3	

3	3	as safe as if you had it already,’ said Iron Hans. ‘You shall likewise

3	

3	3	have a suit of red armour for the occasion, and ride on a spirited

3	

3	3	chestnut-horse.’ When the day came, the youth galloped to the spot, took

3	

3	3	his place amongst the knights, and was recognized by no one. The king’s

3	

3	3	daughter came forward, and threw a golden apple to the knights, but none

3	

3	3	of them caught it but he, only as soon as he had it he galloped away.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	On the second day Iron Hans equipped him as a white knight, and gave him

3	

3	3	a white horse. Again he was the only one who caught the apple, and

3	

3	3	he did not linger an instant, but galloped off with it. The king grew

3	

3	3	angry, and said: ‘That is not allowed; he must appear before me and tell

3	

3	3	his name.’ He gave the order that if the knight who caught the apple,

3	

3	3	should go away again they should pursue him, and if he would not come

3	

3	3	back willingly, they were to cut him down and stab him.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	On the third day, he received from Iron Hans a suit of black armour and

3	

3	3	a black horse, and again he caught the apple. But when he was riding off

3	

3	3	with it, the king’s attendants pursued him, and one of them got so near

3	

3	3	him that he wounded the youth’s leg with the point of his sword. The

3	

3	3	youth nevertheless escaped from them, but his horse leapt so violently

3	

3	3	that the helmet fell from the youth’s head, and they could see that he

3	

3	3	had golden hair. They rode back and announced this to the king.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The following day the king’s daughter asked the gardener about his

3	

3	3	boy. ‘He is at work in the garden; the queer creature has been at the

3	

3	3	festival too, and only came home yesterday evening; he has likewise

3	

3	3	shown my children three golden apples which he has won.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The king had him summoned into his presence, and he came and again had

3	

3	3	his little cap on his head. But the king’s daughter went up to him and

3	

3	3	took it off, and then his golden hair fell down over his shoulders, and

3	

3	3	he was so handsome that all were amazed. ‘Are you the knight who came

3	

3	3	every day to the festival, always in different colours, and who caught

3	

3	3	the three golden apples?’ asked the king. ‘Yes,’ answered he, ‘and here

3	

3	3	the apples are,’ and he took them out of his pocket, and returned them

3	

3	3	to the king. ‘If you desire further proof, you may see the wound which

3	

3	3	your people gave me when they followed me. But I am likewise the knight

3	

3	3	who helped you to your victory over your enemies.’ ‘If you can perform

3	

3	3	such deeds as that, you are no gardener’s boy; tell me, who is your

3	

3	3	father?’ ‘My father is a mighty king, and gold have I in plenty as great

3	

3	3	as I require.’ ‘I well see,’ said the king, ‘that I owe my thanks to

3	

3	3	you; can I do anything to please you?’ ‘Yes,’ answered he, ‘that indeed

3	

3	3	you can. Give me your daughter to wife.’ The maiden laughed, and said:

3	

3	3	‘He does not stand much on ceremony, but I have already seen by his

3	

3	3	golden hair that he was no gardener’s boy,’ and then she went and

3	

3	3	kissed him. His father and mother came to the wedding, and were in great

3	

3	3	delight, for they had given up all hope of ever seeing their dear

3	

3	3	son again. And as they were sitting at the marriage-feast, the music

3	

3	3	suddenly stopped, the doors opened, and a stately king came in with a

3	

3	3	great retinue. He went up to the youth, embraced him and said: ‘I am

3	

3	3	Iron Hans, and was by enchantment a wild man, but you have set me free;

3	

3	3	all the treasures which I possess, shall be your property.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	CAT-SKIN

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once a king, whose queen had hair of the purest gold, and was

3	

3	3	so beautiful that her match was not to be met with on the whole face of

3	

3	3	the earth. But this beautiful queen fell ill, and when she felt that her

3	

3	3	end drew near she called the king to her and said, ‘Promise me that you

3	

3	3	will never marry again, unless you meet with a wife who is as beautiful

3	

3	3	as I am, and who has golden hair like mine.’ Then when the king in his

3	

3	3	grief promised all she asked, she shut her eyes and died. But the king

3	

3	3	was not to be comforted, and for a long time never thought of taking

3	

3	3	another wife. At last, however, his wise men said, ‘this will not do;

3	

3	3	the king must marry again, that we may have a queen.’ So messengers were

3	

3	3	sent far and wide, to seek for a bride as beautiful as the late queen.

3	

3	3	But there was no princess in the world so beautiful; and if there had

3	

3	3	been, still there was not one to be found who had golden hair. So the

3	

3	3	messengers came home, and had had all their trouble for nothing.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Now the king had a daughter, who was just as beautiful as her mother,

3	

3	3	and had the same golden hair. And when she was grown up, the king looked

3	

3	3	at her and saw that she was just like this late queen: then he said to

3	

3	3	his courtiers, ‘May I not marry my daughter? She is the very image of my

3	

3	3	dead wife: unless I have her, I shall not find any bride upon the whole

3	

3	3	earth, and you say there must be a queen.’ When the courtiers heard this

3	

3	3	they were shocked, and said, ‘Heaven forbid that a father should marry

3	

3	3	his daughter! Out of so great a sin no good can come.’ And his daughter

3	

3	3	was also shocked, but hoped the king would soon give up such thoughts;

3	

3	3	so she said to him, ‘Before I marry anyone I must have three dresses:

3	

3	3	one must be of gold, like the sun; another must be of shining silver,

3	

3	3	like the moon; and a third must be dazzling as the stars: besides this,

3	

3	3	I want a mantle of a thousand different kinds of fur put together, to

3	

3	3	which every beast in the kingdom must give a part of his skin.’ And thus

3	

3	3	she thought he would think of the matter no more. But the king made the

3	

3	3	most skilful workmen in his kingdom weave the three dresses: one golden,

3	

3	3	like the sun; another silvery, like the moon; and a third sparkling,

3	

3	3	like the stars: and his hunters were told to hunt out all the beasts in

3	

3	3	his kingdom, and to take the finest fur out of their skins: and thus a

3	

3	3	mantle of a thousand furs was made.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When all were ready, the king sent them to her; but she got up in the

3	

3	3	night when all were asleep, and took three of her trinkets, a golden

3	

3	3	ring, a golden necklace, and a golden brooch, and packed the three

3	

3	3	dresses--of the sun, the moon, and the stars--up in a nutshell, and

3	

3	3	wrapped herself up in the mantle made of all sorts of fur, and besmeared

3	

3	3	her face and hands with soot. Then she threw herself upon Heaven for

3	

3	3	help in her need, and went away, and journeyed on the whole night, till

3	

3	3	at last she came to a large wood. As she was very tired, she sat herself

3	

3	3	down in the hollow of a tree and soon fell asleep: and there she slept

3	

3	3	on till it was midday.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Now as the king to whom the wood belonged was hunting in it, his dogs

3	

3	3	came to the tree, and began to snuff about, and run round and round, and

3	

3	3	bark. ‘Look sharp!’ said the king to the huntsmen, ‘and see what sort

3	

3	3	of game lies there.’ And the huntsmen went up to the tree, and when they

3	

3	3	came back again said, ‘In the hollow tree there lies a most wonderful

3	

3	3	beast, such as we never saw before; its skin seems to be of a thousand

3	

3	3	kinds of fur, but there it lies fast asleep.’ ‘See,’ said the king, ‘if

3	

3	3	you can catch it alive, and we will take it with us.’ So the huntsmen

3	

3	3	took it up, and the maiden awoke and was greatly frightened, and said,

3	

3	3	‘I am a poor child that has neither father nor mother left; have pity on

3	

3	3	me and take me with you.’ Then they said, ‘Yes, Miss Cat-skin, you will

3	

3	3	do for the kitchen; you can sweep up the ashes, and do things of that

3	

3	3	sort.’ So they put her into the coach, and took her home to the king’s

3	

3	3	palace. Then they showed her a little corner under the staircase, where

3	

3	3	no light of day ever peeped in, and said, ‘Cat-skin, you may lie and

3	

3	3	sleep there.’ And she was sent into the kitchen, and made to fetch wood

3	

3	3	and water, to blow the fire, pluck the poultry, pick the herbs, sift the

3	

3	3	ashes, and do all the dirty work.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Thus Cat-skin lived for a long time very sorrowfully. ‘Ah! pretty

3	

3	3	princess!’ thought she, ‘what will now become of thee?’ But it happened

3	

3	3	one day that a feast was to be held in the king’s castle, so she said to

3	

3	3	the cook, ‘May I go up a little while and see what is going on? I will

3	

3	3	take care and stand behind the door.’ And the cook said, ‘Yes, you may

3	

3	3	go, but be back again in half an hour’s time, to rake out the ashes.’

3	

3	3	Then she took her little lamp, and went into her cabin, and took off the

3	

3	3	fur skin, and washed the soot from off her face and hands, so that her

3	

3	3	beauty shone forth like the sun from behind the clouds. She next opened

3	

3	3	her nutshell, and brought out of it the dress that shone like the sun,

3	

3	3	and so went to the feast. Everyone made way for her, for nobody knew

3	

3	3	her, and they thought she could be no less than a king’s daughter. But

3	

3	3	the king came up to her, and held out his hand and danced with her; and

3	

3	3	he thought in his heart, ‘I never saw any one half so beautiful.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When the dance was at an end she curtsied; and when the king looked

3	

3	3	round for her, she was gone, no one knew wither. The guards that stood

3	

3	3	at the castle gate were called in: but they had seen no one. The truth

3	

3	3	was, that she had run into her little cabin, pulled off her dress,

3	

3	3	blackened her face and hands, put on the fur-skin cloak, and was

3	

3	3	Cat-skin again. When she went into the kitchen to her work, and began

3	

3	3	to rake the ashes, the cook said, ‘Let that alone till the morning, and

3	

3	3	heat the king’s soup; I should like to run up now and give a peep: but

3	

3	3	take care you don’t let a hair fall into it, or you will run a chance of

3	

3	3	never eating again.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	As soon as the cook went away, Cat-skin heated the king’s soup, and

3	

3	3	toasted a slice of bread first, as nicely as ever she could; and when it

3	

3	3	was ready, she went and looked in the cabin for her little golden ring,

3	

3	3	and put it into the dish in which the soup was. When the dance was over,

3	

3	3	the king ordered his soup to be brought in; and it pleased him so well,

3	

3	3	that he thought he had never tasted any so good before. At the bottom

3	

3	3	he saw a gold ring lying; and as he could not make out how it had got

3	

3	3	there, he ordered the cook to be sent for. The cook was frightened when

3	

3	3	he heard the order, and said to Cat-skin, ‘You must have let a hair fall

3	

3	3	into the soup; if it be so, you will have a good beating.’ Then he went

3	

3	3	before the king, and he asked him who had cooked the soup. ‘I did,’

3	

3	3	answered the cook. But the king said, ‘That is not true; it was better

3	

3	3	done than you could do it.’ Then he answered, ‘To tell the truth I did

3	

3	3	not cook it, but Cat-skin did.’ ‘Then let Cat-skin come up,’ said the

3	

3	3	king: and when she came he said to her, ‘Who are you?’ ‘I am a poor

3	

3	3	child,’ said she, ‘that has lost both father and mother.’ ‘How came you

3	

3	3	in my palace?’ asked he. ‘I am good for nothing,’ said she, ‘but to be

3	

3	3	scullion-girl, and to have boots and shoes thrown at my head.’ ‘But how

3	

3	3	did you get the ring that was in the soup?’ asked the king. Then she

3	

3	3	would not own that she knew anything about the ring; so the king sent

3	

3	3	her away again about her business.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	After a time there was another feast, and Cat-skin asked the cook to let

3	

3	3	her go up and see it as before. ‘Yes,’ said he, ‘but come again in half

3	

3	3	an hour, and cook the king the soup that he likes so much.’ Then she

3	

3	3	ran to her little cabin, washed herself quickly, and took her dress

3	

3	3	out which was silvery as the moon, and put it on; and when she went in,

3	

3	3	looking like a king’s daughter, the king went up to her, and rejoiced at

3	

3	3	seeing her again, and when the dance began he danced with her. After the

3	

3	3	dance was at an end she managed to slip out, so slyly that the king did

3	

3	3	not see where she was gone; but she sprang into her little cabin, and

3	

3	3	made herself into Cat-skin again, and went into the kitchen to cook the

3	

3	3	soup. Whilst the cook was above stairs, she got the golden necklace and

3	

3	3	dropped it into the soup; then it was brought to the king, who ate it,

3	

3	3	and it pleased him as well as before; so he sent for the cook, who

3	

3	3	was again forced to tell him that Cat-skin had cooked it. Cat-skin was

3	

3	3	brought again before the king, but she still told him that she was only

3	

3	3	fit to have boots and shoes thrown at her head.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	But when the king had ordered a feast to be got ready for the third

3	

3	3	time, it happened just the same as before. ‘You must be a witch,

3	

3	3	Cat-skin,’ said the cook; ‘for you always put something into your soup,

3	

3	3	so that it pleases the king better than mine.’ However, he let her go up

3	

3	3	as before. Then she put on her dress which sparkled like the stars, and

3	

3	3	went into the ball-room in it; and the king danced with her again, and

3	

3	3	thought she had never looked so beautiful as she did then. So whilst

3	

3	3	he was dancing with her, he put a gold ring on her finger without her

3	

3	3	seeing it, and ordered that the dance should be kept up a long time.

3	

3	3	When it was at an end, he would have held her fast by the hand, but she

3	

3	3	slipped away, and sprang so quickly through the crowd that he lost sight

3	

3	3	of her: and she ran as fast as she could into her little cabin under

3	

3	3	the stairs. But this time she kept away too long, and stayed beyond the

3	

3	3	half-hour; so she had not time to take off her fine dress, and threw her

3	

3	3	fur mantle over it, and in her haste did not blacken herself all over

3	

3	3	with soot, but left one of her fingers white.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then she ran into the kitchen, and cooked the king’s soup; and as soon

3	

3	3	as the cook was gone, she put the golden brooch into the dish. When the

3	

3	3	king got to the bottom, he ordered Cat-skin to be called once more, and

3	

3	3	soon saw the white finger, and the ring that he had put on it whilst

3	

3	3	they were dancing: so he seized her hand, and kept fast hold of it, and

3	

3	3	when she wanted to loose herself and spring away, the fur cloak fell off

3	

3	3	a little on one side, and the starry dress sparkled underneath it.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Then he got hold of the fur and tore it off, and her golden hair and

3	

3	3	beautiful form were seen, and she could no longer hide herself: so she

3	

3	3	washed the soot and ashes from her face, and showed herself to be the

3	

3	3	most beautiful princess upon the face of the earth. But the king said,

3	

3	3	‘You are my beloved bride, and we will never more be parted from each

3	

3	3	other.’ And the wedding feast was held, and a merry day it was, as ever

3	

3	3	was heard of or seen in that country, or indeed in any other.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	SNOW-WHITE AND ROSE-RED

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	There was once a poor widow who lived in a lonely cottage. In front of

3	

3	3	the cottage was a garden wherein stood two rose-trees, one of which bore

3	

3	3	white and the other red roses. She had two children who were like the

3	

3	3	two rose-trees, and one was called Snow-white, and the other Rose-red.

3	

3	3	They were as good and happy, as busy and cheerful as ever two children

3	

3	3	in the world were, only Snow-white was more quiet and gentle than

3	

3	3	Rose-red. Rose-red liked better to run about in the meadows and fields

3	

3	3	seeking flowers and catching butterflies; but Snow-white sat at home

3	

3	3	with her mother, and helped her with her housework, or read to her when

3	

3	3	there was nothing to do.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The two children were so fond of one another that they always held each

3	

3	3	other by the hand when they went out together, and when Snow-white said:

3	

3	3	‘We will not leave each other,’ Rose-red answered: ‘Never so long as we

3	

3	3	live,’ and their mother would add: ‘What one has she must share with the

3	

3	3	other.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	They often ran about the forest alone and gathered red berries, and no

3	

3	3	beasts did them any harm, but came close to them trustfully. The little

3	

3	3	hare would eat a cabbage-leaf out of their hands, the roe grazed by

3	

3	3	their side, the stag leapt merrily by them, and the birds sat still upon

3	

3	3	the boughs, and sang whatever they knew.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	No mishap overtook them; if they had stayed too late in the forest, and

3	

3	3	night came on, they laid themselves down near one another upon the moss,

3	

3	3	and slept until morning came, and their mother knew this and did not

3	

3	3	worry on their account.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Once when they had spent the night in the wood and the dawn had roused

3	

3	3	them, they saw a beautiful child in a shining white dress sitting near

3	

3	3	their bed. He got up and looked quite kindly at them, but said nothing

3	

3	3	and went into the forest. And when they looked round they found that

3	

3	3	they had been sleeping quite close to a precipice, and would certainly

3	

3	3	have fallen into it in the darkness if they had gone only a few paces

3	

3	3	further. And their mother told them that it must have been the angel who

3	

3	3	watches over good children.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Snow-white and Rose-red kept their mother’s little cottage so neat that

3	

3	3	it was a pleasure to look inside it. In the summer Rose-red took care

3	

3	3	of the house, and every morning laid a wreath of flowers by her mother’s

3	

3	3	bed before she awoke, in which was a rose from each tree. In the winter

3	

3	3	Snow-white lit the fire and hung the kettle on the hob. The kettle

3	

3	3	was of brass and shone like gold, so brightly was it polished. In the

3	

3	3	evening, when the snowflakes fell, the mother said: ‘Go, Snow-white, and

3	

3	3	bolt the door,’ and then they sat round the hearth, and the mother took

3	

3	3	her spectacles and read aloud out of a large book, and the two girls

3	

3	3	listened as they sat and spun. And close by them lay a lamb upon the

3	

3	3	floor, and behind them upon a perch sat a white dove with its head

3	

3	3	hidden beneath its wings.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	One evening, as they were thus sitting comfortably together, someone

3	

3	3	knocked at the door as if he wished to be let in. The mother said:

3	

3	3	‘Quick, Rose-red, open the door, it must be a traveller who is seeking

3	

3	3	shelter.’ Rose-red went and pushed back the bolt, thinking that it was a

3	

3	3	poor man, but it was not; it was a bear that stretched his broad, black

3	

3	3	head within the door.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Rose-red screamed and sprang back, the lamb bleated, the dove fluttered,

3	

3	3	and Snow-white hid herself behind her mother’s bed. But the bear began

3	

3	3	to speak and said: ‘Do not be afraid, I will do you no harm! I am

3	

3	3	half-frozen, and only want to warm myself a little beside you.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	‘Poor bear,’ said the mother, ‘lie down by the fire, only take care that

3	

3	3	you do not burn your coat.’ Then she cried: ‘Snow-white, Rose-red, come

3	

3	3	out, the bear will do you no harm, he means well.’ So they both came

3	

3	3	out, and by-and-by the lamb and dove came nearer, and were not afraid

3	

3	3	of him. The bear said: ‘Here, children, knock the snow out of my coat a

3	

3	3	little’; so they brought the broom and swept the bear’s hide clean;

3	

3	3	and he stretched himself by the fire and growled contentedly and

3	

3	3	comfortably. It was not long before they grew quite at home, and played

3	

3	3	tricks with their clumsy guest. They tugged his hair with their hands,

3	

3	3	put their feet upon his back and rolled him about, or they took a

3	

3	3	hazel-switch and beat him, and when he growled they laughed. But the

3	

3	3	bear took it all in good part, only when they were too rough he called

3	

3	3	out: ‘Leave me alive, children,

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	  Snow-white, Rose-red,

3	

3	3	  Will you beat your wooer dead?’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When it was bed-time, and the others went to bed, the mother said to the

3	

3	3	bear: ‘You can lie there by the hearth, and then you will be safe from

3	

3	3	the cold and the bad weather.’ As soon as day dawned the two children

3	

3	3	let him out, and he trotted across the snow into the forest.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Henceforth the bear came every evening at the same time, laid himself

3	

3	3	down by the hearth, and let the children amuse themselves with him as

3	

3	3	much as they liked; and they got so used to him that the doors were

3	

3	3	never fastened until their black friend had arrived.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	When spring had come and all outside was green, the bear said one

3	

3	3	morning to Snow-white: ‘Now I must go away, and cannot come back for the

3	

3	3	whole summer.’ ‘Where are you going, then, dear bear?’ asked Snow-white.

3	

3	3	‘I must go into the forest and guard my treasures from the wicked

3	

3	3	dwarfs. In the winter, when the earth is frozen hard, they are obliged

3	

3	3	to stay below and cannot work their way through; but now, when the sun

3	

3	3	has thawed and warmed the earth, they break through it, and come out to

3	

3	3	pry and steal; and what once gets into their hands, and in their caves,

3	

3	3	does not easily see daylight again.’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Snow-white was quite sorry at his departure, and as she unbolted the

3	

3	3	door for him, and the bear was hurrying out, he caught against the bolt

3	

3	3	and a piece of his hairy coat was torn off, and it seemed to Snow-white

3	

3	3	as if she had seen gold shining through it, but she was not sure about

3	

3	3	it. The bear ran away quickly, and was soon out of sight behind the

3	

3	3	trees.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	A short time afterwards the mother sent her children into the forest

3	

3	3	to get firewood. There they found a big tree which lay felled on the

3	

3	3	ground, and close by the trunk something was jumping backwards and

3	

3	3	forwards in the grass, but they could not make out what it was. When

3	

3	3	they came nearer they saw a dwarf with an old withered face and a

3	

3	3	snow-white beard a yard long. The end of the beard was caught in a

3	

3	3	crevice of the tree, and the little fellow was jumping about like a dog

3	

3	3	tied to a rope, and did not know what to do.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	He glared at the girls with his fiery red eyes and cried: ‘Why do you

3	

3	3	stand there? Can you not come here and help me?’ ‘What are you up to,

3	

3	3	little man?’ asked Rose-red. ‘You stupid, prying goose!’ answered the

3	

3	3	dwarf: ‘I was going to split the tree to get a little wood for cooking.

3	

3	3	The little bit of food that we people get is immediately burnt up with

3	

3	3	heavy logs; we do not swallow so much as you coarse, greedy folk. I had

3	

3	3	just driven the wedge safely in, and everything was going as I wished;

3	

3	3	but the cursed wedge was too smooth and suddenly sprang out, and the

3	

3	3	tree closed so quickly that I could not pull out my beautiful white

3	

3	3	beard; so now it is tight and I cannot get away, and the silly, sleek,

3	

3	3	milk-faced things laugh! Ugh! how odious you are!’

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The children tried very hard, but they could not pull the beard out, it

3	

3	3	was caught too fast. ‘I will run and fetch someone,’ said Rose-red. ‘You

3	

3	3	senseless goose!’ snarled the dwarf; ‘why should you fetch someone? You

3	

3	3	are already two too many for me; can you not think of something better?’

3	

3	3	‘Don’t be impatient,’ said Snow-white, ‘I will help you,’ and she pulled

3	

3	3	her scissors out of her pocket, and cut off the end of the beard.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	As soon as the dwarf felt himself free he laid hold of a bag which lay

3	

3	3	amongst the roots of the tree, and which was full of gold, and lifted it

3	

3	3	up, grumbling to himself: ‘Uncouth people, to cut off a piece of my fine

3	

3	3	beard. Bad luck to you!’ and then he swung the bag upon his back, and

3	

3	3	went off without even once looking at the children.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Some time afterwards Snow-white and Rose-red went to catch a dish

3	

3	3	of fish. As they came near the brook they saw something like a large

3	

3	3	grasshopper jumping towards the water, as if it were going to leap in.

3	

3	3	They ran to it and found it was the dwarf. ‘Where are you going?’ said

3	

3	3	Rose-red; ‘you surely don’t want to go into the water?’ ‘I am not such

3	

3	3	a fool!’ cried the dwarf; ‘don’t you see that the accursed fish wants

3	

3	3	to pull me in?’ The little man had been sitting there fishing, and

3	

3	3	unluckily the wind had tangled up his beard with the fishing-line; a

3	

3	3	moment later a big fish made a bite and the feeble creature had not

3	

3	3	strength to pull it out; the fish kept the upper hand and pulled the

3	

3	3	dwarf towards him. He held on to all the reeds and rushes, but it was of

3	

3	3	little good, for he was forced to follow the movements of the fish, and

3	

3	3	was in urgent danger of being dragged into the water.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The girls came just in time; they held him fast and tried to free his

3	

3	3	beard from the line, but all in vain, beard and line were entangled fast

3	

3	3	together. There was nothing to do but to bring out the scissors and cut

3	

3	3	the beard, whereby a small part of it was lost. When the dwarf saw that

3	

3	3	he screamed out: ‘Is that civil, you toadstool, to disfigure a man’s

3	

3	3	face? Was it not enough to clip off the end of my beard? Now you have

3	

3	3	cut off the best part of it. I cannot let myself be seen by my people.

3	

3	3	I wish you had been made to run the soles off your shoes!’ Then he took

3	

3	3	out a sack of pearls which lay in the rushes, and without another word

3	

3	3	he dragged it away and disappeared behind a stone.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	It happened that soon afterwards the mother sent the two children to the

3	

3	3	town to buy needles and thread, and laces and ribbons. The road led them

3	

3	3	across a heath upon which huge pieces of rock lay strewn about. There

3	

3	3	they noticed a large bird hovering in the air, flying slowly round and

3	

3	3	round above them; it sank lower and lower, and at last settled near a

3	

3	3	rock not far away. Immediately they heard a loud, piteous cry. They ran

3	

3	3	up and saw with horror that the eagle had seized their old acquaintance

3	

3	3	the dwarf, and was going to carry him off.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The children, full of pity, at once took tight hold of the little man,

3	

3	3	and pulled against the eagle so long that at last he let his booty go.

3	

3	3	As soon as the dwarf had recovered from his first fright he cried

3	

3	3	with his shrill voice: ‘Could you not have done it more carefully! You

3	

3	3	dragged at my brown coat so that it is all torn and full of holes, you

3	

3	3	clumsy creatures!’ Then he took up a sack full of precious stones, and

3	

3	3	slipped away again under the rock into his hole. The girls, who by

3	

3	3	this time were used to his ingratitude, went on their way and did their

3	

3	3	business in town.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	As they crossed the heath again on their way home they surprised the

3	

3	3	dwarf, who had emptied out his bag of precious stones in a clean spot,

3	

3	3	and had not thought that anyone would come there so late. The evening

3	

3	3	sun shone upon the brilliant stones; they glittered and sparkled with

3	

3	3	all colours so beautifully that the children stood still and stared

3	

3	3	at them. ‘Why do you stand gaping there?’ cried the dwarf, and his

3	

3	3	ashen-grey face became copper-red with rage. He was still cursing when a

3	

3	3	loud growling was heard, and a black bear came trotting towards them out

3	

3	3	of the forest. The dwarf sprang up in a fright, but he could not reach

3	

3	3	his cave, for the bear was already close. Then in the dread of his heart

3	

3	3	he cried: ‘Dear Mr Bear, spare me, I will give you all my treasures;

3	

3	3	look, the beautiful jewels lying there! Grant me my life; what do you

3	

3	3	want with such a slender little fellow as I? you would not feel me

3	

3	3	between your teeth. Come, take these two wicked girls, they are tender

3	

3	3	morsels for you, fat as young quails; for mercy’s sake eat them!’ The

3	

3	3	bear took no heed of his words, but gave the wicked creature a single

3	

3	3	blow with his paw, and he did not move again.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The girls had run away, but the bear called to them: ‘Snow-white and

3	

3	3	Rose-red, do not be afraid; wait, I will come with you.’ Then they

3	

3	3	recognized his voice and waited, and when he came up to them suddenly

3	

3	3	his bearskin fell off, and he stood there a handsome man, clothed all in

3	

3	3	gold. ‘I am a king’s son,’ he said, ‘and I was bewitched by that wicked

3	

3	3	dwarf, who had stolen my treasures; I have had to run about the forest

3	

3	3	as a savage bear until I was freed by his death. Now he has got his

3	

3	3	well-deserved punishment.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Snow-white was married to him, and Rose-red to his brother, and they

3	

3	3	divided between them the great treasure which the dwarf had gathered

3	

3	3	together in his cave. The old mother lived peacefully and happily with

3	

3	3	her children for many years. She took the two rose-trees with her, and

3	

3	3	they stood before her window, and every year bore the most beautiful

3	

3	3	roses, white and red.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	*****

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The Brothers Grimm, Jacob (1785-1863) and Wilhelm (1786-1859), were born

3	

3	3	in Hanau, near Frankfurt, in the German state of Hesse. Throughout

3	

3	3	their lives they remained close friends, and both studied law at Marburg

3	

3	3	University. Jacob was a pioneer in the study of German philology,

3	

3	3	and although Wilhelm’s work was hampered by poor health the brothers

3	

3	3	collaborated in the creation of a German dictionary, not completed until

3	

3	3	a century after their deaths. But they were best (and universally) known

3	

3	3	for the collection of over two hundred folk tales they made from oral

3	

3	3	sources and published in two volumes of ‘Nursery and Household Tales’ in

3	

3	3	1812 and 1814. Although their intention was to preserve such material as

3	

3	3	part of German cultural and literary history, and their collection was

3	

3	3	first published with scholarly notes and no illustration, the tales soon

3	

3	3	came into the possession of young readers. This was in part due to Edgar

3	

3	3	Taylor, who made the first English translation in 1823, selecting about

3	

3	3	fifty stories ‘with the amusement of some young friends principally in

3	

3	3	view.’ They have been an essential ingredient of children’s reading ever

3	

3	3	since.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	End of Project Gutenberg’s Grimms’ Fairy Tales, by The Brothers Grimm

3

3	3	Section 4.  Information about Donations to the Project Gutenberg

3	

3	3	Literary Archive Foundation

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	Project Gutenberg-tm depends upon and cannot survive without wide

3	

3	3	spread public support and donations to carry out its mission of

3	

3	3	increasing the number of public domain and licensed works that can be

3	

3	3	freely distributed in machine readable form accessible by the widest

3	

3	3	array of equipment including outdated equipment.  Many small donations

3	

3	3	($1 to $5,000) are particularly important to maintaining tax exempt

3	

3	3	status with the IRS.

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	The Foundation is committed to complying with the laws regulating

3	

3	3	charities and charitable donations in all 50 states of the United

3	

3	3	States.  Compliance requirements are not uniform and it takes a

3	

3	3	considerable effort, much paperwork and many fees to meet and keep up

3	

3	3	with these requirements.  We do not solicit donations in locations

3	

3	3	where we have not received written confirmation of compliance.  To

3	

3	3	SEND DONATIONS or determine the status of compliance for any

3	

3	3	particular state visit http://pglaf.org

3	

3	3	

3	

3	3	While we cannot and do not solicit contributions from states where we

3	

3	3	have not met the solicitation requirements, we know of no prohibition

3	

3	3	against accepting unsolicited donations from donors in such states who

3	

3	3	approach us with offers to donate.

3	

3	3	

3

3	

3	3	any statements concerning tax treatment of donations received from

3	

3	3	outside the United States.  U.S. laws alone swamp our small staff.

3	3	works.

3	

3	3	concept of a library of electronic works that could be freely shared

3	

3	3	with anyone.  For thirty years, he produced and distributed Project

3	

3	3	Gutenberg-tm eBooks with only a loose network of volunteer support.
